c4J

Glossary

The following glossary provides clear definitions of essential terms used in this book that are frequently misunderstood or potentially confusing.

Yeshua

The original Hebrew name of Jesus Christ, meaning “salvation” or “The Lord saves.” Using the name Yeshua emphasizes Jesus’ Jewish identity, context, and mission within the story of Israel.

Eternal

In modern English, "eternal" typically means endless, everlasting, infinite, or unending—used in phrases like “Eternal Conscious Torment.” The modern definition is based on the Greek philosophical understanding and assumes infinite duration with no endpoint.

In biblical usage, however, "eternal" (Hebrew: olam; Greek: aiōn / aiōnios) does not mean endless time. Depending on the context, it can refer to an age, era, or an undefined period, or it can describe volume, kind, quality, and fullness rather than length—something whole, mature, or reflecting God’s realm.

While some English translations use "age" to translate olam/aion, many popular versions overlook this nuance, opting for "eternal" instead.

Eternal Life

“Eternal life” is an ancient Jewish idiom contrasted with "fleeting life"—a life absorbed in the temporary, the material, and the non-spiritual. It refers not primarily to living forever, but to the quality and depth of life lived in alignment with God’s will. Jesus defines eternal life relationally, not chronologically: “This is eternal life: that they know You…” (John 17:3).

Life

In modern English, life refers to the biological existence of an individual human being or animal.

In biblical usage, however, "life" usually means a rich, full existence marked by blessing, well-being, flourishing, and God’s favor, rather than just physical survival. For instance: "I have come that they may have life, and have it to the full." (John 10:10). Qualitative rather than quantitative. Likewise, to “choose life” (Deuteronomy 30:19) is to live in alignment with God’s covenantal purposes.

Gnosticism

An ancient movement taught that salvation—particularly with respect to the afterlife—came through special knowledge (gnōsis). It regarded the material world as inherently evil and believed that one must be reborn through spiritual (intellectual) enlightenment in order to escape judgement.

Dualism and Manichaeism

Dualism is a philosophical view that reality consists of two fundamental, distinct, and often opposing principles—mind/mental (good) and body/physical (evil). Manichaeism is a form of Gnostic dualism that teaches an unending cosmic conflict between Light (good) and Darkness (evil). Human souls are understood as fragments of divine light trapped within corrupt material bodies, and salvation is conceived as the soul’s escape from matter through special knowledge, strict ascetic discipline, and the eventual liberation of this imprisoned light from the physical world.

Augustine of Hippo

Considered a saint mainly in the Catholic and Calvinist traditions, is a fourth-century Christian theologian whose writings profoundly shaped Western Christianity—both Catholic and Protestant. His theology, heavily influenced by an earlier commitment to Manichaenism, Gnosticism, and dualistic thought, strongly impacted later doctrines of salvation, sin, grace, judgment, predestination, infant baptism, and hellfire within the Western church.

Eternal Conscious Torment

The Augustinian-Calvinist view of hell as a literal place of endless conscious torment of both body and soul, commonly referred to as the “hellfire” doctrine. This perspective has become dominant in Western Christianity and remains especially prevalent among conservative Protestant and Catholic traditions. 🔥

Tikkun Olam

The earliest known appearance of the phrase Tikkun Olam is in the second century, in Mishnah Gittin 4:2–9. From its earliest usage, it expresses the Jewish hope for the repair and renewal of the world, not escape from it. This vision runs parallel to the biblical hope of the “restoration of all things” in Acts 3:21 and the prophetic promise of healing and renewal in Isaiah 35:1–6. In both Jewish and biblical imagination, redemption is cosmic, restorative, and this-world oriented, aimed at the healing of creation rather than its abandonment.

c5P

Preface

The Parable of the Forgotten Tongue

A rabbi once spoke to a village in the language of the soil beneath their feet. He spoke of fire as they knew fire—not as an instrument of sadism, but as warmth for a home, as the heat that hardens clay, as the flame that refines silver and gold by burning away dross. He spoke of blood as they knew blood—not as a payment to appease a violent deity, but as life poured out to undo the power of death through cleansing and purification.

He spoke of judgment as they understood judgment—not as endless vengeance, but as discipline that corrects; as medicine that heals a wounded community; as a refining fire that destroys what deforms so life can grow strong again; as punishment meant to restore the person, not erase them, so wholeness and peace might finally return.

He spoke of salvation as they had already tasted it: rain in a time of famine, the barren woman bearing a child, protection in war, healing in sickness, freedom from chains. And he spoke of eternity not as an infinite stretch of days, but as fullness—life filled to the brim, like a cup overflowing.

Those who heard him nodded, for they had fields to tend, tools to refine, relationships to restore. His words did not frighten them; they instructed them. They did not pull them out of the world, but taught them how to live rightly within it.

But in time, the rabbi’s words traveled beyond the hills, across seas, into lands where the culture and the language were different, and the stories were not the same. There, fire was no longer remembered as a refiner’s flame, but imagined only as destructive. Blood was no longer life, but a symbol of violent rage, terror, and destruction. Justice was no longer the setting right of what was broken, but payback, revenge, and the satisfaction of fury. Salvation was no longer rescue in this life, but escape from the flames in the afterlife. And eternity was no longer fullness, but endlessness—stretched thin like a road with no horizon.

In those lands, people read the rabbi’s words, yet no longer heard his voice. He was no longer seen as a Jewish prophet walking the paths of Jerusalem, but was pictured as a Greek philosopher skilled in Roman categories. They spoke more of systems than stories, more of rules than relationships, more of intellect than love, more of measures than meaning. They argued over the temperature of fire instead of its purpose, over the duration and severity of judgment instead of its restorative purpose, over the distance of salvation instead of its nearness. They spoke of torment as though the rabbi had meant cruelty, though he had spoken of testing and refining—of metal proven true in the flame. Their view of God became fear-based, distant, and cold.

So the words were preserved—but through translations that carried new meanings, and the world that first gave them life slowly receded. The lamp was carefully guarded, yet the oil within it was quietly replaced. The people honored the language, recited it faithfully, defended it fiercely—while grieving, often without knowing why, the mercy it was always meant to reveal.

I use the phrase “Jewish perspective” in the book’s title not merely because I am Jewish, or because this is how I, as a Jewish Bible scholar, read the text. I use it because I believe this perspective reflects how Jews in the time of Jesus—including those who believed in Him and became His disciples—would have naturally understood the language He used.

By “Jewish,” I do not mean the rabbinic tradition that later came to define much of post-Temple Judaism. Rather, I am referring to biblical Judaism: the faith of ancient Israel as revealed in the Scriptures, the very world of thought in which Jesus and His earliest followers lived and spoke.

Concepts such as salvation, redemption, atonement, blood, sacrifice, sin, fire, Sheol, and Gehenna were not foreign, mysterious, or speculative ideas to them. They were familiar, well-established Hebrew idioms, deeply embedded in Israel’s Scriptures, worship, and collective imagination. Jesus did not invent a new theological vocabulary; He spoke within one His audience already knew well.

What is often labeled “Jewish context” today is, in reality, simply original context. It is the soil from which the gospel itself grew. Many modern Christian misunderstandings—especially in the West—arise not because the Bible is unclear, but because these ancient Hebrew categories have been filtered through centuries of non-Jewish, philosophical, and often fear-driven reinterpretations and mistranslations. My aim, therefore, is not to impose a new reading onto the text, but to recover an old one—to reconstruct the gospel as it would have first sounded to Jewish ears long before later traditions reshaped its meaning.

Part I: When the Gospel Was Still Jewish

Part II: #Blood #Atonement #Sacrifice

Part III: A Hebrew Perspective on Christ's Atonement

c70

Introduction

When the original gospel struck a chord, it was first with the Jewish people, who embraced and believed it. From the start, the Gospels highlight that mostly Jews followed Jesus. We read that Jews came to Him all the way “from Galilee, the Decapolis, Jerusalem, Judea, and beyond the Jordan” (Matthew 4:25). This pattern continues throughout the Gospels (e.g., Matthew 4:25, 8:1, 14:13–14, 19:2, Mark 3:7–8, Luke 5:1, 9:11, John 6:2). Even after the resurrection, Scripture records that “a great number of Jews” believed (Acts 14:1; see also Acts 2:41, 4:4, 5:14, 6:7, 8:6, 13:44, 14:1, 17:4). The New Testament writers—mostly Jews themselves—go out of their way to highlight just how many Jews embraced Jesus as Messiah; far more than the modern church wants to acknowledge.

Similarly, the renowned Jewish historian Josephus noted that Jesus “won over many Jews.”1 In fact, according to historians, the ancient church was overwhelmingly Jewish, especially in its first few decades centered in Jerusalem.2 In the early days, Christianity wasn’t its own religion but a movement within Judaism that Gentiles slowly adopted. It’s a striking and unfortunate contrast to today, where the modern “Westernized” Gospel often makes little to no sense to most Jews.

I know this firsthand because I spent 20 years in various messianic ministries where I witnessed hundreds of millions of dollars flow into various messianic ministries in well-intentioned, but ultimately futile, attempts to “convert the Jews to Christ.” I was involved in many of these efforts—and I can tell you with absolute clarity: most donors have no idea how much of the truth is being withheld from them, or how radically different reality is from what they’re being shown through newsletters and polished videos meant for fundraising.

Sure, at first, many Jews became genuinely excited about Jesus—they loved discovering His teachings, His compassion, His love, His courage, His message of the Kingdom. But within a few months, most left never to come back after discovering what Messianic believers actually teach in their churches—the full bundle of Western Augustinian–Calvinist doctrines (the Messianic movement absorbed much of American fundamentalism, especially Baptist, Calvinist, and Pentecostal theology). To use the words of one Jewish rabbi, these new believers came to realize that Messianic Jews are essentially Protestant Christian fundamentalists wearing a yarmulke, pretending to be Jewish.3

Over the years, we noticed a clear trend: more than 95% of Israeli Jews who embraced faith in Jesus eventually walked away from it. Within 6 to 12 months, most returned to traditional Judaism, moved toward secularism, or joined a traditional church like the Catholic Church. Yet, stories of their “conversion” continued to be used for fundraising among Christians, long after they had left. The pattern happened so consistently, it became impossible to overlook.

I discuss this in more detail in my book "Why Don't Jews Believe in Jesus," but after years of seeing this pattern, I came to realize that sending new Jewish believers into Messianic churches and ministries was counterproductive and ultimately caused more harm than good. Once I stopped funneling new believers and prospects into these systems, I noticed something striking: those who remained independent, outside the influence of Westernized theological structures, were far more likely to hold on to their faith despite isolation.

The lesson I’ve come to understand over the years is this: the Jewish people will continue to reject the Western version of the gospel—not because they are rejecting Yeshua Himself, but because they cannot embrace a distorted, foreign interpretation of His message. What’s being rejected isn’t Him, but the later theological framework—developed after the 4th century—that became attached to Him and, in doing so, turned into one of the biggest obstacles keeping Jews from believing.

This realization was also one of the major reasons why I decided—ironically during and following my doctoral studies at Dallas Theological Seminary—to go back and reexamine the theological doctrines I had been spoon-fed throughout my years in messianic churches, Bible colleges, and seminaries. All of them were deeply shaped by the Western framework. I eventually realized that much of what I had inherited was not the faith of Rabbi Yeshua and His Jewish apostles, but a filtered, Greco-Roman Westernized reinterpretation that I needed to deconstruct and investigate from the ground up.

What I came to realize is that many forms of modern Christianity bear little resemblance to the faith Yeshua and His first Jewish followers actually lived. Over the past seventeen centuries, it has been reshaped and overlaid by Roman legal frameworks—often steeped in antisemitism—Greek philosophical categories, European pagan customs, Reformation-era power struggles, and later American cultural religion, before eventually being exported back to Israel. What began as a thoroughly Jewish movement has been buried beneath layers of tradition, incorrect translation, cultural bias, and theological reinterpretation.

To ignore this history is not neutrality; it is historical denial. And it explains why recovering Christianity’s Jewish roots (and by that I mean biblical Jewish, not rabbinical Jewish) feels so threatening to some believers today—it destabilizes a system they inherited and mistook for original.

To be clear, I am not advocating a “Hebrew Roots” return to the Mosaic Law (an impossibility without the Temple, priesthood, and sacrificial system). I am calling for something far more basic and far more faithful: reading Jesus on His own terms to hear His words within the Jewish world that produced them. Understanding His teachings as His original audience would have understood them—before they were filtered through seventeen hundred years of non-Jewish, often antisemitic, reinterpretations.

Only then does the gospel recover its original power, coherence, and beauty.

What Should You Expect?

Thus, in this book, we will do what many today call “deconstruct”—not to destroy faith, but to strip away centuries of doctrinal traditions by returning to the very heart of the matter: the biblical text itself. Our journey will transport us back before the influence of Augustine of Hippo, Thomas Aquinas, Dante, John Calvin, Jonathan Edwards, and John MacArthur. While I engage deeply with those added theological layers in several of my other books,4 in this volume we will look beyond them—peeling back centuries of interpretation—to encounter the raw, unfiltered voice of Scripture in its original context.

In this book, we’ll explore—and try to answer—some of the big ancient questions: What really happened in Eden? What exactly has God saved us from? What did Jesus accomplish through His life, death, and resurrection? How does His sacrifice complete the story told in Israel’s Scriptures? How does blood bring atonement? How does the cross reveal and heal the brokenness of our world? What is the biblical meaning of salvation, justice, and wrath? What is the purpose of hell?

We will also wrestle seriously with Scripture’s repeated and unmistakable claims: that God is “mighty to save” (Zephaniah 3:17; Isaiah 63:1); that He “wants everyone to be saved” (1 Timothy 2:4); that no purpose of His “can be thwarted” (Job 42:2); and that, in the end, “every knee will bow and every tongue will confess allegiance to Jesus Christ” in worship (Isaiah 45:23; Romans 14:11; Philippians 2:10–11).

Taken together, these declarations force us to ask whether God’s will is merely aspirational—or truly effective; whether Christ’s atonement is limited or unlimited in its power; whether divine love is hopeful but fragile—or victorious and unstoppable.

I invite you to set aside your preconceptions and to be both brave and honest in your search for truth. The path may feel unfamiliar—even uncomfortable—but that is often the sign of real growth. My challenge is simple: don’t settle for secondhand answers. Dig deep. Ask hard questions. And have the courage to rediscover the living Word for yourself. The reward is a faith that is not only truer, but deeper, freer, and more transformative than anything one denomination alone could ever offer.

The Bible does not call us to follow traditions and doctrines blindly. It calls us to trust what has been revealed: the sweeping story of a God who saves—who rescues from death and exile, from sin and shame, from fear and every force that stands between humanity and His love.

I pray this work will become a genuine blessing to you.

c7V

Part I: When the Gospel Was Still Jewish

c83

Chapter 1
Understanding The Biblical Concept of Time

The concept of time is understood very differently through the modern Western/Greek philosophical lens than it was in ancient biblical Jewish thought. Western theology, shaped heavily by Greek metaphysics, often imagines eternity as endless time stretching forward without limit. But the biblical writers did not think in those categories. Their language was more relational, experiential, and rooted in the cyclical rhythms of life rather than abstract philosophical speculation. When these ancient ideas are later filtered through Greek philosophical assumptions and then carried into modern languages, the original meaning can easily shift. Words that once described a quality of life, a covenant relationship, or a coming age may slowly take on new meanings shaped by later intellectual frameworks.

This challenge becomes even greater when we consider translation. There are now over nine hundred English translations of the Bible, and this creates a serious difficulty for readers who do not know Hebrew or Greek, since most rely on a single translation and naturally assume it to be neutral or definitive. Yet translation is never neutral. By its very nature, it is constrained by human limitation, shaped by the translator’s linguistic choices, theological assumptions, and cultural framework.

The Text You Choose, the Theology You Get

Rendering ancient texts is an extraordinarily complex task. Translators must navigate dead languages, vanished cultural worlds, idioms with no modern equivalent, and centuries of historical distance. Every choice—every verb tense, every metaphor, every word—is an act of interpretation. And inevitably, each translator brings with them a prior theological framework that shapes how key terms are rendered and how ideas are framed. What readers often receive as “the Bible” is therefore not pure text, but text filtered through tradition, assumptions, and inherited theology.

One major reason there are so many Bible translations is that even scholars cannot agree on how best to convey the depth, nuance, and layered meaning of the original text. Translation is never merely linguistic; it is interpretive. On top of that, translators inevitably bring their own theological assumptions to the task.

For example, many Western translators—Catholic, Protestant, Evangelical—operate within a framework shaped by the doctrine of Eternal Conscious Torment, and that theology often seeps into how certain Hebrew and Greek words and passages are rendered in English.

A striking example is the Parable of the Sheep and the Goats in the Gospel of Matthew 25. In the popular evangelical translation, the New International Version (NIV), Jesus concludes by saying, “They will go away to eternal punishment.” Yet in more academically rigorous translations—such as Prof. David Bentley Hart’s The New Testament: A Translation, published by Yale University—the same line reads, “These will go away into the chastening of that Age.”

The difference is not minor. It reflects radically different assumptions about language, time, judgment, and especially the meaning of the Greek word aiōnios.

David Bentley Hart is not just a scholar; he’s also an Eastern Orthodox Christian, which means he writes from within that tradition. He approaches the same texts with a very different theological imagination—one much less likely to see punitive finality in the language of judgment. This leads not only to different wording but to entirely different visions of God drawn from the same ancient sources.

This also explains why the Hebrew word olam and the Greek words aiōn and aiōnios are so routinely translated as “forever,” “everlasting,” or “eternal” in both Protestant and Catholic English Bibles. These translations reflect later theological assumptions more than original linguistic meaning. In their native cultural and semantic worlds, these words do not denote time without end.

Instead, they typically refer to an age, an era, or a duration whose length is undefined and determined by its purpose—not by infinity itself. At times, they point not to duration at all, but to quality: completeness, fullness, weight, kind, volume, or maturity. The shift from qualitative and purposive meaning to endless temporal duration is not inherent in the words themselves; it is imposed by later doctrinal frameworks that read infinity where the text speaks of depth, fulfillment, and divine intention.

For example, when Jesus says, “Now this is eternal life: that they know You, the only true God, and Jesus Christ, whom You have sent” (John 17:3), it is clear that “eternal life” is not primarily about duration but about depth and quality (to know someone is to have intimacy with them). Here, eternal life is a rich, relational life rooted in knowing God.

The idea of linear, endless time is not native to the Hebrew Bible nor to the Jewish worldview of Jesus and the apostles.5,6,7 Rather, that concept emerged later through Greek philosophical influence—especially analytical Greek thought (particularly from Platonic8 and Aristotelian9 traditions). This philosophical tradition later influenced Christian theology, especially through figures like Augustine of Hippo, who integrated Greek metaphysics into biblical interpretation,10 which then reshaped how Western Christianity came to understand biblical language and the concept of time.11

The Meaning of "Eternal" According to Top Christian Scholars

Even though I'm a native Hebrew speaker and learned Greek in seminary, I’d rather let the real experts do the talking so it doesn’t come off as just my personal opinion.

According to the Interpreter’s Dictionary of the Bible:

Time: The Old Testament and the New Testament are not acquainted with the conception of eternity as timelessness. The Old Testament has not developed a special term for “eternity.” The word aion originally meant “vital force,” “life,” then “age,” “lifetime.”12

(K.R. Crim, G.A. Buttrick)

The renowned theologian Charles Ellicott explains:

Everlasting punishment–life eternal: The two adjectives represent the same Greek word, aionios—it must be admitted that the Greek word which is rendered “eternal” does not, in itself, involve endlessness, but rather, duration, whether through an age or succession of ages, and that it is therefore applied in the New Testament to periods of time that have had both a beginning and ending.13

(Charles Ellicott)

In James Hasting’s Dictionary of the New Testament, it says:

Eternity: There is no word either in the Old Testament Hebrew or the New Testament Greek to express the abstract idea of eternity.14

(James Hasting)

In the Encyclopedic Dictionary of the Bible, it is written:

ETERNITY: The Bible hardly speaks of eternity in the philosophical sense of infinite duration without beginning or end. The Hebrew word OLAM, which is used alone (Ps. 61:8; etc.) or with various prepositions (Gen. 3:22; etc.) in contexts where it is traditionally translated as ‘forever,’ means in itself no more than "for an indefinitely long period." Thus OLAM does not mean ‘from eternity’ but ‘of old’ Gen. 6:4; etc. In the New Testament aion is used as the equivalent of olam.15

(L. F. Hartman, B. Woordenboek)

Richard Francis Weymouth, Doctor of Literature and a Bible translator, explains:

Eternal: Greek: “aeonion,” i.e., “of the ages.” Etymologically this adjective, like others similarly formed, does not signify “during,” but “belong to” the aeons or ages.16

(R. F. Weymouth)

Theologian and Professor Herman Oldhausen wrote:

The Bible has no expression for endlessness. All the Biblical terms imply or denote long periods.17

(Herman Oldhausen)

Professor Knappe of Halle wrote:

The Hebrew was destitute of any single word to express endless duration. The pure idea of eternity is not found in any of the ancient languages.18

(Knappe of Halle)

Charles H. Welch, editor of The Berean Expositor:

Eternity is not a Biblical theme…What we have to learn is that the Bible does not speak of eternity. It is not written to tell us of eternity. Such a consideration is entirely outside the scope of revelation.19

(Charles H. Welch)

G. Campbell Morgan, a British Doctor of Divinity and conservative pastor, served as president of Cheshunt College in Cambridge and wrote:

Let me say to Bible students that we must be very careful how we use the word 'eternity.' We have fallen into great error in our constant usage of that word. There is no word in the whole Book of God corresponding with our eternal.20

(Campbell Morgan)

I could go on and on for an olam, but you get the point—the words so often translated as “forever,” “everlasting,” or “eternal” in English Bibles today were not originally about endless time. In the Second Temple Jewish mindset, when referring to time, they spoke of an age or an era. When paired with words like “life” or “punishment,” the emphasis was not on duration but on depth—on volume, quality, weight, and purpose rather than on mathematical length.

In that sense, eternal life described a divine kind of life, not a stopwatch ticking into infinity. Jesus was offering a transformed way of living—a life of true depth and meaning, grounded in the life of God and available here and now.

The Hebrew/Biblical Concept of Time—Cyclical Rather Than Linear

The Jewish and biblical understanding of time is cyclical rather than strictly linear. While Western theology often views time as a straight line with a clear beginning, middle, and end, the biblical mindset sees time unfolding in cycles, patterns, seasons, and ages. It's more like a spiral than a straight road, with each turn or age building on the last toward eventual restoration.

Time: Bible vs. West

This is evident in the rhythms woven throughout Scripture—the weekly Shabbat, the moon’s monthly cycle, the annual feasts, and the prophetic patterns where themes of judgment, exile, and redemption resound across generations. It’s especially clear in the book of Revelation (consider my short book Revelation: A Jewish Perspective for more details).

These repeating cycles reveal a biblical understanding of time that is rhythmic rather than linear—an unfolding story rather than an endless countdown. These aren’t one-time events but recurring themes that shape the journey of both individuals and communities.

In this framework, judgment is not the final word—it is a stage in a larger redemptive process. From this perspective, hell—if understood as a place of afterlife punishment—might be seen as a temporary, corrective stage within a broader cycle of return, renewal, and healing.

And indeed, in Jewish thought—both modern and ancient21—this present life is understood as an age, and so is hell. Hell is therefore not everlasting but another age beyond this life, serving a purpose rather than existing as endless punishment. It functions as a period of judgment, after which God’s restorative purposes can be fulfilled. Some views—both modern and ancient—suggest that only an extremely small number of persistently unrepentant sinners will ultimately be annihilated.22

In other words, ancient Jewish readers didn’t see time—or afterlife judgment—as frozen in finality. Instead, they anticipated God’s mercy to eventually break through every cycle of darkness. This perspective challenges the idea of never-ending hellfire and opens the door to a far more hopeful and restorative view of God’s justice. But that discussion belongs to a later section of the book.

Now, let’s take a close look at the real meaning behind the Hebrew and Greek words often misunderstood by modern readers.

The Hebrew Word "Olam"

The Hebrew word most often translated as "everlasting," "eternal," or "forever" is Olam. However, Olam does not mean endless time. Instead, when referring to time, it means "age," "a long duration," or "a time period with no disclosed end." It is a flexible term whose meaning is determined by context. It can refer to both time and quality.

Let us look at several key examples of how Olam functions in Scripture:

To the roots of the mountains I sank down; the earth beneath barred me in forever [olam]. But you, LORD my God, brought my life up from the pit.

(Jonah 2:6)

Jonah was inside the great fish for only three days and three nights (Jonah 1:17), yet he describes his experience as lasting olam. This shows that olam can refer to a specific, limited time period.

The forts and towers will become lairs forever [olam], a joy of wild donkeys, a pasture of flocks—Until the Spirit is poured upon us from on high...

(Isaiah 32:14-15)

The period of desolation is described as olam, yet it is explicitly temporary. It will last until God's Spirit is poured out and restoration comes.

Every Sabbath day Aaron shall arrange it before the LORD regularly; it is from the people of Israel as a covenant forever [olam].

(Leviticus 24:8)

The priestly ritual in the Tabernacle was called olam, yet according to the common Christian understanding it ended with the coming of the New Covenant in Jesus Christ (Jeremiah 31:31-32; Hebrews 8:7-13).

I will completely destroy them and make them an object of horror and scorn, and an everlasting [olam] ruin.

(Jeremiah 25:9-11)

This prophecy of Babylonian captivity lasted seventy years, clearly demonstrating that olam spoke of an age as well as severity.

These examples confirm that the Hebrew Olam is not about infinite time.

The Greek Words "Aion" and "Aionios"

The New Testament sees this concept in much the same way as the Hebrew Scriptures.

Strong's Concordance explains:

aión: a space of time, an age

(Strong's 165)

HELPS Word-Studies explains:

aiṓn (see also the cognate adjective, 166 /aiṓnios, "age-long") – properly, an age (era, "time-span"), characterized by a specific quality (type of existence).

(HELPS, 165)

Consider Romans 16:25-26:

Now to him who is able to establish you in accordance with my gospel, the message I proclaim about Jesus Christ, in keeping with the revelation of the mystery hidden for long ages [aionios] past, but now revealed and made known...

(Romans 16:25-26)

Clearly, aionios here refers to a secret from past ages—not an endless duration, otherwise we would never reach the point of its revelation.

Conclusion

In conclusion, the evidence from Hebrew and Greek usage, as well as the testimony of respected scholars, makes clear that the words olam, aion, and aionios were never meant to convey the abstract idea of endless time. Instead, when referring to time, they point to ages, eras, or long but limited durations, often with qualitative significance rather than quantitative measure. The biblical worldview understood time as cyclical and restorative, woven into rhythms of covenant, judgment, and renewal, not as an infinite linear continuum. Later theological developments—especially under Greek philosophical influence and Augustine’s metaphysical framework—reshaped these terms into doctrines of endless punishment and timelessness. Recovering their original meaning opens the way to a more hopeful vision of divine justice, one rooted in God’s mercy and the promise of restoration rather than the terror of unending torment.

cBU

Chapter 2
The Plumb Line of Truth: How We Discern True Doctrine

For I am not ashamed of the gospel of Christ, for it is the power of God to salvation for everyone who believes, for the Jew first and also for the Greek.

(Romans 1:16)

When the typical modern Christian reads this verse, they are often so culturally conditioned that they instinctively interpret it something like this:

For I am not ashamed of the 21st-century Western understanding of the gospel—the one that gives me a ticket out of God’s fiery furnace, as long as I intellectually adhere to the right doctrine—for it is how God spares us from endless torture. First, it’s meant to make sense to the Jew (though today it usually doesn’t), so in reality, it ends up being relevant just to the Gentile.

What Did the Gospel First Sound Like?

Suppose you seek to understand what the Gospel was like when it was preached during the first centuries, before the rise of institutionalized Christianity in the fourth century. In that case, let's hop into our trusty time machine and zip back about eighteen centuries—before the Gospel was blended with Augustine’s Neoplatonism and Gnostic ideas, before it was absorbed into Constantine’s imperial agenda, before it was Romanized and later Europeanized into the form most know today. In other words, we must return to the days of the first disciples and apostles—back when Christianity was simply one more Jewish denomination within Judaism—and ask His first followers: What kind of Gospel did they proclaim?

Michael S. Heiser, a beloved and renowned Old Testament scholar educated in ancient history, Semitic languages, and the Hebrew Bible, acknowledged with much wisdom:

The proper context for interpreting the Bible is not Augustine or any other church father. It is not the Catholic Church. It is not the rabbinic movements of late antiquity and the Middle Ages. It is not the Reformation or the Puritans. It is not evangelicalism in any of its flavors. It is not the modern world at all or any period of its history. The proper context for interpreting the Bible is the context of the biblical writers—the context that produced the Bible. Every other context is alien to the biblical writers and, therefore, to the Bible. Yet there is a pervasive tendency in the believing Church to filter the Bible through creeds, confessions, and denominational preferences.23

(Michael S. Heiser)

Indeed, the proper context for interpreting the Bible and understanding the gospel is the world, language, and mindset of its original authors—not later Western traditions, creeds, or the European and American movements that came to shape modern Christianity.

Strip Jesus of His Jewishness, and You End Up with a Different Gospel

If we want to understand what Jesus accomplished on the cross, we must first understand and reclaim the Old Testament sacrificial system—because that was the language God Himself chose to prepare the world for the meaning of the Messiah's death.

Jesus did not die in a vacuum. His crucifixion was not an isolated event disconnected from Israel's story. The entire framework for understanding sacrifice, atonement, and redemption was already established in the Hebrew Scriptures long before Jesus arrived.

Without understanding how sacrifices functioned in ancient Israel—what they symbolized, how they addressed sin, and what they were meant to restore—we risk projecting foreign, often pagan ideas onto the cross. We begin to imagine God as an angry, bloodthirsty deity demanding violence, instead of the caring, loving Father who guides and teaches, forgives and corrects, purifies and disciplines His people to nurture them and lead them toward wholeness.

In reality, we often read and interpret the Bible through the lens of our own culture—filtering its words through modern assumptions and values. But when we do this, we risk losing its original meaning and context, unintentionally replacing it with ideas that would have been foreign to its first readers.

Why a Jewish Perspective Matters?

I’ve often heard people argue that since “the Jews rejected Christ,” any Jewish context or perspective on Scripture should be dismissed. But this idea is not only historically wrong—since His disciples were Jews—it’s also logically weak and theologically risky.

Dismissing the Jewish context and perspective “because Jews rejected Christ” commits two logical fallacies. First, it’s a genetic fallacy: rejecting an idea solely because it comes from a Jewish source, rather than evaluating whether the idea itself is true. In our case, it’s not really a “Jewish idea” so much as an example of how the first listeners—who were Jews—understood the biblical message, with the beliefs of Jews in the Second Temple period serving merely as context and support.

Second, it’s a faulty generalization: not all Jews rejected Jesus—many followed Him. Christianity did not begin as a new religion detached from Judaism. Sure, some Jews rejected Christ, but in its earliest form, it was a movement within Judaism itself. Jesus was a Jew, as were most of His followers, the first churches, and nearly all the New Testament authors. The early Christians were not raised in evangelical, Catholic, or Baptist homes but were raised Jewish. For over a century, followers of Jesus—both Jewish and Gentile converts ("God-fearers")—understood themselves as part of Israel’s covenant community. They saw Jesus not as the founder of a new religion, but as the fulfillment of Israel’s messianic hope (e.g., Deuteronomy 18:15).

Even though most Jews today do not accept the modern-day concept of Jesus as the Messiah—and often for good reasons—the original Jewish culture, historical context, and linguistic framework of both the Hebrew Scriptures and the New Testament remain indispensable.

Both the Hebrew Bible and most of the New Testament were written by Jews, using Jewish idioms, addressing biblical law, Jewish tradition, and Hebrew prophecy. Without these foundations, the biblical message becomes distorted, disconnected from its roots, and vulnerable to layers of later interpretation that obscure its intended meaning. Without understanding the Jewish context, many biblical passages are bound to be misinterpreted.

Taking seriously the context of the Hebrew Scriptures—and of first-century Judaism—is not about endorsing modern Rabbinic Judaism. It is about recognizing the original soil from which the Gospel emerged. It's also about honoring the root from which it came:

My own brothers and sisters, my own flesh and blood, the people of Israel. To them belong the adoption, the glory, the covenants, the giving of the law, the worship, and the promises; to them belong the patriarchs, and from them, according to the flesh, comes the Christ, who is over all, God blessed forever. Amen.

(Romans 9:3-5)

Fire Up the Flux Capacitor

Jewishless Jesus—Jesus without His Jewish heritage—lacks roots. To truly understand Jesus, His work on the cross, and the message of the Kingdom, we must return to the Jewish world in which the Scriptures were written and first understood. It’s only by recovering and reconnecting with the original context—its language, culture, symbolism, worldview, and of course, the Hebrew Scriptures—that we can truly appreciate the depth, clarity, and coherence of the gospel as Jesus' earliest followers shared it.

If your time machine happens to be out of order and time travel isn’t an option, there are still three other ways to discover how the Gospel was understood from the first to the second centuries. One is to examine the evangelistic sermons and gospel presentations preached by the Jewish apostles, as recorded in the Book of Acts. The second is to examine the Didache—often regarded as the earliest example of the Church Orders genre—which provides a rare window into how the first Jewish followers of Christ understood and practiced the Gospel. The third is by looking at the Apostles’ Creed—a concise ancient declaration of Christian faith.

But no one begins a story in the middle. Before we turn to those three sources, we must return to the beginning—to the prologue.

cD5

Chapter 3
The Gospel According to the Garden of Eden

Movies, parables, and stories typically start with a prologue. If you misunderstand it, the entire plot inevitably starts to warp, because the prologue—the opening—is where the central problem is first introduced. Misread the beginning, and the rest of the story will feel pretty confusing. In the same way, when the Bible’s own prologue—the account of Eden—is misunderstood, it skews everything that comes after, leading to inevitable misinterpretations of key parts of the Gospel. When you dive into a novel or a film, you’re not just curious about who the main characters are—you also want to grasp the central conflict, so you can start guessing what the mission might be and how it will all be resolved.

Since the Messiah did not appear in isolation but as the fulfillment of the Hebrew Scriptures, understanding the significance of His redemption requires seeing it through the lens of the Hebrew Scriptures: Christ came to resolve the most ancient human problem—one that does not begin in Matthew, but at the very opening of the biblical story: in Genesis, within the drama of the Garden of Eden.

However, treating Genesis like a manual or a textbook often leads people to read it in fragments—fixating on isolated verses, filtering Eden through man-made doctrines, and endlessly debating creation theories—while missing what it truly is: a narrative. In doing so, they risk missing the heart of the biblical message.

Worse still, translations and Western categories frequently smuggle in assumptions that flatten or distort the grand story. Genesis is not a book of law or doctrine but a sweeping narrative. And unless we read it as such, we inevitably misread it.

The Hebrew Scriptures and the New Testament are bound for a reason—they aren’t two unrelated tales. The New Testament carries on—and brings to a climax—the story of the Old. Together, they tell one seamless drama: from creation, to humanity’s exile from life, to redemption and resurrection into life again. In the grand sweep of the biblical narrative, the central problem is death. It stands as the great enemy of humankind. Death disrupts creation’s harmony, shatters human hope, and casts a shadow over life itself.

The opening chapters of Genesis lay the groundwork for the entire biblical narrative. And the Eden story, with its two mysterious trees, sets the stage.

Now buckle up and hit the ignition button, because we’re about to take a trip through time…

Between the Two Trees

And the Lord God planted a garden in Eden, in the east, and there he put the man whom he had formed. Out of the ground the Lord God made to grow every tree that is pleasant to the sight and good for food, the tree of life also in the midst of the garden, and the tree of the knowledge of good and evil.

(Genesis 2:8-9)

Genesis also tells us that God gave Adam and Eve a warning:

You must not eat from the tree of the knowledge of good and evil, for when you eat from it you will certainly die. 

(Genesis 2:17)

Pay close attention to the name of the tree. It’s not, as some mistakenly call it, the Tree of Knowledge, but the Tree of the Knowledge of Good and Evil. The real significance is in knowing good and evil.

Adam and Eve did not yet know what death was. They were innocent of it. And it was through a very specific kind of knowledge—the knowledge of good and evil—that they would come to learn what death is, not as an abstract idea, but as a lived reality.

The Hebrew word da‘at (knowledge) refers not merely to intellectual understanding but to experiential knowledge, as illustrated in the verse, “And Adam knew Eve his wife; and she conceived” (Genesis 4:1).

Experiential knowledge is the kind of understanding we gain not merely from books or lectures, but from living, doing, and feeling—like learning to ride a bike, navigating a difficult conversation, or discovering how to cook a favorite meal. It’s personal, intuitive, and often unforgettable because it is shaped by real encounters.

Human beings cannot grow only through intellectual guidance but must also learn from the lessons they gain through real-life experiences.

You can warn a child a thousand times not to touch a hot stove, yet only the sting of heat makes the lesson sink in. In the same way that a physician must both study and practice before becoming truly competent, humanity—first embodied by Adam and Eve—was about to discover the real meaning of "good" and "evil," not as an abstract idea, but as something experienced firsthand.

So, it seems the biblical narrator makes the central human problem unmistakably clear: death.

The Knowledge of Good and Evil

Now, Adam and Eve already had a rough idea of the “good,” at least in its most basic sense—Genesis repeats it again and again: “God saw that it was good” (Genesis 1:4,10,12,18,21,25,31). Goodness was woven into every fiber of creation. Evil was absent—still knocking on the door, but not yet entered.

By eating from the Tree of the Knowledge of Good and Evil, humanity opened the door to new understanding, choosing to gain expertise in good and evil through a lifetime of experience. And how is evil experienced? Ultimately, through death. Death is the full-grown fruit of evil—its final sting. Without death, evil would remain theoretical, an abstract idea without consequence, a story on paper. But death exposes evil for what it truly is: the unraveling of life, the breaking of harmony, the enemy that shows us the cost of choosing against God’s goodness.

Before, we had no capacity for moral knowledge. Animals don’t wrestle with ethics or sin; they don't understand good and evil—but humans, made in God’s image, would.

Think of it like this: gaining this new kind of knowledge is like installing an additional expansion pack to humanity’s operating system. The installation, however, seems to take an awfully long time to complete…

Understanding Good Comes from Understanding Evil

Once death entered the world, humanity began to learn—experientially—what evil truly is. We taste its slow decay. We feel its power in grief, sickness, suffering, and the unraveling of everything we love. And through that contrast, we come to understand that good = life.

What God knew as “evil” in Eden was, for the first time, about to be learned by humans. Yet there’s a brighter side—through darkness, light becomes cherished, and through evil, goodness is truly seen for its beauty, its importance, and its divine origin.

It’s one thing to observe light or study it in a physics class; it’s something else entirely to truly value it—after feeling its warmth for the first time following a year trapped in a cold, lightless underground tunnel.

However, eating from the Tree of Knowledge of Good and Evil also meant giving up access to the Tree of Life.

Losing Access to the Tree of Life

Life comes from God, the source of all creation. The closer we are to Him, the closer we are to life, while turning away from Him leads us toward death.

Humanity chose the hard path—learning firsthand what evil truly is—and we have been on that journey ever since. We see how it kills, corrupts, destroys, and enslaves. Yet God, in His goodness, set a limit to this deadly education. When Adam and Eve ate from the Tree of the Knowledge of Good and Evil, they—and all their descendants—lost access to the other, perhaps more vital tree: the Tree of Life:

And the Lord God said, “The man has now become like one of us, knowing good and evil. He must not be allowed to reach out his hand and take also from the tree of life and eat, and live forever.”

(Genesis 3:22)

The exile from Eden itself proves two things. First, that on the very day they ate from the forbidden tree, humanity died—or at least began the biological process of dying. God was speaking the truth after all. Second, this wasn't an act of wrath but of mercy—God does not wish for humans to suffer forever. By removing access to the Tree of Life, He gave humanity an expiration date—a limit to our pain and suffering. If this was how God responded to sin in the beginning, it’s hard to believe He would later choose to torture billions without end after death.

Death existed before the fall because Adam and Eve needed continual access to the Tree of Life. Mortality was built into humanity from the beginning. Yet death had not taken hold, because it was held in check by communion with the source of life. Genesis never says that Adam became mortal after sin. What it says is that humanity was cut off from the Tree of Life, “lest he eat and live forever.” Immortality was never an inherent human possession; it was a gift, sustained by proximity to life itself rather than by an immortal nature. The fall did not create death. It removed access to the Tree of Life. Once communion was broken, death gained its power.

This is precisely what Paul means when he writes, “sin entered the world through one man, and death through sin, and in this way death came to all people” (Romans 5:12). Death did not originate with Adam, but through Adam it became humanity’s shared and inescapable fate.

This reading fits the text without importing later philosophical assumptions about an immortal soul. Life was always contingent, always relational, always sustained by God. The tragedy of the fall is not that death suddenly appeared, but that humanity was exiled from the source that kept death from having the final word.

Yet the story does not end with death. Scripture is not a tale of despair but of redemption. The Tree of Life—barred in Genesis—reappears in Revelation, restored through the Messiah. This is where the New Testament brings the entire biblical narrative to its climax.

Death is the problem introduced in Genesis, and life—resurrection—is the remedy brought by Christ. That is the overarching biblical story from beginning to end.

The Climax of the Gospel—Death or Life?

Tragically, modern gospel portrayals have often reversed this entirely. Instead of seeing resurrection and life (John 11:25) as the solution to humanity’s deadly condition, it has twisted the message so that a brutal, violent death becomes the supposed climax and solution to humanity’s problem. The result is an unsettling, disturbing message disconnected from the Bible’s own prologue and from the logic of redemption woven through Scripture.

For instance, according to David Platt, a well-known Calvinist preacher and former president of the Southern Baptist International Mission Board, the climax of the gospel is Christ’s brutal death. In his view, Jesus absorbed the full force of divine wrath, satisfying God's fury through violent substitution:

Jesus was pulverized under the weight of God’s wrath––as he stood in our place…How can God show both holy hatred and holy love toward sinners at the same time? This is the climactic question of the Bible, and the answer is the cross. At the cross, God showed the full expression of his wrath….24 All God’s holy wrath and hatred toward sin and sinners, stored up since the beginning of the world, is about to be poured out on him, and he is sweating blood at the thought of it…At the Cross, Christ drank the full cup of the wrath of God, and when he had downed the last drop, he turned the cup over and cried out, “It is finished.” This is the gospel.25

(David Platt)

If you’ve spent enough time around fundamentalists, you’ve likely been taught that “salvation” means God had to destroy Jesus—torturing and killing Him in your place to satisfy His wrath, so He wouldn’t have to torment you in hellfire for ever and ever.

Wyatt Graham, a Calvinist pastor and a director at The Gospel Coalition, defines the gospel in this way:

Jesus bore divine wrath at the cross for our sake, and so protected us from it. This act implies that God hates humans since he would have poured wrath upon humans if not for the work of Christ’s cross.26

In this strange take on the gospel—known as Penal Substitutionary Atonement—a god with a split personality ends up protecting us from himself. But this isn’t really good news at all—it’s a fear-fueled setup where divine justice feels more like revenge than compassion, and the focus shifts from life, healing and restoration to simply dodging a bullet from the boss’s gun—where an enraged authority figure vents his violence by executing someone else in your place, splattering mercy across the floor while calling it justice, and demanding gratitude for the spectacle.

Why Modern Gospel Portrayals Don’t Make Sense Biblically

Some modern portrayals of the gospel don’t match the biblical account. The notion that fixing the problem of death meant God had to bring about more death—by torturing and violently killing Christ—contradicts what the Scriptures actually say, which is that it was evildoers who violently killed Christ, not God:

You, with the help of wicked men, put him to death by nailing him to the cross. But God raised him from the dead.

(Acts 2:23-24)

You killed the Author of life…God raised him from the dead.

(Acts 3:15)

Jesus Christ of Nazareth, whom you crucified but whom God raised from the dead.

(Acts 4:10)

The God of our ancestors raised Jesus from the dead—whom you killed by hanging him on a cross.

(Acts 5:30)

The modern gospel often portrays the Father and the Son as being at odds by accusing God of killing His innocent Son. But according to the Gospel first preached in the Book of Acts, the opposite is true—it was humanity that rejected, tortured, and killed Christ, while all God did was undo our actions by raising Him from the dead. Blaming God for the way Christ was treated is nothing less than demonic.

Furthermore, the mutilated, blood-soaked body of Jesus does not portray divine rage redirected toward humanity’s substitute. Scripture calls Christ “the fullness of God in bodily form” (Colossians 2:9). Therefore, the crucified Christ is not a reflection of God’s fury in some cosmic act of self-flagellation and suicide—it is a reflection of our evil. The cross does not reveal what God hates; it reveals who God is: love, even when pierced.

Who tortured, mocked, and crucified the Son of God? Not God—we did. Humanity did. Do not allow modern theology to portray God as the sadist of Calvary. The sadism was on our part. The brutality was ours. The cross is not a window into divine vengeance but into human depravity—a portrait of what we become when love stands before us undefended.

It was the hardened fists of Roman soldiers that ripped the flesh from His back and crushed thorns into His skull. It was the diseased imagination of fallen humanity—violent, fearful, demon-driven—that conceived the cross: a masterpiece of brutal cruelty, engineered to destroy body, soul, and dignity alike.

Do not dare to call this horror “God’s justice.” The crucifixion was not God’s punishment of the world—it was the world’s assault on God’s love. The cross is not divine retribution; it is divine revelation.

We hate; God loves.

The modern Western gospel is too often a grotesque misrepresentation of the Good News, portraying it as a violent exchange of wrath instead of a narrative about restoration, resurrection, and redeeming love. Nowhere in Scripture does it say God attacked Jesus to avoid punishing you. That idea came from medieval Western thinkers like Anselm of Canterbury and John Calvin.

Besides, if the gospel is that God killed Jesus in our place so He wouldn’t have to kill us, then why do believers still experience death? Why not continue living forever, or be taken directly into heaven like Enoch and Elijah?

Death is still a universal reality. Think about it. Humanity is already in a state of dying. From the moment we’re born, the clock starts ticking—we age, we decay, and we die. That is the consequence of sin and the fall (see Genesis 2:17; 3:19; Romans 5:12; 6:23). There’s no need for an additional layer of afterlife punishment—death is the wages of sin (Romans 6:23).

We Die Because Adam Did

Adam’s sin rests upon all of us because of its effects, not because of inherited guilt—a crucial distinction that stands in sharp contrast to Augustine’s doctrine of Original Sin. Scripture does not present sin as a legal verdict passed down through bloodlines, as though God condemns infants for a crime they did not commit. Rather, it portrays sin as a shared condition—a broken state of existence into which we are all born.

Think of it this way: if a person carrying a fatal disease—like AIDS—brings a child into the world, the child may suffer the consequences of that condition; not because the child is guilty, but because the condition itself has spread. The suffering is real, but the blame is not inherited.

In the same way, Adam’s choice altered the human condition. By eating from the Tree of the Knowledge of Good and Evil, humanity entered into an experiential knowledge of evil—and that knowledge manifests as death. We did not choose the first act of disobedience, but we are all born into its aftermath. Sin is contagious in its consequences, not hereditary in its culpability.

This is the biblical logic: death is not a punitive sentence arbitrarily imposed on innocent descendants (Augustinian-Western theology); it is the natural result of stepping outside the source of life. Adam opened the door, and we all grow up in the same broken house. The story of Scripture, then, is not about an angry God assigning inherited guilt and violently exploding on His son—but about a loving God entering that house Himself to heal it from the inside out.

What Would a World Without Sin Be Like?

We need to consider: what might a world free from sin actually look like? Would such a world ever be feasible?

Take King David, for example. He didn’t write psalms about God’s grace and mercy before he sinned—but after. He first had to experience evil. Concepts like forgiveness and compassion aren’t abstract ideas; they are deeply emotional realities that must be experienced to be fully understood. You can’t truly forgive unless there’s been some wrongdoing to forgive in the first place. Without sin, we’d never know grace. Without brokenness, we’d never know healing. Without darkness, we’d never recognize light.

This isn’t to justify sin or call it “good,” but to acknowledge that evil is an essential part of the human learning experience of good. Our current reality isn’t some “Plan B” God scrambled to implement after the fall in Genesis 3. It’s the intentional design of a wise and all-knowing Creator who made us finite—limited in understanding, capacity, and foresight—so that we could grow, learn, fall, and be redeemed.

Since God made us that way—limited and finite in every way—then condemning imperfect humans to Eternal Conscious Torment for not being like Him is as unjust as punishing your dog for not being able to answer the phone when you are out.

Imperfect People in an Imperfect World

An often-overlooked theological truth is the difference between being good and being perfect. The Bible tells us that God created the world and saw that it was “very good” (Genesis 1:31). But it never says He created humans perfect.

In biblical Hebrew, the word used in Genesis is “tov”—meaning beneficial, pleasing, operative, or fit for purpose. It doesn’t mean flawless, morally perfect, or incapable of error. That word would be “tamim,” which speaks of perfection, moral innocence, or blamelessness and is used in different contexts throughout the Hebrew Scriptures (e.g.,"innocent [tamim] lamb"). The creation story doesn’t use that word—and for good reason.

In a perfect world, there would have been no deceitful serpent in Eden. And if humanity were flawless, it would have known better than to fall for his lies. There also wouldn’t have been a tree that could lead to death. Creation wasn't perfect, but it also wasn’t broken—it was designed to be an experiential space of growth, relationship, and freedom—which requires risk, imperfection, and the possibility of failure.

God is perfect—all-knowing and understanding, all-powerful, all-loving, and unchanging. But neither Adam and Eve nor any of us were ever born reflecting that kind of perfection. We were made finite—restricted in knowledge, poor in emotional depth, and limited in moral understanding. We were made to learn. Like babies.

And in a world like that, “sin” isn’t just rebellion—it’s inevitable. It flows naturally from our limitations, our freedom, and our immaturity. Even qualities like self-focus or selfishness aren’t inherently evil; they become harmful when they diminish or violate others. But in many cases—like putting on your own oxygen mask before helping others when flying—these so-called “selfish” instincts are actually basic survival mechanisms. They’re not inherently sinful; they help us exist, function, and endure in a world full of challenges. It’s only when these instincts become distorted or misused that they turn sinful.

God didn’t make a mistake in creating humans this way. He made a world where people grow through experience, through mistakes, through relationships. He created us not to be perfect (yet), but to be dependent—on Him and on each other. How else would we learn what love is? Imperfection isn't the real problem. The problem is forgetting that God called us good, even with our limitations. The journey of life isn’t about achieving moral perfection—it’s about becoming mature in love through the knowledge of good and evil. Moral refinement is a side effect of growth.

Conclusion

If death was the problem introduced in Eden, then immortality is the answer. God, whose not only the source of life but also of love, solved it by giving humanity the incredible gift of resurrection—life restored from death—the cure for evil’s sting. Even in Eden, the story foreshadows this: humanity will encounter evil through death, but God will one day conquer death.

When Genesis is considered, the gospel isn’t about God appeasing His own wrath through violence and death to avoid later burning us in a cosmic furnace. In Eden, any language of sacrifice, atonement, sin offerings, hell, or punishment is entirely absent—even in the making of garments for Adam and Eve to cover their shame.27 The gospel is instead about God overcoming violence with love, and death through resurrection. God’s justice is not Romanized justice, rooted in retribution, but covenantal restoration grounded in mercy. Not appeasement, but mercy.

That’s why the resurrection—not the torture and crucifixion—is the true climax of the gospel. Jesus didn’t just die for us; He rose for us. He defeated death so that we too might rise and share in His immortal life. Humanity triumphs over the tomb. Death doesn’t get the final word. Life does.

cGH

Chapter 4
The Gospel in Light of the Book of Acts

One might ask, “How do you know you aren’t the one misreading Eden and misunderstanding the gospel? After all, others read the story differently.” If that is the case, then a deeper question presses itself upon us: by what standard do we decide who is right and who is wrong?

The easiest way to understand what the gospel was before institutional Christianity emerged is by looking at the Book of Acts. There we find how the message was spread and what it looked and sounded like in its original, living context—proclaimed by Jewish apostles, rooted in Hebrew Scriptures, and alive with the Spirit’s power. And here’s a spoiler: it doesn’t mention God punishing Jesus, Eternal Conscious Torment in hellfire, or angels feeding believers grapes in paradise.

The Book of Acts begins by describing how “Jesus appeared to them over a period of forty days and spoke about the kingdom of God.” (Acts 1:3).

After the resurrection, Jesus spent forty days not teaching a seminary course on Systematic Theology, but "speaking of the things pertaining to the kingdom of God." (Acts 1:3).

What exactly is the Kingdom of God, and how and when do we get there?

Well, Jesus already declared, “The kingdom of God is within you” (Luke 17:21) and “the kingdom of God has come upon you” (Matthew 12:28). His kingdom wasn’t described as a distant afterlife destination—it was a present, living reality, planted within the hearts and lives of those who follow Him. It was about doing God's will—embodying His way in this world.

The Kingdom of God

The kingdom of God is... righteousness, peace, and joy in the Holy Spirit.

(Romans 14:17)

Paul’s teaching echoed the very same kingdom message Jesus had shared throughout His life and ministry. The Kingdom of God wasn’t some far-off place in outer space; it was about living by the example Jesus set during His time on earth.

Here is the logic: if you are an ambassador of a foreign Kingdom, and your life—like the lives of others from that Kingdom—is marked by love, kindness, forgiveness, gentleness, generosity, justice, faithfulness, and compassion, people will notice. They will be drawn to you. They will sense that you belong to a different order of life—a Kingdom that offers what their hearts already long for.

That attraction is how the gospel first spread. Not through coercion or fear, but through a way of life so compelling that others were willing to leave everything behind to join it, hoping to taste the same blessing, the same depth of life.

Now imagine that Kingdom continuing to grow—love upon love, justice upon justice—until every person is drawn into it. That vision is not a reason to abandon evangelism; it is the very reason for it.

It’s a bit like what the American Dream once was—but on a far higher plane. The American Dream promised freedom, liberty, and the chance to “make it big” materially. The Kingdom of God offers something infinitely better: the chance to make it big spiritually—to find true riches that neither moth nor rust can destroy.

But how?

Participating in the Kingdom of God

The very first recorded teaching of Jesus about the Kingdom of God appears at the beginning of his public ministry in Galilee, when he proclaimed, “The time is fulfilled, and the kingdom of God has come near; repent, and believe in the good news” (Mark 1:15). This moment, preserved in the earliest Gospel, shows that the Kingdom of God was the central theme of Jesus’ preaching from the outset. Jesus drew on Jewish expectations of God’s reign but gave them a new urgency, presenting the Kingdom as a present reality breaking into the world through his ministry and a future hope to be fully realized. Thus, from the very start of His mission, Jesus’ message was about God’s Kingdom breaking into history, inviting both Jews and Gentiles to turn away from all other spiritual and moral paths and to put their trust in Him by following His way of life.

In other words—you participate in the kingdom of God when you love others sacrificially, when you show mercy to the undeserving, when you forgive your enemies, when you uphold justice, when you take care of the poor, when you welcome the stranger, when you comfort the brokenhearted, when you live in humility and truth. By being an active part of His kingdom, you not only experience God’s blessings here on earth but also gain treasures in heaven.

This, rather than evading punishment in a cosmic torture chamber—was the focus of the Gospel message from the very beginning.

The Kingdom of God is not about waiting for heaven. It’s about bringing heaven into the world.

Acts: 25 Major Gospel Presentations

The Book of Acts records about 25 major gospel presentations.28 In all these presentations, we never see anyone preaching about hellfire, escapism, or scaring people as a form of motivation or threat. Instead, the apostles proclaimed how Jesus overcame death through resurrection, the fulfillment of the Hebrew Scriptures, the forgiveness of sins, and the arrival of God's Kingdom. The invitation wasn’t “turn or burn,” but rather a call to believe, let go of old ways, join the new Kingdom by following the Way of Christ, and enjoy a blessed life abundantly.

Paul, often called "the apostle to the Gentiles" (Romans 11:13), consistently modeled his evangelism by first visiting Jewish synagogues (e.g., Acts 9:20; 13:5; 17:1–2; 18:4), preaching not hellfire but Christ’s resurrection from death as the fulfillment of Israel’s hope. Even when preaching to Gentiles, as in Athens (Acts 17:22–31), Paul emphasized the resurrection and the call to repent from idolatry.

When Peter preached to Cornelius and his household (Acts 10:34–43), his message centered on Jesus’ life, death, resurrection, and authority—not eternal flames. Likewise, Paul’s sermon at Antioch (Acts 13:16–41) speaks about justification and forgiveness through Jesus.

Nothing about Original Sin, God punishing Jesus, or Eternal Conscious Torment in hellfire.

The Book of Acts ends with Paul—under house arrest in Rome—still “proclaiming the kingdom of God and teaching about the Lord Jesus Christ with all boldness and without hindrance.” (Acts 28:23-31).

The kingdom gospel preached throughout the Book of Acts had nothing to do with how to escape from hellfire and everything to do with becoming an ambassador of God's kingdom—living it out here and now, following Christ’s example as redeemed people who love, serve, and forgive others in Christ's name (e.g., Acts 3:6).

Overcoming Death: How the Early Jewish Church Understood the Gospel

The book of Acts makes it clear that conquering death was the heartbeat of the gospel as the early Jewish and Gentile believers understood it. For them, the good news was not about escaping endless torment in the afterlife. It was about Christ's decisive victory over the ancient enemy introduced in Genesis: death itself.

What we find is a bold proclamation that Jesus defeated death. That He rose from the grave. That death no longer has the final word over humanity. That the power of the grave has been broken, and life—eternal, incorruptible life—has triumphed:

God raised him from the dead, freeing him from the agony of death, because it was impossible for death to keep its hold on him...Seeing what was ahead, he spoke of the resurrection of the Christ, that he was not abandoned to the grave, nor did his body see decay.

(Acts 2:24,31)

If hellfire were understood as the fate awaiting most of humanity, why didn’t Peter and Paul ever mention it? Why didn't it dominate their speeches in synagogues and marketplaces?

The answer is simple: The Gospel shared by Jesus's Jewish apostles for the first two centuries differs significantly from the gospel that is commonly advocated in many modern Western traditions today, especially the Reformed ones (Evangelical, Calvinist, Baptist, Pentecostal, etc.).

This changes everything. Because if death is the real enemy, then salvation isn’t about getting pulled out of God's furnace—it’s about being made alive again. In Christ. Through Christ. With Christ.

It’s about being grafted back into the life we were cut off from. It’s about re-entering the Garden. Eating from the Tree of Life. Living forever in communion with God—not because we earned it by works or intellect, but because Jesus conquered the thing that was keeping us from living forever—death.

Conclusion

If doctrines like hellfire torment were central to humanity’s destiny and to Christ’s mission, we would expect the apostles—Peter, Paul, Stephen, James, Philip—to have made them central to their preaching. After all, the Acts of the Apostles records the spread of the gospel across Israel and the Roman world. If avoiding Eternal Conscious Torment were the Good News, Acts should be saturated with warnings about it.

Yet the silence is striking: hell (Gehenna) is not mentioned once in Acts.29 Not in Peter’s sermons. Not in Stephen’s defense. Not in Paul’s synagogue debates or public proclamations. This absence is not accidental. It reveals that the apostles did not frame the Good News around Western notions of Eternal Conscious Torment.

They proclaimed resurrection, forgiveness, restoration, the reign of God, and the defeat of death—not threats of endless torture. In other words, they understood Jesus’ teaching very differently from how it is often read through later Western lenses.

If we want to hear the gospel as it was first preached, we must be willing to leap over the last 1,700 years of inherited assumptions and listen again to the apostles themselves.

cJ4

Chapter 5
The Gospel in Light of the Didache

When we learn about the post-Acts Church from extra-biblical documents, such as 1 Clement, Epistle of Barnabas, Shepherd of Hermas, Ignatius’ Letters, Epistle to Diognetus, and Justin Martyr’s works—we never find them claiming that God punished His Son to satisfy divine wrath and avoid tormenting us. Nor do we see them warning about spending forever in flames; none of these ideas are presented.

Even after the biblical canon was completed, we can still examine what Jesus’ disciples taught and the gospel they proclaimed by turning to the Didache—a document that has survived and is still revered to this day.

But first, let’s remember some context.

In the first-century Jewish world, the great spiritual concerns were:

— Faithfulness to the covenant (which some sects believed also embraced oral traditions).

— The arrival of the Messiah (whom some imagined as a warrior).

— The resurrection at the end of the age (unless you were a Sadducee).

— The defeat of evil and the renewal of the world ("Tikkun Olam").

The apostles preached Christ as the fulfillment of these hopes. They presented Jesus not as escapism but as the One through whom God's new creation had begun.

The Didache

The Didache, also known as The Teaching of the Twelve Apostles, is one of the earliest Christian texts outside the New Testament, likely written in the first or early second century.30 Interestingly, despite its strong focus on ethics, community living, and readiness for the Lord’s return, it makes no reference to any of the later-added Western doctrines—just as these are missing from the Book of Acts.

Instead, the Didache draws a sharp contrast between what it calls the “Way of Life” and the “Way of Death.” These terms, however, aren’t metaphysical labels for a future heaven or hell, but rather ethical and spiritual frameworks that guide how one chooses to live—whether to glorify God by living in line with the Kingdom of God or not.

In both biblical and Jewish thought, “life” was never primarily about the afterlife, but about life here and now—a way of living marked by covenant faithfulness, vitality, and communion with God (e.g., Deuteronomy 30:19–20; John 10:10; Romans 8:6).

The Way of Life is marked by love, generosity, humility, and moral integrity; The Way of Death—a path of hatred and self-sabotage that ends in self-destruction—is marked by cruelty, greed, and lawlessness. It’s a life turned inward, cut off from compassion, and blind to the beauty of God’s ways.

While the Didache text certainly warns of consequences for evil and urges repentance and vigilance in light of the coming judgment, it never describes these consequences in terms of Eternal Conscious Torment. In fact, the eschatological section at the end of the Didache (chapter 16) reflects an early Jewish-Christian apocalyptic worldview. It speaks of the appearance of the deceiver, persecution, and the return of the Lord, but again, it's lacking any graphic depiction of everlasting punishment or fiery damnation. The emphasis is far more on perseverance, ethical living, and faithfulness than on the fear of hellfire. The good news wasn’t understood as an intellectual belief one had to master to be “saved,” but as living ethically in a way that glorifies God and brings about life—His blessings.

The Quiet of the Early Church: Listening to the Didache’s Silence

The silence is significant. If the doctrines we hear preached from Western pulpits today were a foundational teaching of Jesus or the apostles, we would reasonably expect to find it here—in a document designed to instruct new believers. Yet the Didache, while deeply concerned with righteousness and divine justice, makes no appeal to such doctrines or to the fear of hellfire as a motivational tool. This suggests that these ideas were foreign to the earliest communities of Jesus' followers.

In this light, the Didache—much like the Acts of the Apostles—offers a rare and invaluable window into earliest Christianity. It reflects a faith where discipleship and communal ethics, shaped by love and mercy, took priority over fear-driven doctrine. Judgment is acknowledged, but it is not framed through the later philosophical machinery of endless torment.

That entanglement came much later. Only after the influence of Augustine of Hippo in the fourth century did Western theology begin to recast judgment in terms of perpetual damnation. The Didache stands as a witness to a simpler, more grounded gospel—one concerned less with metaphysical terror and more with forming a people who walk the way of life.

Later portrayals like the medieval concept of hellfire—a cosmic unending fiery torture chamber—is a theological development far removed from the authentic apostolic preaching. The apostles' original Gospel was good news for all—not a horror story designed to terrify sinners into belief.

Conclusion

Salvation is not God rescuing us from an angry version of Himself. It’s about God rescuing us from what we’ve become without Him. The good news is not that a few of us will receive a "get out of infinite jail" card. The good news is that the consequence of sin—death—has been overcome, and true, immortal, divine life is now within reach.

The gospel is not a threat of abuse, but a proclamation of life. Not wrath satisfied, but death undone. The omission of hellfire in apostolic preaching and the Didache raises important questions. It is not a minor detail—it’s a flashing neon sign telling us that the center of the Gospel was never fear, but hope.

The apostles proclaimed a gospel of restoration and reconciliation, not a gospel of exclusion and torment. They preached Jesus’ victory over death—not an escape plan from flames. Their call was to repent—to turn from idols; to believe—not merely intellectually, but by following; and to enter into life. It was not an invitation to fear a God eager to torment and destroy, but to trust the God who heals, restores, and makes all things new.

cK4

Chapter 6
The Gospel in Light of the Apostles’ Creed

The Apostles’ Creed—whose earliest roots reach back to the late 2nd century, with forms developing through the 4th century onwards—stands as one of the earliest and most enduring summaries of Christian belief.
