Though not written by the apostles themselves, it reflects an earlier “Rule of Faith” that encapsulated the core teachings they passed on. For centuries, it served as a baptismal confession and a benchmark of orthodoxy across the Christian world.

However, after the Apostles’ Creed took shape, various Christian traditions began producing their own creeds, confessions, and doctrinal statements. Over time, this resulted in additional layers of interpretation and dogma—many of which go beyond, and sometimes diverge from, the beliefs the apostles originally proclaimed.

Here’s the English translation of the traditional form, with slight wording variations among translations shown in [brackets]:

I believe in God, the Father almighty, creator of heaven and earth. I believe in Jesus Christ, his only Son, our Lord, who was conceived by the Holy Spirit, born of the Virgin Mary, suffered under Pontius Pilate, was crucified, died, and was buried; he descended to hell [to the dead]. On the third day he rose again; he ascended into heaven, he is seated at the right hand of the Father, and he will come again to judge the living and the dead. I believe in the Holy Spirit, the holy catholic [universal] Church, the communion of saints, the forgiveness of sins, the resurrection of the body, and the life everlasting. Amen.

Descended Where?!

The phrase “he descended to hell [the dead]" may sound confusing to modern ears.

The Greek version of the Apostles’ Creed reads “katelthonta eis ta katôtata.” Meaning, “descended to lower ones,” or “descended to those below.” The Latin version reads “descendit ad inferos,” where “inferos” (not infernos with an “n”!) means “those below,” like the word “inferior.” So, the Latin inferos corresponds pretty accurately with the Greek katôtata.

In the earliest centuries, when the Creed was forming, “hell” was understood in the sense of Hades (Greek) or Sheol (Hebrew)—the realm of the dead, the grave, the place where all dead rest, awaiting the resurrection. To say that Christ descended there was simply to affirm that he truly experienced death in full and entered the common fate of humanity in order to conquer it from within (through resurrection). It wasn’t understood that Jesus entered a place of fiery punishment but that He fully shared in our human death and then triumphed over it.

Jesus descended to the place of the dead (Sheol–Hades–hell) to proclaim the good news of resurrection to those who had already died (See Ephesians 4:9, 1 Peter 3:18–19). He didn’t go down to gloat—He went to announce life to the dead. The gospel reaches into the depths, declaring that even death is not beyond the reach of God’s redeeming love.

Christ’s resurrection reverses the curse of death, and what was lost in Eden is now made accessible through Him.

When the word ‘dead’ was first translated as ‘hell,’ it still carried the meaning of ‘grave.’ But over time, the English word hell shifted in meaning, coming to signify afterlife punishment (whether endless or temporary)—something the original text did not intend. Over time, especially under Catholic theology of the Middle Ages, “hell” hardened into the idea of an infernal realm of fiery punishment. When the Protestant reformers broke away from Catholicism in the 16th century, they inherited this same worldview and carried forward the medieval interpretation of “hell” as Eternal Conscious Torment. Ever since, many Christians have seen God as cruel and even sadistic rather than loving and merciful. No wonder so many today deconstruct their faith: they cannot reconcile God’s love with the idea of punishment divorced from discipline and growth. Truth be told—punishment without purpose is not justice; it is sheer sadism.

Saved from Death

In the first few centuries, no Christian would have imagined Jesus visiting the furnace of torment that modern infernalists/fundamentalists project onto the text. Eastern Christianity, for instance, never quite read the text this way, continuing to emphasize Hades as simply “the realm of the dead”—the grave that Christ broke open in victory.

The Creed was never meant to depict God’s Son descending into a torture chamber, but to affirm that He entered fully into death—that He passed through the grave in order to shatter humanity’s coffin.

When we read the Creed in the way it was intended, a far more hopeful vision emerges: Jesus truly died, truly shared humanity’s common fate, and from within death itself truly broke open the path to resurrection and life everlasting:

For since death came through a human being, the resurrection of the dead has also come through a human being; for as all die in Adam, so all will be made alive in Christ. 

(1 Corinthians 15:21–22)

All die because of Adam, and all will be made alive through Christ. Then comes judgment. Everyone who has died remains in the grave, waiting for the resurrection and the final judgment.

Conclusion

Before we crank up the chrono-engine and return to the present, let’s take a quick moment to recap.

After examining the three primary early sources that reveal what the gospel looked like before Christianity became institutionalized, one conclusion becomes unavoidable: Western constructs—such as being “saved” from an angry God who had to kill His own Son, or from infinite torment in hellfire—were not part of the original apostolic proclamation. These ideas emerged later, layered onto the gospel rather than flowing from it.

Who do you think understood the gospel more faithfully: first and second century Jews—many of whom personally knew Jesus, walked the same dusty roads, and spoke His language—or a fourth-century Gentile theologian named Augustine and a sixteenth-century Gentile European lawyer named John Calvin, seated in their studies and filtering Jesus’ message through foreign translations and Western legal categories? Honestly, the very comparison should make us smile. On the one hand, Jewish eyewitnesses who actually lived the story; on the other, a lawyer half a world and fifteen centuries removed, trying to systematize it into a courtroom framework. It's up to you to decide which sounds closer to the heart of the Gospel…

So far, we have traced how the earliest believers understood the gospel. The remainder of this book will show why they understood it that way—biblically, theologically, and historically. We will also examine how, over the centuries, that original vision was gradually reshaped—at times subtly, at times radically—into the version many have inherited today. What follows is a return to the roots of the Good News, to recover its power, its beauty, and its original meaning.

cM4

Chapter 7
From a Jewish Movement to an Imperialistic Western Religion

It was a quiet morning on the shores of Galilee. The fire crackled softly. The scent of fish rose in the mist. And there stood Peter, still carrying the weight of his three denials like invisible chains. He had seen the risen Christ. He had witnessed the impossible. But restoration is not automatic—it must be spoken, received, and lived.

And Jesus, ever the Shepherd, asked him not for his statement of faith, not for penance, not for grand declarations of ministry vision. He simply asked, three times: “Do you love me?” (John 21:15-17).

I can picture Peter’s first answer as a whisper, eyes fixed on his Master’s feet; his second, spoken with a clearer voice and head held a bit higher; and his third, delivered with a loud, strong, confident tone and a smile spreading across his face.

Three times. Each time, Peter’s wounded heart offered what it could. And each time, Jesus responded not with theological affirmation, but with a command of movement: “Feed my lambs… Tend my sheep… Feed my sheep.”

And notice something critical: Jesus does not say, “Feed your sheep.” He says, “Feed my sheep.” They belong to Him. They are His beloved. And He has entrusted their care not to governments, not to systems, not to charities—but to His followers. Jesus' kingdom was never about control, coercion, or frightening the sheep into obedience. It was about love expressed through service.

Not an Institution but a Movement, Not a System but a Flock

Peter—like all of Jesus’ Jewish disciples—was deeply familiar with the shepherd imagery woven throughout the Hebrew Scriptures. Israel is repeatedly called God’s flock, and God Himself is portrayed as the Shepherd who guides, protects, and restores His people (Genesis 48:15; 49:24; Psalm 23:1; 79:13; 95:7; 100:3; Isaiah 40:11; Jeremiah 50:6–7; Ezekiel 34:11–15, 31; Micah 7:14).

This time, though, something significant was changing. Jesus was appointing Peter—and the group of disciples with him—to take on the role of Israel’s new "priestly" shepherds. Jesus had already warned the existing leaders that their stewardship would be taken away and given to others who would bear its fruit (see the Parable of the Two Sons, Matthew 21:43). And here, in Peter’s restoration, that transfer of spiritual leadership begins to unfold.

Still Jewish. Still Israel. But now under new leadership—Jewish fishermen, tax collectors, and former outcasts—those whom God chose to lead His people not by pedigree or prestige, but by humility, self-giving, and devotion to the Way of Messiah.

It was not simply an ancient metaphor tucked away in mystery. It was an assignment. A calling. A shift from the disciples' inward shame to outward love. From silence to service. From belief to embodiment.

This is where we begin. Because before there was a religion called Christianity, before there was even a collection of letters called the New Testament, there was the nation of Israel. There were the Hebrew Scriptures. And there was Yeshua—Jewish, rooted, speaking in a language shaped by Torah and Prophets, calling back the people of God to what they had always been meant to be: shepherds who reflect the loving heart of the Shepherd.

But this time, they won’t just shepherd Israel—they’ll shepherd the entire world. God didn’t abandon Israel; He ensured His Chosen People would indeed fulfill their mission and become a light to the nations. It was only Israel's leadership that He had to replace.

But much has changed since then. Christianity is no longer a Jewish movement; it has become a distinctly Western religion. And the way the gospel is commonly portrayed today is not how the biblical authors expressed it. It is a picture shaped by later, non-Jewish generations.

This shift emerged through a long chain of distortions: misunderstandings of biblical words and idioms, the loss of Jewish ways of thinking, the confusion of literary genres, and the flattening of rich ancient metaphors into rigid, literal, philosophical categories. Terms that once spoke of restoration, correction, purification, and covenantal consequence were slowly reinterpreted through foreign frameworks and severed from their original Jewish soil. What was once relational and redemptive became abstract, legal, and forensic—less the product of the biblical imagination and more the result of centuries of human reinterpretation.

"It All Started with Paul!"

I often hear people argue Paul founded Christianity. I find it absurd to claim that Paul invented a new religion called Christianity because Paul never understood himself that way, never spoke that way, and never acted that way.

Paul, like other first-century believers, never observed Christmas, Easter, Lent, Advent, or other later church traditions. Instead, he celebrated Israel's feasts of Tabernacles, Firstfruits, Shavuot, Passover, and Yom Kippur, continuing to keep the Jewish festivals (Acts 20:16; 1 Corinthians 16:8). Paul didn’t confess sins in a booth, pray the rosary, or follow later church rituals. Rather, he took part in Jewish purification rites (Acts 21:23–26), arranged for Timothy’s circumcision (Acts 16:3), and lived according to the Jewish religious rhythms of his time, following the practices that shaped Israel’s covenant life.

Paul was a Second Temple Jew—trained as a Pharisee, steeped in the Hebrew Scriptures, and fiercely loyal to Israel’s God. He labeled himself a Hebrew (Philippians 3:5), a Jew (Acts 22:3), and an Israelite (Romans 11:1). He did not abandon Judaism; he embodied a new stream within it—the denomination of those who followed Rabbi Yeshua; Israel's Messiah.

Within Judaism, one measure of a rabbi’s greatness is the number of non-Jewish disciples who come to follow Israel’s God through him. The logic was: if you are truly a blessed rabbi, even gentiles will follow you. In that sense, Rabbi Jesus and, later, Rabbi Paul through Him, founded the largest Jewish denomination in history. That stream may later be called “Christianity,” or any other name, but the reality remains: it is Jewish at its core, regardless of the labels attached to it.

Jews often study through debate. Paul argued with other Jews—often fiercely—that Israel’s story had reached its climax in Jesus of Nazareth, Israel’s Messiah (Acts 9:20; 13:5; 13:14; 14:1; 17:1–2; 17:10; 18:4). His later letters are saturated with the Hebrew Law and Prophets, and the Psalms; with Jewish language, Jewish temple imagery, and distinctly Jewish ethical categories. A man does not invent a “new religion” while relentlessly appealing to the authority of the old one.

Paul didn’t introduce a new god, point to a new scripture, or establish a new moral vision. When he told Timothy that “all Scripture is God-breathed” (2 Timothy 3:16), the New Testament hadn’t yet been canonized. He was referring to Israel’s Scriptures, the Hebrew Bible. He preached that the God of Abraham had acted decisively through the risen Messiah to fulfill His promises and extend blessing to the nations. That is not a new religious invention; it is an intra-Jewish proclamation. Disagreement within a tradition does not equal departure from it.

Nor did Paul invent “Christianity” as a system. He wrote occasional letters to real communities, addressing concrete problems—table fellowship, Torah observance, persecution, allowing non-Jews to join, and unity between Jews and Gentiles. Paul was not founding an institution; he was shepherding communities he believed were already living inside Israel’s long-awaited redemption.

The Gentiles did not replace Israel; they became part of one of Israel’s own movements—what later came to be called “Christianity.” In fact, Jews who followed Yeshua as their Messiah were initially identified by other Jews as Nazarenes (e.g., Acts 24:5), alongside groups such as the Sadducees, Pharisees, Herodians, and Essenes. It was only later, in Antioch, that they were called Christians—likely as a label of mockery or derision (Acts 11:26).

The very idea of “Christianity” as a religion separate from Judaism is a later historical development, shaped by Gentile dominance, Roman categories, Greek spirituality (mostly Neoplatonism and Gnosticism), and post-Temple realities. Projecting that back onto Paul is an anachronism. It confuses later outcomes with original intent.

Paul did not invent a new religion. He proclaimed what he believed was the fulfillment of Israel’s story—and argued that the nations were now being invited into it. For me—as a Jew—the vast number of Gentiles drawn to Jesus does not undermine His Jewishness; it confirms it. It demonstrates that following Jesus is the most compelling stream of Judaism—the one that fulfilled Israel’s calling to draw the nations. If anything deserves to be called the truest expression of Jewish faith, it is the path through which the God of Israel finally reached the world.

The Fourth Century: Where It All Began

A message born in the dust of Judea—fierce, earthy, and unmistakably Jewish—was carried into the halls of Greece and Rome, where foreign philosophies reshaped it into something fundamentally different. What emerged was a gospel refracted through pagan lenses, altered in both form and meaning. As time passed, this Greco-Roman reinterpretation was further Europeanized, producing the version of Christianity most familiar in the modern West.

As a result, the 21st-century Western gospel often sounds something like this:

God the Father is utterly furious with you because you fail to match His perfection. Naturally, He must uphold His justice by tormenting your soul and torturing your body forever in everlasting hellfire. But you’re fortunate: Christ stepped in to absorb the Father’s rage, appease His wrath, and allow a select few to escape what would otherwise be their deserved fate.

This distorted portrayal began to take shape around the fourth century and continued to evolve from there. When Christianity emerged as an official, separate religion in that period, it did not merely drift from its Jewish roots—it became increasingly hostile toward them. Antisemitism, tragically, played a decisive role in reshaping how many Christians came to understand the gospel itself.

Once Christianity became intertwined with Neoplatonism, Gnosticism, and imperial power, distancing itself from its Jewish source was seen as both politically useful and theologically convenient. Over time, this hostility produced interpretations of Scripture that obscured the original context and Jewish identity of Jesus and His message, severed the Church from the Hebrew story that birthed it, and distorted the message of the gospel itself.

As the Church expanded across the Greco-Roman world, it absorbed the culture, categories, and philosophical assumptions of its new environment. The context changed. The language changed. The logic changed. The worldview changed. And with it—the gospel changed.

What was once a vibrant, embodied faith—rooted in living out God’s will in every aspect of daily and communal life—was slowly reduced to a contest of correct ideas, individual confessions, and systematized creeds. Faith became an assent to abstract doctrines. Doctrines became tickets to heaven. The heart and body were exiled, and the mind enthroned.

The Birth of Western Christian Religion (313-325 A.D.)

The intentional historical separation of Jewish elements from Christianity and the disconnect of Jesus' teachings from the Hebrew Scriptures most visibly began with Emperor Constantine (4th century) who actively uprooted Christianity from its Jewish roots in his efforts to establish a distinct, "Jewish-free" identity: “Let us then have nothing in common with the detestable Jewish crowd,”31 declared Constantine. Such efforts included removing Jewish-Christian priests from positions of leadership,32 promoting new doctrines and local practices, and introducing various pagan elements to make the faith feel more familiar and comfortable to Western converts.33 Together, these shifts steadily eroded the Jewish character of the early church and pushed Jewish believers to the margins. Constantine legalized Christianity (Edict of Milan, 313) and initiated the fusion of imperial power and Christian authority. Within just a few decades, Christianity had been reshaped into an entirely new Western imperialistic religion—one that bore very little resemblance to its original, Hebrew foundations.

The First Council of Nicaea, held in 325 AD in Turkey (convened by Emperor Constantine), is arguably the most famous event in post-biblical Christian history. This event was pivotal in early Christianity, and while there were some positive aspects, bishops of Jewish heritage were not allowed to participate in the council.34 This had notable, long-lasting repercussions not only for Christian-Jewish relations but also on Christian theology.

In an effort to “de-Judaize” the faith, the Council eliminated most biblical holidays and altered others, such as moving the celebration of the Resurrection from the Feast of First Fruits to the newly established Easter, intentionally creating distance from the "Jewish" calendar. Their reasoning was explicit: “For it is unbecoming beyond measure that on this holiest of festivals, we should follow the customs of the Jews. Henceforth, let us have nothing in common with this odious people.”35 It was also decided to replace the biblical Passover with Easter and the biblical Saturday Sabbath (Genesis 2:3) with Sunday.36

The persecution of the Quartodecimans signaled the start of a very dark era. The word "Quartodeciman," meaning "fourteeners" in Latin, referred mainly to Jews but also to many devoted Christians who observed the biblical Passover on the 14th of Nisan,37 as stated in Leviticus 23:5.

Performing circumcision was outlawed, and violations were now fervently punishable by death.38 Eventually, Jews were prohibited from holding public office or serving as officers in the military. Later, restrictions were put on where the Jewish people could live, with whom they could do business, and where they could travel.39 By the end of the 4th century, Christianity had merged with imperial Roman power and become structurally and legally antisemitic, largely disconnected from its Hebraic roots.40

The Infusion of Paganism

When Christianity became the imperial religion, it did not emerge in a vacuum. It inherited, adapted, and repurposed many elements from the surrounding pagan Roman world. Pagan temples became churches, civic basilicas became Christian gathering spaces, and imperial festivals were recast as Christian holy days.41 Ritual forms such as incense, processions, vestments, and the veneration of saints and relics—none characteristic of early Jewish‑Christian practice—became standard features of worship.42 Christian theology and intellectual life drew heavily on Greco‑Roman philosophy, especially Neoplatonism, which shaped emerging doctrines of God, the soul, salvation, hell, and the structure of the cosmos.43 In effect, Christianity became the religious heir to the Roman Empire even as it condemned (other kinds of) pagan worship and distanced itself from Judaism.

The result was a new kind of Christianity: legally privileged, culturally dominant, philosophically Hellenized, and deeply intertwined with imperial power. It was no longer the small Jewish sect of the first century but the foundation of a Christian empire, carrying forward Roman forms even as it enforced a new religious order.

From the Faith of Scripture to the Religion of Empire

Theologically speaking, it comes as no surprise that Christianity has completely distanced itself from its Jewish context, profoundly reshaping Christian theology, causing it to lose sight of Jesus and Paul as Jewish rabbis and to neglect the need to interpret their teachings within their original Jewish context.

Christians today often emphasize that the New Covenant replaces the Old, yet they sometimes overlook an essential point: the Old Covenant commandments never spoke of the afterlife. They were about this life—how to treat other humans (Leviticus 19), how to walk in God’s favor (Deuteronomy 11), how to enjoy His blessings (Leviticus 18), and how to avoid the curses and consequences that come from disobedience (Deuteronomy 28).

When Jesus said He came to fulfill the Law (Matthew 5:17) and then delivered the Sermon on the Mount, He continued in the same trajectory. His teaching echoes the heart of the Torah: a way of life that leads to flourishing rather than destruction, blessing rather than curse. Jesus did not shift the focus to a different realm entirely but deepened and clarified the very same divine law and Hebrew logic of blessing that God revealed from the beginning.

Over time, the historical and theological gap turned into a distortion that still influences how the Bible is interpreted and translated today—often seen through a Westernized lens instead of being understood in its genuine Jewish context. By Jewish, I do not speak of the later Rabbinic tradition that eventually shaped much of post-Temple Judaism. Rather, I am referring to biblical Judaism—the faith of ancient Israel—including Moses, David, the Prophets, and, of course, Jesus and His disciples.

Conclusion—The Gospel After Christianity

The Gospel, once a vibrant and radical Jewish way of living life, was taken over by Greek philosophy—mainly Gnosticism and Neoplatonism—and reshaped into an intellectual formula to be mastered; a doctrinal system to mentally adhere to in order to avoid hellfire in the afterlife.

According to the Lausanne Movement, there are approximately "49,000 Christian denominations/rites" today.44 Each Christian group claims that their own interpretation is the true key that unlocks salvation. The Catholic Church elevates transubstantiation and sacramental participation as essential. Pentecostal traditions imply that speaking in tongues is the evidence of genuine salvation. Calvary Chapel treats its particular rapture theory as indispensable. Reformed churches raise adherence to T.U.L.I.P.45 to the level of orthodoxy. Lutherans insist that justification must be understood strictly within their confessional categories. Baptists often claim that their formula of “biblical baptism” validates true faith. Evangelicals demand a definable, often highly emotional, “born-again” experience as proof of salvation. Some holiness movements add sinless perfection or strict moral codes. Seventh-day Adventists insist on Sabbath observance. Messianic groups adhere to dietary laws. Mennonites proclaim that head covering is required for salvation. Others demand adherence to a specific creation-narrative framework or the rejection of modern science altogether. Still others hinge salvation on allegiance to a particular saint, movement, Bible translation, or end-times chart—each confident that their gate is the narrow one, and that God has entrusted its key to them.

In all these cases, grace arrives only after you intellectually agree to a denomination’s preferred theological framework. Salvation becomes conditional—not upon God's love or Christ’s work, but upon the correct doctrinal password.

And from there, it often ceases to be a path of life and instead becomes a doctrine to debate—essentially a fear-based religion.

Regrettably, much of today’s Western Christianity is haunted by this legacy. Immortal life is imagined as a reward for mental agreement with the “right” doctrinal formulas—a cosmic "escape room" challenge where only the intellectually correct avoid fiery punishment while most others burn forever. But for Jesus and his contemporaries, faith was not an intellectual answer to a theological exam for the sake of escapism; faith was a way of living, loving, and being present to God in every moment.

And so we are left with a quiet but unavoidable contrast: the faith that began around a charcoal fire in Galilee—Jewish, relational, embodied, and restorative—versus the religion that later arose in the halls of empire, abstracted, systematized, and severed from its roots. One asked only, “Do you love me?” The other learned to ask, “Do you agree?” To recover the gospel, then, is not to invent something new, but to return—to the Shepherd’s voice before it was filtered through power, philosophy, and fear.

cP2

Chapter 8
Life from the Dead

I’d like to wrap up the first part of the book with Zechariah’s vision from chapter 14.

Why do you believe the Lord descends to the Mount of Olives in Zechariah's vision? Because from ancient times (at least as early as the 8th century BC) to this very day, the Mount of Olives has been a symbol of death.46 It was—and still is—one of the most significant burial sites in the Land of Israel. In the worldview of a person living in biblical times, saying “Mount of Olives” was essentially like saying “the cemetery.” This was the place where the dead were laid to rest. It symbolized the threshold between the realm of the living and the dead.

There were no cemeteries in Los Angeles, Paris, or Sydney in antiquity. For the people of ancient Israel, the Mount of Olives served as a central and visible gateway to Sheol—the underworld of the dead. Zechariah's prophetic expectation that "his feet will stand on the Mount of Olives, east of Jerusalem, and the Mount of Olives will be split in two" (Zechariah 14:4) made perfect sense within Israel's geographic, cultural, and spiritual imagination: The graveyard would open wide and release the deceased—resurrection of the dead. This was Israel’s hope and the mission Christ will fulfill upon His return.

The apostles proclaimed the same coherent story—a gospel not of terror, but of life. They announced that in Jesus, God had forgiven sin and defeated death. And death, in Scripture, is not merely biological; it fractures relationships, corrodes societies, and deforms the world itself. In Christ, God broke into history to heal what was broken—to restore persons, renew communities, and redeem creation.

The call to repent was never a threat. It was an invitation: to turn from destructive paths and step into God’s life-giving work, His mission of Tikkun Olam—the restoration of His creation.

Restoring Individuals

Want to live a meaningful life? Want to see justice win, peace replace violence, and love shatter the chains of hate? Then the Gospel isn’t just an optional path—it’s the way. Jesus didn't call people to escape the world, but to redeem it through Him. The Gospel offers the power to subdue chaos, to live in right relationship with God and others, and to help build the kind of world we were created for—a world where love, truth, and justice reign. This was the apostolic message: that Christ’s resurrection inaugurated a new creation, and those who follow Him become agents of that divine renewal and heavenly rewards.

Fear of Eternal Conscious Torment was never the engine of evangelism. Hope, renewal, and restoration were. The question is: will we take the path that leads to life and blessing, or will we keep repeating the same cycles of self-inflicted destruction?

Renewing Communities

While often interpreted through an individualistic perspective today, the Scriptures are fundamentally communal in nature. Every individual is viewed as part of a larger whole. Salvation isn’t just about living again or changing your life alone; it’s about transforming society as a whole.

While you are no longer doomed to remain under the curse of death—it is still up to us, not only as individuals but ultimately as entire societies, to decide how we live now. Historically, some societies chose to follow the way of Christ; many others did not, and the fruit of those choices is evident all around us.

Listen carefully to Acts 26:18:

I [Jesus] am sending you to them to open their eyes, so that they may turn from darkness to light and from the power of Satan to God, that they may receive forgiveness of sins and a place among those who are sanctified by faith in me.

(Acts 26:18)

The Gospel is not merely about securing life after death; it is about transforming the world now. It is the spiritual conquest of darkness by light, of chaos by wholeness, of destruction by healing. It transfers individuals and societies into a new way of life—one marked by freedom, restoration, and the life God always intended.

The Gospel empowers people to live rightly today, and when whole communities begin to embody God’s peace, mercy, and justice—when societies themselves join His kingdom and are reshaped by His love—we move toward the fulfillment of Tikkun Olam: the restoration of the world.

Conclusion

Let us summarize the first part of the book.

In the first centuries, the Jewish apostles consistently proclaimed victory over death and the resurrection as the decisive turning point in history. They declared Christ to be the fulfillment of the biblical story—found in the Hebrew Scriptures, the Law and the Prophets—and invited all people to participate, not as an escape from afterlife torment in hellfire, but in transformation, forgiveness, reconciliation, and empowerment to share in the inauguration of His kingdom on earth: a kingdom marked by justice, peace, and the renewal of all things.

Every act of love, every word of truth, every glimpse of hope matters.

The Gospel isn’t about escaping from a broken world.

It’s about redeeming it—through the life of Christ flowing through us.

So, perhaps a clearer way to understand Romans 1:16 would be something like this:

For I am not ashamed to live according to the way of Christ, for this way is the power of God to change and reform, to grow and restore, to heal and rescue, to save and renew all who trust it and walk in it—it is first relevant to the Jew, and also to the Gentile.

(Romans 1:16, Eitan's paraphrase)

As we head into the next part of the book, make sure your time machine is working. Buckle up and fire up the ignition—it’s time for another trip through history, this time to the fascinating world of ancient Israel!

cR6

Part II: #Blood #Atonement #Sacrifice

cRD

Introduction: Life is in the Blood

My grandmother survived the Holocaust and came to Israel from Transylvania, so whenever my mom goes heavy on the garlic in her cooking, the Dracula jokes inevitably start—an ongoing tradition in my Jewish mother’s kitchen.

If you've never heard of the Western tale of Dracula, then let me tell you a tale about the windswept peaks and shadowed valleys of Transylvania, a story unlike any you've ever heard…

Once, in the deep forests of Transylvania, they whispered of a man who could not die.

He walked in shadows, cloaked in centuries of hunger. His name echoed through castles and candlelit tales: Dracula. The legend said he thirsted for blood—not for its taste, but for its power. For in the crimson current of life, he found the illusion of eternity.

He did not give. He took.

To live, he had to drain others. Night after night, victim after victim, he pressed his teeth to the necks of the innocent and drew life into his death-cursed body. His immortality was built on the sacrifice of strangers. The more blood he drank, the more he lived. The more he lived, the more he had to feed.

His curse was not just that he consumed blood—it was that he needed to. Desperately. Endlessly. Forever.

His life depended on blood.

And yet—behind the old myth, beneath the superstitions and garlic-laced jokes of grandmothers in Eastern Europe—there was a strange and hidden truth:

Life is in the blood.

Even the darkest fables know this. They know that blood is sacred. That to take it is to take something holy. That whoever controls blood, controls life itself.

But What if the Story Were Told in Reverse?

What if there was another Immortal—One who came from the east and beyond time, not cloaked in shadow, but clothed in light? (Psalm 104:2)

What if, instead of craving blood to live, He gave His blood so that others might live? (Romans 3:25)

What if He, too, stretched out His arms—not to seize a victim, but to embrace the whole world? (John 3:17)

What if He, too, hungered—but not for life to consume, rather for death to defeat? (Hebrews 2:14)

What if His blood, rather than being stolen drop by drop, was poured out willingly, cup overflowing, not to curse, but to cleanse? (Matthew 26:28)

This One, too, walked among mortals.

But He did not hide from the sun. He was the Sun.

He did not lurk in tombs. He emptied them.

He did not drain others to live. He let Himself be drained, so that life eternal might flow into the veins of the dying.

His blood—unlike Dracula’s victims’—never runs dry. It's not weak but eternal.

His life is not prolonged by theft.

It is offered freely, joyfully, abundantly.

Where Dracula leaves bodies pale and breathless, this One breathes life into dry bones.

Where Dracula fears the cross, this One hung upon it—not as victim, but as Victor.

And so the Gospel is not about horror, but hope.

Not about a bloodthirsty god demanding death, but a life-giving God defeating it.

You are about to step into a story deeper than myth and older than legend.

A story of altars and lifeblood, of breath and dust, of temples and transfusions.

It’s a story where blood doesn’t signal vengeance, but life.

Where sacrifice is not punishment, but a gift.

Where atonement is not transaction, but transformation.

This is the story of a Messiah, whose blood speaks a better word.

This is the story of life conquering death.

If Jesus is the Christ who gives life, Dracula embodies the anti-Christ, seeking to drain and destroy it.

God is not like a vampire—He does not need blood to be satisfied or a victim to calm His nerves. God is like Jesus: not taking life to soothe Himself but giving His life so that others may live.

Dracula hides in the shadows; Jesus is the Light of the World.

Dracula feeds on death; Jesus conquered death.

Maybe that's why vampires fear the cross.

So, in my family’s playful whispers about garlic and fangs, there’s a hidden theology waiting to be named:

There is one who craved blood because he lacked life. And One who gave blood because He is life.

Let the legend of Dracula serve as a dark mirror—a reflection of flawed theology and a shadow of the antichrist.

And let Jesus shine as the radiant reversal.

The true Lord of Blood is not a monster. He is the Messiah. And His blood doesn’t haunt. It heals.

cT3

Chapter 9
Blood in a World of Violence—Reclaiming a Sacred Symbol

We live in a world where the sight of blood rarely brings comfort. In fact, it’s usually the opposite. News broadcasts spill it across our screens in stories of war and terrorism. Video games glorify it. Movies drench it across battlefields, back alleys, and broken bodies. Blood, in modern consciousness, is almost exclusively the currency of violence. It signals evil, danger, trauma, war. It is something to be wiped away, disinfected, hidden behind sterile gloves and hospital curtains.

But the Bible tells a different story.

In the Law given to Israel, blood isn’t seen as horror but as life. It’s not a symbol of terror, but of purification. It’s not a mark of ruin, but of redemption. In ancient Israel, blood was not treated as a grotesque byproduct of suffering, but as the sacred element in the architecture of spiritual life. Blood was not a mark of violence but a medium of healing. It sanctified, purified, cleansed, and restored. More than anything, blood meant life.

That may sound foreign to modern ears, trained by centuries of secular thought to see religion through a forensic, even cynical, lens. But to step into the world of the Bible is to walk into a place where every drop of blood pulses with divine meaning. Leviticus 17:11 declares with bold, ancient clarity:

For the life of a creature is in the blood, and I have given it to you to make atonement for yourselves on the altar; it is the blood that makes atonement for one’s life.

(Leviticus 17:11)

Here, blood is not a symbol of judgment—it is a vessel of grace. It is not the sign of God’s anger, but the conduit of His mercy.

Blood was not understood to be the object of wrath, but a divine, "magical" potion that sterilizes the space so that a holy God can dwell among fragile people living in the shadows of sin and mortality.

We must learn to see blood as the ancient Hebrews saw it: not with fear or disgust, but with awe. It was never meant to stir up images of gore and torture. It was always meant to stir the soul toward wonder.

Reclaiming the Meaning of Blood

Blood, in biblical thought, is the tangible expression of life’s most sacred energy—the divine breath made visible. It is life distilled into red. It is the story of a God who meets us in our mortality and offers a path not just through death, but beyond it.

To reclaim the meaning of blood is to recover a truth our modern world has forgotten: life is sacred, and life comes from God. And wherever God gives life, He also provides a way for that life to be restored, even after it has been fractured by sin, shame, or death.

In a pagan world obsessed with death, the Bible’s use of blood was—and still is—a quiet rebellion. It does not glorify bloodshed; it glorifies the life within the blood. The life God breathed into Adam. The life poured out for Israel at the altar. The life offered to the world through the Messiah.

Before we can understand the cross, before we can grasp atonement, we must first purify our vision. We must allow Scripture to reframe our symbols. Only then can we truly grasp what the Gospel—and the reason Christ died—is all about.

"For the Life of the Flesh is in the Blood"—Leviticus 17:11 and the Heart of Atonement

The beating heart of the Hebrew Scriptures is not found in regulations or laws, but in a single, sacred truth whispered into the fabric of Israel’s worship: life belongs to God—and that life, that pulse, that holy vitality, is found in the blood.

Leviticus 17:11 is not merely a law; it is a theological key. A portal. A holy lens through which the entire system of sacrifice, and later the meaning of Christ’s death, can be rightly seen: "it is the blood that makes atonement for one’s life." (Leviticus 17:11b).

Notice the careful wording: it is not death that makes atonement. It is the blood. And what is in the blood? Life.

In other words, it is a somewhat mystic symbol of how life is transfused to redeem that of others.

Leviticus 17 does not speak of vengeance. It speaks of a gift. “I have given it to you,” God says—language not of retribution but of provision. God does not demand blood in anger. He provides it in compassion.

In the ancient world, many nations believed that their gods needed appeasement—pain, fear, death, even torture were sometimes required to bend the divine will.47 But Israel’s God was different. He was not a tyrant demanding satisfaction. He was a Father longing for restoration. His provision of blood was not to satisfy His own wrath but to heal the rupture between life and death, heaven and earth.

This is why blood is the agent of atonement. The Hebrew word for “atonement,” kippur, carries not the sense of wrath but of covering, cleansing, restoration, and reconciliation.

If sin equals death, how can you take it away? How can you hide it and wash it clean? What reconciles us to God is not the suffering or death of an animal, but its life—its blood, its vitality, its sacred energy poured out for the sake of another. It's the spiritual logic behind Jesus' famous statement: "Greater love has no one than this: to lay down one’s life for one’s friends." (John 15:13).

In this way, the altar becomes the meeting place between two worlds: death and life. Sin, which leads to death, is answered not by more death, but by a deeper life—life willing to be poured out in love. Atonement, then, is not about punishment or torture, as it’s often portrayed in Western theology; it’s about reunion.48 It's about a space being cleansed or a torn relationship being stitched back together, not through violent punishment, but through the offering of life itself.

To the modern mind, this may sound mystical, even irrational. But in the ancient Hebrew imagination, it made perfect sense. Just as blood flows to every part of the body, bringing oxygen and vitality, so too did the sacrificial blood symbolically carry life into the center of Israel’s spiritual body—into the very heart of the Tabernacle, into the Holy of Holies, into the soul of the nation. A divine transfusion.

Conclusion

Before we speak of crosses and thorns, we must sit before the altar in Leviticus and hear God say it clearly: “I have given you the blood…because the life is in the blood.”

Leviticus 17:11 is the axis upon which the whole system of sacrifice turns. Without it, the rituals become strange and archaic. With it, they pulse with divine logic. The blood is not brutality. It is the most tangible form of life the ancients had. And God, in His mercy, allowed that sacred symbol of life to stand in the gap between heaven and humanity.

From this foundation, everything else begins to make sense—not only the sacrifices of bulls and goats, but ultimately the offering of Jesus, the Messiah, whose blood—whose life—was poured out not to appease wrath, but to defeat death by the transfusion of life eternal.

cU9

Chapter 10
Understanding Biblical Offerings

One of the most harmful theological misunderstandings is thinking that offerings under the Mosaic Law were meant to appease divine wrath. In reality, there were many kinds of offerings—not one—and the vast majority had nothing to do with sin at all. Even the two that did—Asham (guilt) and Hata’at (purification or sin)—had nothing to do with wrath.

Most offerings were expressions of worship, gratitude, covenant loyalty, fellowship, and devotion. Only a small subset dealt specifically with sin or purification.

The offerings in the sacrificial system described in Leviticus include:

Burnt Offerings (Leviticus 1) – Voluntary acts of worship, expressing complete devotion and surrender.

Grain Offerings (Leviticus 2) – Non-bloody offerings of thanksgiving and recognition of God's provision.

Peace Offerings (Leviticus 3) – Shared meals between God, priest, and worshiper; symbols of restored fellowship.

Purification Offerings (Leviticus 4–5) – Addressing ritual impurity or inadvertent sins.

Reparation Offerings (Leviticus 5:14–6:7) – Acts of compensation when restitution was needed.

Of these, only two sub-categories (Asham and Hata’at) relate to moral or ritual fault. The rest are about communion, celebration, and gratitude. Sacrifice was more of a banquet than a bloodletting. And this tells us something crucial: in the Bible, the primary purpose of the offerings was relational, not retributive.

Even before the Law was given, there are early examples of such offerings, including the story of Cain and Abel. Abel’s gift appears to be a burnt or peace offering of the highest quality (Genesis 4:4), while Cain’s gift resembles a grain offering but lacks the Levitical emphasis on quality, which led to its rejection.

Those who reduce the entire system to sin-punishment mechanics are not only making a grave theological error—they are also dehumanizing Israel’s worship.

The sacrificial system was never meant to substitute righteousness or justice. It was meant to accompany it. It was a tool for sacred order, not a loophole in moral obligation. The God who desires mercy, not sacrifice (Hosea 6:6), is the same God who instituted both.

Jesus Himself echoes this when He quotes Hosea and says to the Pharisees: “Go and learn what this means: ‘I desire mercy, not sacrifice’” (Matthew 9:13). He is not abolishing the entire sacrificial vision—He is re-centering it. Mercy is the goal, not wrath. Life, not death, is the center. God desires a people shaped not by fear and shame but by grace and compassion.

Communal Atonement

When New Testament authors speak of Jesus in sacrificial terms, they do so against this background. Jesus is not offered as a penal substitute absorbing wrath. No. It's about His blood being poured out. He is the final purifier, the healer of the sanctuary, the One whose blood—not as punishment, but as holy life—cleanses the world and welcomes God’s dwelling.

Hebrews speaks of Jesus entering the true heavenly sanctuary, not to suffer punishment, but to present His own blood as the final cleansing that restores communion between God and humanity (Hebrews 9:12–14). John wrote, “The blood of Jesus… purifies us from all sin” (1 John 1:7)—not that it satisfies wrath, but that it cleanses and purifies.

And Jesus, as the Lamb of God, does not abolish this system—He fulfills it by becoming the one who cleanses not just a temple, but universally atones for the whole of creation.

The Essence of Israel’s Sacrificial System: Life, Cleansing, and Covenant Renewal

For ancient Israel, sacrifice was not merely a ritual—it was a language. It was the grammar of reconciliation, the poetry of devotion, and the architecture of communion.

The sacrificial system, as outlined in the Torah and especially the book of Leviticus, was multifaceted, symbolic, and liturgically rich. It was not a monolithic practice aimed solely at “dealing with sin,” nor was it ever intended to function as a violent appeasement of divine wrath. Instead, the sacrifices formed the sacred rhythm by which Israel realigned herself with God’s presence and purposes. They were acts of gratitude, purification, covenant renewal, communal wholeness, and spiritual restoration.

Every sacrifice invited the worshiper to draw near—to approach God—with humility and reverence. Whether through burnt offerings, peace offerings, grain offerings, or purification rites, Israel’s liturgical life pulsed with a single desire: to make space for the holy in the midst of human fragility.

The rich variety of sacrifices described in the Torah (the Pentateuch) reveals a far more beautiful and relational pattern—a way for humanity to draw near to God in gratitude, worship, restoration, and communion. Each category of sacrifice served a different purpose, and remarkably, each finds its ultimate fulfillment in the life, death, and ongoing ministry of Jesus.

Survey of Israel's Offerings

The Peace Offering (Zevach Shelamim) was about fellowship. It was a shared meal between God, the priest, and the worshiper—a symbol of restored relationship and joyful communion. In Jesus, this offering comes to life most vividly at the Last Supper and through the ongoing practice of the Lord’s Table, where believers are supposed to symbolically eat a meal and drink wine in fellowship with Christ, a feast (rather than a tiny piece of dry wafer and grape juice) joyfully celebrating reconciliation and peace through Him (as opposed to the often dull, quiet, and somber atmosphere commonly experienced when this practice is observed in most churches today).

The Gift Offering (Minchah)—typically grain, oil, or incense—was an expression of gratitude, devotion, and generosity. It acknowledged God as the provider of life’s blessings. In Christ, we see this heart of gratitude embodied. His life was a constant offering of thanksgiving to the Father (John 17:4), and through Him, believers are called to offer not just gifts of grain but the very gift of their lives as a "living sacrifice" (Romans 12:1) pleasing to God. Living sacrifice isn’t about itself, but about consistently choosing to love others.

The Burnt Offering (Olah) symbolized total surrender. The entire animal was consumed on the altar, representing complete dedication to God. Jesus fulfills this in the most profound way—giving not just part of Himself, but His whole life, holding nothing back, fully surrendered to the will of the Father even unto death on a cross (Philippians 2:8). In Him, we see the perfect Olah—a life wholly offered in service of others and for God’s glory.

The Purification or Sin Offering (Hata’at) was not primarily about punishment but about cleansing impurity—removing from the altar anything that would defile God's dwelling among His people. Jesus fulfills this as the one whose blood purifies us—the new temple—from all sin (1 John 1:7), not because God needed violence, but because humanity needed His blood to cleanse and purify, enabling us to dwell again in God’s presence.

Finally, the Guilt Offering (Asham) dealt with reparations—restoring what was lost or broken, often accompanied by restitution to the offended party. Isaiah calls Messiah asham (Isaiah 53:10). In Christ, this finds fulfillment as He not only forgives sin but restores what sin has damaged—reconciling humanity to God and healing the wounds of a fractured creation. Christ's eternal blood, unknowingly offered by humanity and accepted by God, symbolizes the Asham.

Each offering in the Old Testament was a shadow—a symbol pointing forward. In Jesus, they are not discarded but fulfilled, elevated, and perfected. The cross is not only about covering sin—it is about peace, thanksgiving, surrender, cleansing, and restoration. In Christ, every category of sacrifice finds its true and final meaning—not in violence for violence’s sake, but in life literally poured out to bring humanity home.

Now, let's examine the last two kinds of offerings in greater detail.

Purification or Sin Offering (hata'at)

Depending on the English Bible translation, the Hebrew word Hata’at may be rendered as “Sin Offering” in some cases and “Purification Offering” in others. This can be misleading. Linguistically, Hata’at may derive from het (sin) or hite (to cleanse, purify). Many Christian commentators and Jewish scholars often prefer the translation “Purification Offering” because the primary focus of the Hata’at ritual was about purifying space—the sanctuary, the altar, and the people.

Even when a personal sin was involved, the focus of the Hata’at offering was not on the worshiper's guilt but on the impurity caused by sin’s consequences. That impurity was seen as contaminating the sacred dwelling of God among His people. In other words, sin left behind a residue—a spiritual defilement that needed to be cleansed, not punished.

What purified that defilement? Not the death of the animal. Not the pain or the loss. It was the blood—life’s sacred symbol—that was applied like holy antiseptic to the altar, veil, and sanctuary furniture (Leviticus 4:6; 4:7; 4:17–18; 4:25; 4:30; 4:34; 8:15; etc.). The blood made the space clean again. Only then could the divine presence continue to dwell in the midst of Israel.

This point becomes beautifully clear in Leviticus 14, during the ritual for cleansing a metsora (a person with a skin disease). Once healed, the individual was to be reintroduced into the camp only after a ritual that included the Hata’at. Here, moral sin is not the issue at all—there is no moral guilt. And yet, a sin offering is made. Why? Because the offering is not about moral fault—it is about removing symbolic death and restoring one to communal life and divine proximity.

Thus, the Hata’at does not speak of condemnation, but reunion. It is not an act of divine vengeance, but divine hospitality. Blood is not spilled to satisfy anger—it is life offered to cleanse what sin had darkened.

In the Bible, sin against God was seen as a relational issue, not a transactional one, unlike how it’s often viewed in Western thought. When Scripture speaks of sin against God, such as in Genesis 39:9, Psalm 51:4, and 1 John 1:9, it points not to a violation of a legal system, but to a wound in covenantal intimacy. When David confesses, "Against You, You only, have I sinned" (Psalm 51:4), he is not denying the harm he did to Bathsheba and Uriah. Rather, he is overwhelmed by the personal rupture between himself and God. Since the Hebrew and Greek words for sin (chata and hamartia) both literally mean "missing the mark," this indicates distortion of trust within a covenant relationship, not a legal violation of an abstract law. Sin fractures shalom—peace and wholeness—damaging our relationship with God, ourselves, others, and creation.

Paul ascribes the Hata’at to Jesus:

He made the One who knew no sin to become a sin offering [hamartia—same word used in LXX for Hata’at] on our behalf...

(2 Corinthians 5:21)

The blood of the Hata’at washes away sin:

They have washed their robes and made them white in the blood of the Lamb.

(Revelation 7:14)

The blood of Jesus, like the blood of the Hata’at, cleanses and purifies. It does not appease divine wrath, but it cleans stains like a detergent. It restores the soul and cleanses the sanctuary of the human heart. It does not absorb punishment—it removes the distance.

Guilt Offering (asham)

The second sacrifice associated with sin is the Asham, often translated “Guilt Offering.” But this offering, too, is often misunderstood when viewed through a modern Western lens. It was not an act of retribution or substitution—it was a ritual of restitution.

The Asham was required when someone had wronged another person or unintentionally violated something sacred. It addressed not just the spiritual aspect of sin, but the social and economic implications. If a person cheated another, or mishandled something dedicated to God, the Asham offering was required—but so was repayment and often an additional twenty percent (Leviticus 5:14–6:7).

The emphasis was not on pain or death—it was on restoration.

Sin here was not a cosmic crime demanding bloodshed, but a breach of trust, a fracture in covenant fidelity. And the appropriate response was not suffering, but reconciliation. The Asham restored what had been taken. It mended what had been torn. This reveals something profound: atonement in Israel was relational, not transactional.

Isaiah 53 is a known prophecy about the Messiah dying for our sins (also quoted in the New Testament pointing to Jesus). Verse 10 uses Asham to describe Messiah's atonement: “...his soul makes an offering for guilt (Asham)...”.

Just as the guilty party in Israel brought forward their offering, the New Testament presents Jesus as the world's asham, the One through whom humanity is reconciled with God—not by absorbing divine fury, but by embodying divine mercy. As Paul writes:

God was in Christ reconciling the world to Himself, not counting their sins against them.

(2 Corinthians 5:19)

This is the Asham logic fulfilled—not punishment, but peace-making. Not abuse, but restitution. Not Christ reconciling God's anger, but Christ reconciling a chaotic world to God.

Notice that none of Israel’s sacrifices were taken by God’s hand—they were always offered by us. In the same way, it was us—sinners—who ended Jesus’ life, and God who raised Him. This is exactly what the New Testament writers affirmed (Acts 2:23-24, 3:15. 4:10, 5:30).

Jesus: The One Who Cleanses, Restores, and Reunites

Just as ancient Israel offered animals in sacred rites of purification and restoration, the New Testament presents Jesus not as a condemned substitute absorbing wrath in our place, but as the one who gives His life to cleanse the world, reconcile the broken, and restore the human temple.

He is the Lamb, but not the lamb upon whom God’s rage is poured out. He is the Lamb whose life is poured out, so that death might be undone. He is the priest and the offering—not to placate the Father, but to bring humanity back into the presence of the Father.

His blood purifies/cleanses/sanitizes the altar of sin. His offering mends what was fractured.

This is why the New Testament writers speak of His blood, not His torment or punishment, as the agent of atonement. “We have redemption through His blood” (Ephesians 1:7). “We are justified by His blood” (Romans 5:9). “The blood of Jesus… purifies us from all sin” (1 John 1:7).

It’s not His death that saves us, but His blood. The focus isn’t on appeasement through death and violence—as in Anselm’s and Calvin’s atonement models—but rather on His blood cleansing, restoring, and giving us life.

Conclusion: Blood—Not Death—Atones

When we step back and behold the offerings through Hebrew eyes, the picture becomes clear: the offerings were never about abuse, wrath, or violence. They were about restoring what had been lost—the presence of God in the midst of His people.

The whole system aimed not at satisfying divine anger but at preserving divine intimacy.

In the same way, Jesus’ death wasn’t some act of cosmic child abuse. It was the moment when human wickedness was laid bare, where the world’s cruel, evil, and vengeful justice met God’s forgiving and restorative justice, making God’s presence available to everyone, and where blood—life itself—was poured out to cleanse the world from its downward spiral toward death.

Blood atones, not because God demands death, but because blood is the vehicle of life. Death was the costly and tragic means through which the blood could be provided. But as Christ demonstrated, even death is only temporary.

And so, the cross does not stand as a monument to divine rage, but as a testament to divine love—a love that purifies, heals, reconciles, and restores the world.

cWS

Chapter 11
Understanding Biblical Sacrifice

Like the biblical offerings, sacrifice is also one of the most misunderstood concepts in both religious and secular discourse. To many, it conjures up images of grim altars, primitive violence, virgins thrown into the fire, and pagan gods who thirst for blood. But in the Hebrew Scriptures, sacrifice is not about death—it is about offering life. It is the mysterious place where life and death meet—not as enemies, but as participants in a deeper, sacred drama.

When a worshiper in ancient Israel brought an animal to the altar, they were not simply killing it. They were offering something precious: a life. A creature—often an animal loved and known by name—that breathed, moved, and had blood flowing through it. The sacrificial act acknowledged a painful truth—we live in a world where death is inescapable, but through life, death can be overcome.

To sacrifice to someone simply means to give them a gift—to offer something freely, not to appease their anger or satisfy a violent demand.

To give someone blood was—and still is—to gift someone with life. And since life belongs to God, every offering was a return. “All souls are Mine,” says the Lord (Ezekiel 18:4). Sacrifice was not a transaction but a restoration.

The act of shedding blood was not divine punishment as to appease God—it was a precious gift. It was not retribution, but representation. Not wrath, but reunion.

The Divine Logic of Sacrifice—Where Life Met Death

There is a profound logic here—one that reaches beyond religion and into the rhythms of creation itself. Nothing lives without something else dying. Whether we eat fish, beef, grain, or harvest fruit, we are living because something else has died. Animals eat one another not out of cruelty, but to survive. Even plants—life pulled from the soil—are consumed in order to sustain us and the animals. There is no life without death.

This truth is hard-wired into the created order: life begets life, and life requires life.

The sacrificial system made this visible, tangible, and sacred. It invited the Israelites to face the value of life and the consequence of sin without being crushed by guilt or fear. Instead, they were drawn into the mystery of grace. The blood of the animal symbolically cleansed sin, while the animal's death symbolically carried the sinner’s guilt into the realm of death. The animal had given the worshiper the gift of life.

This is why sacrifices were offered on an altar—the place where heaven and earth touch. It was a threshold, a seam in the fabric of existence, where mortality and eternity embraced. And on that altar, blood was poured out not as evidence of divine cruelty, but as a holy gift. A reminder that even in the presence of death, life still has the last word.

Jesus in Light of Leviticus

Jesus’ crucifixion must be re-read through this lens. He did not simply die—He offered His life. His blood, like the blood of the ancient sacrifices, became the meeting point between human sin and divine mercy. The cross was not merely a place of execution; it was an altar. And on that altar, the life that flowed from Jesus’ veins was not a symbol of defeat, but of the victory of eternal life.

As we now turn to explore how science echoes this biblical truth, we carry this image with us: sacrifice is not violent death demanded by God. It is the gift of life offered. The place where mortality is swallowed up by something far more powerful: life that gives itself freely.

Scientific and Spiritual Echoes—Blood as the Giver of Life

Ancient Israel did not have microscopes or biology textbooks. Yet in their liturgies, their sacrifices, and their Scriptures, they intuitively grasped a truth that modern science now confirms: life is in the blood.

Today, we understand in precise medical terms what Leviticus 17:11 proclaimed millennia ago. Blood circulates oxygen. It delivers nutrients. It removes waste. It fights germs. It transports hormones. It guards against infection. It keeps our body temperature in balance. It is the internal river that keeps us alive every moment of every day. You can survive without certain organs, but not without blood. Strip away all the poetry and ritual, and the science remains: the life of the flesh is in the blood.

This is why we donate blood—not as a religious ritual to appease the wrath of an angry deity, but as an act of gifting that saves lives, because life is literally in the blood. When someone suffers trauma or undergoes surgery, their survival often depends on the blood of another. And we call it what it is: a transfusion, a gift, a life-saving donation. And there’s another beautiful symbolism here—on the other side of the transfusion, they don’t earn or buy the gift of life; it’s simply given to them.

When we donate blood, we are not merely engaging in a modern medical act; we are unwitting participants in an ancient truth: blood is not merely a symbol of life. It is life itself.

When Jesus stood before a Jewish audience and said, “Whoever drinks my blood has eternal life” (John 6:54), it scandalized them—not because they misunderstood the symbolism, but because they understood it too well. Drinking blood, especially human blood, was forbidden by Torah (Leviticus 17:10). But Jesus was pointing not to the breaking of the Law, but to the fulfilling of a mystery. He was saying: “My blood is eternal, immortal life.”

In this strange and holy claim, Jesus declared Himself the fulfillment of everything Leviticus could only hint at. His blood wasn’t just symbolic—it was substantial. It wasn’t just ritual—it was reality. He wasn’t offering a sacrifice like the ones made at the Temple. He was the Temple, and the Priest, and the Sacrifice, all in one.

And He did not give a drop—He gave all. Poured out, not into sterile tubes, but onto wood and thorns and dirt. His eternal life coursed out of Him so that immortal life could be coursed into us.

This is not mythology. This is spiritual biology. Divine science. A sacred logic that pulses through the veins of both Testaments and into our very bodies: life must be given in order for life to be received.

In a world now filled with clinical language and antiseptic spirituality, we must not forget the raw, red truth at the center of the gospel. Jesus bled not to satisfy wrath, but to transmit life. His blood is not a symbol of divine rage, but the stream of divine life for us to drink from.

Purification, Sanctification, and the Ritual Power of Blood

To modern ears, the words “purify” and “sanctify” can sound abstract, even sterile. But in the Hebrew Scriptures, these were not cold theological terms—they described sacred processes of transformation, meant to bring a person, place, or object into proper standing before the living God.

In Hebrew, to purify (ṭaher) means to cleanse something (e.g., a vessel) that has been contaminated—by death, decay, sin, or disorder. To sanctify (qadash) means to set something apart (e.g., a holy day), to make it holy, to dedicate it for divine use. And at the heart of both these acts—was blood.

But why blood?

Because blood was life. And only life could cleanse what death had touched.

This is the fundamental logic behind the entire Levitical system. The Israelites lived in a world saturated with reminders of mortality: disease, infertility, ritual impurity, physical deformities, corpses. These were not moral failings—they were signs that death had entered the camp.

And death, in biblical thought, was not merely a biological event. It was a spiritual force, a pollutant, a rupture in the divine order—the curse of Eden. Anything touched by death was considered unclean—not because it was morally evil, but because death carried the mark of what is the opposite of God, who is life—pure, unfiltered Life.

The Sacrifice Contained the Potion of Life

So how does one undo death’s defilement? Not through punishment. Not through abuse. Not through shame. But through overcoming death with life—atonement.

That’s why God gave Israel blood—not to frighten them, but to clean them. The blood of the sacrifice—its life—acted as a spiritual solvent, a divine bleach. Blood touched what was unclean and made it pure. Not because of a chemical effect, but because of what it stood for: a life offered to reverse the power of death by purifying it.

We see this principle again and again—purifying and cleansing with blood:

Moses took the blood… and purified the altar. 

(Leviticus 8:15)

You shall purify the altar… and anoint it to consecrate it.

(Exodus 29:36)

He shall sprinkle some of the blood… to cleanse it and consecrate it from the uncleanness of the Israelites. 

(Leviticus 16:18–19)

The blood did not make an angry God less angry. It made the space holy. It didn’t remove moral guilt—it removed death’s residue. It washed the soul. It re-opened the cosmic door to divine fellowship.

Likewise, Jesus' blood does not simply cover our sins like a rug hiding the dirt. It cleanses the stain. It sanctifies us, not just in theory, but in reality—transforming hearts, restoring consciences, and making even the most broken soul a sanctuary for the Spirit of God.

This is why the New Testament echoes the language of purification and sanctification by blood so often:

The blood of Jesus, His Son, purifies us from all sin. 

(1 John 1:7)

…to make the people holy through His own blood. 

(Hebrews 13:12)

…by His blood, He cleanses our consciences from dead works to serve the living God. 

(Hebrews 9:14)

Conclusion

In ancient Israel, blood was poured on altars, sprinkled on priests' garments, and dashed upon the mercy seat. In Jesus, the blood is poured upon the human heart—not to mark us for death, but to mark us for life.

Blood was not regarded as disgusting or grotesque. It was understood as spiritual antiseptic—divine bleach—cleansing, consecrating, and restoring what had been defiled.

And so, we begin to see: blood was not the language of divine rage, but of divine welcome. It was God’s way of saying, "Come closer. Be cleansed. Become pure. Become holy. Be mine."

cYW

Chapter 12
Understanding Biblical Atonement

Some Western models of atonement—such as Satisfaction theory (Anselm, 10th century) and Penal Substitution (Calvin, 16th century)—import foreign languages and categories of punishment, divine wrath, and legal transaction. By contrast, the Hebrew Scriptures portray atonement as something far more relational and restorative, woven deeply into the fabric of communal life in ways many have forgotten or never been taught.

Three Hebrew Terms that Shape Biblical Atonement

In Hebrew, three key words shape the Bible's vision of atonement:

GA'AL—to redeem, often in the context of family obligation, the kinsman-redeemer who steps in to rescue a relative from slavery, debt, or danger.

PADAH — to liberate or ransom, emphasizing freedom from oppression or captivity.

KAPHAR — to atone, to cover, to cleanse, to purify, or to remove.

While English translations often flatten these words into the same term—"redeem" or "atonement"—each Hebrew word carries a distinct flavor and context. The Hebrew imagination was rich with layered meanings, and these words offer us windows into God's redeeming heart.

GA'AL: The Family Rescuer

The Hebrew word ga'al evokes the image of the go'el—the kinsman-redeemer, the family protector. For instance, God "redeemed" Israel from slavery in Egypt by demonstrating His power and love (Exodus 6:6). The redeemer figure had the sacred duty to rescue a relative from slavery (Leviticus 25:47-49), to restore lost land (Leviticus 25:25), or even to execute a murderer (Numbers 35:19). Redemption here is deeply relational. It is not abstract or legal; it is about protection, loyalty, responsibility, and family love. It is about saying, "You belong to me, and I will care for you and protect you."

PADAH: The Liberator

Padah speaks of liberation from oppression. This word reminds us that redemption is about freedom, rescue, and deliverance. It is about breaking chains. In Deuteronomy 7:8, for instance, God “redeemed (padah) you [Israel] from the house of slavery, from the hand of Pharaoh.”

KAPHAR: The Purifier

Most relevant to our conversation about atonement is the verb kaphar, which appears over 100 times in the Hebrew Scriptures. It is also the root of Yom Kippur—the Day of Atonement. Linguistically, Hebrew scholars offer two main interpretations of the word kaphar:

To remove, wipe away, or purge.

To cover or hide.

Conceptionally, the Hebrew Bible speaks of sin using two complementary images: removal and covering.

In Zechariah 3:4, God declares, “See, I have taken away your sin,” portraying forgiveness as the lifting and removal of guilt. In Psalms 32:1, we read, “Blessed is the one whose transgressions are forgiven, whose sins are covered,” presenting forgiveness as concealment from judgment.

Together, these images reveal a rich biblical understanding of atonement: sin is not merely ignored, but dealt with—removed, covered, and no longer counted against the forgiven.

Rather than choosing one over the other, the biblical story holds both together. In Israel's sacrificial system, sins were covered—not ignored, but dealt with in a way that allowed God to dwell among His people despite their impurity. In Christ, they were removed altogether.

The Father Who Covers Sin

The Parable of the Prodigal Son provides a lovely illustration of the KAPHAR atonement.

Quick! Bring the best robe and put it on him...

(Luke 15:22)

Picture the Prodigal Son, utterly lost in his sins and just days from starving. He comes back filthy, smelling of pigs—a striking symbol of defilement to the Jewish audience of Jesus. Now imagine his dad wrapping him up—covering him—as they walk onto the family estate. It’s a technical comparison, but for the Jewish audience, it carried a much deeper meaning. In that simple act, the father not only covered his son's sins, but he also removed his shame. Below is an excerpt from my book, God as Father: Unveiling God's Love for Sinners, Outcasts, Legalists and Jerks Through the Prodigal Son:

The "best robe" belongs, of course, not to one of the servants but to the father and is saved for special events and celebrations. This wasn't just any robe; the word used (στολὴν) means a long robe. The upper class wore that kind of robe. It was a symbol of honor and status. By requesting this robe for his son, who had just returned after squandering his father's wealth, the father wasn't just covering his physical nakedness; he was reinstating his honor, his place in the family, and erasing the shame that came with his prior actions. This gesture, especially in front of the household servants, is a public proclamation that the son's past is forgiven, and his original status in the family remains unchanged. The father didn't only show compassion by forgiving his past mistakes. He extended grace by reminding his son—and everyone else around—that he is still his beloved child.

The father achieves multiple symbolic purposes by putting the best robe on his returning son. First, he restores his son's position in the family, signifying that the son is not a mere servant but holds all the rights and privileges of being a family member. The robe also serves as a clear sign of acceptance, acting as a physical manifestation of the father's unconditional love and forgiveness for his wayward son. This gesture additionally reaffirms the son's identity as a beloved family member, tapping into biblical symbolism where clothing often relates to one's identity (e.g., Genesis 3:21, Isaiah 61:10).

The robe sets the tone for the following celebratory feast, emphasizing the immense joy surrounding the son's return. Furthermore, the robe stands in sharp contrast to what the son was likely wearing—probably filthy rags—which amplifies the transformative power of his father's acceptance and love. 

In the parable, the father’s robe is not merely clothing—it was kaphar made visible. He covered the boy’s filth before the son could wash himself, forgive himself, or even finish his confession. This is the heart of biblical atonement: God does not wait for us to fix our condition before He restores us; He meets us in our ruin and covers us with His own honor. 

Just as ga’al rescues the family member and padah liberates the captive, kaphar wraps the sinner in a restoring embrace. The Prodigal Son is not a sentimental story—it is a living portrait of the atonement Jesus proclaimed: defilement covered, sin forgiven, shame removed, and identity restored.

Jesus Redeems, Covers and Removes Sin

In Jesus, the Lamb of God, we see the ultimate fulfillment: not only redeeming us from the devil (Hebrews 2:14–15, Colossians 1:13–14, Galatians 1:4, - 1 John 3:8) or covering sin (Romans 4:7, 1 John 2:2) but removing it entirely (Hebrews 9:26). As John the Baptist declares, "Behold, the Lamb of God who takes away the sin of the world!" (John 1:29).

Notice that John declares that the Lamb of God "takes away the sin of the world”—not “the Lamb who removes the sins of the few who manage to achieve the right religious denomination, doctrinal formula, or theological precision.” If he had said that, the burden of redeeming, atoning, and removing sin would fall on us—on our mental ability to understand and believe the right creeds or behave a certain way.

Sadly, much of the Western gospel does just that. Perhaps unintentionally, it shifts the power of salvation away from God and onto human effort—our intellect, our theological precision, our morality, our choices. But the true gospel doesn’t attribute 99% of salvation to God and 1% to us. It gives Him all of it.

Conclusion

This is the God Jesus reveals—not a cosmic executioner, but the Kinsman-Redeemer who comes to rescue His family. Not a wrathful monarch demanding a kill, but the Liberator who sets captives free. Not a distant medieval judge requiring torture as punishment before forgiving, but the Purifier who removes sin so that nothing can separate us from His love.

In the Old Testament, sins were covered. In the New Testament, sins are removed. And in both, God's heart beats with love, mercy, and restoration.

c10J

Chapter 13
Atonement as the Purification of Death

In religious lingo, “unclean” often conjures images of ethical filth, shame, or moral corruption. But in the Torah—particularly in Leviticus—uncleanliness had little to do with moral guilt and everything to do with death’s nearness.

The Levitical system created a detailed map of life and death. On that map, anything touched by death—even potential death—was marked as unclean: ritually impure, ceremonially defiled. This didn’t mean morally sinful. It meant in opposition to life. The God of Israel was unwilling to have death in His presence.

The logic of ritual impurity was not arbitrary—it was theological and deeply existential. Touch a corpse? You are unclean. Suffer a skin disease that eats the body like decay? You are unclean. Experience a loss of blood/life—menstruation, ejaculation, or childbirth? You are unclean. Eat an animal that feasts on carrion? You are unclean. Come into contact with mildew, bodily fluids, or physical disfigurement? You are unclean.

Not because these things are morally evil—but because they are profound reminders of life’s adversary and death’s intrusion into the world God created.

Purity Was About Death, Not Ethics

Take tzara’at, often mistranslated as “leprosy.” It wasn’t Hansen’s disease. It was a skin condition—a visible sign of something dying while still alive. A living death. A symbol that life was unraveling. That person had to live outside the camp, not because they were bad, but because they carried an infectious sign of mortality too close to the sanctuary (Leviticus 13:46).

Consider also Leviticus 15, which deals with discharges of semen and menstrual blood. These are not morally sinful acts—they’re natural!
