But they represent the loss of life-potential, the flow of what might have been life. These, too, required purification. Not because sex or menstruation is wrong, but because they remind us that we bleed, and every drop lost is a shadow of death.

Childbirth, that miracle of creation, also brought impurity—not because it was sinful, but because it involved tearing, blood, and the thin veil between life and death. Even in birth, death was near.

The message of the Levitical system was not shame—it was truth: we live in a world where death contaminates everything. And God’s presence won’t dwell there unless death is addressed.

The impurity laws taught Israel to be painfully aware of death’s reach. They cultivated a sense of the sacredness of life. They trained the heart to recognize that approaching God is no casual thing. He is holy (unique, distinct)—and to come near Him requires not moral perfection, but purification. And the only substance strong enough to cleanse death’s touch was not soap but blood—life itself. Even when the High Priest entered God's presence after sprinkling himself with blood, he was not suddenly perfect—he was simply purified.

This is why the blood of Jesus is not about wrath appeasement. It is about death reversal.

We bruise. We break. We injure. We bleed. We spread disease. We kill.

God forgives. God heals. God purifies. God cures.

In Jesus, the forces that rendered us unclean are undone. He touches the leper—and instead of becoming unclean; He makes the leper clean. Christ is not defiled by death. He defeats it!

And so we see: the Levitical laws, far from being outdated or obscure, set the stage for the gospel. They diagnosed the world’s great wound—our estrangement from life—and hinted at the only possible cure: life poured out to cleanse the realm of death. Not shame. Not punishment. But restoration. And what blood can cleanse and purify the entire creation if not that of our Healer Himself?

The Altar Must Be Sterilized—Blood as Spiritual Antiseptic

Modern Christians often think of worship in terms of loudspeakers, colorful lights, music and singing (praise), but ancient worship was more focused on hygiene. The rituals of the Tabernacle were strangely familiar in one regard: they were obsessed with cleanliness—the removal of defilement so that the presence of the living God could remain among His people.

In our post-pandemic world, the idea of sterilizing spaces resonates more than ever. We use bleach, alcohol gel, and disinfectants. A surgeon will not step into an operating room until it is spotless. Not because the doctor is angry at the sick person—but because the presence of contamination would endanger the patient's life.

The Tabernacle was no different. It, too, was a kind of mystical hospital—but not for bodies. For souls. And in this holy hospital, blood was the antiseptic. The priest, like a spiritual physician, would carry life in liquid form and apply it to sacred objects that had been touched by the impurity of a fallen world.

We read in Exodus:

You shall purify the altar when you make atonement for it, and you shall anoint it to consecrate it.

(Exodus 29:36)

In Leviticus:

Moses slaughtered it and took the blood, and with his finger put it on the horns of the altar around it and purified the altar.

(Leviticus 8:15)

And again on the holiest day of the year:

He shall take some of the bull's blood and some of the goat's blood and put it on all the horns of the altar… sprinkle some of the blood with his finger seven times to cleanse it and consecrate it from the uncleanness of the Israelites.

(Leviticus 16:18–19)

Why was blood applied to the altar? Had the altar sinned? No. But the altar dwelled in the midst of a death-saturated world, and every object in the Tabernacle needed to be ritually purified so it could remain a fitting home for the God of life.

The altar was God’s operation table, where heaven and earth touched. For Him to draw near, death had to draw back. And that required more than moral effort or good intentions. It required a cleansing so deep, only life itself could accomplish it. The blood wasn’t cleaning dirt—it was cleaning death.

In this way, figuratively, the blood acted as bleach, as detergent, as hospital-grade disinfectant—only infinitely more sacred and eternally more powerful. It symbolized the removal of every trace of decay, corruption, or separation, not just from the people, but from the very space where God and man would meet.

This helps us reframe the gospel.

Jesus’ blood is not merely the price of our salvation—it is the purification of the Temple. But now the temple is not made of animal hides and stones. It is us. Our bodies. Our hearts. Our consciences. Our inner sanctuaries. “For we are the temple of the living God” (2 Corinthians 6:16). And we, too, must be sterilized—cleansed from death’s residue so God’s presence can dwell there.

Hebrews 9:14 draws this line perfectly:

How much more will the blood of Christ, who through the eternal Spirit offered Himself without blemish to God, purify our consciences from dead works to serve the living God?

(Hebrews 9:14)

Dead works. Impure altars. Contaminated hearts. These are not criminal acts to be punished—they are death-sicknesses to be healed. And the cure is not torment, but transfusion.

Conclusion

The blood of Jesus is the divine antiseptic, applied not to furniture, but to human souls. It disinfects the stain of sin, not through punishment, but through presence—through life being poured into what was dying; divine resuscitation.

And the result? We become walking sanctuaries. Mobile temples. Sterilized spaces where the Spirit of God is pleased to dwell.

c126

Chapter 14
Not Violent Death, But Lively Blood—Correcting the Atonement Lens

The Western Church has long carried the cross—but too often, it has misread the altar.

In the Catholic-Protestant tradition, atonement has often been preached as divine punishment, as the Son absorbing the wrath of the Father, as the execution of justice through violent substitution. In this twisted perspective, God’s justice required a kill—a deadly act of revenge—as though God had slaughtered and killed Jesus to spare us.

Dr. Tim Mackie, the chief theologian and founder of the renowned Bible Project (YouTube videos) who graduated from the Hebrew University in Jerusalem, shared his view of the Western misunderstanding of atonement:

Many of us have inherited a story about animal sacrifice, and it goes something like this:

“The gods are angry with me and are going to kill me. But maybe if I kill this animal and make sure the gods get their pound of flesh, they’ll be appeased and happy. Maybe they won’t kill me or send a plague on my family. Sure, it’s barbaric, but so are the gods…”

Much of popular Christian belief has simply imported a pagan storyline into Leviticus and the stories about Jesus’ death on the cross. The result is a tragic irony. What the Bible is portraying as an expression of God’s love gets twisted into something dark. Our version goes like this:

“God is holy and perfect. You are not. Therefore, God is angry at you, and hates you even, so he has to kill you. But because he’s merciful, he’ll let you bring this animal to him and will have the animal killed instead of you. Thankfully, Jesus came to be the one who gets killed by God instead of me. Jesus rescues us from God, so now we can go forever to the happy place after we die and not the bad place.”

Is this story recognizable to you? If so, you’re not alone. The main problem with this story, to be a bit snarky, is the Bible. More specifically, the problem is that this story has enough biblical language in it that it can pass for what the Bible actually says about animal sacrifice and Jesus’ death. However, when you step back and allow Leviticus and the New Testament to speak for themselves, you can recognize this story as an imposter.49

(Tim Mackie)

I’d also add that the view Mackie is critiquing is probably a big reason why so many people deconstruct their faith and leave, particularly from fundamentalist denominations.

But as Mackie pointed out, the Hebrew Scriptures never claimed that violence or death brought about atonement. They taught something far more mysterious, deeper, and more beautiful—that true atonement comes from life, from self-giving.

Let us be precise: death was the cost of obtaining the blood. But it was not the agent of atonement. The creature’s blood—its life—was the point. Death was the tragic side-effect, the necessary means to access the life-giving blood that could cleanse, sanctify, and restore. Atonement wasn’t received because someone was killed, but because their blood—their life—was given.

The Blood of Christ Cleanseth Us from Sin

In Jewish thought, to speak of someone’s blood is to speak of their life force, their "living Nefesh." When the blood of a sacrifice was poured on the altar, it was not a violent spectacle of a bloodbath. It was a sacred tribute—life given to cover, cleanse, and reconnect what death had fractured.

To equate atonement with death is to flatten the gospel into an overly-simplistic pamphlet—where God is the judge and Jesus is the whipping boy sadistically punished to appease God so we can go free. But that is not the biblical picture of atonement at all. In Scripture, atonement is a relational mystery, not a legal contract. It’s not a transaction—it’s a transfusion.

God is not a wrathful tyrant needing satisfaction for His anger. He is a holy God who longs to dwell among His people—but His holiness means He is the opposite of death and impurity. Blood, in the biblical vision, was the only thing powerful enough to undo death’s contamination.

This is why Jesus’ sacrifice matters—not because it satisfied an outraged god demanding a kill, but because it allowed His life—His blood—to be poured out. It allowed the world to be cleansed once and for all. It allowed the Temple veil to be torn and annulled. It allowed humanity to be reborn.

To say, “Christ died for us” is true—but it is incomplete, misleading, and misses context. The deeper truth is this:

“Christ gave His blood—His life—for us.”

Thus, when the New Testament speaks of Jesus’ atonement, it never elevates His death as an isolated, appeasing punishment. Instead, it constantly highlights the power of His blood:

We have redemption through His blood, the forgiveness of sins…

(Ephesians 1:7)

…justified by His blood…

(Romans 5:9)

…the blood of Jesus, his Son, purifies us from all sin.

(1 John 1:7)

Blood is the centerpiece, not because it was caused by death, but because it represents life.

That is the gospel—not a God pacified by even more violence, but a God stepping into death itself with His own life to resurrect ours.

While some Western doctrines will have you glorify death, the Bible will have you worship the One who conquered it—not through wrath, but through life poured out in love.

The Hebrew Logic of Blood—Life, Not Punishment

In a mind shaped by centuries of Roman law and Reformation-era courtroom metaphors, the atonement is often presented like this:

You sinned against the big boss, and now he is angry. Someone has to die. Jesus steps in to absorb the blow for you. You’re off the hook.

But this story—common as it is—is not biblical. It’s not how Torah speaks. It’s not how Temple worship functioned. And it’s certainly not how the ancient Israelites or Second Temple Jews understood the sacrificial system and atonement.

The ancient Hebrew and Jewish view of sacrifice never centered on punishment and death. It centered on life and restoration.

To sacrifice was not to satisfy divine rage. It was to restore divine relationship. Blood was not a payment extracted by a Dracula-like god. It was life offered by a faithful people to keep the dwelling place of God clean, open, and welcoming.

The animal was not a martyr abused and punished in your place. It was a living being whose blood (life) was used to clear away defilement—to purify the space between heaven and earth, so God’s Spirit could continue to dwell among His people.

This is why the Leviticus verse “life is in the blood” is not theological poetry—it’s the foundation of the entire system. And it’s why Jesus, a Jewish Rabbi rooted in Torah, spoke in this same logic:

Unless you drink My blood, you have no life in you.

(John 6:53)

He was not inviting cannibalism or promoting draculaism. He was inviting union. He was saying: “My blood is eternal in power, and if you receive it, then you will have life everlasting."

This is the reversal of paganism.

Dracula consumes others to live. Jesus gave His blood so others may live. The gospel stands in direct contrast to the pagan concept of atonement—a system rooted in appeasing angry gods through violence and death. Tragically, some aspects of this distorted logic were later adopted into many Christians' views of atonement in ways foreign to the biblical and Jewish context.

Conclusion

Blood is not punishment. Blood is life.

This is why the apostles never preached the cross as a courtroom sentence, but as a sacrificial altar:

Christ loved us and gave Himself up for us, a fragrant offering and sacrifice to God.

(Ephesians 5:2)

“A fragrant offering” refers to the “aroma” created when we sacrifice ourselves for the sake of others. In the biblical imagination, what pleases God is not violence or punishment, but self-giving love. It is when we give of ourselves for the good of others that our lives become a pleasing offering to Him.

That’s Temple language. That’s Torah logic. That’s Hebraic theology.

So, when we say, “Jesus died for our sins,” we must clarify what that means. Not that an offended god demanded a kill to be appeased. But that in Jesus, life was offered to undo death. His blood was poured—not to settle a score—but to cleanse the world and draw us near.

We are not pardoned because someone else was beaten up, tortured and abused in our place. We are forgiven because the blood of Life has washed away the death that separated us from Him.

c147

Chapter 15
Biblical Repentance and the Day of Atonement

Every year in Israel, life comes to a complete halt for one day. Shops close. Roads empty. Even the noise of ordinary existence falls silent. For twenty-four hours, an entire nation stops—not as individuals in isolation, but together. Whether religious Jew or secular, citizen or outsider, everyone is caught up in the same collective pause. This is because Yom Kippur (Yom=day, Kippur=Atonement) was never meant to be a private spiritual exercise. It has always been a national day of repentance—a communal reckoning, shared in stillness, restraint, and return.

To truly understand Yom Kippur—Israel’s national day of repentance and atonement—we must first grasp the biblical concept of repentance. That requires shedding the modern Western lens of hyper-individualism, which distorts the communal, covenantal, and restorative vision that permeates Scripture.

The ancient world—especially the Hebrew worldview of Scripture—was covenantal, communal, and collective. God did not call Moses out of Egypt; He called Israel. He did not redeem one family; He brought out an entire nation.

Likewise, repentance (teshuvah) was never a private or inward-only affair. Ancient Israel had no confession booths, no isolated rituals where individuals whispered sins in order to escape hell. Repentance was about a national return—people turning back to their original vocation, reorienting themselves toward God so they might be healed, restored, and blessed. It was a communal movement back into covenantal faithfulness, not an individual attempt to flee an afterlife incinerator.

Biblical Repentance—Communal in Essence

In the Torah, sin defiled not just the sinner, but the whole camp. One person’s wrongdoing could impact the nation’s standing, while the representative repentance of one—the high priest—would ripple outward to bring blessing to the whole community. The priest did not enter the Holy of Holies just for himself or for a handful of pious saints, but for the nation as a whole. This is why the Day of Atonement—Yom Kippur—was not a private confession ritual, but a national day of repentance and purification; covering the sins of the entire nation of Israel.

In English, it can be difficult to know whether “you” refers to one person or several because the same word is used for both. In Hebrew, however, the verb for repentance—teshuvah—consistently uses the plural form (e.g., Ezekiel 18:30-31, Jeremiah 25:5, Joel 2:12-13, 2 Chronicles 7:14, Zechariah 1:3, Hosea 14:1-2). God wasn’t saying, “You, the individual, must change your private beliefs.” He was saying, “Return, O Israel” (the nation). Repentance was about God healing and preserving His people so His presence could remain among them.

The New Testament continues this collective emphasis, systematically using “repentance” in the plural form (e.g., Matthew 4:17; Luke 13:3, 5; Luke 17:3; Luke 15:7, 10), emphasizing a national call rather than merely individualistic. When John the Baptist declared, “Repent, for the kingdom of heaven has come near” (Matthew 3:2), he wasn’t addressing isolated individuals concerned about their afterlife destinies—he was speaking to the nation of Israel about her moral and spiritual direction.

Jesus, as the long-awaited Jewish Messiah, likewise called Israel to repentance—not to save people from metaphysical torture, but to restore the nation to God and help it avoid the devastating consequences of rejecting Him. He even warned Israel's leaders that rejecting Him is an unforgivable sin (Mark 3:29). That warning tragically came to pass with the destruction of Jerusalem in 70 CE.

The modern Western emphasis on individual salvation as a passport out of hell is alien to the biblical imagination. In Scripture, repentance was not about obsessing over internal guilt or earning forgiveness through sorrow. It was about reorientation—turning from destructive paths, false gods, and unjust systems, and returning to the God who desires mercy, not sacrifice. And just as with ancient Israel, the consequences of failing to repent were not framed as afterlife torment, but as national collapse, exile, and spiritual ruin (see Luke 19:41-44, Matthew 24:1-2).

Repentance, then, was less about individuals avoiding postmortem torment in hellfire and more about a national healing, restoration, and a return to the flourishing life God intended for His people together. To reduce it to a private transaction between “me and God” is not only theologically inaccurate—it flattens the depth of the biblical narrative which is communal in nature and focus.

Faith and repentance are not price tags for forgiveness, but awakenings to what already is. Faith doesn't unlock God's forgiveness—it opens our eyes to its presence. Repentance doesn't pacify God's wrath; it moves us from resistance and opposition to restoration and blessing.

Repentance is not us performing some act to make God change His mind about us; it is us changing our mind about God. It is the reorientation of our lives toward His will—not an attempt to manipulate Him into becoming something other than who He already is.

When Jesus prayed, "Father, forgive them, for they do not know what they do" (Luke 23:34), it was not because His executioners had suddenly offered repentance, confession, or faith. Yet Christ extended forgiveness anyway. This is not an exception but the very revelation of divine nature: forgiveness given before we even ask. Again and again throughout His ministry—healing the paralytic (Mark 2:5), rescuing the woman caught in adultery (John 8:11), redeeming Zacchaeus (Luke 19:9)—Jesus demonstrates that grace precedes faith and repentance. Transformation follows, but grace leads.

The cross, then, must not be seen as a cold legal exchange. It is the marriage bed of heaven and earth, the consummation of divine love. When Jesus whispered, "It is finished" (John 19:30), the perfect passive tense He used meant the action was completed, with ongoing effects forever. Faith does not complete what Christ began; faith is to participate in what Christ has done.

Thus, faith is not a transaction that triggers forgiveness; it is the joyous discovery of a love that was always there. Forgiveness is not unlocked by belief. It is believed because it is already true. We are called not to preach a conditional gospel—"if you repent, God might forgive you"—but to proclaim: "You were forgiven before you knew to ask. Now come home, live in the freedom of that truth, and let love transform your life."

With that in mind, let’s discuss Israel’s Day of Atonement.

Yom Kippur—Atonement as a Collective Reality

In the story of Israel, no day is more sacred, more intimately tied to sin, or more deeply woven into the national soul than Yom Kippur—Israel's national Day of Atonement. It is not only a ritual; it is theology enacted. It is not only remembrance; it is prophetic expectation.

This was the day when Israel came face-to-face with its deepest need: cleansing, restoration, and nearness to God. But one aspect often lost in Christian theology is the communal and collective nature of this atonement. Yom Kippur was not about the isolated individual securing forgiveness for their private sins; it was about the entire covenant community of Israel being covered, purified, and reconciled as one.

And this pattern continues powerfully in the New Testament. Just as Yom Kippur's atonement covered Israel as a nation regardless of each individual's private faith level, so too Jesus's atonement is proclaimed universally in scope and coverage, taking away the sin of the world (John 1:29).

A Day of Purification, Not Punishment

In Leviticus 16, God established a yearly ceremony in which Israel's sins would be covered, cleansed, and removed. But there is nothing in the ritual of Yom Kippur that resembles the violent logic of Penal Substitution Atonement. There is no wrath being poured out on animals. There is no divine punishment. There is no anger directed at the innocent.

Instead, there is cleansing. There is purification. There is restoration.

The day began with the high priest atoning for himself. Just like a surgeon needs to sanitize before stepping into the operating room, the high priest had to atone—cleansing himself of his own impurity—before entering the holy of holies to mediate for Israel (Leviticus 16:6-11). Only after his own purification could he act on behalf of Israel. This reinforces the idea that atonement was not about punishment but a cleansing ritual in preparation for presence.

The Two Goats: A Symbol of God's Mercy

The climax—the very pinnacle—of Yom Kippur was the ritual of the two goats (Leviticus 16:7–22). One was sacrificed, and its blood was used to purify and cleanse the sanctuary, mercy seat, veil, and other sacred items, almost like a liquid detergent or sanitizer. The other, the Azazel goat, became the scapegoat dealing with Israel's sins. However, the scapegoat was not killed for the sins of Israel but carried the sins of the people into the wilderness—out of sight and far away. Azazel is often described as a wild, chaotic desert spirit, and later traditions—especially the Book of Enoch—interpreted that figure as demonic and even Satan himself.50 It was as though, on a symbolic level, the Azazel spirit was being dismissed—its claim neutralized, its power cut off—in the act of sending the scapegoat into the wilderness.

Israel was not appeasing the wrath of God, but confronting the claim of Azazel. The ritual was never about calming divine anger; it was about breaking an accusatory power and sending it away. In this imagination, God is not the one who needs to be paid off. The demonic is.

This echoes the profound theological instinct behind C. S. Lewis’s The Chronicles of Narnia, where the Witch—clearly a satanic figure—demands appeasement through Aslan's life. Lewis’ genius shines in this: Aslan submits not to appease the Father’s wrath, but Satan’s, all while exposing and toppling the false logic of accusation and the power of death itself.

Yom Kippur was a stunning drama: one goat brought cleansing through its blood (life); the other symbolically carried away the sins of the nation.

God did not punish either goat with wrath. They were instruments of God's mercy—signs of His desire to remove sin, not destroy sinners.

The logic of Yom Kippur is restoration, not retribution. In Eden, humanity was exiled from the Garden. In Israel’s communal life, individuals could be exiled from the camp for defiling acts. And Israel itself could be exiled from the Land for covenant unfaithfulness.

But Yom Kippur declares God's heart: I will draw you back!

Jesus Himself became the perfect fulfillment of this. He was both “exiled” from His people, rejected and crucified outside the city (Hebrews 13:12), bearing the sin and shame of not just Israel but the entire world. His blood purified Israel (and the world) like the first goat, and in His crucifixion outside the camp, He symbolically carried away the sins of Israel (and the world) as the ultimate scapegoat. Yet He did not bear them in a drama of divine wrath, but in a mission of divine love—willingly entering our exile to bring us home.

He bore our sins to remove them forever. He died not to appease divine fury but to cleanse and heal—not just one nation but the entire world.

No Wrath on the Animals, No Wrath on Christ

Notably absent from the Yom Kippur rituals is any description of wrath being poured out on the goats. The animals were not tortured. They were not abused. Their blood was used because it represented life (Leviticus 17:11). The first goat's death was necessary only to obtain the blood that purifies. The scapegoat didn't even die—it was sent alive into the wilderness, symbolically carrying away the sins of Israel.

Jesus' sacrifice, like Yom Kippur, was about cleansing, restoration, and reunion with God—not about God pouring out stored-up wrath on the innocent One.

Communal Atonement: The Pattern Continues

What is striking is that in Yom Kippur, atonement was granted to all Israel. The text does not demand that every individual must personally offer a sacrifice, hold to a specific doctrine, or understand every aspect of the ritual for it to be effective. The high priest's actions universally covered the nation. The ritual was for the nation as a whole. This was covenantal, communal atonement.

And this is precisely the pattern the New Testament sees fulfilled in Jesus. His life, death, and resurrection are presented not as a limited private transaction for individual believers alone but as a cosmic act of universal reconciliation:

For God was pleased to have all his fullness dwell in him, and through him to reconcile to himself all things, whether things on earth or things in heaven, by making peace through his blood, shed on the cross.

(Colossians 1:19-20)

Conclusion

The Day of Atonement in Israel's story is the Day of Hope, not the Day of Punishment. It looked backward, acknowledging sin and defilement. It looked inward, inviting repentance and reflection. And it looked forward, proclaiming God's promise to remove sin forever and dwell once again with His people.

Yom Kippur was not about fear of punishment but about confidence in restoration. And Jesus, our High Priest and perfect sacrifice, has fulfilled this ancient hope:

But now he has appeared once for all at the culmination of the ages to do away with sin by the sacrifice of himself. 

(Hebrews 9:26)

This is the gospel: God is not against us. He is for us. His heart is always to cleanse, to restore, and to bring His people home.

The Day of Atonement was never about wrath. It was always about love. And Jesus is the living proof.

c163

Chapter 16
The Biblical Understanding of Wrath, Vengeance, and Justice

Fundamentalists often point out, and with good reason, that human sin provokes God’s wrath. Sin is no trivial matter in Scripture; it disrupts creation, fractures shalom—peace and wholeness—and wounds the relationship between humanity and God.

But when many Westerners hear the word “wrath,” they picture a storming deity with clenched fists, ready to smite and cast into hellfire. For centuries, this image has haunted pulpits and darkened the gospel. But the Hebrew Scriptures offer a very different vision—one far more tragic, far more loving, far more relational, and far more profound.

So, before we rush to imagine God’s wrath as molten fury, violent punishment, or volcanic rage, we should pause and return to the very first moment humans sinned—back to the Garden of Eden.

Do you remember how God’s alleged “wrath” manifested the moment Adam and Eve disobeyed?

Was there thunder, screaming, or a violent attack? Did God pull out his belt or sink them into lava?

None of the above.

Instead, the scene is marked by grief, divine sorrow, and a surprising tenderness: God covers their shame.

Back in the Garden

After Adam and Eve hid in fear, God did not strike them down. He did not drag them out of the bushes, whip them, or scorch them with fire. He called to them. He sought them. And He clothed them. God did not hide His face from them; they were the ones who hid.

In the ancient Near East, clothing was far more than a piece of garment—it was a statement, a symbol of identity, status, and vocation. Scripture consistently uses garments to mark a person’s calling or position: the priestly garments (Exodus 28; 29:5–9), Jonathan giving David his robe as a sign of covenant and succession (1 Samuel 18:4), Elijah passing his mantle to Elisha (1 Kings 19:19), and the royal enthronement garments spread beneath Jehu (2 Kings 9:13).

So, when God clothes Adam and Eve with “garments of skin” in Genesis 3:21, He is doing far more than covering shame. He is reaffirming their priestly identity and mission to subdue and have dominion over creation (Genesis 1:28) and recommissioning them as His image-bearers—now within a fallen world. Instead of lashing out at them, He appoints them priests over all creation.51

This is not the action of an enraged deity but of a compassionate Creator who refuses to abandon His children. Even in judgment, He restores their dignity, equips them for life beyond Eden, and preserves their role as the ones through whom His redemptive story will unfold.

God, who knew exactly what they had done, moved toward them—not with rage, but with relational restoration.

So, where many claim the very first picture of divine wrath is, we see not a cosmic temper tantrum but a redemptive response. Yes, consequences follow—they are sent out of the garden—but even their exile is laced with protection and promise. The narrative centers not on God abandoning humanity, but God accompanying them into a new experience.

I’m aware of the Calvinistic interpretation of Genesis 3 that claims God violently killed an animal to atone for Adam and Eve’s sin. But that idea is not in the text. It is not exegesis—it is eisegesis, an attempt to read later theological assumptions back into the story. Genesis simply says God made garments of skin and clothed them; the text offers no description of a kill, sacrifice, wrath, violence, blood, or atonement.

If you want to dive deeper into this, we can look at the so-called “penal substitution” passages in the Bible, like Genesis 3, the binding of Isaac, Isaiah 53, and more. I explore these in detail in my book, The “Gospel” of Divine Abuse: Redeeming the Gospel from Gruesome Popular Preaching of an Abusive and Violent God, where I show why these readings collapse under the weight of the biblical evidence itself.

God’s Wrath is Restorative

The imagery of wrath used throughout Israel's Scriptures is intentionally severe. Hebrew terms such as qetsef, chemah, and za‘am communicate intensity—anger, burning heat, divine indignation—in a language ancient people could grasp. Yet the actions themselves are ultimately loving, because God’s judgment is restorative by nature.

Israel repeatedly provoked God through injustice and the worship of other gods, yet His judgments were always temporary. Every exile came to an end. Israel, no matter how sinful, has never ceased to exist, and as God promised, never will cease to exist (e.g., Jeremiah 31:35–37)—a fact evident in its existence even today.

The idea that God's wrath is restorative is beautifully expressed in Isaiah 1:24–26:

Ah! I will vent my wrath on my foes… I will thoroughly purge away your dross… I will restore your leaders as in days of old… Afterward, you will be called the City of Righteousness.

God's wrath led to purification. His wrath, in its truest biblical sense, is like a fire that refines, not a wildfire that destroys. It's discipline that purges away impurity. It is judgment that leads to redemption.

God’s wrath, then, is not the wrath of a cruel despot—it is the holy ache of a Father whose love burns precisely because sin wounds the ones He cherishes. His wrath is not aimed at annihilation but at healing the fracture that sin has introduced.

The real question is not whether God has wrath, but what kind of wrath He has. Is it the fury of a vengeful tyrant, or the passionate flame of divine love, cleansing and renewing?

The first sin reveals much about God’s justice, yet even more about His compassionate heart.

God's Wrath Isn't Human Rage, but Divine Withdrawal

In biblical terms, God’s wrath is not divine fury but divine absence.

It’s what happens when the Giver of life and protection steps aside; when God’s presence—the force that holds everything together—is withdrawn. In the Bible, wrath is less about an act of violence and more about the quiet that follows after divine love has been ignored for too long.

This is the pattern woven throughout Israel’s story. Again and again, when Israel chose idols over God, oppression over justice, and ignored His ways, God did not erupt in uncontrolled anger. Instead, He withdrew, allowing them to have what they demanded—the experience of the prodigal walking a path defenseless and alone—and to face the consequences of that choice:

Surely the arm of the LORD is not too short to save, nor his ear too dull to hear. But your [Israel] iniquities have separated you from your God; your sins have hidden his face from you, so that he will not hear.

(Isaiah 59:1-2)

And that withdrawal, that vacuum, is what we call “wrath.”

Wrath is the warmth of the sun being covered. The silence of the beloved no longer speaking. It is God saying, “If you will not have Me, then I will not force Myself upon you.” It is not a tantrum—it is the ache of rejected love.

This is why the sacrificial system was so essential. It was not a way to “pay off” an angry God. It was a way to maintain the presence of the God who longed to dwell among His people. It was Israel's way of saying, "We want you here!" The Tabernacle was not built to satisfy God's ego. It was built so that God's nearness—His Spirit—would remain.

When Israel rejected that closeness—when they defiled the sanctuary or pursued other gods—it wasn’t that God lost it and struck them with lightning. It was that He was no longer welcome, no longer there to offer protection.

And into that vacancy rushed all the terrors of a fallen world: invading armies, plagues, famine, chaos, exile. Not because God struck—but because God stepped back and allowed Israel to suffer the consequences of their decisions.

The New Testament doesn’t really change much. We see this clearly in Romans 1. Paul writes:

The wrath of God is being revealed…
Therefore God gave them over…
Because of this, God gave them over…
So God gave them over…

(Romans 1:18, 24, 26, 28)

Wrath, according to Paul, is God giving people over to their own desires. It’s not rocks falling down from the sky—it’s more like the father in the parable of the Prodigal Son, letting his son walk away and suffer the consequences of his choices. It’s not about seeking revenge, but about a sorrowful letting go—a step back that lets sin run its course and teach its own lesson.

The Greek word orgē, commonly translated “wrath,” does not describe cold rage or sadistic anger; it comes from a root associated with intense inner movement or passion—the same linguistic family that later gives us orgasm, a word denoting swelling, excitement, overwhelming force, or surge. Both derive from the same Greek verbal root ὀργ, meaning “to swell, be excited, be in heat,” and both ultimately trace back to the Proto‑Indo‑European root wrog, “to swell, grow, become strong.”52

In Scripture, orgē points to God’s burning passion in response to chaos and injustice—a deep, settled intensity that often results not in violent outbursts but in God withdrawing and allowing consequences to unfold. It is not about God losing control, but more about God’s love reaching a boiling point where He steps back so disorder can be exposed, confronted, and ultimately healed.

Paul isn’t introducing a new concept—he’s echoing a consistent theme from the Old Testament. God's wrath, as seen throughout Scripture, is about allowing people to walk the path they’ve chosen and face the natural consequences. It’s a pattern of divine discipline, not retaliation.

Psalm 78:49–50 literally combines “wrath” with “gave them over”—directly echoed by Romans 1.

Lamentations 4:11 describes wrath as destruction caused by God removing His protection and allowing Zion to fall, which mirrors the abandonment judgment concept in Romans 1.

In Isaiah 10:5–6, God uses a foreign nation as an instrument of wrath, allowing Israel’s enemies to rise against her—not by unleashing random fury but by stepping back and no longer shielding her from the consequences of rebellion. Once again, wrath is interpreted not as explosive rage but as God stepping back and giving people over to the results of their own choices.

I know some might raise objections by pointing to exceptions such as the Flood or the destruction of Sodom and Gomorrah. But those events belong to a different category altogether, involving fallen angels and the Nephilim—an entirely separate subject, and one addressed in another book.53

Rage Doesn't Appease Wrath

Think about it logically—you don’t appease someone’s fury with rage—you calm it with compassion, understanding, and peace. Meeting anger with more anger only fuels the fire; it’s love and forgiveness that break the cycle. This is divine logic: “Do not be overcome by evil, but overcome evil with good.” (Romans 12:21; see also Matthew 5:39-44, Ephesians 4:31–32).

I’m not talking about pacifism. There are times when you must defend yourself and those you love. I’m talking about revenge for the sake of revenge—violence driven not by necessity or protection, but by the intoxicating desire to retaliate.

Likewise, you don’t make peace with your angry neighbors by attacking them (nor by pouring out your anger on your innocent son instead). True reconciliation doesn’t come through retaliation—it comes through forgiveness, mercy, humility, and love that breaks the cycle of vengeance.

In this light, we can understand the role of blood and sacrifice more deeply. The shedding of blood didn’t “appease” wrath—it welcomed God to remain by symbolically cleansing and purifying His sacred "guestroom." It was a sign that the people of Israel still desired Him. Still welcomed Him. Still sought His nearness.

And when they didn’t—when they abandoned the sacred rhythms of worship—it was as if they said, “We don’t need You. We’ll manage.” And God, honoring their choice, let them walk without Him. His absence was His wrath.

This is why Israel’s most severe judgment in the Bible was not divine violence, but divine withdrawal. God stepping back. Protection lifted. Exile imposed. Enemies invading—not because God turned violent, but because His presence was no longer restraining the chaos. The terror was not that God hurled fire and lightning from the sky on Israel, but that He was no longer there to shield her.

And this makes Jesus’s sacrifice all the more beautiful. He came not to endure His Father’s rage but to bring the Father’s presence back to a world that had pushed Him away. His blood is not the shield against divine anger—it is the welcome mat for divine return.

Jesus's blood restores what we lost in Eden. It reopens the door that was closed by our rejection. It brings back the Presence that once walked among us in the cool of the day.

Human Vengeance vs. Divine Justice

In our modern culture, the word justice often carries the sharp edge of vengeance, violent rage, and erratic retaliation. We imagine revenge through cinematic lenses—gritty anti-heroes, blood-stained justice, explosive battles for payback. To exact vengeance is to settle the score. It's emotional. Brutal. Personal. Yet when Scripture speaks of vengeance, particularly in Hebrew, it invites us into a very different image—one not driven by ego, pain, or rage, but by restoration and the long arc of divine mercy.

The Hebrew Naqam originates as a primitive root meaning “to avenge.” In Scripture, however, it transcends human revenge and reflects God’s covenantal justice—a divine act of restoring balance. God's justice defies the shallowness of human revenge fantasies. It means more than retaliation—it implies setting things right, a divine act rooted in restorative justice.

When humans seek vengeance, they are often led by bruised egos or unresolved pain. Revenge is rarely about justice—it’s about feeling better. We lash out, not to restore wholeness, but to satisfy our wounded pride. Consider how nations go to war, families break apart, reputations are destroyed—all under the pretense of "justice," when in truth, it’s pain begging to be seen.

God's vengeance is not like this.

Scripture is replete with a radically different vision of divine vengeance. The justice of God is not chaotic fury—it is focused, purposeful, and redemptive. It is justice that heals. Consider Isaiah 61:2:

To proclaim the year of the LORD’s favor and the day of vengeance of our God, to comfort all who mourn…

Did you catch that?

God’s day of vengeance and comfort are spoken in the same breath. The vengeance of God isn’t for entertainment or ego—it’s to comfort the mourners. It’s not to destroy sinners but to deliver the brokenhearted from what sin has done to the world. Vengeance is not God’s wild rage—it’s His passionate justice rushing in to rescue what evil tried to destroy.

True love doesn't compromise justice, but neither does it abandon mercy. Strip mercy from justice, and you're left with something harsh and cold: a rigid system that may demand what is "right," but offers no grace to the broken. Justice without mercy breeds fear, not freedom; anxiety, not peace; anger, not healing. It crushes the spirit instead of restoring it. Without mercy, justice becomes a weapon rather than a cure. But when justice is tempered by mercy, it becomes redemptive—it doesn’t just expose what’s wrong, it invites healing and transformation. That is the love we see in God, and that is the love we are called to reflect.

Love is justice—is the fulfilling of the law, for God as well as for his children. This is the reason of punishment; this is why justice requires that the wicked shall not go unpunished—that they, through the eye-opening power of pain, may come to see and do justice, may be brought to desire and make all possible amends, and so become just.54

(George MacDonald)

If God truly never changes, then His merciful judgment, forgiving nature, and loving character must remain consistent—yesterday, today, and forever.

Let God Be the Judge

Paul echoes this divine logic in Romans 12:19:

Do not take revenge, my dear friends, but leave room for God’s wrath, for it is written: "It is mine to avenge; I will repay," says the Lord.

Why does Paul tell us to stand back and leave room for God? Not because God’s payback will be harsher, but because His vengeance serves a purpose we can’t accomplish—restoration.

Human vengeance only knows how to punish. Divine vengeance knows how to purify. We judge from pain. God judges out of love.

Renewal, Not Retaliation

The Hebrew prophets constantly remind us: God's judgment is aimed at renewal. Even His most dramatic acts of wrath—like the plagues in Egypt—were designed not for destruction but for repentance and redemption.

Yes, the Nile turned to blood. Yes, Egypt trembled under judgment. But through these signs, hearts were stirred. Egyptians (not just Israelites) joined the Exodus (Exodus 12:38). God’s "vengeance" created the cracks in Pharaoh’s empire so that the light of deliverance could break through.

Vengeance, then, was not an explosion of divine fury. It was an invitation to salvation.

Just as the plagues humbled Egypt and set both the Hebrews and many Egyptians free, the judgments in Revelation invite the nations to turn and be healed.

To Retaliate Is Carnal. To Forgive Is Divine.

When we try to take justice into our own hands, we only perpetuate the pain. The one who hits back only guarantees the next blow. But when we choose mercy, when we surrender vengeance, when we freely forgive, we create space for God to step in—not just to deal with the wrongdoer, but to transform both them and us. This is the divine logic:

Bless those who persecute you… Do not be overcome by evil, but overcome evil with good.

(Romans 12:14, 21)

We are not called to be agents of divine punishment. We are called to be agents of divine mercy. When we forgive those who hurt us, we mirror God's redemptive heart. Forgiveness is not pretending the wrong didn't happen. It's saying: "I will not continue in this endless cycle. I will not let your sin turn me into something evil. I will protect myself."

Jesus, the true embodiment of divine justice, didn’t demand cosmic revenge from the cross but instead called out for mercy, saying, “Father, forgive them.”

Divine Judgment Seeks Restoration

A good judge in a morally healthy society doesn't hand out punishments to feel better. A good judge discerns truth, weighs circumstances, and seeks justice that leads to restoration. In God’s economy, vengeance is never the final word. Redemption is:

I take no pleasure in the death of the wicked, but rather that they turn from their ways and live.

(Ezekiel 33:11)

Even divine wrath has this longing built into it—not to destroy, but to awaken. Not to shame, but to heal. God reveals the sin not to humiliate the sinner, but to expose the wound so it can finally be healed.

Every human heart will one day have to stand before, and give account to, Love Himself.

Divine judgment is God’s purifying truth unveiled—every mask removed, every lie exposed, every wound healed in light.

For some, that light will feel like scorching torment because it burns away illusion; for others, it will feel like homecoming. But the fire is the same—it is the divine fire of God's love. His wrath is not the opposite of His love; it is love’s refusal to tolerate what destroys its beloved.

When the church teaches about judgment, it should approach it with compassion, not with pride or a sense of triumph. To proclaim the gospel is to say: “The fire of God’s love is coming—and it can heal you now, or it can burn away what you cling to later.”

A Love That Judges

When people say, "God is love," they often mean it in a soft, harmless way. But "woke" love that never says “No,” never disciplines, never corrects—is not love at all. Love must judge. Love must speak. Love must protect.

Divine judgment is what happens when God's love collides with human injustice. It is not the opposite of grace—it is grace in action. And naqam is the justice of God flowing from a heart that refuses to let evil have the last word.

When Jesus cleared the temple courts, it wasn’t judgment divorced from love. It was love that had reached its breaking point with injustice. He turned over tables not to destroy but to cleanse the Temple. His naqam wasn’t an eruption of temper—it was a purification of sacred space.

And in the grandest expression of divine vengeance, Jesus bore injustice to give us mercy. He absorbed human wrath to unleash divine grace.

Conclusion

Wrath, then, is not God’s emotional outburst of anger—it’s Him drawing back from all that destroys love, justice, and life. It’s not a divine tantrum, but a holy resistance to evil—the burning passion of a God who refuses to be indifferent to injustice, yet allows humanity to face the consequences of its choices.

God’s wrath is more like that of a loving, heartbroken parent than a raging, cruel dictator throwing a divine tantrum. A good parent disciplines to guide and restore, not to harm or abuse. In the same way, God’s justice is aimed at healing, not destruction.

To live in the spirit of God's justice is to resist retaliation and entrust justice to God. It means: Speaking the truth without becoming bitter. Seeking justice without losing mercy. Refusing to repay evil with evil. Trusting God to deal with those who hurt us—even when it takes longer than we'd like.

Most of all, it means living in such a way that our response to wrong doesn't mirror the world's instinct for vengeance but reflects God’s instinct for redemption. “Vengeance is mine,” says the Lord. Not because He’s angrier than us—but because He’s wiser, purer, and more committed to saving the world than we’ll ever be.

So when you’re treated unfairly, don’t seek revenge. Trust God and the authorities He has established to handle justice. If someone hurts your feelings, don’t hit back—pray instead. Forgive, because they don’t truly understand what they’re doing. Leave the work of vengeance in God’s hands.

c19U

Chapter 17
The Problem with the Two Western Models of Atonement

More than once, my Jewish-Israeli friends have confronted me with a painfully honest question—one that perfectly exposes how the gospel is so often presented to them by Messianic and evangelical missionaries and how deeply it is misrepresented by many Christians today. Below is one example of such a question I received (translated from Hebrew):

If the Christian “good news” really comes from the Prince of Peace, why does it sound like a horror story whenever Messianic evangelists preach it? It sounds like a cosmic suicide where God sent Himself to kill Himself in order to save us from Himself but then only a few are actually saved from Him. Why does it come across as a gospel in which Jesus throws Himself under the bus to protect us from His abusive Father—an enraged deity supposedly marching toward humanity with a cosmic whip in His hand? Why does the path to “peace” look like a blood-soaked hostage exchange demanded by a wrathful God?

And they also point to how the whole performance is always held together by a handful of verses yanked violently out of context and stitched into a theological Frankenstein.

It’s a brutally fair question—and it reveals just how grotesquely distorted the modern message has become from the original Gospel Jesus actually preached.

So Why Do Some Modern Gospel Depictions Often Sound So Different?

For many Christians today, particularly those shaped by the Reformed or Evangelical traditions, the answer to the question of why Jesus died on the cross has become fixed: Penal Substitutionary Atonement (PSA). This theory, which paints the crucifixion as God punishing His Son in our place, has dominated Christian thought for the last few centuries. But this was not always the case. In fact, the PSA model and its predecessor, the Satisfaction Theory, are relatively late inventions—born not from the soil of the Hebrew Scriptures, but from feudal Europe and Western legal imaginations.

Penal Substitutionary Atonement was systematized about 500 years ago by the reformer John Calvin. Raised and trained within Catholicism, Calvin essentially modified the Catholic atonement doctrine of Satisfaction (although today, many Catholics no longer believe in it), making it even more violent and transactional. In Calvin's model, God is portrayed as a wrathful judge—a moral monster—demanding a kill in order to forgive even the slightest offence.

It is no surprise that the PSA model almost always goes hand in hand with the doctrine of Eternal Conscious Torment hellfire (ECT).

When Calvinism Infected American Christianity

When preachers such as George Whitefield, John and Charles Wesley, and, perhaps most notably, Jonathan Edwards introduced the Calvinistic PSA and ECT to America, existential fear spread them quickly, particularly among Puritans and similar groups. On July 8, 1741, Edwards delivered his most renowned sermon, “Sinners in the Hands of an Angry God,” which had a major influence on shaping the theology of American Christian fundamentalism. Below is a brief excerpt from his sermon:

The God that holds you over the pit of hell, much as one holds a spider or some loathsome insect over the fire, abhors you, and is dreadfully provoked. His wrath towards you burns like fire; he looks upon you as worthy of nothing else but to be cast into the fire. He is of purer eyes than to bear you in his sight; you are ten thousand times as abominable in his eyes as the most hateful, venomous serpent is in ours.

It would be dreadful to suffer this fierceness and wrath of Almighty God one moment; but you must suffer it to all eternity. There will be no end to this exquisite, horrible misery. When you look forward, you shall see along forever a boundless duration before you, which will swallow up your thoughts, and amaze your soul. And you will absolutely despair of ever having any deliverance, any end, any mitigation, any rest at all. You will know certainly that you must wear out long ages, millions of millions of ages in wrestling with this Almighty, merciless vengeance. And then, when you have so done, when so many ages have actually been spent by you in this manner, you will know that all is but a point to what remains. So that your punishment will indeed be infinite…

The world will probably be converted into a great lake or liquid globe of fire, in which the wicked shall be overwhelmed, which will always be in tempest, in which they shall be tossed to and fro, having no rest day and night, vast waves and billows of fire continually rolling over their heads, of which they shall forever be full of a quick sense within and without; their heads, their eyes, their tongues, their hands, their feet, their loins and their vitals, shall forever be full of a flowing, melting fire, fierce enough to melt the very rocks and elements; and also, they shall eternally be full of the most quick and lively sense to feel the torments; not for one minute, not for one day, not for one age, not for two ages, not for a hundred ages, nor for ten thousand millions of ages, one after another, but forever and ever, without any end at all, and never to be delivered.55

Today, Edwards' ideas, rooted in PSA and ECT, continue to have a strong influence in America, particularly among Baptists, Calvinists, Pentecostals, Calvary Chapel members, Brethren assemblies, Evangelicals, Messianics, certain Mormons (LDS), and other fundamentalist groups.

The Roots of PSA: Anselm’s Satisfaction

If the PSA model represents the Protestant view of atonement, the Satisfaction model represents the Catholic view—the root from which Protestantism emerged (though again, many Catholics have since moved away from it). That’s also why these two models are so similar theologically. To better grasp the PSA model (16th century), it helps to first understand the Satisfaction model upon which it was built (10th century).

Anselm of Canterbury (1033–1109), an Italian Benedictine monk, lived during a time when society was governed by the feudal system. Honor, allegiance, and hierarchical submission were the foundations of Europian justice. A commoner who insulted a knight or king would not simply be forgiven. That would be seen as weakness. Honor had to be restored—and restoration meant punishment. Severe, often lethal punishment.

In that cultural framework, Anselm developed what became known as the Satisfaction Model of Atonement. He argued against the Church Fathers' historical atonement theories and claimed that sin was not just wrongdoing, but an offense against the infinite honor of God. Since humans had dishonored the divine King, someone had to make restitution. But no mere human could satisfy God’s infinite honor. Only God could do that. So, in Anselm's view, God became human in Jesus so the Father could kill Him and restore His own honor. Jesus’ punishment was the payment of an unpayable debt. Divine honor was satisfied, and humanity could be forgiven.

While it may sound noble, Satisfaction was soaked in the values of feudalism: honor, hierarchy, and vengeance. Rather than a loving Father in the context of a relationship, it cast God as a medieval angry king whose dignity required death to be appeased.

From Satisfaction to PSA: Logic of Violence

In the 16th century, John Calvin radicalized Anselm’s vision. A trained lawyer, Calvin built upon the Satisfaction model and gave us what is now known as Penal Substitutionary Atonement (PSA). In PSA, the central claim is that not only God’s honor but also His justice demand eternal conscious punishment for every sin, whether big or small. Yet, instead of punishing us, God places that punishment on Jesus, who takes our place. The full force of divine wrath falls on the innocent Son. In PSA, it is God who punishes—torturing and killing Jesus. This is at least how Calvinist and Evangelical preachers view it:

We must remember, however, that sin did not kill Jesus; God did. The suffering servant’s death was nothing less than a punishment administered by God for sins others had committed.56 

(John MacArthur)

God’s anger at sin and hatred of sinners causes him to pour out his wrath on Jesus.57

(Mark Driscoll)

Jesus was not swept away by the wrath of uncontrolled men. He was bruised by his Father. Why? To resolve the tension between the Father’s love for his glory and his love for sinners.58

(John Piper)

In this "cosmic suicide" perspective, it is not simply that Jesus paid our debt on our behalf by offering His precious eternal blood—He also had to endure God's violent sentence. Quoting verses out of context,59 PSA casts Jesus as a divine whipping boy, absorbing the cosmic punishment humanity deserved. In this model, God's justice is satisfied not by forgiveness, but by violence. Divine love and divine wrath are pitted against one another in a grotesque transaction.

Here, God's love was no longer about self-sacrifice—absorbing and forgiving human sin—but had become a stage for divine cruelty and cosmic violence, with God portrayed as a fearsome grand torturer.

But let us ask honestly: what kind of love is this?

Would we call it love if a father punished his innocent son to spare his guilty brothers? Would we call it justice?

This theology resembles not the heart of a loving Father, but the profile of a sadistic abuser. It teaches us to see God as angry, explosive, and appeased only through killing. It turns God into a tyrant demanding nothing but perfection. As one famous Calvinist once put it, “God only accepts absolute perfection.” (John MacArthur).60

Conclusion

What began as good news has, in too many mouths, curdled into a sanctified horror tale—a Father pacing heaven like a tyrant king, a Son crushed beneath a sentence He did not deserve, and salvation offered only through terror. A gospel born in biblical mercy was disfigured by foreign imaginations until love was recast as violence and justice as a divine abuse. The result is a theological monster stitched together from fear, power, and misplaced zeal—one that repels the wounded and terrifies the faithful. Yet the grotesque mask is not the face of God. Strip it away, and the scandal reappears: not a whip, but open arms; not appeasement through cruelty, but victory through self-giving love. The true gospel does not coerce with nightmares—it heals, restores, and calls the world home.

c1B8

Chapter 18
Misunderstanding Forgiveness: A Debt Forgiven Is Not Paid

If you punched me in the face and I chose to forgive you, it would not be because I punched you back—or punched someone else in your place. That would not be forgiveness; it would be retaliation. Forgiveness means I absorb the wrong done to me rather than pass it on.

If God had to torture and kill Jesus instead of us in order to forgive (PSA), then what we have is not forgiveness at all—it is violence redirected.

The Meaning of Forgiveness

Both the Satisfaction and the Penal Substitutionary Atonement theories rest on the assumption that, for forgiveness to occur, a debt to God—whether framed as lost honor or as violated justice—must first be paid. But that is not forgiveness; it is payment.

Forgiveness is about releasing someone from what they owe you, not taking your anger with them out on someone else. If you’ve paid off your debt to the bank, it’s not forgiveness; when a debt is forgiven, it’s not actually paid. These are two entirely different realities that should not be confused.

Forgiveness, in its truest and most beautiful form, is the act of releasing someone from what they rightfully owe—not because the debt was transferred to another, but because the offended party chose to absorb the loss out of love. This is exactly how Jesus taught us to understand forgiveness. He said, “Forgive us our debts, as we forgive our debtors” (Matthew 6:12), meaning that forgiveness is not a transactional exchange or substitution of penalty, but a free act of mercy.

If God had to be paid before He could forgive, then forgiveness is no longer a free gift nor is it grace—it becomes a transaction, a settlement, a business deal. But the gospel tells a different story. The cross was never about God being paid so He could forgive; it was about God showing us He already forgives—not because of an event but because that's His nature. Jesus didn’t die to persuade God to forgive us; He died to show us God’s forgiveness. True forgiveness comes without conditions. It’s a gift of pure, unearned grace that flows from the heart of the forgiver, simply because they take joy in showing mercy (Micah 7:18).

This means that Jesus gave His life for our sake, not for the sake of appeasing His Father. Jesus’ death without retaliation toward humanity was a display of divine grace, and His resurrection was a mighty demonstration of divine power.

The Reversed Gospel

To say that Jesus had to be tortured on a cross to convince the Father to forgive us is to reverse the Gospel into paganism and misrepresent the very character of God. The cross was not the price God demanded to change His heart toward humanity; the cross was the price that human sin demanded to extinguish divine love—and even that failed.

God's forgiveness is free because it flows from His nature—not from a violent settlement He demanded. The cross reveals God's forgiveness much more than it enables it.

To say otherwise would be like saying:

"God is love... but only after being paid off."

That’s not the gospel of Jesus. That’s a reversal.

God Already Forgave Jesus's Enemies

On the cross, just moments before His death, Jesus prayed one final, often overlooked prayer:

Father, forgive them...

(Luke 23:34)

He wasn’t asking for His friends. He wasn’t interceding on behalf of the faithful.

Jesus asked God to forgive His accusers, His torturers, His murderers—His narcissist enemies.

These were the very people who mocked Him for years, then beat Him, humiliated Him, and nailed Him to a piece of wood. In that moment of unimaginable pain, Jesus didn’t curse them to hellfire for their disbelief. He didn’t seek fiery revenge. He asked for mercy. He pleaded for forgiveness.

The question is: Did the Father turn down the Son’s plea? In much of Western Christian theology, the common answer is yes.

We are told that unless those individuals confessed their sin, repented, believed, and converted in the correct doctrinal formula, God refused to forgive them—even though His own Son explicitly asked Him to.

But what does that say about the heart of the Father? And what does it say about the unity between Father and Son—if one asked for mercy and the other stubbornly denied it? Was Jesus just saying empty words, offering a dramatic but ultimately meaningless request?

Or is it possible—no, likely—that the heart of the Son is also the heart of the Father, and that this prayer was answered in full?

Because if Jesus truly revealed the nature of God, then “Father, forgive them” was not just virtue signaling or wishful thinking on His part. It was a glimpse into divine love—a love that forgives before it's asked, that embraces before it's understood, and that opens the door of grace even while the hammer still strikes the nails.

Before the Gospel Was Reversed

None of what I’m presenting here is new. I’m simply echoing what Jesus’ earliest disciples and the first generations of Church Fathers taught—those closest to the Apostles, those who inherited the faith before it became entangled in later philosophical systems. Not one of them taught the Protestant Penal Substitutionary Atonement or the Catholic Satisfaction theory; these are much later constructions, shaped by medieval assumptions rather than by the Jewish worldview of Jesus and His earliest followers.

In the first centuries of Christianity, the dominant vision of atonement was called the “ransom” view: humanity held in bondage (by Satan), Christ entering our captivity, and—through His death and resurrection—breaking the chains from the inside, not by satisfying a debt to God, but by setting the captives free from the evil one.

In this book, my aim isn’t to completely dismantle the Protestant PSA or the Catholic Satisfaction models, nor to delve deeply into other atonement theories—that’s something I’ve already done in a previous work, The “Gospel” of Divine Abuse: Redeeming the Gospel from Gruesome Popular Preaching of an Abusive and Violent God. Instead, here I will simply highlight a few essential points as to why I find these two Western perceptions of the atonement troubling.

Ethical and Theological Objections

Father-Son Schism: PSA fractures the unity between Father and Son. Pitting them one against the other, it paints a violent divide between the Father and Son, contradicting Jesus’ words: "I and the Father are one" (John 10:30).

Forgiveness Reimagined: True forgiveness is letting go of debt, not redirecting anger or punishment to someone else. God doesn’t need a transaction to forgive. Jesus didn’t die to change God’s mind about us. He came to change our minds about God.

Distorted Sense of Justice: Punishing the innocent—substituting the guiltless for the guilty violates the most basic sense of biblical justice (e.g., Ezekiel 18:20). We rightly recoil from a human judge who does this. Why would we think God's justice contradicts the Bible?

Love Undermined: The message that God "loves you so much He killed His Son for you" is deeply troubling. Love does not require violence to be genuine. As N.T. Wright notes, "Simply adding the word 'love' to divine violence makes it worse, not better."61

Disproportionate Justice: Eternal Conscious Torment for finite sins, as demanded by most proponents of Penal Substitutionary Atonement and Satisfaction theories, is morally incoherent. Even human courts recognize proportionality as a basic requirement of justice. Is God’s justice truly less merciful—less rational—than the standard of justice He commands from us? (Exodus 21:24).

Traumatic and Triggering: The image of God as an abuser traumatizes those who have experienced violence. It replaces the Good Shepherd with a cosmic punisher, only further pushing people away from Him.

Violence as Sacred: I don't believe Jesus was a pacifist. But PSA and Satisfaction theories make violence not just necessary but holy. It elevates violence as salvific. This is a dangerous message that resembles Islamic beliefs more than ancient Jewish thought.

Distorted Gospel: The gospel is reduced to a legal transaction rather than revealed as a love story. It becomes a courtroom drama instead of a work of relational restoration—a bleak and hopeless narrative in which most of humanity is written off as doomed rather than healed.

Scriptural Misuse: Verses are cherry-picked to support PSA and Satisfaction, always out of context. Leviticus is read through Calvin, not Moses. The cross is interpreted through Roman law, not Hebrew sacrifice.

Fear-Based Religion: PSA and Satisfaction theories rely on fear as the main motivator for belief. But "perfect love casts out fear" (1 John 4:18). If your gospel is based on terror, it is not the good news for all people (Luke 2:10).

Pagan Echoes: The idea of appeasing an angry deity through sacrifice echoes pagan religions, not Israel's covenantal worship.

Historical Latecomer: PSA and Satisfaction theories were not believed by the early church. They are products of the Catholic church (10th century) and the Reformation (16th century), foreign to both biblical Judaism and the earliest Christians.

I do not care whether you call yourself Catholic or Protestant. I have dear friends who are both—Protestant pastors and theologians, as well as Catholic theologians, priests, and even a Cardinal. Call yourself whatever your conscience allows. What concerns me is the vision of God, Jesus, and the gospel that you actually hold in your heart.

Western theories of atonement not only misrepresent God's character but also subtly shift the burden of salvation back onto us, making it depend on our intellectual beliefs, moral conduct, or the strength of our trust. But that’s shaky ground. It breeds anxiety, not assurance, because deep down, you’re never quite sure if you believe enough, understand correctly enough, do enough good, or trust deeply enough. Over time, this leads to emotional stress, spiritual exhaustion, and in many cases, deep discouragement and depression.

The true gospel, by contrast, brings peace (Matthew 11:28, John 14:27), not pressure. It shifts the weight off us and onto God. That’s why it brings genuine rest, lasting peace of mind, and freedom for the soul—not a demand to perform or the correct boxes to check, but an invitation to let go of fear and trust in what has already been accomplished.

A Better Way: Life, Not Death, at the Center

PSA/Satisfaction glorifies death and violence, portraying God as a bloodthirsty sadist who finds satisfaction in bloodshed and death. But the true gospel reveals a God who heals, restores, and gives life—not one who demands a kill to be appeased. Jesus did not come to absorb God's violent wrath. He came to reveal God's loving face.

The cross is not about divine abuse. It is about participation. It is not a transaction. It is a transfusion. Jesus enters into our sinful world not to judge it but to save it. He joins us in our suffering and death, not to appease the Father but to heal humanity. His blood (life) and resurrection, not His torture and death, are the turning point of history.

As Bible scholar Michael Heiser wrote:

Death was a problem that needed solving—for everyone. This makes the focal point of God’s plan the resurrection, not the violent death. In other words, it ultimately wasn’t the death of Jesus that brought about redemption for lost humanity. It was the resurrection.62

(Dr. Michael Heiser)

In the logic of love, Jesus is not a stand-in for punishment; He is the Wounded Healer. The Loving Priest. The One who shows us that God does not abandon us in our suffering—He joins us in it so we can be transformed from the inside out.

Conclusion

Jesus did not die to change God’s mind about us—from anger to kindness—He died to change our minds about God—to reveal that the Father has always been ready to forgive, restore, and embrace His lost creation.

Believing the wrong model of atonement tragically distorts not only the Gospel but also the character of God revealed in Jesus—turning the gospel of love, mercy, and reconciliation into a courtroom drama of violence, retribution, and anxiety.

Yet when we turn to the Hebrew Scriptures, we find a very different picture: one not painted in retribution but in restoration; not focused on appeasing rage but on renewing life and communal wholeness.

To recover the essence of biblical sacrifice is to rediscover not only what atonement truly meant for Israel, but also what it means to say that Jesus is our atoning Lamb—not a lamb smashed under God's boot, but one who brings life where there was death, purity where there was decay, and union where there was distance.

The gospel does not require cosmic torture to be powerful. It does not need a wrathful Father killing His Son to be moving. That is not love. That is not justice. That is sadism.

The cross is where God, in Christ, drinks the cup of human wrath, takes in our violence, and still chooses to forgive us. That’s beauty—and more than enough.

c1DE

Chapter 19
The True Climax of the Gospel

As we near the close of this section, every true story reaches the moment when its deepest meaning is finally unveiled—when everything that came before is revealed as preparation. In the gospel, that moment is not merely the death of Jesus. A story grounded in restoration rather than revenge cannot end with a corpse, but with the power that overcame it: resurrection. His blood—His very life—proved unconquerable, for it is of an eternal quality.

Let’s summarize what we’ve learned so far.

Death could not hold what was never subject to corruption. And in conquering death, Jesus unveils the central truth of the gospel: that life, not destruction, is God’s final word.

It is not His death that redeems, resurrects, and saves us—it's His precious blood that holds eternal life that does.

Jesus' blood was not like the blood of bulls and goats, which had to be offered repeatedly. His was different. His was incorruptible, infused with divine vitality. His blood was not just symbolic—it was eternal substance: “How much more will the blood of Christ, who through the eternal Spirit offered himself…” (Hebrews 9:14)

Jesus’ blood holds eternal worth and impact because His life itself is eternal in worth and effect.

This changes everything. It reframes the gospel away from appeasement-through-torture and centers it on transfusion of life, triumph of the resurrection over death, and restoration of relationship. The climax is not wrath being vented through divine violence—but life being poured out, shared, and never running dry.

This is why Scripture doesn’t say, "God killed Jesus so He can spare you." It says:

"…you were redeemed… with the precious blood [life] of Christ…" (1 Peter 1:18–19)

"…justified by His blood [life]…" (Romans 5:9)

"…redeemed through His blood [life]…" (Ephesians 1:7)

"…made holy through His blood [life]…" (Hebrews 13:12)

"…peace made through His blood [life]…" (Colossians 1:20)

"…freed us from our sins by His blood [life]…" (Revelation 1:5)

Not one of these verses suggests Christ was God's punching bag.

Not one celebrates the violence done to Him.

All of them focus on His blood—because His blood held His life, and His life was divine.

Jesus’s blood is not about the Father's anger. It is about death finally being defeated on our behalf.

The climax of the gospel is that God infused humanity with His own indestructible life to rescue it from perishing in the decay of sin and death.

The gospel is not death appeasing God. That's how pagans used to appease their gods. The gospel is not God demanding bloodshed like a mafia boss seeking satisfaction. The gospel is the God of life giving us eternal life through divine blood transfusion.

You have been brought near by the blood of Christ.

(Ephesians 2:13)

And this blood is not running out. It is not drying up. It is not locked in the past. It flows. Even now.

Life-Transferred—The Divine Blood Transfusion

Imagine this: a person is dying. Their organs are failing. Their face pale. Their breath shallow. The doctors rush in, not with anger, but with a gift—a blood bag, a lifeline. The needle pierces skin, and slowly, cell by cell, oxygen and life begin to flow back into their body.

They live—not because someone was punished, but because someone gave their blood.

This is the logic of the Gospel. Not a judge’s gavel—a transfusion. A heart pouring its life into another. A loving parent giving their life for their children's sake.

And what is blood, if not life made visible? It carries breath, immunity, healing, energy. Blood flows to the body’s wounds with an army of restoration. It knows where to go. It remembers where the pain is. It rushes to heal.

Jesus’ blood does the same. God didn't kill Christ instead of killing us. Christ died for us—so He could cover us with His blood—give us His life.

Now, read it together:

"Drink from it, all of you. This is My blood" (Matthew 26:27-28). "Unless you drink the blood, you have no life in you." (John 6:53).

These words once scandalized His listeners. And rightly so. But Jesus was not inviting cannibalism. He was unveiling the mystery of union. To drink His blood is to receive His life-force—His divine vitality—eternal life into our own mortal bodies. It is to become, as Paul says, “a new creation” (2 Corinthians 5:17). Mystically. Truly.

The blood of Christ flows to the wound of the world—death. And it fills that wound with eternal life that cannot die.

This is not theology for the mind alone. This is medicine for the soul.

The old sacrifices offered temporary healing and purification because animals are mortal. But Christ is immortal, and His blood, once for all, reaches deeper. It does not just clean the outer man. It cleanses the conscience (Hebrews 9:14). It does not merely forgive—it renews.

Now, read it together:

“The life is in the blood…” (Leviticus 17:11) “…and the blood of Jesus, His Son, purifies us from all sin.” (1 John 1:7).

The logic is ancient. The Spirit is eternal. The effect is immediate.

His blood received; our life renewed (Romans 6:4).

The Final Cleansing — Why Life in the Blood Conquers Death Forever

If the story of humanity is the story of exile from life, then the story of redemption is the story of returning to it. But how does one return to life while still surrounded by death?

The Hebrew Scriptures offer a radical answer: life is stronger than death—when it's divine.

This is why blood was always the chosen symbol. Not because it was grotesque, but because it was the most intimate, most vital, most sacred part of living existence. It spoke of lineage and covenant. It signified the unseen miracle that made dust live. It carried oxygen and power—it carried life!

And that is why, in the Tabernacle and later in the Temple, blood became the purifier. It was the great cleanser—not of dirt, but of death’s stain. Just as we instinctively reached for sanitizer during the pandemic after shaking a hand or touching a doorknob, so Israel instinctively turned to blood as the deep spiritual solvent:

In fact, the law requires that nearly everything be cleansed with blood, and without the shedding of blood there is no remission.

(Hebrews 9:22)

It's not because God seeks death and violence, but because without the giving of life, there is no reversal of death.

Unlike the pagans, Israel's sacrificial system was never about vengeance. It was about life and relationship.

It allowed the people to draw near again. It restored the relationship. It cleaned the sanctuary—first the Tabernacle, then the Temple, and finally the human heart.
