And now, through Jesus—the true Lamb, the eternal High Priest, the indestructible Tabernacle—the final cleansing has come.

His blood did what no sacrifice before could do. It cleansed not only for a period, but for infinity. Not only space, but conscience. Not just impurity, but identity. Not just the physical altar, but the inner soul.

Jesus' blood didn’t just cover sin—it removed it. It didn’t just postpone death—it defeated it:

The sting of death is sin, and the power of sin is the law. But thanks be to God! He gives us the victory through our Lord Jesus Christ.

(1 Corinthians 15:56–57)

This is the triumph at the center of the gospel—not wrath satisfied, but death swallowed up.

Not torment glorified, but life resurrected. Not condemnation declared, but creation restored.

Jesus didn’t come to be tortured for us. He came to donate His life—His blood—for us. He came to transfuse us with eternal breath.

In the end, blood was not the symbol of how angry God was about sin. It was the symbol of how far God would go to reclaim the world He loves.

So, we end where we began:

The life is in the blood… and I have given it to you.

(Leviticus 17:11)

He has given it. Freely. Fully. Forever. And it is enough.

Amen.

c1FT

Part III: A Hebrew Perspective on Christ's Atonement

c1G0

Introduction: Why Did Jesus Die?

The cross was not a detour, nor a moment of divine improvisation. It was the culmination of a long and sacred story—rooted in Israel’s Scriptures, anticipated by the prophets, and woven into the very architecture of the biblical narrative. In His death, Jesus did not merely address individual guilt; He brought history itself to a decisive turning point. He fulfilled Israel’s vocation, disarmed the powers, entered death to defeat it from within, and opened the path toward the renewal of all creation.

Many Christians focus so narrowly on sin that they miss the larger horizon of the cross. Yes—sins were forgiven, cleansed, and removed. Yes—the resurrection of the dead was secured through Jesus’ victory. But forgiveness, as central as it is, was not the whole story. What else was accomplished on that cross? What ancient drama reached its climax in that moment? And how did a single event in history reframe the entire trajectory of humanity’s shared story?

Throughout history, the Church has proposed multiple theories to explain Christ’s death, some of which overlap—even if imperfectly—with the Bible’s own theology of atonement. When the cross is read within its original Hebrew and biblical framework, it emerges as far more than merely a mechanism for dealing with “our sins.” It stands as the climactic convergence of covenantal, cosmic, and restorative themes that are too often overlooked.

With that in mind, I invite us to examine the cross within its Hebraic context, highlighting five frequently neglected dimensions of Christ’s atonement:

Christ Died Because He Was Our Perfect King-Redeemer

Christ Died Because He Was Our Perfect Chief Rabbi

Christ Died Because He Was Our Perfect Scapegoat

Christ Died Because He Was Our Perfect High Priest

Christ Died Because He Was Our Perfect Life-Giving Healer

c1GR

Chapter 20
Jesus Died Because He Was Our Perfect King-Redeemer

To understand the heart of the King of Israel, start here: He’s not only a warrior, not only just, not only powerful—He’s a Redeemer.

Throughout countless movies, legends, and fantasy epics, kings are portrayed as power-hungry rulers—enthroned in towering castles, surrounded by elite soldiers, insulated from the pain and cries of ordinary people, and feared more than loved. Their strength is measured by the size of their armies, their wealth by the walls that hide them, and their authority by how easily they can crush those beneath them. But this is not the kind of king Jesus is—not even close.

The heartbeat of the Torah is the God who saves—tangibly and literally. The God who sees His people in bondage and moves heaven and earth to set them free.

Consider how God first introduced Himself to Israel:

I am the LORD your God, who brought you out of Egypt, out of the land of slavery. 

(Exodus 20:2)

This is the same God who, just days earlier, had instructed the Israelites to offer something precious—a lamb (Exodus 12:3-7). In a slave economy, where every possession carried immense value, letting go of a lamb was not a small gesture; it was a profound act of sacrificial faith. Yet what they were about to receive in return was infinitely greater: redemption.

The Act of Redemption

The way for an Israelite to redeem something in the Torah was simple: You gave something in return.

If you wanted to redeem land or property, you paid its price (Leviticus 25:25).

If a poor family member sold themselves into slavery, a GOEL— a kinsman-redeemer—could buy them back, paying whatever was necessary to restore them (Leviticus 25:47-49).

Redemption always cost the Israelite something.

And there is no greater love than redeeming someone at the highest cost of all—by giving your life for them:

Greater love has no one than this: to lay down one's life for one's friends.

(John 15:13)

Indeed, in the ancient world, a great king was often also a mighty warrior. Israel knew this. Their greatest hope was for a King Messiah who would set Israel free from Roman occupation. But God’s wisdom far surpassed the logic of human warfare. The occupation He came to dismantle was far greater than Rome alone—a domination deeper than legions and banners, aimed not merely at an empire, but at the enslaving powers behind every empire.

So, Jesus was a King, but with strategies no human general could fathom. He conquered not by taking life—but by giving His own.

He staged the ultimate rescue mission in history. He came to overthrow the ancient serpent of Genesis 3. He came to drive out the ruler of this world. He came to break the chains of death and liberate humanity from its slavery to sin and fear.

Jesus: The Perfect King-Redeemer

Jesus was not merely another redeemer—one more heroic figure rescuing an individual or even a nation, as history has seen many times before. He is the perfect, final, and ultimate King-Redeemer, who came to save not a fragment of humanity but the whole world. He is the God who is truly Mighty to Save—the Lion of Judah. And yet, He is the divine warrior-King who conquers without crushing, who saves without domination, who triumphs not through force but through self-giving sacrifice.

Few have captured this paradox more vividly than C. S. Lewis in The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe. Edmund, seduced and enslaved by the White Witch—a clear satanic figure—becomes a traitor, bound by the Law of Narnia, which demands his life as payment. The Witch insists on her “right” to Edmund, not because Aslan—the Christ figure—demands blood for sin, but because evil feeds on accusation, fear, and legalistic condemnation. The demand does not originate in goodness; it originates in the logic of the Accuser.

Aslan steps forward, not to appease a wrathful God, but to rescue Edmund from the enemy’s grasp. He offers His life in Edmund’s place—not out of legal necessity, but out of love. This is substitution reframed: not a divine demand for a kill, but a deliberate act of liberation.

Here Lewis stands in sharp contrast to Penal Substitutionary Atonement. In The Chronicles of Narnia, the sacrifice is not offered to placate God, but to free the captive. It is not God who kills the Son; it is Satan who overreaches. The Father does not require death to forgive—the Accuser demands death to maintain control. And in pressing his claim, he unwittingly engineers his own defeat.

The Witch accepts the bargain, convinced she has won. But she does not understand what Lewis describes as "the deep magic" of love—the divine logic written before accusation, before violence, before death itself. Aslan rises. Death is undone. The Witch’s power collapses. Edmund is redeemed—not from God, but from the dominion of evil.

Picture it like this:

A child is lured away from safety by a stranger's lies. Despite every warning, the child follows, deceived and captured. The parent does not stop loving the child. The parent does not want to kill the child or torture them forever because they rebelled and didn't follow instructions. The parent does not leave them enslaved. No—the parent goes after them, confronts the captor, and offers themselves as a ransom to set the child free.

This is what Jesus did for us and why He is our perfect King-Redeemer.

In this way, Lewis gives narrative flesh to a profoundly biblical truth: Jesus, the Lion of Judah, does not save us from His wrathful Father, but from the grip of Satan. He conquers not by killing His enemies, but by absorbing their violence and nullifying their claims. His greatest weapon is not domination, but sacrificial love—and that love proves unstoppable.

The Ransom Motif: A Biblical Pattern of Liberation

The New Testament speaks powerfully of Jesus’ death as a ransom—a price paid to liberate captives from bondage:

For even the Son of Man did not come to be served, but to serve, and to give his life as a ransom for many.

(Mark 10:45)

For there is one God and one mediator between God and mankind, the man Christ Jesus, who gave himself as a ransom for all people. 

(1 Timothy 2:5-6)

Much like the witch-deceiver who lures Edmund in the Narnia story, humanity’s fall in Genesis 3 led to a catastrophic spiritual captivity. We exchanged the voice of God for the lie of the serpent—and in doing so, came under the dominion of Satan: the accuser, the deceiver, the one who holds the power of death (Hebrews 2:14). From that moment, the human story was marked by exile, bondage, and the desperate need for rescue.

But God, the King of redemption, did not abandon His creation:

Since the children have flesh and blood, he too shared in their humanity so that by his death he might break the power of him who holds the power of death—that is, the devil—and free those who all their lives were held in slavery by their fear of death.

(Hebrews 2:14-15)

The Cosmic Drama: Jesus vs. Satan

The cross was not only a sacrifice—it was a battlefield. It was a cosmic confrontation between the forces of darkness and our perfect King-Redeemer. Satan thought he could destroy Jesus through death—but God used Satan’s own weapons against him.

Paul describes it like this:

When you were dead in your sins... God made you alive with Christ... having disarmed the powers and authorities, he made a public spectacle of them, triumphing over them by the cross. 

(Colossians 2:13-15)

Imagine it: the cross as a divine trap. Satan, blind with pride, rage and greed, reached out to seize Jesus—not realizing that the very act of killing Him would be his own defeat:

None of the rulers of this age understood it, for if they had, they would not have crucified the Lord of glory.

(1 Corinthians 2:8)

God outmaneuvered Satan entirely. He did not answer violence with greater violence, but unveiled a superior wisdom: love. Self-sacrificial love became the ultimate strategy—the true antidote to sin and its consequence, death itself.

This is why Jesus spoke of coming to "plunder the house of the strong man" (Luke 11:21-22).

This is why John says Jesus came "to destroy the works of the devil" (1 John 3:8).

This is why Jesus proclaimed: "Now the ruler of this world will be driven out” (John 12:31).

Our King-Redeemer has defeated the ruler of this world.

c1JT

Chapter 21
Jesus Died Because He Was Our Perfect Chief Rabbi

In the world of ancient Israel, a rabbi was far more than a schoolteacher or lecturer. A rabbi was not someone who merely stood in front of a classroom with a board and notes. No—a rabbi embodied truth. A Rabbi lived the lesson. A Rabbi taught with flesh and blood.

Gurus often retreat into mountains with their disciples, far from anything that might provoke or disturb them. A rabbi, however, walks the same soil as everyone else—shouldering the same dust, breathing the same air, sharing the same struggles.

To follow a rabbi in the Jewish world was to imitate his entire way of life—not merely to memorize his teachings, but to walk in his footsteps. It meant learning how to speak as he spoke, act as he acted, and respond to the world as he responded.

This is why the Gospels portray Jesus repeatedly saying, “Follow me.” It was not an invitation to a classroom, but a summons to a radically new way of being human. It was a call to wash the feet of sinners, to forgive one’s enemies, and to extend mercy to those who do not deserve it.

The Rabbi Who Became a Servant

Rabbi Yeshua, then, is not merely the world's greatest spiritual teacher—He is the embodiment of God's wisdom lived out in real life. And while He taught using parables (Matthew 13:34), His greatest lesson was not with a sermon—but with His own life and death.

Rabbi Yeshua taught love not in theory, but in scars.

His death revealed that true love is sacrificial, but also exposed the moral bankruptcy of this world. Humanity, left to itself, crucified its only Righteous One. That is the ultimate commentary on human systems, politics, religion, and power—we murdered Love when He walked among us:

This is the verdict: Light has come into the world, but people loved darkness instead of light because their deeds were evil.

(John 3:19)

His death reflects our injustice, but it also reflects God's merciful justice—instead of retaliating with violence, Jesus answered with self-sacrifice. Instead of calling down legions of angels to avenge His blood, He forgave His enemies. Instead of condemning the world, He laid down His life to save it. What a spectacular lesson our rabbi taught us about God's love!

This is what separates Rabbi Yeshua from every other teacher in history. He did not only teach about sacrificial love—He fully incarnated it.

And this is what the early Church came to understand. Christ's death was not about God dying in some metaphysical sense (since God cannot cease to exist). Rather, it was about God demonstrating, within time and space, the highest form of love imaginable—a love willing to enter suffering, a love willing to die with us and for us.

Now We Know How to Love

We love because He first loved us.

(1 John 4:19)

Rabbi Yeshua didn’t just teach us what love is; he showed us how to love. His death became the ultimate moral lesson. A lesson designed not to satisfy divine anger—but to awaken lifeless hearts, for such an act can soften even the hardest ones.

Before Jesus, humanity understood love through instinct or self-interest. But at the cross, we learned a new definition: Love is self-giving and all-inclusive (yes, even for His enemies).

Love is the willingness to lose so another may gain. To lower oneself so another may rise. To forgo life so another may live.

This is why Jesus is different from Muhammad; he draws us not through intimidation or terror, but through love.

This is the Rabbi we follow. He doesn’t win us over by force; he wins us with his kindness.

He does not demand our obedience out of fear. He awakens our hearts by grace.

Mary Magdalene became a living example of this. Having experienced the redeeming love of her Rabbi, she devoted her life to Him—not out of guilt, not out of fear, but out of love awakened by love.

Similarly, Jesus’ disciples weren’t defined by doctrine or creed—which they knew nothing about—but by love:

By this everyone will know that you are my disciples, if you love one another.

(John 13:35)

This understanding of the cross tells us that Jesus' death was meant not to appease divine rage but to spark a moral awakening—to shatter our selfishness, to melt our hardened hearts, to provoke us to turn from darkness to light. Only love has this power.

Love heals wounds no argument can heal.

Love restores relationships no punishment can restore.

Love reconciles enemies no sword can reconcile.

Yeshua is not only the Lord of lords but also the Rabbi of rabbis. He died to teach God’s love in a way that would never fade into a distant memory of history, but remain a vivid, unforgettable demonstration—an everlasting reminder—of just how far God's love will go to redeem and restore His creation.

This is why He died. So that we, watching Love lay down its life, would be forever changed—and then go and do likewise—lay down our lives for those we love, and even for the sake of those who do not love us back.

c1M4

Chapter 22
Jesus Died Because He Was Our Perfect Scapegoat

Human beings are imitators by nature. We do not desire in isolation; we learn what to desire by watching others. When everyone in a community reaches for the same things—status, power, approval—desire intensifies into rivalry. Rivalry becomes conflict, and conflict threatens to tear a society apart. When communal tension reaches its breaking point, history shows us what fearful humans instinctively do: they turn to a scapegoat. A single individual or group—often the Jews—absorbs the blame, the anxiety, the rage of the community. Violence is redirected onto the chosen victim, and—at least temporarily—the community feels appeased, cleansed, relieved, restored. The feeling of peace is real, but the mechanism is dark.

Throughout history—in tribes, in nations, in religions—societies have found temporary peace by placing collective blame on a single individual or group. From the early Christian persecutions in the Roman Empire to the brutal targeting of Jewish communities during the Black Death, the hysteria-driven trials of the Salem “witches” in the 1690s, and even the conviction of Sacco and Vanzetti in 1920s Boston. This "scapegoat" is rarely guilty of the crime. Their guilt is constructed. Their “otherness” is magnified. Their removal or death becomes the false solution to the community's crisis. A collective catharsis.

The violence poured out on them is not justice—it is survival disguised as justice.

We can see an aspect of this pattern in the Yom Kippur scapegoat, not as divine endorsement of violence, but as a sacred exposure of a human impulse. The scapegoat did not appease God's wrath; it symbolically carried the accumulated sins, failures, and communal burdens away from the people, into the wilderness, where they no longer defined the community. In doing so, the ritual acknowledged a deep truth about human psychology: our tendency to externalize guilt and seek relief by transferring blame onto another. The biblical ritual restrains that instinct, ritualizes it, and empties it of bloodshed. The scapegoat reveals how easily humans reduce redemption to removal, justice to expulsion, and righteousness to mere relief—an error later generations repeated by transforming symbolic, corrective rituals into literal systems of punishment and everlasting blame.

The Cross that Exposes Our Violence

Jesus is the ultimate scapegoat. He is the innocent One. The blameless One. The One who embodied truth, love, and purity—and yet was hated, rejected, accused, tortured, and executed.

Why? Because His very presence revealed the brokenness of human systems.

When Jesus stood before the religious leaders, before Pilate, before the angry mob, it was not divine justice that demanded His death—it was human rage, envy, fear, and hate.

The religious leaders coveted His influence. The political powers feared His kingship. The crowd, whipped into a mimetic frenzy, cried out for blood: “Crucify Him!” (Mark 15:13)

But unlike all previous scapegoats in history—whose death maintained the illusion of justice—Jesus shattered the mechanism forever. Because God raised Him from the dead.

The resurrection was not only the defeat of death—it was the public vindication of the Victim.

It declared once and for all: This Man was innocent. This violence was unjust. This system is corrupt.

In this view, the power of the cross lies here—in exposing humanity's darkest habit: the need to blame, exclude, and destroy those we fear or envy, even if they are good and righteous.

The cross is both salvation and indictment. It exposes how far we are willing to go to defend our illusions of righteousness and our distorted sense of justice—especially when those impulses are baptized in religious language. When unnecessary violence is justified in the name of divine law, what is being served is not God’s justice but humanity’s desire to sanctify murder by dressing it in holiness.63 And yet, the cross also saves us. It absorbs our violence without returning it, receives our hatred without retaliation. This is the depth of God’s love for us—a love that confronts us truthfully without abandoning us to despair.

The cross and resurrection together form the loudest call to a new kind of humanity. A life free from rivalry. A life free from violence. A life rooted in forgiveness, compassion, and reconciliation.

Here, Jesus did not die to satisfy God's wrath and anger. He died to expose humanity's.

He died as the perfect Scapegoat—willingly (John 10:17–18) entering into our violence, bearing it, and then rising to offer life instead of revenge.

He died to teach us that the way forward is not through exclusion, but embrace. Not through retaliation, but reconciliation.

This is the kingdom Jesus inaugurated. This is the path He walked. And this is the way He calls us to follow. To stop participating in the most ancient game of blame (Genesis 3:12-13). To stop scapegoating others to secure our fragile sense of worth. To become, like Him, people of mercy—people of forgiveness. Individuals who embody radical love, even in the face of injustice. Jesus not only preached this, but He also exemplified it at the very peak of His unjust suffering (Luke 23:34).

Golgotha—Human Corruption Meets Divine Love

Golgotha stands as the summit of humanity’s darkest crimes—pride, rivalry, vengeance, scapegoating, and the lust for power and domination—all laid bare and brought under judgment.

It was the moment when human civilization, with all its claims of order and justice, was unmasked for what it truly is: a power struggle maintained by violence so corrupt that it did not hesitate to crucify the Son of God Himself in the name of “truth,” "justice," and “righteousness.”

Yet Golgotha is not only the site of humanity’s greatest sin—it is also the place where we encounter the greatest revelation of divine grace. There, in the suffering of Christ, we see sacrificial love in its purest and most powerful form: a love that absorbs violence without returning it, that forgives while being pierced, and that conquers not by killing, but by dying.

Thus, to gaze at the cross is to see more than wood and nails. It is to see every twisted impulse of the human heart exposed—and every wound answered by grace.

He took the lies we believed, carried the shame we bear, and absorbed the judgment meant for His accusers. But that’s not where it ends. The cross isn’t just a mirror—it’s a window. Through it, we see God as He truly is: not proud or vengeful, but forgiving and full of self-giving love. A God who walks through our poison to bring healing. A God who takes the very image of the curse and turns it into salvation.

And so the pattern breaks: the Victim rises. Other societies silenced their fears by casting blame on the innocent, but Jesus unmasks the lie and rewrites the script.

The cross shows us who we are; the resurrection shows us who God is.

In Him, the scapegoat does not stay dead—He becomes the cornerstone of a new humanity—a Kingdom no longer built on rivalry, fear, and violence, but on forgiveness, mercy, and reconciling love. This is the path He opens. This is the life He calls us to step into.

c1N8

Chapter 23
Jesus Died Because He Was Our Perfect High Priest

While Scripture names only one person—Jesus—as a mediator, Moses, Israel, and the Church were all called to function in a priestly role (Exodus 19:6; 1 Peter 2:9). In ancient Israel, the priest stood at the intersection between heaven and earth. A priest was not simply a religious official performing rituals—a priest was a representative. The priest bore the name, identity, and hopes of Israel before the living God. He was Israel’s bridge.

But Israel’s priests were only shadows—limited, temporary, flawed. No priest could truly represent Israel perfectly before God. Their sacrifices had to be repeated. Their own sins had to be atoned for before they could intercede for others.

This is why the apostle John, in his heavenly vision, broke down in tears when he saw the scroll of God’s ultimate purposes sealed shut—because no one in heaven or on earth was found worthy to open it (Revelation 5:1-4). Creation longed for a priest—a true mediator—but none could be found.

Until Jesus:

See, the Lion of the tribe of Judah, the Root of David, has triumphed. He is able to open the scroll and its seven seals!

(Revelation 5:5)

Jesus is the Great High Priest that Israel—and the world—had been waiting for. But He was not a priest like the others.

Israel’s priests entered the Holy of Holies with the blood of bulls and goats. But Jesus entered the true heavenly sanctuary with His own blood—His own life—offered willingly on behalf of the world:

But when Christ came as high priest... he entered the Most Holy Place once for all by his own blood, thus obtaining eternal redemption.

(Hebrews 9:11-12)

Here, "eternal" does not refer to time in the later Greek-philosophical sense—it refers to quality rather than duration.

When Hebrews' mysterious author64 wrote that Christ “obtained eternal redemption,” he is not saying Jesus' blood lasts longer than time. Rather, he is saying that Christ’s blood is of a different order—a heavenly, incorruptible, divine kind. Thus eternal, not by quantity but by quality.

The Perfect Priest, The Perfect Representative

To be a priest is to represent your people (Exodus 28:1, Leviticus 9:22, and Numbers 6:22–27). But how could Jesus represent us—the human race—unless He became one of us Himself?

This is why the New Testament emphasizes that Jesus lived the full human story. He did not skip childhood. He did not bypass hunger, exhaustion, or loneliness. He even died. Christ entered fully into the human experience—but unlike Adam, who can be seen as humanity's first priest, Christ responded with perfect obedience.

Christ’s obedience was infinitely more demanding. Adam fell in a paradisiacal garden surrounded by beauty, with only one restriction to keep. Jesus, by contrast, obeyed in the garden of Gethsemane—a garden not of delight but of anguish. In that wilderness of suffering, under the crushing weight of the Law, abandoned by friends, surrounded by enemies, and staring straight into the cup of death, He did not flinch.

Where Adam failed in ease, Jesus was faithful in agony.

Adam listened to the voice of the serpent. Jesus crushed the serpent’s head by listening to the voice of His Father.

Christ as Our Patriarch

In ancient Israel, the patriarch—in a sense the family’s priest—carried the destiny of his entire household. His choices shaped the fate of everyone under his authority. If he sinned, the family suffered the consequences; if he triumphed, the family shared in the blessing. This is why we read that Achan’s household faced judgment alongside him (Joshua 7:1–26), and why Korah and his household were swallowed up together (Numbers 16:1–35). Their stories reflect an ancient communal worldview: the head represents the body, and the body participates in the outcome of its representative.

When the first priest—Adam—sinned, humanity inherited death.

When the perfect priest—Christ—obeyed, humanity inherited life.

This is why Paul calls Jesus the “Second Adam”—the New Patriarch-Priest of a New Humanity.

And since Adam’s decision brought death upon humanity. Christ’s faithfulness brought life:

For since death came through a man, the resurrection of the dead comes also through a man. For as in Adam all die, so in Christ all will be made alive.

(1 Corinthians 15:21-22)

Just as David fought Goliath not merely for himself but on behalf of all Israel—so Jesus fought against sin, death, and Satan on behalf of humanity.

This is not merely symbolic. It is covenantal. This is why Scripture says:

Just as sin entered the world through one man, and death through sin, and in this way death came to all people...For just as through the disobedience of the one man the many were made sinners, so also through the obedience of the one man the many will be made righteous. 

(Romans 5:12, 19)

Our Living Priest Intercedes for Us

Jesus’ priesthood didn’t end at the cross. His life on earth was the offering, and upon ascending, He continues His ongoing priesthood. Right now, at this very moment, Jesus stands at the right hand of God, representing us, interceding for us, advocating for us as our perfect High Priest:

Christ Jesus who died—more than that, who was raised to life—is at the right hand of God and is also interceding for us.

(Romans 8:34)

Jesus’ intercession does not mean He is persuading a reluctant Father to forgive us—as if God were still angry after the cross. Rather, to intercede means to represent, advocate, and continually mediate God’s grace to humanity—God is on our side (1 John 2:1, Hebrews 7:25)! His work on the cross accomplished forgiveness once and for all, but His ongoing intercession means that the risen Christ remains our living High Priest, constantly bringing humanity into reconciliation with God. It is not about changing God’s mind, but about applying and sustaining the healing and restoration that the cross already accomplished.

No earthly priest could do this. No Levitical sacrifice could achieve this. Only Jesus—our High Priest—could stand before God and represent humanity with nail-scarred hands.

This is the beauty of priestly atonement in the Hebrew Scriptures. Not wrath-appeasement. Not divine sadism. But covenant representation. Sacrificial love.

This is why He died. This is why He lives. And this is why we can approach the throne of God with confidence. He is our Perfect Priest. Forever.

Therefore, brothers and sisters, since we have confidence to enter the Most Holy Place by the blood of Jesus... let us draw near to God with a sincere heart and with the full assurance that faith brings. 

(Hebrews 10:19, 22)

We are redeemed because He was faithful, not because we are.

c1PZ

Chapter 24
Jesus Died Because He Was Our Perfect Life-Giving Healer

If God could die—truly die—He would cease to be God. This is a fundamental truth that sits at the very heart of biblical theology. God is, by definition, life itself. God is uncreated, unkillable, eternal, everlasting, and overflowing with being. Mortality belongs to creation, not to the Creator.

This is why when we speak of Jesus’ death, we are not talking about God ceasing to exist—but about God, in Christ, entering into human death so that He might conquer it from within—like a cure, a secret potion, a divine antidote to a deadly illness. For humanity to overcome death, we needed something greater than fragile human life—we needed divine, eternal life.

"O Death, Where Is Your Victory?"

All of us are subject to the curse of death. Even those whom Christ graciously resurrected—like Lazarus—died again. Their life was restored temporarily. It was a gift—but it was not yet victory over death. Why? Because death can always reclaim what is merely human. But death has no claim over what is divine.

This is why Jesus is not merely another prophet. Not merely another martyr. Not merely another holy man who died for a good cause. Jesus is the life-giving Healer because He carries within Himself the life of God—eternal, incorruptible, deathless life. This is the logic underlying Jesus's mystical statement: "For the bread of God is the bread that comes down from heaven and gives life to the world" (John 6:33), and why he later declared, "I am the living bread that came down from heaven" (John 6:51).

Jesus is not only the one who teaches the way to life—He is the source of life. Jesus feeds us with His own divine life. To eat of His flesh and drink of His blood is to mystically participate in the life of God Himself—a life stronger than death.

If Jesus were merely human, then His death would have been heroic, tragic, but powerless. Like every righteous sufferer before Him, He might have been vindicated, remembered, or even resurrected temporarily—but His blood would not have the power to give life.

But Jesus is the divinity who "made himself nothing by taking the very nature of a servant, being made in human likeness" (Philippians 2:7). Thus, death could not hold Him because death has no true power over divinity. This is why Jesus could say with unshakable confidence:

I give them eternal life, and they shall never perish; no one will snatch them out of my hand.

(John 10:28)

Notice: not, “I will show them eternal life.” Not, “I will teach them about eternal life.” But simply, “I give them eternal life.”

And Jesus wasn’t thinking of just a select few. Later, He explains:

And I, when I am lifted up from the earth, will draw all people to myself.

(John 12:32)

This is why the entire New Testament—from the Gospels to the letters of Paul to the book of Revelation—insists that eternal life—the kind that resurrects—is not found in a holy place, religious system, or even a set of creeds and doctrines. It is found in the Creator revealed to us through Christ. He’s not just our priest, He’s the one who gives us life.

Life is relational. Life is connection. Life flows from God—through Christ—to us.

Just as a branch withers and dies when separated from the vine, so humanity was withering under the curse of exile and death because it was cut off from the Tree of Life.

Jesus came to graft humanity back in; to reconnect us to the very life of God, and to heal us with eternal life.

Jesus Did Not Die to Suffer Divine Wrath—He Died to Pour Out His Life

For I am the LORD who heals you.

(Exodus 15:26)

His death was not a defeat. His death was a doorway. He entered death willingly—so that He could destroy it from within. Christ can be imagined as a divine Physician who enters a quarantined city ravaged by plague—not to be overcome by the disease, but to cure it with His own incorruptible life. The inhabitants, already transformed into something less than human, fail to recognize the Healer and kill Him instead. Yet His blood—saturated with the antidote—spills into the streets, spreads through the city, and reverses the infection of death itself, restoring life where decay once ruled.

Jesus, as the life-giving healer, absorbed the full weight of human death—and then reversed it.

If “the wages of sin is death” (Romans 6:23), then death itself is the visible consequence and power of sin. That’s why Paul can later say, “The last enemy to be destroyed is death” (1 Corinthians 15:26). Once death is gone—out of the equation—the very wage of sin is gone. Meaning sin/death has been fully overcome. In other words, God’s final victory isn’t just forgiving sins—it’s erasing their ultimate consequence: death itself!

This is why Paul can declare in triumph:

Death has been swallowed up in victory! Where, O death, is your victory? Where, O death, is your sting?

(1 Corinthians 15:54-55)

If eternal life has been flowing into the world since and through the life of Christ our Healer, then death has truly lost its power.

But if eternal life is reduced to an escape plan—an afterlife destination earned through intellectual assent or moral performance—then death was never truly defeated, nor was it the final enemy at all.

You Already Have Eternal Life

Throughout his gospel, John repeatedly emphasizes that the believer already “has eternal life” (e.g., John 6:47; 5:24). And in his letter:

I write these things to you who believe in the name of the Son of God so that you may know that you have eternal life.

(1 John 5:13)

Notice the verb tense: has/have, not "will have someday after death" and not "will receive only at the resurrection." If “eternal life” were primarily about the quantity of days—about unending time—then believers wouldn’t die at all. But they do.

This reveals something crucial: eternal life is first and foremost about quality, not duration. It is a kind of life—God’s life—breaking into the present. A life one can begin to experience now through union with Christ, long before the resurrection completes it.

John isn’t merely promising immortality later; he is declaring a transformed life now—a participation in God’s own eternal reality that reshapes how we live, love, and understand the world even in this moment.

We do not follow a dead teacher. We do not follow a fallen martyr. We follow the living Lord—the Great Healer—whose life now flows in us like living water.

I have come that they may have life, and have it abundantly. 

(John 10:10)

We needed eternal, divine life. And through Jesus, we have it. Not life after death only—but life stronger than death. Eternal life is a vivid, divine, and powerful life that begins now.

Jesus’s death was far greater than handing out “get to heaven” tickets to those who recite a specific prayer or subscribe to the right doctrinal formula. He came to redeem the entire human story. He came to shed His life-giving blood into the world. His mission was to restore creation, heal what was broken, and reconcile all things to God (Colossians 1:20). The cross wasn’t the end of a transaction—it was a life transfusion that began a cosmic transformation.

Final Conclusion—Why Jesus Died

As our King-Redeemer, He didn’t seize power; He surrendered it. He ransomed us not with armies or threats, but with His own life, breaking the grip of evil through self-giving love. As our chief Rabbi, He didn’t just instruct us with words, but taught us with wounds. His death was His greatest sermon—a living parable of divine sacrificial love that melts hardened hearts and calls us to live by that same love. As the ultimate Scapegoat, Jesus absorbed humanity’s blame and exposed our systems of violence for what they truly are: unjust, broken, and in need of healing. By bearing our rejection and rising again, He didn’t validate our wrath—He dismantled it.

He is also the High Priest who didn’t just stand in for us—He stepped into our full humanity, carried it, and redeemed it. In Him, the barrier between heaven and earth was torn, and we now stand welcomed. But even more than that, He is the divine Healer who didn’t avoid death—He walked straight into it to destroy it from the inside out. He gave us not just forgiveness, but eternal life—His life—a life stronger than death, pulsing now through all who believe.

Jesus didn’t die to satisfy vengeance. He died to infuse the dying world with indestructible life. And because of that, the world is not just saved—it is slowly made new.

In the chapters ahead, we’ll dive into the question—what are we really saved from, and what does salvation actually mean.

c1SV

Part IV: Understanding Salvation

c1T2

Introduction: Salvation—Not What You've Been Taught

Every Christmas, Christians across the world gather to worship, singing songs of deliverance and celebrating a Savior who came to save the world. Yet few ever stop to ask the most basic and unsettling questions: Save what? Save from what? Save whom? Save how? And save for what purpose?

Soteriology—salvation—is among the most debated and most misunderstood doctrines in modern Christianity, both in what it claims to mean and in the questions it dares—or refuses—to ask.

In this section of the book, we will not approach salvation through the inherited lenses of evangelical revivalism, Baptist preaching, Calvinist systems, or Catholic scholasticism. Instead, we will return to the original scene—to the soil, language, and worldview in which the gospel was first proclaimed. We must set aside medieval categories and Western philosophical abstractions and listen again to the heartbeat of the biblical story itself. But before we can do that, we must first understand how we arrived at our current assumptions—and how far they have carried us from the original vision.

c1TC

Chapter 25
When Greek Dualism Hijacked the Christian Faith

As Christianity moved westward, it absorbed the dualisms of Greek philosophy and Gnostic speculation. In these systems, the world is a battleground—spirit versus matter, light versus darkness, soul versus body. The material world is often viewed with suspicion, even contempt. The ultimate goal becomes escape—“going to heaven” and leaving the “evil” world behind to burst in flames.

This dualism finds echoes in modern Christian debates, especially where salvation is framed as individual and intellectual, and the world is seen as disposable. The Greek mind, focused on individualism, knowledge (gnosis), intellectual truth and escapism, replaced the biblical and Jewish emphasis on relationship, action, and community restoration.

When Christians adopt the belief that matter is worthless, corrupt, or ultimately doomed, the consequences are devastating. The world becomes disposable (despite our calling in Genesis 1:28). Care for creation is sidelined. The body is neglected. The poor and the suffering are reduced to “spiritual problems” to be explained away, rather than brothers and sisters to be loved, fed, and clothed.

But Jesus—and the ancient Jewish tradition He stood within—insist on the opposite: the world is worth saving (John 3:17). Judaism called this tikkun olam—the restoration of the world. This vision shifts the gospel away from the Greek fantasy of the soul escaping the body and toward the biblical hope of bodily resurrection. It declares that the physical world matters, that it is redeemable, and therefore worth loving. Every act of kindness, every work of justice, every moment of beauty becomes a place where God’s salvation can break into the present.

Ironically, the resurrection is the most anti-dualistic doctrine in all of Scripture. It proclaims that we are not souls trapped in bodies waiting to escape, but embodied beings—soul and body inseparably woven together as a homogenized whole. You cannot fully live without your body, because God never created you as a fragment. From the beginning, you were meant to be whole.

This is why, in the earliest proclamation of the gospel, salvation did not mean fleeing the physical world. It meant redeeming it.

We Are Not Ghosts Seeking Escape from the Evil Body

The Gnostic distinction between body and soul is not supported by the Bible. According to biblical teachings, the dead do not ascend to a different place known as "heaven" or "hell." Instead, they are merely dead, or, using biblical terminology, "asleep."

Jesus, speaking about Lazarus, said, "Our friend Lazarus has fallen asleep; but I am going there to wake him up." (John 11:11). Jesus did not ascend to heaven (or hell) to pull Lazarus back to life in our world. He simply raised him—saved him from death.

Likewise, when Paul wrote to encourage the Thessalonians, he wrote: "But we do not want you to be uninformed, brothers and sisters, about those who have fallen asleep...through Jesus, God will bring with him those who have fallen asleep." (1 Thessalonians 4:13-14).

The dead are not alive somewhere else. They are simply asleep.

But Didn't Jesus Say the Criminal Would Be in Paradise ‘Today’? (Luke 23:43)

No, Jesus did not say the criminal would be in Paradise with him “Today.” People often point to Luke 23:43, where Jesus speaks to the repentant criminal on the cross. Some English translations suggest that Jesus told the man, “Today you will be with me in paradise.” However, a closer examination of the Greek text and other biblical passages suggests a different reading of Luke 23:43.

Ancient Greek manuscripts had no punctuation—no commas or periods. This means that the placement of the comma in some English translations is a matter of interpretation rather than direct textual evidence.

Other translations of Luke 23:43 support an alternative reading, a few examples below:

“Verily I say unto thee this day: With me shalt thou be in Paradise.”65

“Verily, to you am I saying today, with Me shall you be in paradise.”66

“I tell you this today; you will be with me in Paradise.”67

“Verily, to thee I say this day, with Me shalt thou be in the Paradise.” 68

These renderings show that the phrase “this day” could be referring to when Jesus was making the statement, rather than when the event of going to paradise would occur.

In Greek, adverbs like “today” (sémeron) typically modify the verb that they are closest to. In Luke 23:43, “today” is placed immediately after “I say to you,” making it natural for it to modify “I say” rather than “you will be with me in paradise.”

“I Tell You This Today”?

The biblical phrase “this day” often functions as an emphasis marker rather than a reference to a literal 24-hour period. A more natural modern rendering could be:

“Today I tell you,”

“I assure you today,”

"Today, I make you this promise,"

“Mark my words today.”

Jesus was a Jew, and in His Bible, “this day” or “today” often serves to highlight the urgency and certainty of a statement. For example:

See, I have set before you today life and good, death and evil. 

(Deuteronomy 30:15)

Or,

I testify against you this day that you shall surely perish.

(Deuteronomy 8:19)

Thus, in Luke 23:43, when Jesus tells the criminal, “I tell you today, you will be with me in paradise,” He is emphasizing the certainty of His promise, not the timing of when it will be fulfilled.

Jesus Didn't Go to Paradise That Day

If Jesus was telling the criminal that both of them would meet in paradise later that day, it contradicts other clear biblical teachings:

1. Jesus Was Not in Paradise But in the Grave for Three Days

Matthew 12:40: “…so will the Son of Man be three days and three nights in the heart of the earth.”

Jesus did not go directly to heaven but was in the grave for three days. This aligns with the Apostles’ Creed, which states: “He descended to the underworld.”

2. First Resurrected, Then Descended

1 Peter 3:19: “After being made alive, he went and made proclamation to the imprisoned spirits.”

This passage indicates that Jesus descended into the realm of the dead, not ascended to heaven.

3. Jesus Had Not Yet Ascended After His Resurrection

John 20:17: After His resurrection, Jesus tells Mary Magdalene, “Do not cling to me, for I have not yet ascended to the Father.”

If Jesus had already visited heaven on the day of His crucifixion, He would not have said this.

Under Construction

Paradise wasn’t ready yet: Jesus said, “I go and prepare a place for you… I will come back and take you to be with me that you also may be where I am” (John 14:3). However you interpret this, one thing is clear: the place isn’t ready yet—which means it wasn’t yet prepared for the criminal either.

In summary, Jesus was making a promise, not a time statement. Rather than saying the criminal would be in paradise that very day, Jesus was making a solemn declaration: “I tell you today, you will be with me in paradise.” This promise assured the criminal that he would ultimately be with Jesus, but not necessarily on that exact day.

Conclusion

In the end, the gospel does not point us away from the world but deeper into it. It does not teach us to despise the body, abandon the earth, or spiritualize suffering into abstraction. It announces that God has chosen to heal what is broken, to raise what has fallen asleep, and to restore what has been wounded—flesh and soil, memory and hope. Resurrection is not an escape plan but a promise: that nothing God has made is disposable, that love enacted here and now matters eternally; and that the world, though scarred, is still the place where God insists on dwelling, redeeming, and making all things whole again.

Lazarus of Bethany (John 11:38–44), Jairus’ daughter (Mark 5:21–43), the widow of Nain’s son (Luke 7:11–17), Tabitha (Acts 9:36–42), Eutychus (Acts 20:7–12), and many saints in Jerusalem (Matthew 27:52–53) weren’t spirits brought back down from heaven, but were truly raised from the dead—"awakened" from being "asleep"—in their own bodies. Likewise, we too will awaken with new bodies (1 Corinthians 15:51–52, 2 Corinthians 5:1–2, Philippians 3:20–21, Romans 8:23).

The Gospel is not about escapism of the spirit, but about restoring both body and soul.

c1VA

Chapter 26
Western Salvation: How the Gospel Was Reduced to a Doctrinal Quiz

Clearly, the popular pulpit slogan that salvation is “a free gift of grace” does not hold up under the weight of Western theology. At best, it works only on the surface level.

If salvation ultimately depends on our understanding—on our finite minds and limited perception—then it becomes contingent on the correctness of our intellectual grasp. But what exactly must that understanding include? Is it simply professing belief in a historical figure named Jesus, a Jewish rabbi who lived in Israel 2,000 years ago, who was the Son of God that was sacrificed for our sins? If so, then what are we to make of the 49,000 Christian groups, movements, and sub-denominations that all profess this in some form? Are they all "saved"? Or is there some additional knowledge required—some doctrinal precision like the Trinity—that determines salvation?

For instance, Catholics and Protestants both profess faith in Christ and His divinity, in His sacrificial atonement, in the Trinity, and more—yet for centuries they have condemned one another to hellfire over various other doctrines. So, where does this intellectual mind game end? Must salvation really hinge on subscribing to a precise set of doctrinal formulas?

How well-developed and precise must your Christology, Hamartiology, Bibliology, Soteriology, Eschatology, Pneumatology, and Ecclesiology be to be "saved"? And if humanity is really as foolish and dumb as it sometimes appears to be, then why would a God—who created us with very limited wisdom and restricted understanding—base His willingness to spare us from His cosmic incinerator on passing such a confusing intellectual test? Would a loving Creator really design a system where escaping Eternal Conscious Torment hinges on our ability to comprehend complex theology? It makes no sense. It would be a sick game and a setup for failure, not an act of grace.

If salvation depends on our intellectual understanding, then we’re left with confusion, fragmentation, and endless debate. It becomes chaotic, uncertain, and ultimately doomed to fail. That’s why 49,000 Christian groups vehemently argue over every single aspect of faith. And it's why salvation can’t depend on us. It must be up to God. All of it. Salvation is His work, not ours. As Scripture declares, He—not we—is Mighty to Save (Isaiah 63:1; Zephaniah 3:17).

God is Greater Than Systematic Theology

Imagine a father buying his son a birthday gift—even after the son has rebelled, offended him, and broken his trust. If the father gives the gift anyway, not because the son earned it but because love compels him, that is genuine, unmerited grace.

But if the father makes the gift conditional—“I’ll give it to you only if you do X, believe Y, or apologize first”—then it is no longer a free gift. It becomes something to be earned, a transaction rather than grace.

So it is with God. If His salvation—whatever that word means—depends on human understanding, virtue, or performance, it is no longer a gift but a reward. True grace—unconditional, inclusive, universal, and flowing from the very heart of God—does not wait for worthiness; it creates it.

It is simple logic: a gift ceases to be free the moment it must be earned.

If salvation rests on passing a theological exam in intellectual correctness, then how is that grace? No one can ever be secure. Fear becomes the engine of faith: fear of being wrong, fear of missing one obscure truth, fear of belonging to the wrong denomination. And history has shown what that produces—endless divisions, schisms, accusations, and even bloodshed. In the end, salvation by “right doctrine” is not good news at all, but a life driven by suspicion, fear, and despair.

Take, for instance, the great Catholic-Protestant conflicts in Europe; historians estimate that as many as 15 million people died.69 And over what? Salvation theories and theology. How ironic! And if that is the bitter harvest of Western theology, then surely some serious questions must be asked.

Good News for All?

If nearly all of humanity—except for a small few—were to be tortured forever in hell, calling that “good news” would be morally absurd.

If we discovered that an extinction-level meteor was on its way and would incinerate 95% of the human race, no sane person would frame that as hope. It would be a catastrophe. To celebrate it as God's goodness because you happened to survive would be morally grotesque.

The "Good news that will cause great joy for all the people" (Luke 2:10), by definition, must be good for all.

But if 95% of humanity is doomed to Eternal Conscious Torment, and you happen to be among the elect and get to escape it, calling that reality “good news” and celebrating your own rescue does not make you faithful—it makes you narcissistic.

The God Who Is Truly Mighty to Save

The LORD your God is...the Mighty One who saves.

(Zephaniah 3:17)

If salvation does not rest on our intellectual precision or theological understanding, then it rests on God alone—and that changes everything. It means we can finally stop striving, stop proving, stop fearing that we might think or believe the wrong thing. We can rest. Peace becomes possible, not because we understand perfectly, but because God is faithful, patient, and good—and our lives are held, not by our certainty, but by His love.

This is not the god of MacArthur and Edwards who "holds you over the Pit of Hell, much as one holds a Spider, or some loathsome Insect, over the Fire, abhors you, and is dreadfully provoked."

It is the loving Father of Jesus Christ, who runs toward the sinner while he is still far off, who interrupts confession with embrace, who restores before explanation, and who celebrates return rather than threatening exile—a Father whose justice is mercy in motion, whose holiness is love unashamed, and whose arms are already open long before repentance finds its words.

The Father Who Understands

God’s knowledge and understanding are far beyond our own, surpassing the limits of human reasoning. To suggest that our salvation hinges on our intellectual grasp of doctrines is not only unrealistic—it’s morally troubling. The gospel is not a test of doctrine that most of humanity is destined to fail, but a free gift of grace. If redemption were based on human understanding, which is not only severely flawed, limited, and lacking but often also shaped by trauma, indoctrination, culture, and other external factors, it would imply that we are the ones saving ourselves. But that is the opposite of grace.

Yes, some people actively reject God—but the real question is whether they are rejecting Jesus Himself or a distorted caricature of Him, filtered through two thousand years of theological overlays. From my experience, it’s pretty much always the latter. People are not turning away from Christ; they are recoiling from a version of God they cannot recognize, cannot trust, or cannot love—a fractured, contradictory, and often abhorrent portrayal, such as that found in the theology of Jonathan Edwards, which bears little resemblance to the face of God revealed in Jesus.

I’ve personally debated Jesus with thousands of religious and secular Jews alike. And here’s the conclusion I’ve come to: even the most aggressive atheists aren’t truly attacking God; they’re attacking a distorted version of a hateful God. Likewise, even the most zealous religious Jews aren’t actually rejecting Jesus—they’re rejecting a paganized and often antisemitic portrayal of Him.

In both cases, people are responding not to the God of Scripture or the Jewish Messiah, but to a false image—usually the caricature handed to them by the fundamentalist voices I’ve highlighted throughout this book.

What they reject is not the God of Scripture, but the God invented by those who speak the loudest and love the least.

They are not pushing back against the Creator of heaven and earth, or the Father revealed in Jesus the Messiah—they’re pushing back against a twisted portrayal shaped by fear, shame, manipulation, and bad theology.

And honestly? I don’t blame them. If the “god” they were shown were truly like that, I would reject him, too.

What Would Jesus' Father Do?

So what does the real God do with that? Does He look at a wounded human being—someone rejecting a cruel caricature of Him—and say, “You should have loved that idol anyway”? Or does He see the pain, the confusion, the manipulation that distorted their vision of Him, and judge them according to the light they actually had—saving them despite their inability to recognize Him clearly?

The God of Scripture is not careless with context. He knows the difference between rebellion against truth and resistance to abuse done in His name. Like the good judge that He is, He weighs intention, formation, trauma, and honesty of heart. He knows when someone is only turning away from a distorted mask that others forced onto His face.

Which brings the question into sharp focus: would a God who is perfectly just and perfectly loving hold people accountable for a limited, distorted understanding of Him? Or would it be far more cruel—far more unlike the God of Abraham and the Father revealed in Jesus—to condemn people for refusing to worship a false god who was never truly Him at all?

I was once in touch with a Hasidic Jew who came to faith in Jesus and was then discipled by Calvinists in the Israeli Messianic movement. They told him that God had predestined him to believe and escape hell. “So that means God also predestined my parents, grandparents, and sisters to eternity in hellfire?” he asked. Their response devastated him. Unable to reconcile such a view of God, he left the faith entirely and returned to Judaism.

No human turns away from loving-kindness unless something deeply wrong, like trauma, indoctrination or bad theology, has warped their perception of it. Our human understanding is limited because we see only dimly. But one day, all will stand before God, see Him clearly, understand Him fully, and will no longer have a reason to reject His grace.

Conclusion

Salvation can only be a one-sided act of God—unearned, unprovoked, and untouched by human contribution. It does not rest on even the smallest percentage of us, on our limited understanding or fractured abilities, but wholly and completely on Him alone.

c1WU

Chapter 27
“Salvation” in the Hebrew Bible and New Testament

When read through biblical eyes, salvation is not about escaping a cosmic furnace or avoiding unending torment in God’s so-called torture chamber. Nor is it a ticket out of this world in some mystical sense. In the biblical imagination, salvation is deeply practical and profoundly holistic.

Jesus’ resurrection sets the pattern—His body isn’t discarded but transformed and glorified. The ultimate hope is not to leave earth for heaven, but for heaven and earth to embrace once more (Revelation 21:1-5).

God’s salvation isn’t about burning the world to ashes but freeing it from corruption and restoring it to its fullest potential. God doesn't throw away what’s broken. He heals, restores, and makes all things whole again. Salvation is not about relocation—it’s about renewal.

Salvation: Holistic Restoration, Not Soul Escape

Before we can fully grasp how Jesus used the term “salvation,” we first need to consider how the Bible—especially the Hebrew Scriptures, which were the only sacred texts available to believers in His time—uses the word “salvation.” Spoiler alert: it’s not about your soul going to heaven when your body dies. It’s not about escaping hellfire, either. In the Bible’s original context, salvation meant something far more grounded, more communal, and more urgent—deliverance from danger, restoration from exile, winning a battle, healing sickness, and the return to God’s presence and blessing.

Take the Exodus story—the most foundational salvation story in the Jewish imagination. Israel wasn’t saved from an afterlife incinerator. They were saved from slavery. From oppression. From Pharaoh’s chokehold:

That day the LORD saved Israel from the hands of the Egyptians…

(Exodus 14:30)

God didn’t save them by snatching them into heaven. He came down into their suffering and led them out. That’s salvation. Not an escape from the world, but liberation. Exodus. Homecoming.

The Hebrew word for salvation—yeshuah—is deeply connected to this theme. It carries the meaning of deliverance, rescue, healing, and restoration. And if the Hebrew word sounds familiar, that’s because it shares the same root as the name Yeshua—Jesus.

Every time someone in the Hebrew Bible cries out for salvation, they’re asking God to intervene in real life. To heal, to protect, to redeem. It’s never about a cosmic courtroom or an abstract afterlife. It’s always relational, communal, and earthy.

For example, in Deuteronomy 33:29, Moses says, “Blessed are you, Israel! Who is like you, a people saved by the Lord?” Here, salvation is about national identity. God saved the people from Egypt, led them through the wilderness, and promised them a land, a future, and His presence. Salvation was the ongoing story of God’s faithfulness to His people, not a moment of personal belief or a private spiritual contract.

Similarly, Psalm 34 says, “The Lord is close to the brokenhearted and saves those who are crushed in spirit.” That’s not talking about the afterlife. That’s talking about struggling right here, right now.

Psalm 18 likewise opens with a cry for salvation from enemies and a declaration, "I have been saved!" (Psalm 18:3). King David was not speaking about being saved from hellfire in a distant afterlife. The context makes this unmistakably clear: salvation meant deliverance from concrete threats—military enemies, violence, chaos, spears at his throat. In Psalm 18, God appears as a storm-riding warrior, tearing open the heavens to intervene and rescue His beloved. That is salvation in the biblical imagination.

Even the prophets—Isaiah, Jeremiah, Ezekiel—when they speak of salvation, they’re pointing to return from exile, to national renewal, to protection from harm, to the healing of the land and the people.

“In returning and rest you shall be saved,” (Isaiah 30:15) Isaiah says. This verse wasn’t directed at individuals in the 21st century to warn about afterlife punishment, but rather to the nation of Israel, emphasizing trust in God’s covenant promises to her.

Thus, in the Hebrew Scriptures, salvation means blessing (Genesis 49:18), protection from harm and enemies (Exodus 14:13–14; Psalm 18:2), healing (Isaiah 53:5; Mark 5:34), justice (Isaiah 45:21; Luke 18:7–8), winning a war (1 Samuel 14:23; 2 Chronicles 20:17), restoration (Jeremiah 30:17; Acts 3:21), and ultimately, the renewal of all creation (Isaiah 65:17; Romans 8:21; Revelation 21:1–5).

Salvation in the New Testament—Still the Same Story

The words most often translated as “salvation” or “saved” in the New Testament do not mean “rescued from afterlife divine punishment in hell.” Rather, "sózó" carries the deeper sense of being rescued, healed, made whole, restored, and delivered from real danger—mostly physically.

In Matthew 8:25, when the disciples feared for their lives in the storm on the Sea of Galilee, they cried out, “Lord, save us!”. They didn't ask Jesus to forgive their sins and rescue them from afterlife hellfire torment but to deliver them from physical danger by calming the storm.

In Mark 5:23, when Jairus pleaded with Jesus, “My little daughter is at the point of death; please come, that by coming, You may lay Your hands on her, so that she will be saved,” he was begging for his child’s physical healing.

In Luke 8:48, the woman who touched Jesus’ cloak was told, “Daughter, your faith has saved you; go in peace.” Jesus wasn’t referring to her faith saving her from a torturous afterlife, but rather to healing her physical disease (see verse 43) in that very moment.

In each case, "salvation" is not about paperwork in heaven, but about real healing, rescue, and transformation in this life.

Jesus Saves

Jesus came to save people: to cure their illnesses, to heal their wounds, to set them free from whatever oppresses them—whether demonic, emotional, physical, or societal—and to restore them to their true identity as God’s image-bearers. His salvation is a present reality: the healing of our brokenness, the liberation from our sin, and the renewal of our hearts and relationships.

The Gospel is not a message of fear and legal bookkeeping, but of deep healing and transformation (and no, the Book of Life in Revelation isn’t about bookkeeping—learn more in my book on Hell). Jesus’ salvation reaches into every part of our existence, calling us to live now as fully renewed children of God, reflecting His love, wholeness, and compassion into the world.

But somewhere along the way, Christianity lost the plot. The Jewish message of the Jewish Messiah was ripped from its Hebrew roots, filtered through Greek philosophical categories, and eventually rewritten with the language of Roman courts, contracts, and cosmic punishments soaked in paganism. But if you go back to the New Testament itself—and read it like a Jew living in the first century—you’ll find that its understanding of salvation is still the same story told in the Hebrew Bible: rescue, restoration, healing, protection, deliverance, blessing, homecoming.

When Jesus said He came to “seek and save the lost” (Luke 19:10), He wasn’t talking about plucking disembodied souls from the fires of Eternal Conscious Torment. In fact, when saying this, He had just finished restoring a man named Zacchaeus—a Jewish tax collector hated by his community. Jesus dined with him, welcomed him, called him a “son of Abraham.” That’s salvation. A broken man, estranged from his people, restored to dignity, identity, and belonging.

Jesus wasn’t creating a new religion where salvation suddenly meant an escape from some cosmic furnace. He was fulfilling an old promise—that God would gather the scattered, heal the sick, bring the outcast home, raise the dead, renew the mind, give hope and a new way of living. That’s what His salvation was. That’s what Yeshua-Jesus was named for.

Later, when Paul writes about salvation, he echoes the same theme. In Romans 10, he prays for Israel to be “saved”—not because he’s worried she'll burn forever (nations don't burn in hell, do they?!) but because Israel is missing the fulfillment of her own story and calling. He’s heartbroken that they’re cut off from the blessing, the restoration, the Messiah they’ve waited for. And in Ephesians 2, he says, “It is by grace you have been saved…” but he’s talking collectively to Gentiles—and he doesn’t mean escape from hell. He means the inclusion of the Gentiles into the people of God, into the covenant, into the blessing.

The New Testament consistently uses sōzō—“to save”—in tangible, earthly contexts: healing the sick, raising the dead, restoring the broken. The blind man? “Go, your faith has saved you.” He wasn't saved from hell but from blindness. The leper? “Your faith has saved you.” He wasn’t saved from hell either but from leprosy. Salvation wasn’t a doctrine to be mastered for the sake of afterlife escapism. It was a lived experience. It was tangible and immediate.

In summary, the New Testament didn’t alter the biblical meaning of salvation—Western theologians did. Jesus simply deepened its Hebrew roots and broadened its reach. He came not only to free Israel from exile but to rescue the whole world from every kind of exile—sin, shame, sickness, and death. His mission was to pave the way for tikkun olam—heal what’s broken, restore what’s lost, and unite all creation with God.

So How Did We Get To Salvation from Hellfire?

Walk through almost any city in America long enough and you’ll eventually hear it—the familiar question shouted on street corners and spoken in revival tents: “Are you saved?” And almost without pause comes its shadow twin: “If you died tonight, do you know where you’d go?”

This version of the gospel, drilled into the minds of millions, presents salvation as an urgent escape plan—a spiritual insurance policy to avoid hellfire and secure entry into heaven. And the whole deal, they insist, can be yours instantly—just recite a scripted prayer from a folded pamphlet, and voilà, salvation delivered on the spot!

But what if the question itself is based on a huge misunderstanding?

Ask the average Baptist, Calvinist, Pentecostal, or Evangelical, and the answer to what we are saved from usually comes quickly—“We are saved from Eternal Conscious Torment in Hell!” But the Bible never actually says that in any straightforward way.

The version of salvation most Christians inherit today does not arise organically from the Bible itself, but from centuries of theological development shaped by Greek philosophy, Roman legal categories, and the fiery imagination of medieval Catholicism—later refined and systematized by Protestant reformers. Within this framework, the doctrine commonly called Eternal Conscious Torment assumes that humanity’s default state is damnation, and that Jesus came primarily to save us from His own Father by absorbing the blows of divine wrath in our place, thereby allowing a small minority to escape everlasting punishment.

But from Genesis to Revelation, the biblical warning is strikingly consistent. It is not about afterlife torture, but about separation from life itself. From the very beginning, humanity’s deepest obsession has been to defeat death—to transcend it, outwit it, or overcome it by any means necessary. Death is the tyrant. Death is the curse. Death is the final enemy. And death—not Eternal Conscious Torment—is what we ultimately need to be saved from.

As you remember, in the Bible’s prologue—Genesis 2:17 specifically—God's initial warning wasn’t about Eternal Conscious Torment, but rather about death: “In the day you eat of it, you will surely die.” Thus, the enemy is not torment—it’s death. Consequently, the good news of the gospel is that Jesus came to conquer death, not to rescue us from a medieval idea born of poor translations and misinterpreted metaphors and parables.

The day Adam and Eve sinned, they didn’t fall into a burning pit. They began to die. Literally. Not in some esoteric, hidden, mystical way, but physically. And that curse of death—that unraveling of life—has defined the human experience ever since.

In Romans 5:12, Paul says, “Through one man sin entered the world, and death through sin, and in this way death came to all people.” And this is why he says in 1 Corinthians 15:26 that “the last enemy to be destroyed is death.”

Now think about it logically: if hellfire—something dreadful that awaits after death—were the ultimate threat, Paul would not say that the last enemy to be destroyed is death, but hellfire.

Clearly, for Paul, salvation was not rescue from afterlife hellfire—it was deliverance from Death itself.

Death, in Scripture, is not just biological expiration. It is sickness. Brokenness. Alienation. Fear. Emptiness. Destruction. Oppression. Shame. Guilt. The decay of everything we were made to be. The breakdown of relationship—with God, with others, with ourselves. It’s the slow suffocation of joy, meaning, connection, and purpose. That’s why people often say they’re dying inside. The salvation they crave is real and immediate, not something to wait for until after death.

Jesus ultimately came to save us from death—but walking in His way also saves us from its effects, whether physical, emotional, or relational. This is precisely why the Gospels portray Him moving through the world not only raising the dead but reversing death’s reach: healing the sick, restoring the broken, casting out demons, feeding the hungry, cleansing lepers, welcoming outcasts, and forgiving sinners.

For the Hebrews, “salvation” was never about escaping to another realm in the afterlife. It was, first and foremost, about the restoration of life here and now—renewal, healing, deliverance, and wholeness—the repair of the world, tikkun olam.

Jesus doesn’t say, “I am the escape hatch from hell.” He says, “I am the resurrection and the life” (John 11:25).

In other words, Jesus saves by undoing the effects of death wherever He finds them. His ministry reveals God’s intention from the beginning—not to abandon creation, but to restore it. Not escapism, but renewal.

Jesus Holds the Keys to the Cosmic Graveyard

When Jesus rises from the grave, He doesn’t return battered and proclaim that He settled some cosmic legal debt to God by taking on His wrath. Instead, He appears with the holes left by human nails in His hands, declaring victory over death. He says, “I was dead, and now look—I am alive forever and ever! And I hold the keys of death and Hades” (Revelation 1:18). Hades—often translated "hell"—is the ancient Greek word used to describe the grave—not a specific graveyard or geographic location, but a symbolic realm where the dead are imagined being at rest, asleep. It’s not a place of Eternal Conscious Torment, but of waiting. And the good news? Jesus holds the key to unlocking humanity's cosmic graveyard. That means He can—and will—unlock its gates. He has the authority to call the dead to life, to empty the grave, and to bring resurrection to all. That is what His salvation is all about. The healing of all that death has fractured.

Humanly speaking, you can’t destroy what’s already dead—you can only bring it back. Dead people aren’t further destroyed because they already are, but they can be raised again. So when Scripture says, "Death and the grave are thrown into the lake of fire" (Revelation 20:14), it means the grave itself is no more and death is ultimately dismantled.

Throwing death and Hades—the grave—into the Lake of Fire (also known in Judaism as the River of Fire—a symbol of cosmic refinement and divine purification) signifies their ultimate defeat and removal from the human story. It's not about perpetual torment; it's about the cleansing of death itself and the final purging of all that stands against God (Revelation 19:20;20:10;20:14-15;21:8).

It’s the final undoing of the curse from Eden. Resurrection replaces the grave. Or in Paul's words, “Death has been swallowed up in victory.” (1 Corinthians 15:54).

And look how the Bible ends—with the solution to the very problem it began with:

He will wipe every tear from their eyes. There will be no more death.

(Revelation 21:4)

The biblical story concludes not with a negative declaration, “Most of you will spend forever being broiled,” but with a positive promise, “There will be no more death.”

Again, the New Testament introduced nothing that the Hebrew Scriptures hadn’t already taught:

He will swallow up death forever. The Sovereign Lord will wipe away the tears from all faces.

(Isaiah 25:8)

That’s biblical salvation—overcoming death.

Not hell. Not God's wrath. Not Satan. Death. And how does God destroy it? Through resurrection.

This is why, until Augustine of Hippo in the fourth century, the earliest Christians were not fixated on an afterlife hell. Their proclamation was resurrection. They did not tremble before visions of perpetual flames; they rejoiced in the decisive victory of Jesus over the grave.

They didn't preach a comfy afterlife for the lucky few who passed a theological test. They preached the good news of the Kingdom of God.

So if you’re still thinking of salvation as a cosmic fire insurance policy, you’ve missed the beating heart of the Gospel.

Conclusion

The biblical story of salvation—from Genesis to Revelation—is the story of God's cosmic redemption; saving humanity from death and restoring life.

Salvation is being rescued from slavery in Egypt. Salvation is being brought back from exile. Salvation is God delivering His people from enemies, from oppression, from famine, from fear. Salvation is healing the sick, restoring the outcast, feeding the hungry. Salvation is about life—people restored, relationships reconciled, lives protected, wounds healed, and death itself defeated.

The Good News is not that a select few might narrowly escape unending punishment. It is that all will be raised to life (and judgement)—not because of anything we have done, nor because of knowledge we have acquired, but because our God is truly Mighty to Save. And this is not merely a future promise. It is a kind of divine life that can be entered into now—not by reciting a formulaic prayer, but by walking the way Jesus walked and living as He lived.

c1ZB

Chapter 28
Understanding Salvation Redefines Life’s Purpose

There’s a much more meaningful and beautiful way to see our purpose than treating it like some cosmic "escape room" designed to dodge endless torment. Life's meaning is about growing in the knowledge of who we are, who God is, and what it means to love. That journey inevitably involves failure, struggle, and sin—not as goals, but as part of the learning process.

Grace, compassion, forgiveness—these aren't just theories. They must be experienced to be understood. Without sin, Adam and Eve would never have grasped the depth of God’s mercy. Without failure, we’d never taste the sweetness of redemption. Like light is only fully appreciated after darkness, love is most powerful when it’s shown to the undeserving.

Most Christians treat Genesis 3 as a tragic detour—a “Plan B” because humans messed up. But what if God knew that free beings would fall? What if this world was never supposed to be a flawless paradise, but a space for learning and transformation?

The Bible never says Adam and Eve were created perfect (tamim). They were created tov (good/functional)—with the ability to grow. Perfection doesn't need to grow. Imperfect humans do. And growth requires space for error, experience, and transformation. That’s not a design flaw. That’s divine wisdom.

Life's Meaning When Death Is Only Temporary

Sin brings pain—that’s undeniable. Sin causes suffering, injustice, betrayal, loss. But through that very pain, we grow. We gain wisdom. We develop empathy. We learn to forgive, to hope, to fight for justice. It’s the hardness of life that carves out our character.

We’re not AI robots. We’re not Divine either. We’re human. And being human means learning through imperfection.

Sin is harmful—but it’s also a mirror. It shows us who we are, what we’re capable of, and how desperately we need grace. It is meant to lead humanity to repentance, reconciliation, creativity, healing, and maturity. Societies and individuals alike often improve only after facing the consequences of wrongdoing.

According to clinical psychologist Jordan Peterson, sin may be the most significant catalyst for growth, wisdom, and creativity:

The thing that people don’t understand about creativity is that there’s no reason to be creative unless you have a problem to solve. If someone dies on you young and you’re forced to fend your way in the world and deal with that kind of trauma, you have to put yourself together in a creative manner, and it’s no joke. And so, early negative experiences allied with high intelligence and this kind of temperament that we were talking about are one of the things that foster creative production.
