Parents are misinformed about this sort of thing because they think that if they just do laissez faire things with their children, you know, “you can do anything you want”, they’ll automatically be creative. That’s the stupidest thing you could possibly imagine because that isn’t how creativity works. Creativity emerges when you put serious constraints on things.70

(Jordan Peterson)

Peterson's logic is simple: No pain, no gain. It’s not a spiritual excuse—it’s a hard truth. Life shapes us through struggle. And God, rather than condemning us for it, meets us there—not with wrath and fury, but with grace and healing.

Even our poor decisions, even those who do evil—and even evil itself—end up serving a purpose within God’s larger story. Not because God desires wickedness, but because its very existence creates contrast. It sharpens our moral vision. It helps us discern the difference between good and evil, light and darkness, what builds up and what tears down. Without contrast, goodness becomes invisible; without opposition, virtue cannot be recognized for what it is.

In God’s providence, even the darkest acts become unwilling teachers. They reveal what kind of world we don’t want, what kind of people we must not become, and what kind of Kingdom God intends to establish—a Kingdom defined not by destruction, but by healing, justice, and love.

The Divine Intent Behind Human Limitations

God could have “downloaded” all truth into our minds from birth—but He didn’t. Instead, He gave us only the basics: instincts, curiosity, and a hunger to learn. The rest comes through experience—and often through painful ones.

God is perfect—all-knowing, all-loving, unchanging. But when He created us, He didn’t make us perfect like Him. He made us human. And humanity, by design, is limited—in knowledge, understanding, and emotional capacity. Our limitations also mean we act out of impulse. We judge poorly. We make mistakes. We err.

But these limitations are not a flaw—they are part of the design. And if we were created this way, then it would be profoundly unjust to condemn us to Eternal Conscious Torment for the very limitations God Himself built into us. And if He won't, then it means life's purpose has nothing to do with escaping hellfire.

The other day, I came across a discussion in a Messianic WhatsApp group run by the Messianic Bible College in Israel. People—mostly its own Bible college students—were debating which members of the Jewish Bibas family—the children and their mother kidnapped by Hamas and murdered in Gaza—were old enough to burn in hell.

Let that sink in.

Imagine theology students arguing over whether a ten-year-old deserves Eternal Conscious Torment because they weren’t “saved” by a certain age—as if God, who created that child with limited understanding, would then punish them—forever—for not being morally perfect or for lacking theological precision. That’s like burning a zebra for failing a math test or being unable to climb a tree.

This isn’t just bad soteriology. It’s a distortion of God’s character and a grotesque misunderstanding of the Gospel.

Perfect People Don't Need Help

The truth is, our imperfections are not accidents, nor do they pose a threat to God. They are purposeful. God created us with physical, mental, and emotional limitations not to shame us, but to invite us into something deeper—growth and relationship.

If we had perfect knowledge, unlimited emotional wisdom, and flawless moral instincts, we wouldn't need each other—and we wouldn’t truly need God either. But in our vulnerability, we’re drawn into community. We learn love, humility, forgiveness, patience, sacrifice—not by being self-sufficient, but by depending on one another.

This interdependence is not a weakness. It's sacred. It's how we grow. It's why we need God. It's how we experience and practice love.

Even more, our limitations create the space where free will can exist. Without the ability to choose—even wrongly—love would not be real but robotic, and faithfulness would be meaningless. God didn’t create a world of hand puppets. He created people who could choose, learn, grow, fail, and try again.

It’s through these struggles, questions, and moral challenges that real character is formed. And in those moments when we face our own weakness, we often become most aware of our need for others, and for God’s guidance, strength, and grace.

Fundamentalists like John MacArthur claim that “God only accepts absolute perfection.”71 But this claim is not only theologically flawed, but emotionally damaging. These ideas portray God as a distant, cold, and harsh tyrant rather than the loving Father revealed in Christ. This view of God breeds anxiety, not peace. Depression, not joy.

To suggest that infinite torment is a fitting punishment for the very limitations we were born with—for being human—is not only illogical, but it is also morally indefensible. It twists the gospel from being rooted in love to being driven by fear.

The biblical story, at its heart, is not about achieving perfection—it’s about growth and restoration. It's not about measuring up—it’s about being lifted up. God is not standing at a distance with folded arms and a whip. He’s the Father who runs to meet the prodigal, the healer who touches the unclean, the Shepherd who leaves the ninety-nine to rescue just the one.

To be human is to be flawed—and to be deeply loved nonetheless. That is the true good news.

We Learn From (Painful) Experiences

Yes, sin causes suffering. Much like you—I’ve lived it, been shaped by it, and contributed to it. But without experiencing pain and failure, we would never grow in wisdom or character. It's life’s pain and struggles that shape us the most.

Artificial Intelligence can process data, but it can’t feel sorrow, regret, or relief. That’s what makes us human—our ability to feel, to fail, to hurt, and to learn and grow from it. If we never sin, we’d never need forgiveness. And without forgiveness, we’d never grasp the depths of grace or the richness of love.

As Jesus said, “Whoever has been forgiven little, loves little” (Luke 7:47). Those who’ve fallen and been lifted know something that no textbook can teach. The emotional, spiritual, and relational weight of grace can’t be understood abstractly—it has to be lived.

Yes, sin destroys. But it's only temporary because God promises restoration—a resurrection, a new body, a new earth, a renewed world (Philippians 3:21; 2 Corinthians 5:1–10; Revelation 21:1–5). That hope is the foundation of forgiveness. Even if someone wounds you beyond repair—even if they take your life—God will make all things new. No pain will be wasted. That's the good news.

Conclusion

The hopelessness exposed by sin drives us toward something greater than ourselves. It teaches us that what we need is not just self-improvement, but healing. And that healing does not flow from existential fear or religious intimidation, but from encountering a God who understands us—a God who does not cancel us when we fail. A God who draws nearer, not farther away, in our brokenness.

Contrary to what many have been taught, sin does not threaten God. It does not intimidate Him or diminish His holiness. Jesus, our healer, never recoiled from the sick or the sinful. He touched them. He restored them. He forgave them. He loved them.

Jesus is what God is like.

So no—sin is not the end of the story. More often, it is the beginning of a deeper one: the story of grace, transformation, redemption, and a love greater than anything we could ever produce on our own.

No matter how far you fall or how deeply you fail—rise. Stand upright. Walk in grace. God loves you, and there is always more than enough grace for you!

c20Z

Chapter 29
The Communal Aspect of Salvation

In today’s hyper-individualistic culture, salvation is often presented as a private transaction between the sinner and God—a personal decision, a solitary prayer, a moment that “secures” one’s afterlife destiny. While personal transformation is certainly crucial, it’s far from the whole picture.

In the Bible, salvation is primarily communal. It’s not just about me being saved. It’s about a collective restoration of the family, the people, the nation, and ultimately—the world.

Consider the Exodus once more: God saved an entire nation—many of whom were doubters, complainers, half-committed worshipers of Egyptian gods, and spiritually immature. Why? Because God rescues communities, not just a few isolated “deserving” individuals.

The Individual—Always a Thread in the Greater Tapestry

Salvation in Scripture is communal as much as it is personal. God liberates a people, shapes a people, carries a people—not merely a handful of spiritual elites.

The same theme echoes through the prophets. When Israel sins, it's the nation that is exiled. When they repent, it's the nation that is restored. As God says through Jeremiah, “I will surely save you out of a distant place… Jacob will again have peace and security” (Jeremiah 30:10). Not Jacob the individual—but Jacob the people, the tribe, the nation (Israel).

This collective mindset continues into the New Testament. When Paul told the Ephesians that "it is by grace you have been saved" (Ephesians 2:5), he wasn’t addressing isolated individuals worried about hell. He was speaking to a community of Gentile believers who were once outsiders but who have now been brought near. They were “dead” not in the sense of personal moral guilt, but because they, Gentiles, were cut off from God’s covenant family. And salvation means being welcomed in. Reunited. Included.

So when Paul tells the Ephesians (2:8–9), the Galatians (2:16), and the Romans (3:28; 5:1) that they were saved by faith alone, faith means trust. We walk by faith, not by sight, which means we live in trust. But it’s not a passive trust; we get up, move, change direction, and align our lives. It means entrusting oneself to the God of Israel. The Gentiles were welcomed into the covenant family simply by trusting God’s promises—to keep them, to bless them, and to make them part of His redemptive plan. They were saved—joined into the covenant—by trusting God.

But they were not saved from hellfire torment—that idea is nowhere in the text. They were saved from a life of waste, meaninglessness, idol worship, perishing, and estrangement from the covenant. That is what Paul tells Gentile believers they have been rescued from. Salvation, for Paul, is deliverance from a broken way of life and incorporation into a healed one. He wrote with entire communities in mind, not specific individuals.

They “switched teams” not because they earned it through effort, moral achievement, or religious performance, but because salvation is God’s gift. They did not pay for it. They did not merit it. They simply recognized—or were in the process of recognizing—what God had already done in Christ and stepped into it by trust.

This is why the “you” in most (if not all) biblical passages about salvation and repentance is plural—more like “y’all” than “you.” It is a national call to Israel (e.g., Ezekiel 18:30-31, Jeremiah 25:5, Joel 2:12-13, 2 Chronicles 7:14, Zechariah 1:3, Hosea 14:1-2, Matthew 3:2; 4:17, Luke 13:3, 5; 15:7, 10; 17:3) that expanded to the gentiles. Salvation was never meant to be limited to the individual or confined to a private confession in one’s heart; it’s something we step into together. A new community. A new family. A new humanity.

The Bible Speaks in We, Not Me

Many people today struggle to understand why collective punishment appears in parts of the Hebrew Bible. But we must remember that the ancient worldview saw the individual as inseparable from the community. A person’s sin was rarely viewed as an isolated moral failure but as a rupture in the communal fabric. The whole household, clan, and sometimes the entire nation bore responsibility for the environment that shaped the individual.

This is why the Torah contains examples where the consequences of sin fall upon more than just the perpetrator. Consider Achan in Joshua 7: his secret act of disobedience brought defeat upon all Israel in battle, and his entire household faced judgment alongside him. To modern Western readers, raised in a highly individualistic society, this feels shocking and unfair. But in the ancient Near Eastern mindset, the family and clan were the soil in which a person’s character grew; they shared both honor and shame, blessing and curse.

The individual was always understood as emerging from—and shaped by—the collective.

Today, psychology actually confirms what the biblical world assumed all along: individuals do not form in a vacuum. The vast majority of murderers, serial abusers, rapists, and violent offenders come from profoundly dysfunctional environments—homes marked by neglect, trauma, addiction, or generational violence. In other words, the fault is not solely personal but collective. The community, the system, and the family all participate—actively or passively—in shaping the very conditions that produced the offender.

This ancient insight is largely absent from today’s “me, myself, and I” culture—and from much of modern Western theology, with its hyper-individualistic focus on my personal salvation, my personal sin, and my personal relationship with God. Scripture, however, presents a far more communal worldview. The actions of one can harm many (Adam) or bless many (Jesus). God engages humanity not merely as isolated individuals, but as interconnected members of families, tribes, and entire nations.

Salvation Is Communal Because Repentance Is Communal

We touched on this earlier, but it deserves deeper attention: repentance—so often reduced today to a private, emotional, inward moment—was, in Scripture, fundamentally a communal act.

Hebrew prophets like Joel and Isaiah called the nation to repent: “Return to me with all your heart… Gather the people, sanctify the congregation.” (Joel 2:12-13). The Hebrew words used for “repent” and “re/turn” are spoken in the plural. “Return, y’all.” Not “you personally.” The call was always collective.

In the New Testament, too, when John the Baptist declared, “Repent, for the kingdom of heaven has come near” (Matthew 3:2), and when Jesus echoed these exact same words (Matthew 4:17), who were they addressing? Random individuals passing by? Not at all. The Greek word Metanoeite (“Repent”) is in the plural form.

John and Jesus spoke as Israel's prophets to their nation, collectively urging her to repent, just as the prophets before them had done. This wasn’t a private call to personal salvation from hell; it was a national summons to return to God, to turn from corruption, injustice, and spiritual complacency, and to prepare for the arrival of God's kingdom. Not adhering to this did not condemn Israel to a lifetime in hell, but rather brought devastation upon it, as demonstrated by the destruction of Jerusalem and the Temple, along with the exile of Jews in the year 70AD.

This has massive implications. It means the Gospel is not about saving a few individuals from a fiery, torturous future. It’s about redeeming whole communities, whole cultures, whole systems. It’s about healing the world—tikkun olam.

That’s why the early Christians "were together and shared everything. They would sell their land and the things they owned and then divide the money and give it to anyone who needed it" (Acts 2:44-45). That’s why they cared for widows, welcomed outcasts, and built new social structures rooted in love and justice. They weren’t individuals waiting to be raptured from Earth. They were a community participating in its restoration. They understood that salvation had begun, and they had a key part in it.

Thus, biblical salvation is never just “me and Jesus.” It’s Immanuel—“God with us.” It’s a new covenant family—Jew and Gentile, male and female, slave and free—brought together under one Head, one Body, one Spirit.

Biblical Collectivism vs. Western Individualism

In both the Old and New Testaments, identity was deeply corporate. But Greco-Roman philosophy, particularly Stoicism and Epicureanism, had emphasized the individual pursuit of the good life, marking a departure from the communal orientation of Hebrew thought.72 This trajectory deepened in the 2nd and 3rd centuries with the rise of Gnosticism, which stressed secret personal knowledge (gnosis) as the individual's path to salvation. In Gnostic teaching, redemption was not communal but centered on the individual’s inner awakening, who had to be mystically reborn, standing in sharp contrast to the biblical emphasis on shared faith, sacraments, and community.73 The movement toward individualism gained further momentum in the 4th and 5th centuries with Augustine, whose introspective writings, most notably Confessions, highlighted the inner self and the "personal relationship" with God, representing a decisive step in the development of individualism within Western theology.

Once Christianity became institutionalized in the 4th century, the communal aspect was gradually buried under layers of philosophical interpretation. The infusion of Gnosticism and Neoplatonism by Augustine74 reshaped the gospel message into something foreign to its Jewish roots. Over time, the Western gospel evolved into a maze of personal confessions, doctrinal debates, and theological examinations—far removed from the communal, relational, life-giving message Jesus actually taught.

What began as a call to embody divine love became, in many circles, an intellectual system to be mastered, argued, and defended. The heart was replaced with propositions and creeds. And the simplicity and beauty of the original gospel were overshadowed by centuries of philosophical and theological reinterpretation.

Ultimately, the world's salvation is not about what we do, but about what God has done for us. Jesus didn’t lay down His life so we would grovel like condemned criminals. He gave His life to show us our true worth, proving we are all beloved children—even the prodigals—of a Father who would go to any length to bring us home. And just like earthly children don’t have to earn their parents’ love, we don’t have to earn God’s. But we do get to choose how we live in response—whether we walk in that love or turn away from it—and the face consequences (like filling our belly with the pods meant for the pigs; Luke 15:16).

Conclusion

Salvation has always been aimed at the communal level and expressed through love, justice, mercy, wholeness, and restored relationships—with God and with one another. In the teaching of Jesus, salvation is not a spiritual loophole or a legal transaction. It is an invitation into God's blessings and into a way of life—a way defined by healing the sick, restoring the broken, lifting up the poor, and loving neighbors and enemies alike (Matthew 10:8).

This is exactly how the apostles understood Jesus’ message. They didn’t go around frightening people with threats of fire that would burn their skin forever. They preached about lifting the lowly, feeding the hungry, honoring widows, and practicing radical generosity: “Share with the Lord’s people who are in need. Practice hospitality” (Romans 12:13). “Give proper recognition to widows who are really in need” (1 Timothy 5:3). “Don’t look to your own interests, but each of you to the interests of others” (Philippians 2:4). This is salvation in action. That's what a saved community looks like.

So maybe it’s time to retire the word “saved”—or at least save it from the hellfire narrative that’s corrupted its meaning. And instead, return to the original biblical language: deliverance, healing, liberation, redemption. These words speak of being set free. And we’re not saved from God, but by Him—from everything that keeps you apart from Him. And it’s not just about individuals, but whole communities too.

This is salvation: not abandonment but restoration. Not escape, but resurrection. Not fear, but freedom. Not somewhere above and someday in the future, but here and now.

My parents’ generation repaired what was broken. Ours tend to discard and replace. But I do not believe God treats His creation that way. God does not throw away what is broken, wounded, or flawed. His intention is not to burn the world to ashes, but to liberate it, heal it, and bring it to its intended fullness (Revelation 21:5).

c22B

Chapter 30
The Universal Aspect of Salvation

The greatest act of heroism in the human experience is self-sacrifice—when a hero gives their life to save the lives of others. And the very definition of a superhero is precisely this: someone whose sacrifice saves many, many lives, not just a few. The more lives rescued, delivered, or protected through that sacrifice, the greater the heroism becomes.

If this is true on a human level—where we honor those who save dozens or hundreds—how much more does it hold from a divine perspective?

If Jesus’s sacrifice touched only some lives but not all, it would still be a good sacrifice, yet one that could be surpassed. And if a greater sacrifice were possible, then logically, there could be a greater Christ and an even greater God. Therefore, it logically follows that God’s salvation through Christ must be universal in nature.

Now let’s explore this idea in depth…

The Superhero Who Saved the World

For I did not come to judge the world, but to save the world.

(John 12:47)

If you were to rescue a great ship from sinking—thus saving every passenger from certain death—not all would respond with gratitude. Some would go on living as if nothing had happened: resentful, unkind, even hostile toward the very one who saved them and toward others as well. In that sense, though they were genuinely saved, they would still choose to live in darkness, inwardly untouched by the grace that spared their lives.

The fact of their salvation from death remains unchanged. But how they live in response—and the consequences of that way of life—certainly does.

I, when I am lifted up from the earth, will draw all people to myself…

(John 12:32)

What a breathtaking metaphor: Jesus, lifted up—resurrected, exalted, and ascended—will draw a few preelected all people to Himself. Just as He triumphed over death, all of humanity will be swept up into that same victory. Not everyone will appreciate it—at least not right away—but all will be drawn by Jesus's work.

Yet throughout much of Christian history, especially since the 4th century, the gospel has often been reduced—limited to a select few. Calvinism, for instance, formalized this in the doctrine of "Limited Atonement," teaching that Jesus’ sacrifice was not truly for everyone, but limited—only for the pre-chosen elect. According to this view, Christ did not die for the world but only for a few—the elect. Calvinists argue that if Jesus died for all yet not all are saved, His atonement would somehow be a failure. Therefore, they insist, He must have died only for those predestined to be saved.

But this doctrine faces serious challenges—not only morally, but biblically. Not only is the invitation universal, but so is the application. Christ’s blood is not merely offered to all and applied to a few. It is poured out for the world and effective for the world. Anything less reduces the cross to a sincere gesture that ultimately fails, rather than the decisive, victorious act of God to reconcile all things to Himself.

A careful look at Scripture reveals an overwhelming, undeniable pattern: God’s plan of salvation is not partial nor exclusive, but universal. Scripture consistently uses words like “the world,” “all,” and “every” when describing Christ’s mission and work. Far from presenting salvation as an exclusive club, the Bible thunders with the message that God's grace is a free gift extended to everyone, with no exclusions or conditions, and without exception.

The Universe Doesn’t Orbit You

Nowhere in the New Testament are we instructed to “pray and ask Jesus into your heart.” That phrase is a later evangelical invention. The idea is also theologically confused, since the New Testament repeatedly teaches that Jesus is at the right hand of the Father while the Holy Spirit is the one sent to dwell within believers.

When we speak of salvation, we often make it small. We reduce it to individual choices, moments at an altar, or formulas and prayers to “get to heaven.” But the Bible paints a much bigger picture. It speaks of a cosmic battle, not just for human souls, but for the triumph of God's will over everything that opposes it—evil, sin, rebellion, death, and the spiritual powers and principalities that seek to enslave creation. If God is truly God, then His salvation must ultimately include all parts of His creation.

As we saw in previous chapters, the Hebrew Scriptures' redemption was never a narrow, one-dimensional, individualistic concept limited to the afterlife of an individual. It was holistic and about restoring not just people but entire communities, nations, and even land. The scope and vision were always grand:

For God did not send his Son into the world to condemn the world, but to save the world through him.

(John 3:17)

Christ's death—His atonement—was not a limited transaction fulfilling a single requirement. Rather, it was the culmination of the biblical narrative—a story that always pointed to a cosmic healing, universal restoration, and the salvation of the world.

Preachers often say, “If you were the only person on earth, Jesus would still have come and died just for you.” While the sentiment behind that statement is heartfelt, it subtly narrows the scope of the New Covenant. It reframes the cross as primarily individualized, rather than recognizing its vast, cosmic reach.

The New Covenant is not merely personal—it is universal. It is no longer confined to one individual, one people, or one nation, but embraces the whole world and, indeed, all creation. Jesus did not come only for you in isolation; He came to reconcile all things to God and to restore the entire created order. The cross is not diminished by personal love—but it is expanded by its universal purpose.

God's Universal Scope of Salvation Is Not a New Testament Invention

The Lord is good to all, and his mercy is over all that he has made.

(Psalm 145:9)

God’s mercy is not limited. His kindness embraces all of creation.

He will swallow up death forever; and the Lord God will wipe away tears from all faces.

(Isaiah 25:8)

God’s victory over death is not partial. It will touch every face, not just a chosen few.

To me every knee shall bow, every tongue shall swear allegiance.

(Isaiah 45:22-23)

The future God promises is one of universal recognition and worship, not partial allegiance.

All we like sheep have gone astray… and the Lord has laid on him the iniquity of us all. 

(Isaiah 53:6)

All have gone astray. All are included in the redemptive work laid upon Christ.

All flesh shall come to worship before me, declares the Lord.

(Isaiah 66:23)

A day will come when worship is universal, when every heart turns toward its Creator.

This means even hell—however one understands it—must be temporary; a corrective justice for the sake of restoration.

Divine discipline—that of a loving Father—must always aim toward repentance and restoration. Its purpose isn’t to crush or abuse, but to correct, restore, and bring about transformation. God does nothing as a judge that he wouldn’t do as a father. Only a temporary punishment can have a true and positive purpose, thus:

For no one is cast off by the Lord forever.

(Lamentations 3:31)

Without the hope of redemption, punishment becomes mere sadism—but when guided by love, it becomes a temporary path to healing and renewal.

Even those who suffered the death penalty, the loss of the privilege of life in this age, are not beyond God’s redemptive reach. They too will be raised, brought into judgment, and held accountable. That judgment involves real consequences, corrective and purifying in nature, but it is not endless torture. Resurrection leads to reckoning, not to eternal torment. God’s justice restores order and heals what is broken; it does not perpetuate suffering without end.

God's Universal Scope of Salvation According to the New Testament

I bring you good news of great joy that will be for all the people.

(Luke 2:10)

The good news was not for a few, but for all the people.

And all humanity will see God’s salvation.

(Luke 3:6)

God's salvation will be witnessed by all flesh—not hidden, not restricted to time or place.

The true light, which gives light to everyone, was coming into the world.

(John 1:9)

Jesus’ light is universal, shining upon every human heart.

And I, when I am lifted up from the earth, will draw all people to myself.

(John 12:32)

If Jesus draws all, then no one can permanently remain outside His pull. His love is stronger than human misunderstanding and rebellion, stronger than Satan’s deception.

For all have sinned… and all are justified by his grace as a gift.

(Romans 3:23-24)

All are justified because His grace is a free gift.

One trespass led to condemnation for all… so one act of righteousness leads to justification and life for all.

(Romans 5:18-19)

Adam’s fall affected everyone; Christ’s righteousness restores everyone.

For God has consigned all to disobedience, that he may have mercy on all.

(Romans 11:32)

God’s mercy matches the reach of human disobedience—completely.

As in Adam all die, so in Christ shall all be made alive.

(1 Corinthians 15:22)

There is no death Adam caused that Christ will not undo.

One has died for all… 

(2 Corinthians 5:14)

Christ’s death embraces all people in its scope.

At the name of Jesus every knee should bow… and every tongue confess that Jesus Christ is Lord.

(Philippians 2:9-11)

The future will see a universal confession of Christ’s lordship—not through coercion, but through adoration.

Through him to reconcile to himself all things, whether on earth or in heaven.

(Colossians 1:19-20)

God’s plan is nothing less than the reconciliation of everything.

God our savior, who wills everyone to be saved.

(1 Timothy 2:3-4)

If this is God's desire, what could ever stand in the way of His triumphant will?

We have hoped in a living God who is the Savior of all human beings.

(1 Timothy 4:10)

Not the Savior of some—the Savior of all.

The grace of God has appeared, bringing salvation for all people.

(Titus 2:11)

God’s grace doesn't discriminate. It brought salvation to all.

Jesus… so that by the grace of God he might taste death for everyone.

(Hebrews 2:9)

Jesus didn’t taste death for the sake of only an elect few—but for everyone.

The Lord… is patient toward you, not wishing that any should perish, but that all should reach repentance.

(2 Peter 3:9)

God’s patience reveals His heart: He desires all to find repentance. Can anything resist His will forever?

He is the propitiation for our sins, and not for ours only but also for the sins of the whole world.

(1 John 2:2)

The sacrifice of Jesus extends to the whole world—not a corner left untouched.

I heard every creature in heaven and on earth and under the earth… saying, "To him who sits on the throne be glory and honor!"

(Revelation 5:13)

Every creature—dead or alive—will join in worship. Every voice will rise in adoration.

Behold, I am making all things new.

(Revelation 21:5)

God’s final act is the renewal of all things—not the abandonment of most.

Everyone Will Be Brought Back to Life

The universal aspect of salvation means that everyone will be brought back to life:

Do not be amazed at this, for a time is coming when all who are in their graves will hear his voice and come out—those who have done what is good will rise to live, and those who have done what is evil will rise to be condemned. 

(John 5:28–29)

This may sound confusing to the modern ear: what is the point of resurrecting everyone with a new body if, moments later, most are supposedly “condemned”? The confusion dissolves once we examine what Scripture actually means by condemnation versus life.

A Western reader tends to equate “life” with mere biological existence or physical survival. But Jesus was not a twenty-first-century biologist or a modern philosopher. He was a first-century Jewish rabbi. In His world, life did not mean simply continuing to breathe. It meant blessing and joy, wholeness and peace, purpose, flourishing, and harmony with God. To live was to participate in the fullness of divine life—not merely to exist. Life wasn't about quantity (duration) but about quality:

The thief comes only to steal and kill and destroy; I have come that they may have life, and have it to the full.

(John 10:10)

That meaning remains in Judaism even today.

For instance, at Bar Mitzvahs, weddings, and every Shabbat, Jews around the world raise a glass of wine and toast “L’CHAIM!”—“To life!”—meaning, to joy, blessing, and flourishing.

So, some will rise to enjoy God’s highest blessings—sharing in the intimate nearness of His Kingdom, joining the great feast, the celebration prepared for those who lived faithfully. Others, though resurrected, will experience loss, judgment, and condemnation. They will stand before God not as enemies to be tortured, but as souls whose lives bear the consequences of what they chose, neglected, or rejected. They will not be like refined gold, but like alloyed gold that still needs to be refined and purified in fire.

In other words—someone can be condemned for their wrongdoing without being sentenced to everlasting torment. They may not be “great in the Kingdom," they may gnash their teeth in regret, feeling the weight of missed opportunities. But this does not mean God will throw them into a burning pit and torment them forever in lava.

Conclusion—God's Salvation is Universal

From Genesis to Revelation, the Bible tells a beautiful story of God’s boundless love and His plan to redeem the world. Again and again, we encounter the language of inclusion: all, every, the whole world. The gospel is not a restricted offer to a select few but an unstoppable river of grace that will eventually engulf and restore the entire cosmos.

If all have fallen short—and Scripture declares that they have—then all are equally included in Christ’s work of redemption. Salvation is not limited by human stubbornness, religious systems, or doctrinal precision. It flows from the heart of a God whose love is boundless, whose power is unmatched, and whose will is sure.

The good news is bigger, wider, and more beautiful than we ever dared hope: In Christ, all things will be made new. All.

c279

Chapter 31
The Grace That Offends the Religious Mind

Let’s be honest: deep down, many Christians struggle with the idea that “bad people”—murderers, abusers, even sworn enemies—might also be forgiven and restored. Grace sounds wonderful until it’s extended to those we hate. Jonah hated it. So did the older brother in the prodigal son story. Grace offends our sense of fairness—until the day we realize we need it just as much.

As a Jewish person who lost family members in the Holocaust, I would rather see Hitler—yes, even Hitler—repent and bow before Jesus Christ than burn forever. God’s restorative justice must triumph over human emotional vengeance, or else justice itself becomes another form of violence.

The Scandal of Grace

The notion that eventually everyone will be saved is a hard pill to swallow. Perhaps there are even exceptions—few who became so wicked that they effectively lost the image of God within them and are ultimately annihilated. Yet the idea that most—if not all—will be restored presents a view of God as supremely powerful, wise, loving, and inclusive.

God’s power is not measured by how many He can destroy in a single blow, but by how many He can save, rescue from evil, and heal from their own brokenness.

This perspective suggests that God's love and redemptive power are so immense (Romans 8:38-39) that even in the afterlife—no soul is beyond His reach, illustrating a God who fulfills His desire that none should perish but that all should come to repentance (2 Peter 3:9). It implies a God whose plans are unstoppable and whose purposes are so grand that they encapsulate the entirety of creation within His redemptive embrace. This view acknowledges the ultimate victory of good over evil, and of love over hatred, showcasing God's infinite wisdom in designing a creation where every part has a place in the world to come.

Conversely, the idea that only a few are eventually saved limits God's power and effectiveness. It suggests that despite His desire to save all, He either chooses not to (Calvinism) or is unable to achieve this outcome. This could imply that God’s capabilities are thwarted by human stubbornness or by the powers of evil. Such a viewpoint might portray God as less capable of achieving His will or that His wisdom is restricted to only a subset of His creation, leading to a partial victory over evil, which questions the omnipotence attributed to Him.

God: Greater Than We Dare to Believe

Many Christians have been raised to see salvation as a kind of exclusive club—a reward for behaving a certain way, believing the right creeds, or belonging to the right group. The idea that “everyone eventually gets in” feels, to some, like it cheapens the value of their own faith or group. But God’s love is not finite. Grace is not diluted by being extended for all; it is magnified. Mercy doesn’t run out. It multiplies.

Fear can pressure a person into short-term compliance, but it cannot produce long-term transformation. Fear may stop a behavior for a moment, but it cannot change the heart that desires it. That’s why fear-based religion so often collapses into cycles of guilt, shame, and exhaustion: people obey only as long as the threat feels close. Once the door is shut, the behavior returns—because nothing inward has been healed. Fear suppresses; it does not renew. It coerces the will, but it cannot set the mind free.

Love, on the other hand, transforms from the inside out. Love awakens desire, restores dignity, and draws the heart toward goodness freely, not by force. The New Testament is unambiguous: “Perfect love casts out fear” (1 John 4:18), not the other way around. Jesus never said, “Fear me so you will obey me,” but rather, “Follow Me,” “Abide in My love,” “I call you friends.” Love produces faithfulness that is joyful, durable, and wholehearted. Someone who follows out of love will stay committed long after someone who follows out of fear has given up. Fear may raise a flag or start a journey, but only love can sustain it—and only love can make it beautiful.

Sadly, highly religious people often cling to fear because it provides a sense of control, certainty, superiority, and psychological safety in a world that feels unpredictable without it. Let's unpack some of these reasons.

Religion Flourishes on Fear

Religion, in many of its institutional forms, is structured around fear because fear is the glue that keeps the system intact. If a community can convince you that your afterlife hinges on adhering to its doctrines, submitting to its authority, and maintaining membership through your time, energy, tithing and donations, then the system becomes self-sustaining. No religion can sustain itself without selling something—an idea powerful enough to persuade you to fund it and keep it alive. And nothing sells more effectively than fear.

The promise is safety: Stay with us, believe as we tell you, follow our rules, and you will be secure in the afterlife. Otherwise, our God will barbecue you forever. The threat is exclusion: Stray from us, and you risk everything. This dynamic—fear paired with promised protection—ensures loyalty, preserves institutional power, and keeps the machinery running by collecting financial support.

But if, as Paul wrote, “God was in Christ reconciling the world to Himself” (2 Cor. 5:19), then the church’s role is not to control access but to announce freedom. Not to judge but to heal. Grace always threatens systems built on exclusivity, fear, and control.

The Weight of Tradition

For over 1,500 years, Western Christianity—especially post-Augustine—has taught that unbelievers—as well as many believers—face Eternal Conscious Torment. This doctrine was passed down like family heirlooms: inherited, familiar, and rarely questioned. When challenged, verses taken out of context are used to shut people down. So when the church proclaims, “Christ died for us,” it often adds an invisible footnote: “...but only if you do X or believe Y.” That’s not good news to all. That’s a boundary on God's grace. And it misses the scope of the Cross.

Justice, Misunderstood

Too often, the religious mind equates justice with payback—someone must suffer, someone must burn. So if God eventually forgives all, is He still “just”?

It was almost comical—if it hadn’t been so morally disturbing—how fundamentalists accused Kirk Cameron of “misunderstanding justice” simply because he rejected Eternal Conscious Torment. As if true justice required God to operate like a psychopath, compelled to satisfy a sadist’s craving by torturing people forever. That wasn’t a defense of justice; it was a projection of cruelty onto God and a sanctification of moral insanity. If someone fundamentally misunderstood justice, it certainly wasn’t Cameron—it was those who mistook sadism for righteousness and dared to call it holy.

Biblical justice isn’t retribution—it’s restoration. The Cross doesn’t appease God’s wrath like a transaction or payment. It conquers evil, absorbs violence, forgives enemies, and restores humanity. God's justice is not about settling cosmic scores, but about healing the wound.

Imagine This for Just One Moment

Let go of what you think is "orthodoxy."

Put aside human creeds and religious doctrines.

Don’t be defensive.

Don’t be apologetic with yourself.

Now, with your God-given imagination, picture this:

What would it look like if every single soul—across time, culture, and history—worshiped God simultaneously?

No more suffering and pain.

No more division and fear.

No more confusion and condemnation.

No more death.

Just pure, unified awe rising from every heart and filling the entire universe.

Every knee ever created bowing in honor. Every tongue ever made confessing the name of Christ in loud praise.

Breathe in. And hold on to that image for five seconds.

Now ask yourself—how did that make you feel?

Did it bring sadness or joy? Depression or hope?

Did it make you think less or more of God?

Did it make you feel distressed, or did it awaken something deep in you—a longing to celebrate God's unimaginable power and grace?

Did it feel like the end of something…

Or the resurrection and restoration of everything?

Conclusion

Ultimately, a belief that embraces the salvation of all is a celebration of God’s absolute sovereignty and unbounded grace, presenting a God who is not only powerful enough to create the universe but also loving enough to ensure that every part of it is, eventually, reconciled to Him. This magnifies His glory and demonstrates His profound wisdom in orchestrating a plan where His love and justice triumph absolutely.

c28X

Chapter 32
"Eternal Life" and John 3:16

For God so loved the world that he gave his one and only Son, that whoever believes in him shall not perish but have eternal life.

(John 3:16)

John 3:16 is one of the most quoted Bible verses—often seen on soda cups and burger wrappers—but it’s also among the most misunderstood and taken out of context. Two key phrases, in particular, are often misinterpreted by modern readers.

The first phrase is "perish." While some assume it means being tormented forever in hell, the original hearers would not have understood the word "perish" in that way. In its original context, "perish" refers to waste, ruin, destruction, or the tragic loss of what could have been—not Eternal Conscious Torment.

The second phrase is "eternal life"—a rich and meaningful Jewish phrase that doesn’t point to quantity, like living forever, but to quality or kind—a way of life fully rooted, and deeply aligned with God's will. It represents the highest expression of spiritual living.

Let's dig deeper…

"Perish"—Lost Rather Than Perpetually Tormented

The Greek word “perish” (Apollymi) does not mean “to be tortured forever,” but speaks about this life and means to be ruined, lost, waste, or broken—like saying "her life is ruined," "he is a broken person," "such a waste of life," or "they are a lost soul." It's a metaphor for a life that has gone astray.

Jesus Himself defines the word through His three parables in Luke 15—the lost sheep, the lost coin, and the lost son. In each story, the thing that was “apollymi” was not tormented in hell forever; it was simply missing, lost, out of place, or estranged. And in every case, what was lost was eventually found, restored, and brought home again! That is the biblical meaning of “perish”: not endless torment, but a temporary state of being lost—always within the reach of a God who seeks until He finds.

The first-century followers of Jesus understood this metaphor from the Hebrew Scriptures—like Jeremiah 50:6—where Israel is described as having “perished” (or “been lost,” depending on the translation) in exile, yet was ultimately restored.

They recognized that in Jesus's stories what’s “lost” or “perished” is something valuable to God—something He searches for, longs for, and ultimately restores. So, to “perish” doesn’t mean being tortured, tormented, or wiped out—it’s about a life that’s drifted off course, lost its way, squandered, and left to waste.…

You Can't Have It Both Ways

If “eternal life” were nothing more than unending existence, then the logic collapses. If some people are said to suffer forever in hell, then they too would possess “eternal life”—just a version defined by endless torment. But Scripture does not speak in contradictions. If eternal life were merely a span of infinite time, then eternal punishment would qualify as eternal life as well.

The conclusion is unavoidable: eternal life must mean something else. It is not about duration, but about kind. It is a quality of life—life aligned with God, shaped by His teaching, animated by His Spirit, and rooted in communion with Him.

Eternal Life Now, Not After Death

Throughout his writings, John repeatedly insists that the believer already has eternal life (John 6:47; 5:24; 1 John 5:13). Notice the tense: has, not 'will have someday after death,' and not 'will receive only at the resurrection.' Eternal life, according to John, is a present possession.

If “having eternal life” were primarily about quantity—about endless duration—then believers would likely not die at all. But they do. The fact that death still occurs exposes the category mistake. Eternal life is not defined first by how long it lasts, but by what kind of life it is.

This makes John’s theology unmistakably clear: eternal life is, first and foremost, about nature, not duration—a divine kind of life already at work in the present, long before the grave is emptied.

“Eternal Life” in Biblical Judaism

A striking insight emerges when we examine how the Jewish phrase eternal life—chayei olam in Hebrew—was understood in the time of Jesus and in later Jewish thought.75 Chayei olam was considered an honorary term and was often set in contrast with chayei sha’ah—literally, “superficial life”, “fleeting life,” or “perishing life.”

Chayei sha’ah describes superficial kind of life consumed by mere survival: working, earning, eating, sleeping—endlessly managing the necessities of existence without ever touching its purpose. It is life reduced to maintenance. By contrast, chayei olam refers not to infinite duration, but to a depth and quality of life rooted in God—life infused with meaning, blessing, and participation in the eternal purposes of heaven, even while lived on earth.

In contrast, chayei olam referred to a spiritually charged way of life—one lived in alignment with divine values. It described a blessed existence shaped by righteousness, purpose, and enduring significance. While it did carry implications beyond death, its primary emphasis was never escapist or otherworldly. It was about living now in harmony with God’s will—participating in eternal life in the present, not postponing it to the afterlife.

This understanding sheds light on Jesus’ words in John 17:3:

Now this is eternal life: that they know you, the only true God, and Jesus Christ, whom you have sent. 

(John 17:3)

Here, "eternal life" is not described in terms of time or location, but in terms of essence, quality, substance. Eternal life is a unique kind of spiritual understanding. It isn't about physics (time and space) but about union. To "know" someone means to be close and intimate with them (e.g., Genesis 4:1). Eternal life—knowing God—begins now, not after death.

To “know God” in this sense is not merely cognitive or intellectual. It is relational, experiential, and transformative—it’s to live chayei olam here and now.

Therefore, “eternal life”—in the Hebrew mindset—wasn’t about time, duration, or going to a far-off place after death. It was about a kind of life—a life rooted in God, rich in meaning, filled with love, peace, justice, and divine presence. It was the kingdom of God—breaking into the present.

Furthermore, since Jesus hadn’t yet died, the phrase “whoever believes in Him shall have eternal life” (John 3:16) couldn’t have been referring to a guaranteed ticket to heaven based on belief in His sacrificial death. Instead, it was an invitation to enter a new way of living. To believe in Him meant following His way. To move from carnal desires into true, blessed, abundant life by walking His path.

Eternal Life Is To Know God

According to Jesus, eternal life wasn’t about floating in the clouds forever—it was about truly knowing God, His will, His character, and His ways. And knowing God isn’t some magical download of facts or memorizing doctrinal lines; it’s something lived out in real relationships—through loving others.

To know God means learning what He loves and what grieves Him, understanding what He desires from us, and discovering how to walk with Him in trust and obedience. It’s knowing what brings Him joy, how to align our hearts with His, and how to live a life that reflects His character.

Knowing God isn’t abstract like a seminary degree—it’s personal, practical, and deeply transformative. It’s not just about intellectually believing God exists and that Jesus is the Messiah; it’s about becoming one with Him, one with His will—living the very life we were created for. Eternal life wasn’t about endless time, but about the honor of experiencing the highest kind of spiritual life. Quality, not quantity.

Why the West Thinks as It Does

Since Jews in biblical times did not understand “eternal life” as an everlasting, never-ending duration, we shouldn’t interpret it that way either. This interpretation did not come from the Hebrew Scriptures or from the Jewish worldview held by Jesus and His disciples. Instead, it arose later, shaped by Greek philosophical categories—an obsession with quantifying time, metaphysical permanence, abstract timelessness, and mathematical measurement.

Greek philosophical influence, combined with later Christian misunderstandings of Jewish idioms, disconnected the phrase from its original context and transformed it into a statement about duration and the afterlife rather than divine quality and participation in God’s life.

The Hebrew mind was not concerned with time in the abstract. The concept of time was never debated. For the ancient Israelites, the focus was on relationships, covenant faithfulness, divine presence, and blessing. It was about the quality of life, not the number of years.

Yes, many Jews believed in immortality—not as infinite linear time, but as life so strong that it can't be extinguished.

This perspective also clarifies the meaning of the “narrow road that leads to life” (Matthew 7:14). It is not a biological or literal statement about the afterlife, but a spiritual one—a narrow way that leads to a rich, blessed, abundant kind of life: a life aligned with God.

When Jesus spoke about eternal life, small gates, and narrow roads, He had not yet gone to the cross. No one listening to Him imagined that He was about to die for their sins so they could go to heaven.

Whoever Believes = Whoever Follows

Whoever hears my word and believes him who sent me has eternal life and will not be judged but has crossed over from death to life.

(John 5:24)

Whatever “whoever hears my word and believes” meant in that moment, it could not have meant “believe that I will one day die for your sins.” Reading it that way imports a later theological system back into an earlier teaching—a textbook case of anachronism.

Instead, Jesus was calling His hearers not to a future transaction, but to a present way of life. The narrow road leads to life now—a life of faithfulness, blessing, and alignment with God’s reign—long before the grave.

Jesus was a Jewish rabbi. In that world, to hear a rabbi’s word did not mean passive listening or intellectual agreement—it meant obedience, imitation, alignment. To hear was to follow. To believe was to entrust one’s life to the teacher’s wisdom and way. People did not seek out rabbis for abstract doctrines, but for a path. They believed a true teacher carried the wisdom—the secret, so to speak—to a life of blessing, meaning, and harmony with God.

To believe Jesus, then, was to entrust oneself to His way of life and to begin walking it. Hearing and believing are inseparable from following. Living by His teachings is living eternal life—a blessed, God-aligned life—here and now, liberated from perishing life and condemnation, and crossing over from judgment to life.

So when Jesus says, “Follow me,” “Believe me,” or “Hear my word,” He is doing something radically subversive. He is placing Himself above every competing voice—religious authorities, political powers, social expectations—and calling people to trust His way as the supreme path to life. Eternal life, in this sense, is not postponed until after death; it is entered the moment one crosses from death into life by walking in His way.

If I were to paraphrase John 3:16 for someone unfamiliar with the Jewish worldview and context, it might sound something like this:

"For God so loved the world that He gave up His one and only Son, that whoever follows His ways shall not waste their lives but have a blessed life because it's aligned with God's will."

Case Study: Why "Eternal Life" Couldn't Be About Escaping Eternal Conscious Torment in Hellfire

Teacher, what good thing must I do to inherit eternal life? 

(Matthew 19:16)

Jesus responds, “If you want to enter life, keep the commandments” (Matthew 19:17). Notice the language: eternal life and entering life are treated as interchangeable. Not as an escape from hell, but as participation in the fullness of God’s kingdom blessings.

"Eternal life," "entering life," "entering through the narrow gate," "entering the kingdom," "walking the narrow path," and "inheriting the kingdom"—these are not metaphors for an afterlife destination, but for a kind of life—already available to us in the present.

Jesus is not offering a transactional shortcut to heaven for those who merely agree with Him intellectually. He was inviting the rich man into a transformed existence aligned with God’s reign—here and now, in embodied faithfulness, not deferred salvation.

Then, Jesus also added, “If you want to be perfect, go, sell your possessions and give to the poor…” (v.21). Here, perfection isn’t moral flawlessness—it’s becoming one with God's will. It's sacrificing for His kingdom to enjoy His blessings.

If “eternal life” or “entering life” meant escaping hell by adhering to doctrine, obeying commandments and performing good deeds, then Jesus would have been teaching salvation by works. But Jesus wasn’t offering an escape route from divine torture; He was simply inviting the man—and us—into life with God. A life of divine abundance, a life aligned with God’s nature, a life of deep, spiritual intimacy with glorious rewards in the age to come.

The rich man’s wealth was the obstacle, not because money damns, but because it dulled his soul to the kind of life Jesus was offering. The man wanted to be blessed by God—yet wasn’t willing to let go of earthly wealth to receive heavenly treasure.

Paul's "Inherit" is Jesus' "Inherit"

The word “inherit” is key. In both Jesus’ conversation with the rich man and Paul’s writings (e.g., 1 Corinthians 6:9–10), inheriting the kingdom is not about escaping never-ending torment—it’s about receiving one's portion or reward in the present age and in the age of Messianic restoration.

When the rich man asks what he must do to inherit eternal life, he’s not talking about avoiding hell, but about receiving heavenly reward—gaining a share in God’s kingdom. Jesus responds by inviting him into a life of sacrificial discipleship—but the rich man turns away, unwilling to let go of his wealth. The rich man had to give up his wealth to gain an even greater reward—becoming a disciple of Jesus Christ. Literally.

In the same way, Paul warns that “neither thieves nor the greedy nor drunkards nor slanderers nor swindlers will inherit the kingdom of God” (1 Corinthians 6:9–10). This isn’t about an afterlife of Eternal Conscious Torment; it’s about forfeiting the rewards and blessings of God’s kingdom, both now and in the age to come.

“Eternal Judgment”

In biblical language, “eternal judgment” does not mean an endlessly ongoing process of judging, but symbolizes a complete, decisive, and irreversible judgment—one whose results are permanent, not one that is endlessly administered.

A clear example is Sodom and Gomorrah. Jude 7 speaks of them undergoing “the punishment of eternal fire,” yet the cities are not still burning today. The fire was not infinite but decisive and total—it accomplished its purpose. Again—quality rather than quantity.

Likewise, Scripture says Edom’s fire “will not be quenched night or day” (Isaiah 34:9–10), yet Edom is not presently aflame. The imagery communicates finality and completeness, not infinite duration of time.

To summarize, in the Bible, eternal life describes the quality, value, or kind of life, not its length in measurable time. Likewise, eternal judgment means a judgment that is thorough, conclusive, and beyond appeal—not one that perpetuates punishment forever:

For the wicked there are punishments, not perpetual, however, lest the immortality prepared for them should be a disadvantage. But they are to be purified for a brief period according to the amount of malice in their works. They shall therefore suffer punishment for a short space, but immortal blessedness having no end awaits them...the penalties to be inflicted for their many and grave sins are very far surpassed by the magnitude of the mercy to be showed to them.76

(St. Diodore of Tarsus 320—394 AD)

In short:

Eternal judgment (or eternal fire) = A judgment that's total. Complete. Accomplished. Not endless torment, but an irrevocable act whose consequences fully stand.

Eternal life = intimacy with and knowledge of God, blessings, reward, spiritual fulfillment.

Conclusion—The World Was Perishing Until God Sent His Son

In the Hebrew imagination, eternal spoke less of duration and more of depth—of quality, weight, fullness. Eternal life was a divine kind of life: intimacy with God, spiritual vitality, meaning, and blessing. It stood in stark contrast to perishing—not as endless conscious misery, but as a life drained of purpose and communion. This is not the Western obsession with infinite timelines or mathematical duration, but a profoundly Jewish vision of life infused with God. When Jesus spoke of “eternal life,” He was not offering an endless clock. He was offering a transformed way of living—a life finally aligned with its Creator.

The all-encompassing scope of John 3:16 is impossible to escape. God so loved the world—not a fraction, not a select group within it, but the world in its entirety. And the very next verse removes all ambiguity:

For God did not send His Son into the world to condemn the world, but to save the world through Him.

(John 3:17)

So we must ask the unavoidable question: did Christ fail? Did the Son sent to save the world succeed only marginally—five percent, ten percent, a lucky remnant—while the rest slip beyond redemption?

If God’s intent were merely to rescue a small subset of humanity from infinite torment, the text would speak the language of exclusion, not the sweeping, world-embracing vocabulary of divine love. What makes God truly Mighty to Save is not merely that He desires salvation, but that He accomplishes it. His will is not a fragile wish contingent on human comprehension or performance. When God purposes redemption, He does not merely offer the possibility of salvation—He brings salvation to its intended completion.

The world was perishing. God sent His Son. And God does not fail. Yet you are invited to step into this kind of life—eternal life—not through mental assent or theological agreement, but through becoming a true disciple of Jesus Christ. Eternal life is entered by walking in His ways, embodying His teachings, and allowing His life to take shape in yours here and now.

c2BX

Chapter 33
The Gnostic Shadow Behind Today’s Gospel

In the ages to come He might show the immeasurable riches of His grace.

(Ephesians 2:7)

There is a persistent religious arrogance in the West—the assumption that we have God neatly figured out, the sin of certainty. But that illusion will eventually give way to something far richer: the joy of becoming lifelong students of divine mystery.

If God is boundless in nature, inexhaustible in wisdom, and eternal in knowledge, then His knowing is not static or finite. It is infinite, living, and endlessly unfolding—and so must be our posture before Him.

Logically, this resonates with the nature of infinity itself. You cannot exhaust an infinite source. And if we are created in the image of God, then our souls are not satisfied by stasis. We long to grow, expand, and uncover what is hidden. That longing is not a flaw—it is evidence of design.

In other words, we know very little, so we should always remain humble and teachable.

That means neither I, nor your pastor, nor a seminary professor knows everything—no matter how authoritative we may appear standing behind a pulpit, dressed in a suit and tie, speaking with confident certainty. Appearance and confidence are not the same as omniscience, and no human voice can exhaust the mystery of God.

This also means paradise won't be boring. It’s not a frozen place of singing the same songs over and over again. It is a never-ending exploration of beauty, love, wisdom, and relationships. Imagine learning new dimensions of grace every day. Imagine meeting love in deeper ways every hour.

Morally, this calls us to embrace a lifelong openness to learning. It rebukes spiritual pride and reminds us: if we are to learn forever, then we must stay humble forever. And if there is no ceiling to what God can show us, then every person we meet may carry a glimpse of His truth we’ve yet to see.

This is why I regard Systematic Theology as a well-intentioned but ultimately flawed human attempt to force God into a box. It offers the illusion that God has been figured out, neatly organized, and resolved—when in reality it is an effort to confine the Infinite within tidy categories He never agreed to inhabit.

What I've Learned From Teaching Systematic Theology

I spent about 20 years serving in fundamentalist Messianic ministries and academia. These schools and ministries were heavily influenced by fundamentalist Christian groups, primarily grounded in Calvinistic and Pentecostal doctrine.

Theologically, the Messianic scene in Israel is overwhelmingly Reformed (Calvinist)—especially among the wealthiest and most influential ministries. And while the term Systematic Theology doesn’t have a single inventor, over time it developed into the central pillar of Reformed theology.

We were sent to study fundamentalist Calvinistic theology at American institutions. Then we returned and taught it rigorously at our "messianic" Bible college, indoctrinating a new generation as if our theology represented the singular and final truth of God. In our human—and often arrogant—attempt to dissect and categorize the Infinite like a laboratory specimen, we routinely tore verses from their context and forced them into neatly constructed, man-made systems. In doing so, we became architects of a rigid box, attempting to contain the very Spirit we claimed to serve.

And the irony persists. To this day, there is only one Systematic Theology available to the Messianic movement in Hebrew—a Calvinistic volume first published in 1986 and only recently translated and promoted by One For Israel Ministry. A single Reformed framework, imported, elevated, and treated as definitive—offered to a people whose Scriptures were never meant to be imprisoned in such a system.

The Failed Attempt to Systematize God

Back then, the more systematic and polished our messianic doctrine became, the narrower and more exclusive our spiritual tolerance grew. As our theology hardened, our capacity to live with disagreement collapsed. The explosion of boycotts within such a tiny movement was not accidental—it was inevitable. I could already see where that road ended: the walls of our beliefs closing in tighter and tighter until, eventually, the path would be wide enough for only one person to walk it.

That trajectory explains much of the dehumanization I witnessed—and still witness—within the Messianic movement. Jews, Catholics, and even fellow Protestants dismissed as “merely nominal” Christians are casually condemned to Eternal Conscious Torment. When theology becomes a weapon of certainty, compassion is the first casualty. What remains is not faithfulness, but fear disguised as conviction.

And this is the framework that I—like many other ex-fundamentalists—eventually had to dismantle, piece by piece, in order to recover a vision of God that is actually faithful to our Jewish Scriptures, grounded in the Jewish roots of our faith, and aligned with the Spirit and teachings of our Jewish Rabbi. Of course, I’m not talking about today's rabbinic form of Judaism, but rather the version found in the Bible and associated with Jesus (e.g., Luke 2:21-41).

Why am I telling you all this? Because it’s helpful in understanding where I came from and the theological DNA that shaped Israel's modern Messianic movement and its ministries (all originally founded by Westerners)—a lens that absorbed, rather than questioned, Western/Augustinian assumptions about God, atonement, salvation, hell, and even the very heart of the gospel. Global donations didn’t just fund the movement; they defined its theology.

And what I came to witness during my time in the movement is that wealth mixed with doctrine and dogma often serve as tools to divide, turning anyone who disagrees with you into an “other.” They strip people of their humanity and dignity, elevating their own doctrinal statement to something idolized and worshipped, believing salvation depends on doctrinal precision.

The Gnostic shadow is unmistakable in the assumption that salvation is achieved through mastering intellectual knowledge.

Fundamentalism often comes across as overconfident, speaking as if it holds the ultimate truth. Wisdom, on the other hand, walks with humility, aware it can never grasp the entire picture (1 Corinthians 13:12).

For We Walk By Faith, Not By Sight

Belief doesn’t require knowing everything. Faith was never meant to be total comprehension. Scripture itself affirms belief without sight, trust without full understanding, and hope rooted in what is still unseen (Hebrews 11:1; John 20:29; 2 Corinthians 5:7; Romans 8:24–25; 1 Peter 1:8–9). And that’s perfectly fine, because then you won’t insist on forcing everyone into a “you-shaped” theology.

If you were blind, you would not be guilty of sin; but now that you claim you can see, your guilt remains.

(John 9:41)

God extends compassion and mercy to the humble and teachable, but He confronts—and judges—the intellectually proud and spiritually arrogant. Blindness is not the greatest danger; the claim of perfect sight is.

Humility is not a weakness—it is a gift. When faith no longer depends on having all the answers, it stops feeling threatened by questions and loses the urge to coerce others into the narrow confines of its own dogma. Humility makes room for strength without cruelty, conviction without domination, and truth without fear. It allows us to believe deeply without dehumanizing those who believe differently, to walk confidently without trampling dignity.

Imagine what Christianity might look like to the outside world if Protestants did not damn Catholics to hell, and Catholics returned the favor—if disagreement did not require mutual condemnation. Imagine a faith mature enough to disagree without anathematizing, to hold convictions without weaponizing them.

I say this as someone who believes the entire Western—both Catholic and Protestant—framework of the gospel is deeply distorted. And yet, I also believe that every person within those systems is fully loved, fully seen, and fully embraced by God. Truth does not require contempt, and love does not require uniformity.

To Be "Born Again" Into Modern-Day Gnosticism

“If you agree, you can be born again right now by simply repeating this short prayer after me,” we would tell people on streets and university campuses—handing out tracts, dressed in matching evangelistic T-shirts.

The phrase “born again” in John 3:3 couldn’t have originally meant believing in Jesus’ death for the forgiveness of sins, since at that point he hadn’t died yet, and his death wasn’t anticipated. This implies that, originally, the term carried an entirely different meaning.77

The belief that the faith God is after is intellectual agreement with the right doctrine or creed—and that it can "save" someone from Eternal Conscious Torment in hell is, at its heart, the essence of Gnosticism—an ancient heresy that was absorbed into Western Christianity in the 4th century.78,79,80

Scholars argue that this was caused mainly by Augustine of Hippo,81 who was influenced by Gnostic thought primarily because he spent nearly a decade as an auditor within Manichaeism, a late‑antique religious movement widely recognized by historians as a major expression of Gnostic cosmology. Although Augustine rejected Manichaean doctrine at one point, researchers note that his mature theology—like his views on evil, the will, and interiority—retains conceptual marks shaped by his earlier immersion in a dualistic, knowledge‑centered system. Modern studies of Augustine’s intellectual development emphasize that his writings, his doctrine of the bound will, and his intense introspective method in Confessions all reflect a mind formed in dialogue with Gnostic categories even as he reworked them into Christian orthodoxy. As Johannes van Oort observes, Augustine’s long engagement with Manichaean teachers and texts left “a permanent imprint on his theological imagination,” even after his conversion to Nicene Christianity.82

The Gnostics believed salvation came through gnosis—special, hidden from the world knowledge that “enlightened” the soul and enabled rebirth. In a disturbingly similar way, many modern Western gospel presentations reduce salvation to what a person can mentally grasp or intellectually affirm in order "to be born again." The result is a gospel transformed into a cerebral Gnostic exam, where eternal destiny hinges not on healing, transformation, or participation in divine life, but on passing a test of correct beliefs.

Whatever way Christianity may have been infiltrated by Gnosticism in the past, what truly matters is that today Gnosticism still undermines the core message of the gospel in the West—just under a different guise. It's an ancient heresy that's all around us, and it reduces salvation to a private theory—an esoteric knowledge one must intellectually affirm to be born again and escape Eternal Conscious Torment in hellfire. In this distorted view, the gospel became a mental transaction: believe the right idea, pray a prayer, check the right mental box, and you're off the hook.

You can even test yourself to see how much Gnosticism has influenced your thinking.

Consider a simple example: someone believes in Christ, prays, and is told they are “saved.” Later in life, however, they intellectually begin to question or disagree with a doctrine such as the Trinity, or have theologically shifted from one denomination to another. Are they still saved?

Many Christians instinctively answer 'no.' But that response reveals something important. It shifts salvation from what God has done in Christ toward correct knowledge of doctrinal formulas. In other words, salvation becomes dependent on your mind's ability to possess the right information.

It’s a repackaged Gnosticism disguised as Christianity.

Biblical faith, however, was not about mastering intellectual formulas but about hearts transformed to better love others.

Theology is crucial because what creed you affirm or the doctrine you follow will shape how clearly you see God and represent (or misrepresent) Christ to the world. Knowing what you believe matters—but above knowledge, Scripture tells us that love is what matters most (1 Peter 4:8; 1 Corinthians 13:13).

True theology gives birth to true faith—not to sterile intellectualism, but to a faith embodied in love. Love is not a feeling to be admired or a concept to be debated; it is something practiced. It moves toward others. It acts. It gives. It sacrifices itself for the sake of another.

At the end of the day, everyone is a theologian of sorts, because everyone holds beliefs about the Divine. The difference is that some are simply bad theologians—and that truth applies to Christian theologians as much as anyone else.

Understanding Biblical Faith

In biblical times, belief wasn’t an abstract idea or a formula you mentally signed off on. Belief was a living force—so potent, so embodied, that Jesus said it could move mountains (Matthew 17:20), or in modern language—make the impossible possible. Faith wasn’t mere assent; it was an active trust that reshaped how a person lived and loved, and what kind of path they walked. Following the right way of faith—Jesus’ way—meant living a blessed life that showed God’s will in action.

Genesis 15:6 says, “Abram believed the Lord, and He credited it to him as righteousness.” This verse is often referenced as support for the doctrine of salvation by faith alone—particularly in Protestant theology—but interpreting it this way removes it from its original ancient context.

In Genesis 15, Abraham’s faith is not abstract or merely intellectual. It follows a dramatic covenantal promise in which God tells a childless, aging man that his descendants will outnumber the stars that he could see. Abraham’s belief is not just mental agreement—it is trust that manifests in action: leaving his land (Genesis 12); building altars; preparing sacrificial animals (Genesis 15:9-10); and later even being willing to offer up his son (Genesis 22). His faith is embodied faith—faith that does something:

Was not our father Abraham considered righteous for what he did when he offered his son Isaac on the altar? You see that his faith and his actions were working together, and his faith was made complete by what he did. 

(James 2:21-22)

James' point was not that Abraham was “saved” by works. His faith had nothing to do with escaping hellfire or securing paradise after death—none of that is in the text. Nor are we “saved” by what we do or by what we believe. We are saved—forgiven and will be raised—not from ourselves, for it is the gift of God. Humanity doesn't save itself; He alone is the Savior of the world.

Abraham’s faith was not an abstract belief but a lived obedience. It meant leaving Ur of the Chaldeans (Genesis 11:31) and walking roughly 2,500 kilometers—about 1,500 miles—leaving safety, familiarity, and certainty behind, and stepping into a promised unknown. And Abraham's faith brought him a different kind of salvation—the biblical kind, filled with divine blessings and protection.

My point, and I think James would agree, is this: in biblical terms, faith was something you did, not merely something you assented to intellectually. In Abraham’s case, faith meant trusting God enough to move, to obey, and to stake his life on God’s promise.

James presents Abraham as the definitive proof that genuine faith is never passive or merely theoretical. True faith reveals its authenticity through action. Whether in his willingness to cross the earth or to offer Isaac, Abraham demonstrated that trust in God is completed, matured, and made visible through obedience. Biblical faith, therefore, is not a slogan to memorize or a confession to recite, but a lived reality—faith embodied in costly, concrete movement.

The God of Israel does not begin with rage. He begins with covenant. He begins with love. There is no threat of Eternal Conscious Torment anywhere in Genesis—or anywhere in the Hebrew Scriptures, for that matter. What we find instead is promise.

Abraham’s faith is not fear-driven but promise-shaped. It is trust in God’s intention to bless him, to work through him, and to make him a blessing to the nations (Genesis 12:2–3; 15:5–6). Faith here is covenantal loyalty—alignment with God’s redemptive plan for the healing of the world. It is not an escape hatch from postmortem torture, but a commitment to participate in God’s work of restoration here and now.

Saving Faith Is Lived, Not Merely Believed

If you believe—as I once did—that “salvation” means rescue from hellfire and is secured by “[intellectual] faith alone,” then what you are really saying is that your intellectual understanding saves you from hell while the beliefs of others damn them to it. That posture almost inevitably breeds condescension and spiritual arrogance. You “figured it out.” Or worse, God uniquely “chose you.” Salvation quietly becomes a badge of superiority.

Once you believe that it is your faith that moves God’s hand, the weight of salvation subtly shifts from God to the individual. Salvation becomes something you must mentally grasp, correctly interpret, and personally execute. It is no longer something God has decisively done for you, but something you must successfully perform—intellectually, doctrinally, and psychologically.

But truth be told, “saving faith” never had anything to do with escaping Eternal Conscious Torment in the first place. Saving faith is the kind of faith that actually saves. It saves the starving by feeding them. It saves the homeless by sheltering them on cold winter nights. It steps out of the boat—and out of the cozy seat in the church building—into storm-tossed waters. It chooses sacrificial love, even toward an enemy. It stands courageously for justice, but also for the oppressed, even when it costs something.

This is the faith Jesus embodied. Not passive intellectual assent, but living fidelity to God expressed through love, mercy, and courage.

Jesus was not a Greek philosopher constructing abstract theories. He was a Jewish rabbi, teaching through truth and action how to live in a way that glorifies God.
