This is also the faith God recognized in Abram: a heart willing to trust, to leave certainty behind, to venture into the unknown, and to stake everything on unseen promises. The question, then, is not which faith produces fruit—every faith does. The real question is: what kind of fruit does your faith produce? Because the fruit always reveals the root.

Good fruit is not merely maintaining religious traditions, attending church every Sunday, or reading the Bible every morning. These practices can be good and meaningful, but they are not what the New Testament means by “the fruit of the Spirit.”

When faith is genuine and biblical, its fruit is unmistakable: love, joy, peace, righteousness, patience, kindness, gentleness, humility, compassion.

But when the fruit is rotten—arrogance, pride, harshness, selfishness, impatience, cruelty, corruption, aggression, ostracization—then something is profoundly wrong with the faith itself, regardless of how many seminary degrees one holds or how eloquently one preaches from a pulpit. And this is not only true of individuals, but of entire churches, movements, and denominations.

Faith that saves will always look like love in motion. Anything else is just theology pretending to be faith.

Jesus' Own Saving Faith

Think about it: Jesus never roamed the countryside terrorizing people with questions like, “If you died tonight, would you go to heaven or hell?” Instead, He healed lepers. He fed the hungry. He spoke blessing over the poor. He announced that the kingdom of God was at hand.

In Jesus’ vocabulary, faith and salvation were never an afterlife lottery. They were the in-breaking of God’s reign into real time. They were the good news that what was broken is being restored, what was lost is being found, and what was dead is rising to life.

That is the saving faith Scripture calls us to embrace. It is about restoration, healing, liberation, return, and life. And it begins here—now—by unlearning what we have inherited and rediscovering what the Scriptures actually proclaim. It begins by dismantling man-made gods and allowing our vision to be re-formed by the incomprehensible yet revealed God—the God of Abraham, Moses, and Jesus.

The Incomprehensible God

Acts records Paul preaching to the Athenians at the Areopagus and pointing out the altar to the “unknown God” (Acts 17:23). This is a poor translation. Agnostos Theos is the incomprehensible God, the one beyond human understanding and wisdom. That’s exactly why Paul chose that altar. But when people today try to put that God in a box and define every aspect of Him “systematically,” they end up placing themselves above the Almighty, creating a god in their own image.

The study of biblical theology is deeply valuable, and as a theologian, I wholeheartedly affirm its significance—after all, it’s exactly what we’re engaging in throughout this book. However, I must also point out how greatly the gospel message has been skewed by the Systematic theology taught in so many seminaries today.

Faith and salvation were never meant to hinge on intellectual mastery, as though paradise could be earned by holding the correct ideas about God. The moment human intellect or “special enlightenment” becomes the key to salvation, we drift into Gnosticism and distort the very heart of the Gospel—shrinking it from a message meant to transform lives, societies, and the world into a system obsessed with being right to avoid Eternal Conscious Torment.

Modern gospel presentations often dilute the power of grace by shifting the focus from what God has decisively done to what we must figure out, comprehend, or get right so He does not torture or burn us in some cosmic incinerator. In doing so, the gospel is reduced to a fear-driven theology exam rather than proclaimed as what it truly is: a bold announcement of God’s loving, redemptive action in the world—an invitation to have our lives transformed and to participate in the healing of everything around us.

Conclusion

Release the heavy burden of needing to “be right,” and step into the freedom of being loved. Lay down the urge to win theological debates, and let it be replaced by a deeper desire to love others well. Your faith is not a fortress to be defended, but a garden to be tended—a quiet, courageous trust that blossoms in the dirt of everyday life through every act of kindness and every pursuit of justice. Trust that the Incomprehensible God is not waiting for you to solve a theological puzzle, but is already walking beside you, inviting you to be the hands that heal and the heart that reflects His infinite grace in this world.

In the end, faith is not about intellectually mastering God in seminary, but about being mastered by love. The moment we release the illusion that we must have everything figured out, we become free to learn, to grow, and to be transformed. God is not threatened by our questions, nor confined by our systems; He invites us into an endless journey of trust, humility, and discovery. And in that posture—not of certainty, but of openness—we finally find rest, because our hope was never meant to stand on knowledge or perception, but on a God who is infinitely patient, endlessly gracious, and faithful far beyond our understanding.

c2ET

Chapter 34
Is God Truly Mighty to Save?

What makes God God, by definition, is that He is the absolute best conceivable being—not just great but perfect in every possible way and lacking nothing in anything that can be. And if God is truly God—perfect in power, wisdom, and love—then His will must ultimately prevail.

The Hebrew Scriptures are unshakably clear: “Our God is in the heavens; He does whatever pleases Him” (Psalm 115:3). “Is anything too difficult for the LORD?” God asks Abraham (Genesis 18:14). Through the prophet Isaiah, He declares, “My word… will not return to me empty, but will accomplish what I desire and achieve the purpose for which I sent it” (Isaiah 55:11).

God isn’t just a reflection of our best ideas, but far greater than anything we can imagine. God’s omnipotence means He is not just stronger than every created being—He is infinitely stronger. His omniscience means He is not just wiser than any angel, human, or beast—He perfectly knows every thought, every detail, every outcome, every possibility. And His omnibenevolence means He is not just better than others—He is pure good. He doesn’t merely want good things for the world—He is good.

If this is true—if God is truly as powerful, wise, and loving as Scripture proclaims, then the most important question in all of theology is this: will His will ultimately be done?

Can God Fail? Can We Trust His Word?

The Apostle Paul gives us the heart of God’s will in plain words: “God our Savior, who wants all people to be saved and to come to a knowledge of the truth” (1 Timothy 2:3–4). Not some. Not a chosen few. All.

This verse is not controversial in what it says—but it becomes deeply controversial in how many Christians try to explain it away. Some theologians, particularly in the Calvinist T.U.L.I.P. tradition,83 argue that in practice, God only chooses to save a few ("Unconditional election"). And that Jesus died for them only, not for all, rendering His love limited ("Limited atonement").

But if God truly wills the salvation of all, and yet only a few end up saved, a disturbing dilemma emerges. Either God lacks the power to accomplish His will—rendering Him weak—or His love is somehow deficient (wanting to save only a few), implying that a greater love is possible, a world in which He saves more than He does here.

Either option strips God of what makes Him God. Worse still, it portrays Him as ultimately surrendering to fragile human will, distorted understanding, and the very forces of evil that shape them—rather than reigning over them, overcoming them, and bringing His purposes to completion.

It is utterly absurd to imagine that the stubbornness of a finite, limited, and often confused creature could exert greater influence than the will of God Himself.

But the God of Scripture doesn’t fail. “No purpose of Yours can be thwarted,” says Job (Job 42:2). “He works all things according to the counsel of His will” (Ephesians 1:11), confirms Paul. If God wants something—and He is God—then He eventually gets it.

Which means this: if God truly wants all people to be saved—to come to the knowledge of Him—then that is precisely what will ultimately happen. Anything less would render Him less than God.

The Cosmic Battle—What’s at Stake?

Imagine a cosmic battle between God and the forces of evil, each vying for control over human souls. If most of humanity ends in destruction—or if the enemy claims even one soul that God loves—can we truly say God has won? What kind of victory would that be? An end in which the enemy drags billions into Eternal Conscious Torment, while God watches powerlessly from the sidelines? If that's the story, then Satan isn’t defeated. He’s extremely successful.

But the biblical authors disagree. The Hebrew prophet Isaiah declares that “every knee will bow, and every tongue will swear allegiance” (45:23). Paul echoes this in Philippians 2:10 and Romans 14:11: “Every knee will bow… every tongue will confess.” These aren’t the desperate confessions of the doomed in their final moments before the axe falls, but the willing surrender of hearts that have finally seen the truth. The truth that God is not a tyrant, but a Father. Not a monster, but a Healer. Not a torturer, but a Redeemer.

If God is truly “mighty to save” (Zephaniah 3:17), then Christ’s mission “to save the world” (John 3:17; John 12:47) is not a hopeful wish—it is a certainty. He will not lose the war. Not to evil. Not to sin. Not to death. Not to human stubbornness. Not to demonic powers.

He came to rescue the world, and He will accomplish exactly what He came to do—all of it.

The Weak god of Calvinism

One of the most tragic missteps in Christian history was the rise of a theology that, in the name of honoring God's sovereignty, ended up diminishing His love. Calvinism's doctrine of Unconditional Election teaches that God has pre-elected only a tiny portion of humanity to be saved, predestining the rest to burn forever by divine decree. Jesus, in this view, died only for "the elect," while the rest were excluded from the start. This is called Limited Atonement in Calvinistic theology.

But let's be honest about what these “unconditional election” and “limited atonement” doctrines really mean: These doctrines show that His love is limited and conditional, His grace is diminished, and His will to save all people and the world is either partially effective or an outright lie. The god of Calvinism couldn't be the God of Jesus, even when he tries his best.

Calvinism stands in stark contrast to the New Testament. Jesus healed the crowds. He fed the multitudes. He forgave sinners freely and extended His mercy to Gentiles, Samaritans, prostitutes, and tax collectors. His life was an embodiment of inclusion and universal love. How, then, can we imagine His death was only for a limited few?

If God only chose some to be saved, and left the rest to damnation, we are no longer dealing with a Father who “so loved the world” (John 3:16), but with a sovereign who plays favorites and lets most of His image bearers burn.

But that’s not what the Gospel says. It says that God “wants all people to be saved” (1 Timothy 2:4). That "God is the Savior of all people" (1 Timothy 4:10). That Christ died for “the sins of the whole world” (1 John 2:2). That “God was in Christ reconciling the world to Himself” (2 Corinthians 5:19). That “as in Adam all die, so in Christ all will be made alive” (1 Corinthians 15:22). The picture is not narrow or selective—it’s universal and inclusive.

According to Romans 5:12, “sin entered the world through one man, and death through sin, and in this way death came to all people.” If Adam’s sin truly condemned all humanity (to death, not to hell), but Christ’s work saves only a few, then Adam’s failure proves stronger than Jesus’ victory. Yet Scripture says the opposite: “As in Adam all die, so also in Christ all will be made alive” (1 Corinthians 15:22), and “one act of righteousness leads to justification and life for all” (Romans 5:18). A gospel where Adam affects everyone but Christ rescues only some diminishes Christ and contradicts Paul’s logic that grace must abound more than sin (Romans 5:15–21).

Limited Atonement is a weak atonement. A partial victory is not a real victory. And a god who can’t—or won’t—save what He loves is not the Father of my Rabbi. If Jesus came to destroy the works of the devil (1 John 3:8), then His success will be total. If He promised to draw all people to Himself (John 12:32), then He will. If He is the Good Shepherd who leaves the 99 to find the one that perished into the wilderness, then eventually, every last one will be brought home.

God does not lose. Not even one.

The Power of a Perfect Will

And when all things shall be subdued unto him, then shall the Son also himself be subject unto him that put all things under him, that God may be all in all.

(1 Corinthians 15:28)

If you believe that God is all-powerful, all-knowing, and most loving, then the only logical conclusion is that He will save all and that He will be all in all.

Now, let me explain…

The question we must all wrestle with is not “Does God want to save everyone?”—Scripture already answers that (1 Timothy 2:4). The real question is, will He succeed?

Is God truly wise enough, loving enough, and powerful enough to accomplish His will—the salvation of all people? Or is He ultimately thwarted by fragile human will? By Satan? By sin? Does He merely wish for all to be saved, only to stand powerless when they resist?

If God is the wisest of all, will He not eventually persuade? If He is love itself, will He abandon His own purpose? And if He cannot accomplish what He wills, then is He really the God revealed in Scripture?

God is not a hopeful optimist, crossing His fingers that at least some humans will choose wisely. He is not reactive. If His will is that ultimately all be saved, then that’s precisely what will happen. If it is not done—then it wasn’t really His will. Or worse—He is not strong enough nor wise enough to accomplish what He desires, and thus we should look for a better god elsewhere.

The Church Fathers' Understanding of God's Will

This is where the logic of the early Church Fathers kicks in. If God is truly omniscient (all-wise), omnipotent (all-powerful), and omnibenevolent (most-loving), and if He truly desires that all be saved—then the only conclusion that makes any logical or theological sense is that all will be saved. This isn’t just my own logic, but the way many Church Fathers—long before MacArthur, Edwards, Calvin, or Augustine—understood the Gospel:

Let us reflect how free from wrath he is towards all his creatures. God does good to all, but most abundantly to us who have fled for refuge to his compassions. God is the all-merciful and beneficent Father.84

(Clement of Rome; 35-99 AD)

For as one who has been thought worthy of a divine and desirable name, in those bonds which I bear about, I commend the Churches, in which I pray for a union both of the flesh and spirit of Jesus Christ, who is the Saviour of all men, but specially of them that believe; by whose blood ye were redeemed; by whom ye have known God, or rather have been known by Him.85

(Ignatius of Antioch; Died 108 AD)

We can set no limits to the agency of the Redeemer to redeem, to rescue, to discipline in his work, and so will he continue to operate after this life.86 …All men are Christ's, some by knowing Him, the rest not yet. He is the Savior not of some and the rest not. For how is He Savior and Lord, if not the Savior and Lord of all?87

(Clement of Alexandria; 150-215 AD)

There is a resurrection of the dead, and there is punishment, but not everlasting. For when the body is punished the soul is gradually purified, and so is restored to its ancient rank. For all wicked men, and for demons, too, shall be restored to their former rank…. So then, when the end has been restored to the beginning, and the termination of things compared with their commencement, that condition of things will be re-established in which rational nature was placed, when it had no need to eat of the tree of the knowledge of good and evil; so that when all feeling of wickedness has been removed, and the individual has been purified and cleansed, He who alone is the one good God becomes to him 'all,' and that not in the case of a few individuals, or of a considerable number, but He Himself is "all in all." And when death shall no longer anywhere exist, nor the sting of death, nor any evil at all, then verily God will be "all in all."88

(Origen of Alexandria; 185-254 AD)

The Son "breaking in pieces" His enemies is for the sake of remolding them, as a potter his own work; as Jeremiah 18:6 says: i.e., to restore them once again to their former state.89

(Eusebius of Caesarea; 260-340 AD)

Christ has become the body of the whole of humanity, that, through the body that he was kind enough to assume, the whole of humanity might be hidden in him.90

(Hilary of Poitiers; 310–367 AD)

Flesh was taken up by the Logos to liberate all humans and resurrect all of them from the dead and ransom all of them from sin.91

(Athanasius of Alexandria; 296-373 AD)

The Word seems to me to lie down the doctrine of the perfect obliteration of wickedness, for if God shall be in all things that are, obviously wickedness shall no longer be in them. For it is necessary that at some time evil should be removed utterly and entirely from the realm of being.92

(St. Macrina the Younger; 327-379 AD)

For it is evident that God will in truth be "in all' when there shall be no evil in existence, when every created being is at harmony with itself, and every tongue shall confess that Jesus Christ is Lord; when every creature shall have been made one body. Now the body of Christ, as I have often said, is the whole of humanity.93

(Gregory of Nyssa; 335-395 AD)

Mankind, being reclaimed from their sins, are to be subjected to Christ in the fullness of the dispensation instituted for the salvation of all.94

(Didymus the Blind; 313-398 AD)

While the devil imagined that he got a hold of Christ, he really lost all of those he was keeping.95

(St. Chrysostom; 347-407 AD)

Clearly, some of the early Church Fathers had a far more Jewish view of Christ's atonement than most medieval Western theologians and Reformers.

Conclusion

If God is truly God, then love is not a gamble and mercy is not a risk. What He wills, He completes; what He begins, He brings home. The story does not end with loss, but with fullness—every shadow healed, every fracture mended, every wandering heart found. In the end, God will be all in all, and love will have the final word.

c2HB

Chapter 35
Every Knee, Every Tongue

In the grand narrative of Christian theology, Satan is portrayed as the principal adversary—the deceiver, the accuser, the one who works relentlessly to lead humanity away from God. From the start, knowing the limitations of human understanding, his greatest weapon has been persuasion. From the beginning, in the Garden of Eden, Satan’s strategy was not brute force, but cunning—tricking Adam and Eve to believe that God's word could not be fully trusted. This ancient lie still echoes today. Yet if we truly accept that God's attributes—His wisdom, power, and goodness—surpass those of all, including Satan, then the devil’s capacity to deceive and corrupt is ultimately temporary, limited, and doomed to failure. His influence, however real and devastating for now, can never outlast God's power to save and restore His creation.

Let’s revisit Paul’s bold citation of the prophet Isaiah:

As surely as I live, says the Lord, every knee shall bow to me, and every tongue shall give praise to God.

(Romans 14:11)

Notice the force of that declaration. God anchors the promise to His own existence: "As surely as I live." To claim that anything could ultimately prevent this from happening is to suggest that some power exists capable of frustrating God’s omnipotence—placing limits on the limitless, binding the infinite. It would mean that God’s own life could somehow fail. And that is not theology; it is absurdity.

No lie of Satan, no confusion of humanity, no stubbornness of the heart will ultimately stand against the loving, redeeming will of God. Not because God will overrule our free will, but because we will finally know Him face to face. Satan’s strategy has always been to obscure God’s love, twisting it into something petty, fearful, conditional, or transactional. In this way, he corrupts human hearts, not by forcing them into rebellion, but by feeding them a distorted image of the One who created them for love. Sadly, Satan often uses the Church to do exactly that. As one theologian admitted, "Theologians have done more to hide the gospel of Christ than any of its adversaries."96

The Battle Over Human Understanding

The deepest spiritual battle is fought over human understanding. If Satan’s war is not over territory but over hearts, then the battlefield is the human mind—and his primary weapon is misrepresentation. The biblical vision of God is not that of a petty tyrant demanding obedience through fear, but of a Father whose love ultimately conquers every heart. His victory is not achieved through domination, but through transformation.

At this stage, God permits momentary bewilderment, "until the time comes for God to restore everything, as he promised long ago through his holy prophets." (Acts 3:21).

This is the bedrock of the prophetic vision—that every mind will eventually see clearly, and every heart will awaken to the truth of who God really is. This is not a naïve dream or wishful thinking but God's own promise:

Then I heard every creature in heaven and on earth and under the earth and in the sea and all that is in them, singing, “To the one seated on the throne and to the Lamb be blessing and honor and glory and might forever and ever!”

(Revelation 5:13)

Not some. Not most. But every creature—everywhere—will worship the Lamb.

Every Knee Will Bow

The phrase “every knee will bow” paints a vivid and powerful future scene, but it’s often misunderstood. Some imagine it through the lens of fear—as if God will humiliate and forcibly crush people into submission, like a tyrannical medieval king demanding loyalty:

Either by choice or by force, every knee will bow...Some by God's grace have been enabled to bow by choice and others will bow by force.97

(John MacArthur)

John MacArthur’s portrayal of God imagines a cosmic tyrant—more akin to Ali Khamenei or Kim Jong Un—who forces worship at sword point, brutally driving the majority of humanity into submission and compelling them to mouth praises they do not mean. It is a vision disturbingly closer to the dictatorships of North Korea or the Islamic Republic of Iran than to the Father revealed by Jesus. This is not the God who wins hearts through self-giving love, but a god who secures obedience through terror—an image utterly foreign to the gospel and unrecognizable in Christ.

But that's a misunderstanding of the world of the Bible.

In the ancient world, to bow the knee before someone was an act of profound respect and allegiance, a gesture reserved for a select few, like kings and deities. It signaled trust, honor, and devotion. We see it in the story of Shadrach, Meshach, and Abednego (Daniel 3), where refusing to bow to a false king was a declaration that only the true God deserves such homage.

The biblical picture is far more beautiful than that of MacArthur's: it’s about a universal recognition of God's goodness, love, and rightful authority—not forced humiliation, but willing awe and joyful surrender. A bow not out of terror, but out of wonder and recognition.

Even those "under the earth"—meaning even the dead—will ultimately give honor to Jesus in the end.

Thus, when Scripture declares that every knee shall bow—including those of the dead—it is envisioning a universal moment of joyful surrender, not forced submission in God's dungeon. It pictures a day when all creation will, in awe and love, willingly kneel before the goodness, wisdom, and majesty of God, singing His praises.

Every Tongue Will Confess

Confession with the tongue carries a similar significance. In Hebrew, the word "tishaba" used in Isaiah 45:23 conveys the idea of taking an oath—publicly declaring allegiance. When a subject swore loyalty to a king, it meant pledging heart, soul, and life to that sovereign. Thus, the confession described here is not the forced admission of a humiliated enemy, but the joyful declaration of one who has come to trust and love the true King.

Certain Islamic groups have been known to force captives onto their knees and into making ideological declarations to Islam and Allah before executing them.

But the Bible paints a completely different picture. Bowed knees and confessing tongues are not signs of cosmic terrorism, sadism, humiliation, domination and degradation. They are signs of cosmic healing. They represent humanity, finally and fully, falling in love with its Creator.

When every knee bows and every tongue confesses, it won’t be because God has crushed their heads to the floor with His boot or forced them down in humiliation against their will. It will be because He has healed and purified them. It will be because their eyes have finally been opened to see what they could not see before—the unimaginable beauty, mercy, and overwhelming love of God. Their surrender will not be squeezed out by violence, but drawn out by awe.

So we are left with only two possible conclusions. Everyone—living and dead—will ultimately recognize Jesus as the Messiah and confess Him as such. From there, the paths diverge sharply. Either that universal confession leads to universal restoration, or God proceeds to endlessly torment the vast majority—pouring out unceasing lava, never permitting release from a cosmic torture chamber, while looking on with sadistic, grim satisfaction.

If that sounds extreme, understand this: it is not a caricature. It is the logical outcome of how fundamentalists explicitly frame the doctrine. In that vision, God’s final victory is not the healing of creation, but the eternal preservation of suffering as a monument to His power.

That god may inspire fear. He may command silence. But he bears no resemblance to the Father revealed by Jesus Christ.

A chilling—and tragically revealing—expression of this theology comes from one of Calvinism’s most prominent voices, Paul Washer, who declares:

The moment when you take your first step through the gates of hell, the only thing you will hear is all of creation standing to its feet and applauding and praising God because God has rid the earth of you.98

(Paul Washer)

The god of Calvinism couldn’t possibly be the same as the God of Israel and Jesus Christ.

If Any Are Lost Forever, God’s Attributes Are in Question

The logic becomes unavoidable:

If even one soul were to remain in permanent rebellion forever, if even one heart could persistently resist the love and wisdom of God, it would mean that God's will had been thwarted, His wisdom bested, His love overcome. It would imply that Satan had scored a permanent, even if small, victory. It would place a limit where Scripture places none.

Again, I can imagine a rare exception in which someone so persistently and permanently refuses repentance—even in the age to come—that God may ultimately harden their hearts (Exodus 7:3-4) and remove them from existence rather than restore them. In such a case, annihilation would serve as a final judgment rather than endless torment.

But as a general rule, I believe the trajectory of Scripture points toward cosmic restoration. God’s mercy is expansive, persistent, and restorative. The biblical story repeatedly portrays Him as a pursuing lover, a correcting father, and a mighty redeemer, seeking to bring all back into fellowship rather than abandoning them to destruction.

For how could the love of the God who declared that "love never fails" (1 Corinthians 13:8) ever fail to accomplish what it truly desires?

It is not sentimentalism to believe in the universal restoration of all things—it is fidelity to the character of God. If we take seriously the biblical declarations about God's nature—His omnipotence, omniscience, and omni-benevolence—then we must also take seriously the logical conclusion of the inevitable and complete triumph of His redemptive love.

Restoration Is Not Wishful Thinking—It’s Divine Necessity

The restoration of all things is not only a comforting idea; it is a theological necessity. It is the logical and inevitable conclusion of God’s nature as revealed in Scripture.

If God’s power is truly perfect, no creature can forever resist Him.

If God’s wisdom is truly perfect, no deception can forever blind.

If God’s love truly never fails, no heart can remain forever closed.

Thus, the universal restoration of the world—"Tikkun Olam"—is not simply one interpretive option among many. It is the only outcome consistent with the fullness of who God is.

Anything less would mean that sin, Satan, or human stubbornness could ultimately place a limit on the will of God. It would mean that rebellion could outlast redemption, that death could outshine life, that hatred could defeat love.

But Scripture, thank God, says otherwise:

Then comes the end, when he delivers the kingdom to God the Father after destroying every rule and every authority and power. For he must reign until he has put all his enemies under his feet. The last enemy to be destroyed is death.

(1 Corinthians 15:24-26)

The final enemy is death itself, not people. Ending death is like eradicating a disease—through curing and healing. Overcoming death is about restoring everything to its intended wholeness.

Christ reigns, not until He has crushed and humiliated all people, but until He has crushed all forces that destroy people.

In the end, when time gives way to infinity, the name of Jesus will be spoken by every tongue not by force nor out of terror, but out of wonder. Every knee will bend, not under compulsion, but in awe. Every heart will be drawn not by threat, but by the unstoppable, undeniable magnetism of divine love.

Because, when the Truth is fully revealed, and the scales fall from every eye, there is only one possible response: worship freely given.

This is the true final victory of Christ—not partial, not hesitant, but full, complete, radiant, and everlasting.

This is the God of the Gospel. This is the Gospel of Life. And in this Gospel, death, sin, and darkness have no final word. Love does.

All Means All

Some will insist, “But God gave us free will—and He won’t override it!”

That’s true. He doesn’t override it—He transforms it. He knows every heart, every wound, every lie we’ve believed, every trauma that keeps us from trusting Him. He doesn’t control us; He convinces us, persuades us, pursues us, softens us. He leads us to repentance with kindness upon kindness, mercy upon mercy, until even the hardest heart melts—even if that melting comes only after experiencing the “fires” of His love in the afterlife.

Everyone means everyone. All means all.

This is not coercion. It is not submission through fear. It is the outcome of divine goodness and irresistible grace.

The Logic of Victory

Let’s be honest. If God ends up saving only a few, then the enemy wins. If Christ’s blood is shed for all but effectively atones only for some, then we are left with a gospel of failure. A gospel where the powers of evil and human stubbornness outsmart divine grace. A gospel where sin, death, and hell get the last word.

But that’s not what the Jewish authors of the New Testament taught.

The early Christians understood Jesus’ death and resurrection not as a partial fix, but as a cosmic breakthrough. They didn’t go around asking people, “Are you saved?”—they went around proclaiming, “God has acted! The world has changed. A new creation has begun. Death has been defeated. Love has conquered.”

This is the core logic of the Gospel:

Premise 1: God is “the mighty one who saves” (Zephaniah 3:17).

Premise 2: "God loves the world" (John 3:16).

Premise 3: His love means He "wants to save all" (1 Timothy 2:3–4).

Premise 4: "Love never fails" (1 Corinthians 13:8).

Premise 5: God’s purposes cannot be thwarted (Job 42:2; Ephesians 1:11).

Conclusion: If God loves all, wills to save all, and His love and purposes cannot fail, then God will ultimately save all. Guaranteed.

This is pure logic rooted in Scripture, God's character, and covenant. To say otherwise is to imply that God’s plan was fragile from the start. That His love was not persuasive enough. That Christ’s sacrifice was not sufficient enough. That death and sin and rebellion will still claim their victory at the end of time.

But if God truly is who He claims to be—the wisest, the strongest, and the most loving—then the salvation and restoration of all things isn’t merely possible; it’s the natural result, both from a biblical and logical standpoint.

Conclusion

God’s will cannot be thwarted. His love cannot fail. And His salvation cannot be partial or unfinished. God does not lose. He is not engaged in a cosmic tug-of-war in which Satan walks away with ninety-three percent of humanity while God settles for a small remnant of survivors. That vision is not victory—it is defeat dressed up as sovereignty.

No—God is Mighty to Save. And in the end, He will do precisely what He set out to do: save all.

This is not merely the internal logic of my theology. It is the logic of God’s own love. And love—Scripture insists—never fails.

c2M8

Chapter 36
How Will the Story End?
Conclusion

Christians have told the story of how everything ends in two main—and profoundly different—ways. The first is a Westernized version that dominates much of modern Christian thought. The second, rooted in the biblical Jewish worldview, was embraced by many of the earliest Church Fathers.

Version One: The Western Ending

In the first telling, the story of the end begins with Jesus returning in glory. At last, everyone sees the truth and acknowledges Him as Lord. The moment is radiant: rebellion is purged, every lie exposed, sin no longer holds sway. It feels like the perfect, long-awaited resolution.

But then the story takes a dark, jarring, brutal turn.

In this version, Jesus suddenly casts most of humanity into a fiery pit where they will suffer endless torment—because they believed the wrong thing, belonged to the wrong denomination, or failed to live up to a moral standard during their few fleeting decades on earth. Here, divine love reveals itself as limited and conditional. Victory is only minimal. Salvation feels like a bait-and-switch, something most people never truly had a fair shot at receiving.

Version Two: The Ancient and Biblical Ending

The second version tells an entirely different story.

Here too, Jesus returns in glory, and every eye recognizes Him. But now the ending is not a reversal—it is the fulfillment of everything the Scriptures have been pointing toward.

Sin—not sinners—is purged (Hebrews 9:26).

Every tear is wiped away (Revelation 21:4).

Death itself is destroyed (1 Corinthians 15:26).

Creation is liberated and restored (Romans 8:21).

God becomes “all in all” (1 Corinthians 15:28).

In this vision, love does not fail. Victory is not partial—it is complete. Salvation is not a prize for a small remnant but the healing of the whole world. Heaven is not imagined as a VIP club or a gated community for the spiritually elite but as a great family table where no chair is left empty.

Two Sources, Two Visions

These distinct endings emerge from two very different sources.

The vision of universal restoration flows from the ancient prophetic worldview and the biblical Jewish theology of Tikkun Olam—of a God who searches until every lost sheep is found, who refuses to abandon even the most wounded soul, who lovingly restores the broken world through justice, mercy, and truth.

The harsher ending is shaped largely by Greek philosophical categories, ruthless Roman law, and imperial domination—Western ideas more concerned with punishment, hierarchy, and exclusion than with healing and community.

The Character of God Determines the True Ending

Scripture tells us plainly:

“If your enemy is hungry, feed him; if he is thirsty, give him something to drink” (Romans 12:20).

And: “Love your enemies” (Matthew 5:44).

And: “For the Lord will not reject forever” (Lamentations 3:31).

God does not command us to show mercy while He will show none. He doesn’t tell us to love and forgive our enemies while planning forever to abuse and torment His own.

In the end, the story closes in a way that reflects the heart of its Author. The God revealed in Jesus is not defeated by human failure, nor satisfied with partial victory, but faithful to the end—until love has done all it came to do. The final word is not exclusion but restoration, not endless torment but healing, not a world divided forever but one made whole. This is the hope the Scriptures dare us to imagine and trust: that mercy outlasts judgment, that love never gives up, and that when God is finally “all in all,” nothing He has made will be missing from the joy of His renewed creation.

Conclusion

Later Western thinkers such as Augustine of Hippo, Thomas Aquinas, and John Calvin produced impressive and sometimes brilliant theological constructions. Engaging with their ideas can be stimulating and even beneficial. But we must be clear about what they were and what they were not. They were regular human beings attempting to reason about God from within severe limitations. None were native speakers of Hebrew or Greek. None were Jews. None lived inside the biblical world they were interpreting. Their linguistic access was partial, their cultural distance enormous, and their philosophical categories often foreign to Scripture itself. Not to mention their ignorance of native idioms (e.g., "Lake of Fire," "Book of Life," "Gnashing of Teeth," and more) that even professional translators often misinterpret.

We should therefore expect them to be wrong about many things. Their writings may be insightful, but they carry no intrinsic authority. Respect for their intellect must never harden into submission to their conclusions. Theology belongs to Scripture, not to later interpreters, no matter how influential or revered.

Even those whom I would consider heretical still have something to teach us. We can learn from their theological errors. Engaging critically with their writings can sharpen our thinking, expose our blind spots, and compel us to clarify what we truly believe. Sometimes we grow not only through agreement, but through serious disagreement.

c2NG

Part V: Rethinking Hell

c2NR

Introduction: Let's Talk About Hell

There is far more to say about hell than can responsibly fit into a few chapters. That is precisely why I devoted an entire separate volume to the subject— Hell: A Jewish Perspective on a Christian Doctrine. In that book, I engage in a thorough, text-by-text examination of every major passage typically cited in discussions of hell: “unquenchable fire” (Matthew 3:12), “weeping and gnashing of teeth” (Matthew 8:12), “the worm that never dies” (Mark 9:48), the parable of Lazarus and the rich man (Luke 16), “the furnace of fire” (Matthew 13:42), “outer darkness” (Matthew 22:13), “the lake of fire” (Revelation 20:14–15), “fire and brimstone” (Revelation 21:8), and many others.

Rather than skimming these texts or relying on inherited assumptions, the book traces their Jewish background, literary genre, historical context, and theological intent—unpacking what they meant then, before importing later doctrines into them. I highly recommend checking out that volume for a thorough and thoughtful look at the doctrine of hell and its foundations, explored with depth, clarity, and faithfulness to Scripture.

In this book, however, we’ll limit ourselves to a focused overview of the most essential aspects of hell.

c2P1

Chapter 37
The Biblical Meaning of the Word "Hell"

Language is never static; it evolves, and meanings shift over time. Words often drift far from their original sense. For example, awful once meant “full of awe” or “worthy of reverence,” whereas today it means “very bad.” The word gay once meant joyful or lighthearted; now it primarily refers to sexual orientation.

The same goes for words like “eternal” and “hell” that we come across in English translations of the Bible. Historically, hell referred to the realm of the dead—the grave, the underworld—not a place of everlasting torture. This view is also widely acknowledged and accepted in both Christian and secular academia.99,100,101,102,103 It was only later, through cultural, philosophical, and theological developments, that hell came to be viewed as an afterlife of either temporary punishment, as in Judaism, the early church fathers, and several Eastern Christian denominations, or Eternal Conscious Torment, as in Islam and in traditions shaped by Augustine of Hippo, like Protestantism and Catholicism. Understanding this linguistic shift is crucial, because reading modern meanings back into ancient texts and translations inevitably distorts what the biblical authors were actually saying.

Where the English "Hell" Came From

The English word "hell" originates from the Old English "Hel" or "Helle," which was derived from the Proto-Germanic term "haljō."104 This term generally referred to the underworld (the graveyard), or a mystical hidden place. "Haljō" is also related to the Old Norse word "Hel," which referred both to the underworld—a realm of the dead—and the goddess who ruled over it in Norse mythology. In Norse belief, this underworld was where souls who did not die in battle would go.

As Christianity spread through Europe, hell was adopted and adapted within Christian theology to describe a place of punishment for the wicked, further shifting its meaning from a concept of cosmic graveyard to the more specific notion of hell as a place of punishment.

The evolution of the word hell:

- Proto-Germanic: "haljō" first referred to a concealed or hidden place and was used to describe the underworld—realm of the dead.

- - Old Norse: "Hel" was both the name of the underworld and the deity presiding over it.

- - - Old English: "hel" or "helle" referred to an afterlife place of punishment.

- - - - Modern English: "hell" refers to a place of punishment after death, sometimes excruciating and perpetual.

Four Words Translated "Hell"

English translations frequently employ the term "hell" as a replacement for four distinct Hebrew/Greek words: "Géenna," "Sheol," "Tartarus," and "Hades." However, these words are not exactly interchangeable synonyms, and each carries its unique connotations and meanings. Let's see some examples.

Sheol:

For thou wilt not leave my soul in hell [sheol]; neither wilt thou suffer thine Holy One to see corruption.

(Psalm 16:10, KJV)

Hades:

And death and hell [hades] were cast into the lake of fire. This is the second death.

(Revelation 20:14, KJV)

Géenna:

And if thy right eye offend thee, pluck it out, and cast it from thee: for it is profitable for thee that one of thy members should perish, and not that thy whole body should be cast into hell [Géenna].

(Matthew 5:29, KJV)

Tartarus:

For if God spared not the angels that sinned, but cast them down to hell [tartarus], and delivered them into chains of darkness, to be reserved unto judgment.

(2 Peter 2:4, KJV)

Clearly, translating these four distinct biblical concepts with the single English word “hell” collapses their meanings and badly distorts the text. The problem is not the Hebrew Bible or the New Testament, but the word itself. Historically, hell referred to death or the grave. Over time, it absorbed later afterlife imagery that the biblical authors did not share.

None of these four original terms describes a realm of eternal fiery torture. That idea is imported, not derived. By retrofitting later meanings onto ancient words, translators and theologians have replaced careful exegesis with theological anachronism, turning texts about death, judgment, and restoration into something they were never meant to say.

Sheol, Hades, Géenna, Tartarus

Sheol is a Hebrew word used in the Old Testament to describe the realm of the dead (e.g., Genesis 37:35, Job 14:13, Psalm 16:10, Ecclesiastes 9:10), often translated as "the grave" or "the pit." It is portrayed as a shadowy place where the dead are asleep, regardless of their beliefs or moral choices during life. Sheol is not associated with punishment or reward but is simply the state of being dead and hidden from the living.105
In English translations of the Bible, the term is frequently rendered as "hell."

Hades is the Greek word equivalent of Sheol,106 representing the underworld or the realm of the dead. In Greek mythology, Hades is both the name of the god ruling this domain and the place itself. In the New Testament, Hades similarly stands for the unseen place where souls metaphorically go after death, awaiting resurrection. Like Sheol (Hebrew), Hades (Greek) is not inherently a place of torment or bliss but a temporary holding place for the dead.107
In English translations of the Bible, the term is frequently rendered as "hell."

Géenna (or Gehenna) originates from the Hebrew word "Gehinnom," meaning the Valley of Hinnom, which is located in Jerusalem, running along the southern and western edges of the Old City. Historically, it was associated with much death, from the worship of Moloch and the burning sacrifice of children to the city’s garbage dump, where trash was constantly burned and bodies were discarded. In Second Temple Judaism, the Valley of Hinnom became associated with shameful death and disgrace.108 It could represent the ostracization or death of an individual, or the downfall and destruction of a city or nation.

In the New Testament, Jesus refers to Gehenna as a symbol of dishonorable death. In some Rabbinic literature, "Gehinnom" became associated with an afterlife disciplinary process, a destination for the wicked where they get to atone for their sins.109 
In English translations of the Bible, the term is frequently rendered as "hell."

Tartarus, in Greek mythology, is a deep abyss used to imprison the Titans far beneath Hades. It signifies the lowest part of the underworld, a jail-like place. In the New Testament, particularly in 2 Peter 2:4, Tartarus is used metaphorically to describe the gloomy, dark place where rebellious angels are held, awaiting judgment. Tartarus appears in the Septuagint translation of Job (40:20 and 41:24) and in Hellenistic Jewish literature, such as the Greek text of the ancient Book of Enoch. In Enoch, God places the archangel Uriel "in charge of the world and of Tartarus" (20:2), understood as the prison for 200 fallen Watchers (angels).110
In English translations of the Bible, the term is frequently rendered as "Hell."

Clearly, none of these terms suggest a place of Eternal Conscious Torment ("Hellfire"), so translating them all as “hell” should be reconsidered in future English Bible versions. For now, the term “hell” will likely keep causing confusion for readers of translations that apply it to some or all of the Hebrew and Greek concepts mentioned.

In the Concordant Expositions,111 Bible scholar James Coram explains:

The Old English “hell,” denoted that which is covered (hidden or unseen). Consequently, it once served as a suitable translation of the Greek hades, which means “imperceptible” or “unseen.” In modern English, however, due to the corrupting influence of human tradition, “hell” has come to mean “the abode of the dead; the place of punishment after death [in which the dead are alive].” Consequently, since in modern English the notion represented by the term “hell” constitutes, to say the least, interpretation, not translation, it is unconscionable for modern translators to render either the Hebrew sheol or the Greek hades by this expression. 

Yet it is worse still, whether in old English or modern English, to render the Greek tartarosas and especially the Greek geenna, also as “hell.” Such “translations” are not translations at all; they are but the product of circular reasoning and hoary tradition. Whatever one’s understanding may be concerning the matters to which these words make reference, as a translation of the Original, the rendering “hell,” in all cases, is wholly unjustifiable. Yet it is this very rendering, the single term, “hell,” for all these distinct words in the Original, which has spawned all the familiar talk concerning “hell” which prevails among “Bible-believing Christians” today.

(James Coram)

Jesus Never Spoke of Hell—He Spoke of Gehenna

Now, let’s poke a stick at one of fundamentalism’s most untouchable sacred cows…

Jesus spoke more about hell than anybody else in the Bible. In fact, He spoke more about hell than everybody else in the Bible combined.112

(John MacArthur)

John MacArthur is completely wrong. Jesus never spoke of the Augustinian–Calvinist vision of hell as Eternal Conscious Torment, so common in Western theology. He spoke of Gehenna—a term no first-century Jew would have understood in that way.

Gehenna (short for Gey Ben Hinnom—the Valley of Hinnom) was a real, physical place just outside Jerusalem. It appears repeatedly in the Hebrew Scriptures as a site associated with death, great shame, judgment, and destruction (e.g., Jeremiah 7:31–33; 19:2–7; 2 Kings 23:10). In ancient Israel, the valley served as the city’s dump—a spot where trash was constantly burned, where dead animals and the bodies of the disgraced were tossed, giving worms a steady supply of food that never ends. It was also where the poorest and most marginalized scavenged the refuse for scraps to survive.

Jesus described Gehenna as the place “where the worms that eat them do not die, and the fire is not quenched” (Mark 9:47-48) since the worms always had food to consume and the flames constantly burned the trash.

Thus, in the Jewish vocabulary, Gehenna symbolized a shameful or deadly outcome, not an afterlife incinerator. It represented death, loss, exclusion, destruction, and the collapse of a meaningful life. A fate worse than honorable death—either living as an outcast (socially dead), or dead entirely.

When Jesus spoke about Gehenna (e.g., Matthew 5:22, 29–30; 10:28; Mark 9:43–48), He was drawing on a familiar Jewish image to convey real historical judgment, social breakdown, disgrace, and devastating consequences—not otherworldly unending torment after death.

We must remember, Yeshua wasn’t a 21st-century Bible Belt preacher in a suit and tie, but a first-century Prophet of Israel and Jewish rabbi living and teaching in Jewish Israel, ministering among His own people. His words were directed first and foremost at His Jewish audience in their specific context, while also prophetically calling the entire nation of Israel to repentance, justice, mercy, and faithfulness to the covenant.

Tragically, as a nation, Israel rejected that call. And within a generation, the warning became reality. In 70 AD, Jerusalem was destroyed by Rome, the Temple was burned, and according to ancient sources, over a million Jews were killed.113 This was eternal judgment in the most biblical sense—not endless, but total, decisive, and irreversible for that generation and system. It’s likely that many of these bodies ended up being thrown into the Valley of Hinnom.

In short, Jesus’ use of Gehenna was a prophetic warning about the consequences of injustice, violence, moral decay and covenant rebellion—not a blueprint for an afterlife furnace.

Case Study: To Be Thrown Into Gehenna (Matthew 5:27-29)

Let's examine Matthew 5:27-29 as a case study:

You have heard that it was said, "You shall not commit adultery." But I tell you that anyone who looks at a woman lustfully has already committed adultery with her in his heart. If your right eye causes you to stumble, gouge it out and throw it away. It is better for you to lose one part of your body than for your whole body to be thrown into Gehenna. 

(Matthew 5:27-29)

No, Jesus was not advocating self-mutilation. It strains all credibility to imagine that the same Jesus who had just said, “Do not think that I have come to abolish the Law or the Prophets” (Matthew 5:17), would immediately contradict both Moses and Himself by implying everlasting fiery torment for inward desire alone—a concept entirely absent from the Hebrew Scriptures and foreign to Israel’s Law.

I also find it deeply ironic that many who insist on taking the threat of being “thrown into hell [Gehenna]” with wooden literalism suddenly abandon that approach when Jesus says, “gouge it out and throw it away.” If they were consistent, we would be surrounded by fundamentalists missing an eye—or two. Yet, curiously, the violent threat is treated as literal while the self-mutilation command is quietly spiritualized. One half of the metaphor is pressed to its harshest extreme, while the other is safely neutralized. That inconsistency reveals not faithfulness to Scripture, but selective literalism driven by theology rather than context.

In any case, if Jesus were speaking literally about tearing out eyes to avoid hellfire, the logic collapses on its own terms. He would have known perfectly well that sight is not required for imagination or desire; even the blind can still experience lust and inner fantasy. Losing an eye doesn’t change what’s in your heart or on your mind.

His words are not anatomical instructions pulled from a modern medical textbook. They are prophetic hyperbole—an established rabbinic teaching device—intentionally shocking, intentionally exaggerated, meant to awaken moral seriousness. Jesus is exposing the gravity of sin, not prescribing salvation by self-mutilation, and certainly not introducing a new doctrine of never-ending afterlife torture.

So what was the point Jesus was trying to make?

Understanding that lust is a natural part of human nature, His point was that it’s the gateway to adultery—before someone commits the act, they first imagine it. In 1st-century Jewish society, getting caught in adultery could mean becoming an outcast scavenging in Gehenna, or worse, being stoned to death (Leviticus 20:10) and tossed there.

Jesus simply taught that every action begins in the heart—you don’t commit murder unless hatred has taken root there first. You don’t steal unless you’ve first coveted something that isn't yours. This was His point and warning to His young disciples, and to us as well.

Sadly, when Western theology turned antisemitic, it severed itself from its Jewish roots. And when context is stripped away, misunderstanding is inevitable. Detached from the Jewish world in which Jesus lived and taught, His words are easily distorted—and the result is tragic: the God of Scripture is reimagined as a cruel, sadistic, volatile tyrant rather than the faithful, compassionate, loving Father revealed in Christ.

Conclusion

Few topics make Christians shift uncomfortably in their seats like hell. Yet it remains one of the most influential—and most misunderstood—teachings in Christian theology. Much of the confusion persists because the passages associated with hell are rarely studied in their original context, their metaphors are flattened into literal threats, Hebrew and Greek terms are mistranslated or oversimplified, and alternative readings are dismissed before they are even considered.

Because this doctrine profoundly shapes how we view God, humanity, justice, and salvation itself, it cannot be treated casually or inherited uncritically. For that reason, I strongly encourage you to read my book Hell: A Jewish Perspective on a Christian Doctrine, where these texts are examined carefully, historically, and faithfully—allowing Scripture to speak on its own terms rather than through the fears and assumptions we have been taught to project onto it.

c2SG

Chapter 38
Hell—A Short Historical Overview

Before we can meaningfully discuss hell, we must first clear away centuries of inherited assumptions and return to the world in which Jesus actually lived and taught. Hell, as imagined by many modern Christians—a place of endless conscious torture—did not drop fully formed from the pages of Scripture. It emerged gradually, shaped by cultural, philosophical, and theological developments far removed from first-century Judaism. To read Jesus rightly, we must step back into His Jewish context, where images of fire, judgment, and Gehenna were not weapons of terror but familiar metaphors for purification, correction, and restoration. This chapter traces that original landscape—Jewish, biblical, and early Christian—to show how the language of hell was first understood, how it later shifted, and why recovering its original meaning radically transforms our understanding of God, judgment, and salvation.

Hell in First Century Judaism

Ideas of the afterlife—whether imagined as a shadowy underworld or as a purifying fire—existed in many ancient cultures long before Christianity. These early notions were largely symbolic, primitive, and undeveloped. Even within ancient Israel, Gehinnom was not originally the fully formed “hell” of later Jewish theology. It developed over time, often understood as a temporary place where wrongdoing is confronted and dealt with—not as an Eternal Conscious Torment.

Origen (185-253 AD) was an early Christian scholar, ascetic, theologian, and a prolific writer who wrote roughly 2,000 treatises. He has been described as "the greatest genius the early church ever produced."114

Origen wrote extensively about the Jewish context of the gospels and concluded:

Now as we found that Gehenna was mentioned in the Gospel as a place of punishment, we searched to see whether it is mentioned anywhere in the ancient Scriptures, and especially because the Jews too use the word… We find a certain confirmation of what is said regarding the place of punishment, intended for the purification of such souls as are to be purified by torments, agreeably to the saying: "The Lord comes like a refiner's fire, and like fullers' soap: and He shall sit as a refiner and purifier of silver and of gold."

Origen, Against Celsus, VI, 25.

In other words, even Jews who associated Gehenna or hell with afterlife punishment saw it as temporary rather than everlasting. Refining and purifying, rather than tormenting or destroying.

At its core, the imagery Jesus employed was concrete, familiar, and unmistakably Jewish: the refining of precious metals by fire. Scripture returns to this metaphor again and again. The Hebrew Bible speaks of God refining His people like silver and testing—even “tormenting”—them like gold in a furnace (Malachi 3:2–3; Zechariah 13:9; Psalm 66:10). Fire is not the enemy of the metal; it is the means by which the metal is purified.

The New Testament carries this same imagery forward without interruption. Human works are examined and tested “as through fire” (1 Corinthians 3:13–15), and trials are said to refine faith the way fire refines gold (1 Peter 1:7). The metaphor never changes. Fire does not exist to destroy what is precious, but to burn away what is false—until what remains is genuine, resilient, and fit for glory.

Thus, in 1st-century Judaism, the metaphor of fire was associated not with Eternal Conscious Torment, but with testing, cleansing, and purification.

According to HELPS Word-studies, the Greek word basanizó, often translated as "torment," can also mean "to test" or "to examine." Therefore, "tormented in fire" can be understood as "tested or refined in fire."

Jesus drew from this same vocabulary. To His original Jewish audience, fire symbolized a painful yet purposeful process—refining, reshaping, and restoring. Fire burns away dross so that what remains is clean, perfected, refined, and truly valuable. This is likely how His disciples understood it: not as never-ending sadism, but as a refining fire that ultimately serves a redemptive purpose.

Of course, some people accumulate far more “impurity” over a lifetime—hatred, cruelty, selfishness, violence. For them, the refining process may indeed be long and painful. But prolonged purification is not the same as everlasting despair. Fire was never meant to destroy the gold, only the dross—until what God always intended finally emerges in clarity and beauty.

Since God hates sin, not the sinner, He will destroy the sin, not the person. This was—and still largely remains—the Jewish understanding of hell.

Hell in Early Christian Schools

In the first centuries of the Church, theology was taught primarily through six major catechetical schools, and their views on hell varied. These schools—much like modern seminaries—were the intellectual and spiritual centers of Christian formation in their day, each representing a distinct theological tradition rather than a single, uniform doctrine. Let’s take a quick look at these ancient schools and what they taught about hell.

Alexandria: The Catechetical School of Alexandria (2nd century) was one of the most influential theological centers in early Christianity. It gained fame for its allegorical reading of Scripture and distinctive approach to theology. Alexandria's teachings, known as "apokatastasis" (the ultimate restoration of all things) proposed that hell served as a corrective and purifying phase, ultimately leading all souls to salvation through God's love and mercy.115

Antioch: The School of Antioch (3rd century) was known for its emphasis on the literal and historical interpretation of Scripture. This school also taught that hell was a temporary state designed to purify and ultimately restore all souls to God. This approach emphasized God's ultimate plan for the reconciliation and salvation of all creation.116

Cesarea: The theological school in Caesarea (3rd century), influenced by Origen (who first studied under Clement in Alexandria) and other prominent theologians, also believed and taught that God's judgment was not endless but rather a process through which souls would be purified and eventually restored to their original, sinless state.117

Eastern Syria: The Eastern Syrian school (4th century), part of the broader Syriac Christian tradition, taught that divine punishment was temporary and aimed at bringing about repentance and healing, emphasizing the compassionate nature of God.118

Asia Minor (Turkey): The theological school in Asia Minor (3rd century) took a different approach, teaching annihilationism. This view held that the wicked would ultimately be eliminated—cease to exist—rather than being restored or subjected to unending torment.119

Hippo: The school in Hippo (late 4th–early 5th century), named after Augustine of Hippo, was located in northwestern Africa and was the only one that taught Eternal Conscious Torment. This school viewed afterlife punishment as "hellfire"—a place of never-ending torturous punishment and suffering for the unbeliever. It emphasized the severity of sin over grace and what was believed to be the absolute and punitive nature of divine wrath and judgment.120

Ironically, what was once seen as a rare and minority belief about hell in the early centuries has now become the dominant view in the West, largely due to the adaptation of Augustinian theology by both Catholicism and Protestantism.

The Three Main Views of Hell Today

After studying the doctrine of hell for several years, I’ve come to see that Christian (and broader religious) beliefs about final judgment generally fall into three major categories:

1. Eternal Conscious Torment (ECT / Hellfire)

Today, the Augustinian view is the prevailing perspective among fundamentalist Protestant and Catholic groups, and it’s also the view of hell held in Islam. It sees hell as everlasting, unending torture—a punishment that serves no purpose beyond retribution. There is no healing, no restoration, no transformation—just an infinite continuation of pain and suffering.

To me, this portrays God as sadistic, cruel, merciless, vengeful, and unrelentingly punitive—a picture I find to be completely at odds with the God of Yeshua revealed in Scripture.

2. Annihilationism (Conditional Immortality)

This view—held by several Christian traditions—teaches that certain people are ultimately destroyed rather than tormented forever. Depending on the specific version, those who face this fate may be described as the wicked, the unbelieving, or those who persistently reject God even in the afterlife. After the final judgment, their existence simply comes to an end. Morally, this position is far less severe than Eternal Conscious Torment. Yet depending on how it is framed, it can still leave most of humanity facing ultimate extinction rather than restoration.

Though I believe a case can be made for some form of annihilationism, it still introduces a serious logical problem. Scripture repeatedly affirms the resurrection of all the dead (John 5:28–29; Acts 24:15; Revelation 20:12–13). If that is the case, if God were to marvelously bring unbelievers back from the dead only to wipe them out again moments later, resurrection would seem like nothing more than a fleeting moment of life granted just to stage another death—something that feels unnecessary, cruel, morally dubious, and theologically confusing. In this scenario, the resurrection feels like a brief, meaningless interruption that downplays the significance of Christ’s triumph over death.

This then raises an unavoidable question: what is the purpose of resurrection if it simply returns most people to the same state as before—nonexistence? Would God truly summon the dead back to life only to extinguish them again, as though resurrection was a cruel formality rather than a redemptive act? Such a notion sits uneasily with Scripture’s own testimony, where God declares,

I take no pleasure in the death of the wicked, but rather that they turn from their ways and live.

(Ezekiel 33:11)

3. Restorative Judgment (Universalism / Apokatastasis)

This view, embraced by many early Church Fathers and deeply influenced by Jewish thought, is still most clearly upheld in Oriental and Eastern Orthodox traditions and by few Catholic thinkers. It sees judgment not as retributive punishment but as a disciplinary process. Fire isn’t meant only to condemn but to refine—purifying, healing, and restoring. In this understanding, hell exists, but it’s not the ultimate end.

A common misconception—and frequent criticism—of Christian Universalism is the claim that it teaches “all roads lead to God” and that actions carry no consequences. This is a strawman that misrepresents the position entirely. Universalism holds that salvation is found in Christ alone, but that His atonement covers all humanity, and that eventually everyone will come to understand and believe.

In this view, God’s justice—even in the afterlife—aims at setting things right, not inflicting unending suffering for its own sadistic sake. If God is truly omnipresent, present everywhere without exception, then He must also be present in hell, whether understood as the grave or as punishment. As Psalm 139:8 unequivocally proclaims:

If I ascend up into heaven, thou art there: if I make my bed in hell, behold, thou art there.

(Psalm 139:8)

There is no realm where God simply is not. That means hell cannot be the absence of God (as some Christians claim), but the presence of God experienced differently.

In that light, hell is not God withdrawing in hatred, but God remaining in faithfulness. It is His holy, purifying love encountered by those not yet healed by it. The same fire that warms and gives life also burns away what is false. What changes is not God, but the condition of the one who encounters Him.

There are various versions of universalism, annihilationism, and ECT, with some positions even blending elements of more than one view. For example, certain universalists argue that annihilation may apply to a small number of individuals who became so radically evil that they effectively lost the image of God.

I am not here to tell you what you must believe. And while I generally try to avoid theological labels, I personally find that some form of universalism best aligns with the biblical vision of God: a God who judges not in order to destroy, but in order to save, a God who is “reconciling all things to Himself” (Colossians 1:20), and whose mercy ultimately triumphs over mere retribution. I believe Jesus came to save the world, and Christ’s atonement truly affects all, with the goal of universal restoration of creation.

Conclusion

In the end, the fire does not exist to annihilate hope, but to rescue it. What God judges, He judges in order to heal; what He exposes, He exposes in order to restore. The last word is not torment, but transformation—not exile, but homecoming. The God revealed in Scripture is not a destroyer of souls, but a refiner of hearts; not a tyrant who delights in suffering, but a Father who refuses to abandon His children to their brokenness. This vision does not weaken justice—it fulfills it. It does not cheapen grace—it magnifies it. And it does not diminish God’s holiness—it reveals it as love, blazing and unstoppable. So take courage. Whatever darkness remains is not final. Whatever fire awaits is not purposeless. The story is still moving toward life, and God will finish what He began—until all that was lost is found, all that was broken is healed, and all creation finally rests in the wholeness it was always meant to know.

c2UC

Chapter 39
The Augustinian Fallacy

Much of the confusion surrounding the mistranslation and misunderstanding of olam and aiōn as “endless” or “everlasting” time can be traced directly to the influence of Augustine of Hippo (354–430 A.D.). Deeply shaped by Neoplatonic philosophy,121 Augustine reasoned that because God is infinite and timeless, all divine actions—including judgment and punishment—must also be infinite, timeless, and without end. This reasoning is most evident in The City of God, Book XXI, where he argues for the idea of Eternal Conscious Torment.

But his conclusion did not emerge from the Hebrew Scriptures, nor from the Jewish worldview of Jesus and the apostles. It was a philosophical assumption imposed upon the text. In one decisive move, the biblical notion of age—purposeful, qualitative, and God-directed—was collapsed into the Greek abstraction of timeless eternity. The result was a radical reinterpretation of judgment, one that permanently reshaped Western theology and transformed corrective, age-bound judgment into endless metaphysical punishment.

Neoplatonism

Neoplatonism is a philosophical and mystical development of Plato’s thought that arose in the 3rd century CE, primarily through the work of Plotinus. It teaches that the human soul can ascend back to divine unity through contemplation and purification.122 Unlike Plato’s conversational style, Neoplatonism systematized his ideas into a structured metaphysical hierarchy, merging Greek philosophy with spiritual thought.

Augustine encountered Plotinus’ ideas through Latin translations of Plotinus’ works. Modern scholarship emphasizes how deeply Augustine was shaped by Plotinus’ Neoplatonism. For example, the Brill monograph describes Plotinus as Augustine’s “most important non‑biblical source.”123

Thus, it is widely agreed that Neoplatonism profoundly shaped early Western Christian theology through the work of Augustine of Hippo.124

Augustine of Hippo

Here is something most Christians are never told: Augustine of Hippo, the single most influential theologian in Western Christianity, was one of the few major Church Fathers who was functionally illiterate in both Hebrew and Greek.125 He relied almost entirely on Latin translations—translations that often flattened Hebrew imagery and altered Greek nuance. As a result, key metaphors, idioms, and cultural assumptions that would have been obvious to Jews of the biblical world were lost to him.

Because of his limited facility with Hebrew and Greek, Augustine famously misread several key biblical texts. One of the most consequential examples is Romans 5:12—specifically the phrase that was rendered in Old Latin as “in whom all sinned.”

Augustine relied on a Latin translation that implied humanity sinned in Adam, as though all humans literally participated in Adam’s transgression. From that mistranslation he constructed the doctrine of inherited guilt: the idea that every human being is born legally culpable for Adam’s sin. This single interpretive error became the theological cornerstone of Original Sin as later developed in both Catholic and Protestant traditions.

But the Greek text says nothing of the sort.

Therefore, just as sin entered the world through one man, and death through sin, and in this way death came to all people, because all sinned.

(Romans 5:12)

Paul does not say “in whom all sinned.” He says, “because all sinned.” The difference is enormous. Paul’s argument is about the universality of human sin—what humans actually do—not about an inherited legal guilt passed down biologically from Adam. Death spreads because all sin, not because all once sinned as Adam.

As native Greek speakers, Eastern Christian theologians and Church Fathers never accepted Augustine’s reading precisely because it did not align with the Greek text. And modern scholars across denominational lines now agree: the Latin wording misled Augustine, and an entire theological system was built on that mistake. What began as a translation error hardened into dogma—and reshaped Western Christianity’s understanding of sin, guilt, and salvation ever since.

Augustine’s decade-long immersion in Manichaeism—a dualistic religion obsessed with cosmic conflict, inherent corruption, and punitive justice—left deep psychological and theological scars that later resurfaced in his fixation on inherited guilt, divine wrath, and a God who saves by condemning most.

Augustine’s theological legacy rests on an extraordinarily dark—and deeply controversial—view of the human condition. Augustine’s doctrine of Original Sin goes far beyond the early church’s teaching (all sin, thus all die, thus all need grace). He redefined Adam’s sin as inherited moral guilt, meaning every human—including infants from the moment of birth—is born already condemned and deserving of Eternal Conscious Torment judgment.

Ken Wilson, who earned his doctorate in theology with a thesis on Augustine's life and teachings from the University of Oxford, summed it up perfectly:

Because infants have no personal sin, Augustine deduced their baptisms for forgiveness of sin must be based upon their inherited guilt (reatus) from Adam’s first sin.126

(Ken Wilson)

In Augustine’s framework, infants enter the world condemned, not because of anything they have done, but because guilt itself is inherited. But inherited guilt clearly contradicts the biblical message: "The child will not share the guilt of the parent, nor will the parent share the guilt of the child.” (Ezekiel 18:20).

To explain why some are “rescued” from this cosmic indictment while others are not, Augustine adopted the doctrine of predestination/election.127 Not an election for a role and mission (e.g., Romans 9:10–16) but for being saved from Eternal Conscious Torment. And not predestination as in everything eventually serving God’s plan, but rather about preselecting who would be saved from hell before they were even born. Many ancient pagan religions embraced some form of predestination that overrides human agency and free will, including the Greco‑Roman world, where the Fates controlled every life; Mesopotamian religion, with destinies inscribed on the Tablets of Destiny; Egyptian belief, where gods like Shai assigned each person’s fate; and Norse mythology, where the Norns fixed the destinies of gods and humans. But predestination was an unfamiliar idea to the Israelites, as the Bible placed a strong focus on human choice and responsibility.128 The only form of “election” found in Scripture is election for a role, a vocation, or a mission—not election to personal salvation from hellfire. As Romans 9 makes clear, God chooses people for something, not over others for eternal destinies.

Augustine broke from the biblical tradition and leaned toward pagan ideas, claiming that God had already chosen a set number of people to receive saving grace while intentionally denying it to most others129—who were then severely punished for a condition they were born with and powerless to change. This isn’t mercy in limited form; it’s cruelty dressed up as Systematic Theology.

To defend his views, Augustine famously appealed to Psalm 51:5 as proof of Original Sin—reading it as a metaphysical claim that guilt is inherited from conception. But when the verse is read carefully, on its own terms, within the biblical context and its linguistic structure, it teaches nothing of the sort. David is not making a universal statement about human nature, nor is he claiming that infants are born morally culpable before God. He is speaking autobiographically, describing the brokenness that marked the story of his life from its very beginning.

Behold, I was shapen in iniquity; and in sin did my mother conceive me.

(Psalm 51:5)

The Hebrew grammar is decisive. The preposition translated “in” does not denote a legal status of guilt; it typically marks sphere, context, or circumstance—“within the situation of.” The verbs are passive and biographical: “I was brought forth.” Then the focus sharpens explicitly to the maternal act: “in sin my mother conceived me.” Grammatically, David is not the subject of the sin; his mother is the subject of the conceiving. The phrase locates the event within a sinful or tragic situation, not within David as an object of inherited blame. The parallelism of Hebrew poetry reinforces this: the two lines restate and intensify the same idea—that from the very beginning of his life story, sin and brokenness surrounded him.

We know David felt rejected and forsaken by his parents (Psalm 27:10) and brothers (1 Samuel 17:28). Ancient Jewish tradition130 fills in the gap. David’s mother, Nitzevet, endured years of silent suffering and social disgrace to protect her son. After Jesse separated from her due to doubts about his own lineage, he sought to conceive a child through a maidservant. Unbeknownst to him, Nitzevet and the maidservant secretly switched places, resulting in David’s conception. Because Nitzevet never exposed the truth, her pregnancy appeared illicit. She bore the shame alone, while David grew up treated as an illegitimate outcast within his own family (1 Samuel 16:11). His earliest years were shaped not by divine condemnation, but by human suspicion, injustice, and exclusion because his mother conceived him in sin—literally.

Read in this light, Psalm 51:5 becomes what it plainly is: a confession that David’s life began amid sin, scandal, and suffering—not a doctrinal treatise on inherited guilt. To extract from this verse a universal theory of Original Sin as Augustine did is to impose a later theological framework onto a lament that was never meant to carry such metaphysical weight. It is not exegesis; it is retrojection.
