Furthermore, the logic drove Augustine to one of his most disturbing conclusions: infant baptism was not merely important, but absolutely essential. Unbaptized babies who died, he insisted, were excluded from heaven and consigned to hell—understood not metaphorically, but as a place of literal, physical fire that forever torments both body and soul without ever destroying them. In Augustine’s system, God creates babies knowing most of them will suffer forever, calls it justice, and demands gratitude for sparing a few.

If this theology sounds familiar, it's because Augustine’s pessimism about humanity and theological fixation on inherited guilt profoundly shaped Western Christianity—later softened in places within Catholicism, but radicalized in Protestantism—leaving a legacy in which fear eclipses love, grace becomes selective, and God increasingly resembles a cosmic prosecutor rather than the Father revealed by Jesus.

How Augustine’s Formative Years Shaped His Theological Vision

Having spent most of his life in North Africa—primarily in what is now Algeria and Tunisia—Augustine was largely unfamiliar with the Jewish worldview, its symbols, metaphors, and modes of thought that shaped both the Hebrew Scriptures and the teaching of Jesus and the New Testament. Yet his interpretations, filtered through the Latin language and Neoplatonic philosophy, would go on to define Western theology for centuries.

This is not a small detail. Imagine a thinker in Ecuador reading a Spanish translation of Sophocles’ Antigone and then building an entire philosophical system on misunderstood metaphors and mistranslated words. Now imagine that these misinterpretations become, a thousand years later, the doctrinal foundation for major branches of a world religion.

That is precisely what happened with Augustine. His belief about “hell” as Eternal Conscious Torment—a concept foreign to Second Temple Judaism and to all other ancient Christian schools of theology—shaped the early Catholic understanding of hell. Centuries later (16th century), ECT was picked up by two towering Reformers: John Calvin and Martin Luther. Catholics themselves, they continued to embrace most of Augustine’s theological beliefs—even after the Reformation—without returning to the Jewish worldview, likely because they were both highly anti-Semitic as well.131 (You’re invited to learn more about the deep antisemitism of both Calvin and especially Luther in my book, "Why Don’t Jews Believe in Jesus.")

Augustine's Logical Fallacies

Building on linguistic misunderstandings and theological fallacies—largely imported from Neoplatonism—Augustine concluded that because God is infinite, hell, which he imagined as a very literal place of fire,132 must be infinite as well: without end, without mercy, and without any corrective or redemptive purpose. In his framework, punishment was not meant to heal or restore, but simply to punish—again and again—forever.

This marked a decisive break from the older, more Jewish understanding of divine justice, grace, and judgment, in which correction and restoration were integral to God’s dealings with humanity. That earlier vision dominated Christianity for its first three centuries, long before Augustine’s synthesis took hold. Yet Augustine’s doctrine of hellfire as Eternal Conscious Torment went on to cast a long shadow over Western theology, haunting both Catholic and Protestant traditions ever since.

However, Augustine's argument commits several errors in reasoning known as "logical fallacies."133

First, the fallacy of equivocation: Augustine collapses God’s infinite nature into the finite reality of creation, treating human sin and historical judgment as though they must share the same ontological properties as God Himself.

Second, the fallacy of false analogy: Augustine assumes that because God’s love—as a divine attribute—is infinite, His punishments—as divine actions—must also be infinite in the same way. But attributes and actions are not identical, and Scripture never treats them as such.

Even in his own lifetime, Augustine’s interpretation was far from universally accepted. What later became dominant in the West was not the settled consensus of the early Church, but one theological trajectory among several—and a controversial one at that.

To this day, it is the Eastern and Oriental Orthodox traditions that have most faithfully preserved the pre-Augustinian vision of judgment and hell: a far more nuanced understanding of time, correction, and divine mercy—one that refused to collapse biblical language into abstract metaphysics or infinite retribution. That distinction has never disappeared. As one well-known Orthodox theologian succinctly puts it:

Hell appears in the shadow of the cross as what has always already been conquered, as what Easter leaves in ruins, to which we may flee from the transfiguring light of God if we so wish, but where we can never finally come to rest—for, being only a shadow, it provides nothing to cling to (as Gregory of Nyssa so acutely observes). Hell exists, so long as it exists, only as the last terrible residue of a fallen creation’s enmity to God, the lingering effects of a condition of slavery that God has conquered universally in Christ and will ultimately conquer individually in every soul. This age has passed away already, however long it lingers on in its own aftermath, and thus in the Age to come, and beyond all ages, all shall come home to the Kingdom prepared for them from before the foundation of the world.134

(David Bentley Hart)

What Augustine Got Wrong About God's Nature

Augustine is regarded as a saint in some traditions, but even "saints" often get things wrong. In his case, it was his understanding—or misunderstanding—of God's nature.

Before God created time and space, there was no time at all. God’s life, therefore, is not quantitative but qualitative. He does not exist for an infinite number of days along some endless timeline, because time itself did not exist prior to creation.

God cannot die not because He has accumulated unlimited time, but because He is immortal by nature—unaffected by sin or death, perfect, self-sufficient, and unchanging in who He is. His immortality flows from His very being, not from the length of His existence.

Likewise, in the resurrection, we will receive a new body that is immortal and life that is imperishable. We will no longer be vulnerable to sickness, aging, or death. We will become like God in the sense that nothing can destroy us:

When the perishable has been clothed with the imperishable... then the saying that is written will come true: "Death has been swallowed up in victory."

(1 Corinthians 15:54)

Eternal life, then, is not about living endless days. That is only a byproduct—a side effect. In its truest sense, eternal life is about sharing in a kind of life like God’s own: a life that is untouchable by death, incorruptible by sin, and ultimately indestructible.

Conclusion: Think Like a Hebrew, Not a Greek

In conclusion, Augustine’s Neoplatonically shaped interpretation of olam and aion introduced a rigid, endless view of divine punishment into Western Christianity that departed sharply from the restorative justice envisioned in Jewish thought and preserved in many of the Eastern/Orthodox traditions. By equating God’s infinite nature with infinite retribution, Augustine committed logical and linguistic errors that gave rise to the Western doctrine of Eternal Conscious Torment. Yet the biblical and Jewish tradition reminds us that eternity is not about endless duration but about the quality of incorruptible life in God—a life that conquers death, restores creation, and embodies the victory of resurrection rather than the permanence of punishment.

To understand eternal life is to stop thinking like a Greek philosopher or a modern engineer and start thinking more like a Hebrew prophet and a Jewish rabbi. The Bible's vision of life is not primarily concerned with endless time but with blessedness, wholeness, closeness to God, and victory over death—immortality.

c2WH

Chapter 40
Eternal Conscious Torment Fails the Test of God's Character

If an alien spent five minutes scrolling through Christian posts and videos on social media today, it would quickly become obvious why there are 49,000 Christian groups and sects worldwide. Christians can barely agree on anything—least of all on salvation itself. And if one’s afterlife destiny truly depends on solving a theological puzzle that even Christians cannot agree on, then salvation begins to look less like a free gift of grace and more like a cruel game of cosmic Russian roulette.

Such a vision does not reveal a loving Father. It depicts something far darker: a deity presiding over confusion, disproportionately punishing creatures for failing to decipher competing doctrines He allowed to fracture endlessly. That is not grace—it is perilously close to an abusive experiment masquerading as justice.

With that in mind, I will now present three moral and theological arguments against the doctrine of Eternal Conscious Torment.

Eternal Conscious Torment Fails the Test of Divine Guardianship

God is our guardian because He created us, knows us, and sustains our every breath. We are all His offspring (Acts 17:28). We are under His responsibility. Like a faithful shepherd, He watches over what is His own—not from obligation, but from love. We are not self-made or forgotten; we are held, protected, and guided by the One who gave us life.

When evangelizing unbelievers, Christians often reach for the analogy of God as a loving Father who loves them deeply—because it resonates, because it draws people in. But let’s stop and actually test that analogy, rather than invoking it rhetorically and exempting it from scrutiny.

A good parent/guardian teaches, nurtures, and corrects their children/wards. Even if they rebel, a loving guardian does not abuse them by burning them in the fire or casting them into a torture chamber to suffer forever. If God is truly a better father than any human parent (Matthew 7:11), why would He operate by standards we would find abusive in any human relationship?

If we, as imperfect human beings, would never burn our own children alive for disobedience (unless we are psychopaths), how can we believe that a God of infinite love and mercy would commit something infinitely worse?

Even earthly parents know that when children misbehave, correction should be measured, temporary and purposeful—aimed at growth. But punishment designed only to inflict endless suffering? That is not justice. That is cruelty. If hell truly meant infinite torment with no chance of healing or change, then it would serve no purpose except to gratify divine sadism. However, Scripture teaches that God’s justice is restorative, not vengeful; His love corrects, heals, forgives enemies, and seeks reconciliation. Unending torture accomplishes none of this.

This is the big difference between a religion driven by fear and the true Gospel of a God who is completely kind, forgiving, and loving. If the primary purpose of human life is simply to avoid Eternal Conscious Torment, then something is profoundly wrong with how we have imagined both God and ourselves. Life becomes a fearful Escape Room contest, not a meaningful journey; faith becomes anxiety, not transformation.

For what kind of loving God would torment most of humanity forever with no redemptive goal? Why would the One who commands us to forgive without limit (Matthew 5:44; Luke 17:4) refuse to forgive His own offspring and image bearers? And how could infinite punishment be fair for finite beings—fragile minds shaped by trauma, culture, fear, misinformation, and distortion?

If God is a loving Father, then His justice must look like love. And if He created us with limited understanding, why assume that once we stand before Him—seeing clearly for the first time—we would not see, understand, repent, and turn to Him freely?

A theology built on fear collapses under its own contradictions. A theology built on love, restoration, and the character of Christ is the only one that makes sense of Scripture—and the human heart.

Eternal Conscious Torment Fails the Test of Redemptive Grace

We love to sing about the sweetness of God’s amazing grace—but do we truly grasp what grace actually means?

Human ideas of “justice” are often fueled by emotion, wounded pride, and a thirst for revenge. That is precisely why so many find a dark comfort—and even a perverse satisfaction—in the image of a monstrous god who tortures their enemies forever. The doctrine of Eternal Conscious Torment does not reveal divine grace; it mirrors human vindictiveness. It gratifies something ugly within us: not the longing for restoration, but the craving for payback. Hell becomes a theological permission slip to say, “They may have hurt me here, but one day my God will make them suffer far worse—forever!”

But pause and test this vision against reality. If your child came to you and said they had converted to Buddhism or Judaism, would you burn them at the stake? Of course not. And if you—with all your limitations, fears, and moral failures—would never commit such an atrocity, why imagine that God, whose love infinitely surpasses yours, would do exactly that simply because someone died unconvinced?

Grace that ends in endless torture is not grace at all. It is revenge wearing religious language.

[Trigger warning: sensitive content ahead]

Picture this: your unbelieving child, who had converted to Buddhism or Judaism, suffers a horrific tragedy—kidnapped, assaulted, tortured, and ultimately murdered by a true monster. The perpetrator is caught, tried, their crimes laid bare before the world, and sentenced to unending torment.

You attend the first session of their punishment. Red-hot irons sear their flesh. Teeth ripped from their mouth. Fingernails torn out. Skin flayed. You watch in silence, and for a moment, you feel something like relief—vindication, a dark echo of justice.

A month passes. You return. The torment hasn’t stopped. Kneecaps shattered. Joints pulled from their sockets. Their tongue severed. Face burned. What was once screaming is now only hoarse, animal whimpering. You leave feeling justice is being served.

But in time, the fire of revenge cools, and bitterness returns, hollow and cold. So you come back again. Years later. Skin peeled away to show raw muscle. Scalp torn off. Empty sockets where eyes once were. Suspended in place, the burning flames slowly melt the skin. The spectacle is endless, grotesque. A decade passes—yet somehow the body still clings to life. The creature still feels everything. Acid hisses as it eats through what remains of its limbs. Bones are splintered, mangled beyond recognition. There is no release, no death—only unending conscious suffering.

It’s terrifying to read this, I know. But this is the picture many hold of hell: Eternal Conscious Torment, pain inflicted forever, without healing or redemption.

But let’s be honest, in the common Christian view, it’s not just monsters but all unbelievers who are doomed to endure Eternal Conscious Torment in hellfire. In this awful scenario, that means your “unsaved” child would be likewise suffering in hell forever, too.

It is easy to imagine monsters like Hitler suffering forever. That thought feels emotionally satisfying. But the reality of Eternal Conscious Torment is far darker. Most who hold to it do not consign only Hitler to endless suffering—they consign most of humanity, including the millions Hitler tortured, starved, and burned alive, to the same fate of unending torment in hellfire.

This is the quiet horror of the doctrine. It does not merely punish perpetrators of extraordinary evil; it sweeps up victims alongside them. Under ECT, the grandma and her grandchild in the concentration camps and the architect of the camps share the same fate—not because of justice, but because they failed to pass a particular theological threshold. That is not moral clarity. It is a moral collapse.

According to Charles Spurgeon, a foundational and towering figure in many Protestant traditions, including the Baptist and evangelical:

When thou diest, thy soul will be tormented alone; that will be a hell for it: but at the day of judgment thy body will join thy soul, and then thou wilt have twin hells, thy body sweating drops of error, and thy soul suffocating in a sea of fire.... There is a real fire in hell, as truly as you have a real body—a fire which shall burn the body, but yet not consume it; it shall melt the iron, but the metal shall not pass away; it shall burn the skin, but the skin shall never be consumed. Every nerve shall be a highway for the hot chariot of pain to travel on; every vein shall be a canal for the river of fire to roll through; and every bone shall be a scorched pillar of agony.135

Likewise, according to John MacArthur:

Agony, banishment, brimstone, curse, darkness, deprivation, destruction, distress, fire, teeth grinding, guilt, hopelessness, loneliness, pain, suffering, pressure, prison, punishment, ruin, separation, shame, contempt, smoke, sulfur, torment, trouble, trash heap, weeping—all forever.136

(John MacArthur)

But this isn’t divine justice. It’s the "justice" of tyrants—the fear-mongering of regimes like the Islamic Republic of Iran or North Korea, where pain and torment are mistaken for righteousness and justice.

Endless torment doesn’t fix anything; it doesn't restore what was lost. It doesn’t bring a dead child back to life. It only feeds vengeance, never reconciles.

The gospel offers something profoundly different and better. Not endless torment for criminals and unbelievers, but restoration of all. The promise that one day, every broken thing, every murdered child, every shattered life will be made whole again. That even the worst of sinners will bow their knees and confess that Jesus Christ is Lord—not because they’re crushed, but because they finally see and understand.

That’s hope—not revenge. That’s the good news of the gospel.

Punishing the wicked doesn’t bring back the innocent whose lives were lost. No matter how severe the consequences, they cannot restore what the wrongdoers have taken away. How would torturing a million Germans bring my lost Jewish family members back to me? Only the true Gospel offers genuine hope—not endless retribution, but the hope in the restoration of all things—I will get to see them again!

Truth be told, Eternal Conscious Torment offers nothing more than a counterfeit emotional release—a revenge fantasy dressed up in religious language. It gives people permission to cling to bitterness while calling it righteousness. Rather than doing the difficult, transformative work of forgiveness, healing, and grace, ECT hands them a theology that baptizes resentment and declares infinite suffering to be “justice” for those who caused them pain. It does not heal the wound; it sanctifies it.

But this is not the heart of God revealed in Jesus. It is the projection of our pain onto the divine, creating a god in our own broken image.

No amount of revenge will ever bring back a lost loved one. The eternal torment of others cannot undo your grief or heal your broken heart. Only Christ offers something truly redemptive: resurrection of those you've lost! He will raise everyone to life again. Every tear will be wiped away. No more death. No more trauma. No pain will last forever.

The deepest wounds of history will not be ignored—they will be healed and made new. (Revelation 21:5). That's God's grace to us.

Eternal Conscious Torment Fails the Test of Corrective Justice

A punishment born of love is meant to guide and correct—to heal, not to break; to restore, not to destroy. God’s discipline does not erupt from rage, but flows from love. It is a refining fire, not an inferno of vengeance. Like a good Father, God disciplines His children in order to bring them back—whole, humbled, healed, and transformed.

Eternal Conscious Torment, by contrast, serves no redemptive purpose whatsoever. It does not teach. It does not restore. It does not reconcile. It does not heal. It is not justice. It is cruelty for cruelty’s sake. In this doctrine, punishment is emptied of meaning and inflated into endless sadism. The Augustinian-Calvinistic god it portrays is not a healer but a sadist and tormentor—one who delights in unending suffering with no goal beyond inflicting pain. Unsurprisingly, this vision steadily deadens compassion and erodes moral sensitivity in those who defend it.

Consider the reality of the human condition. Most people are shaped from birth by culture, fear, trauma, ignorance, survival instincts, mental illness, distorted beliefs, and inherited wounds. Much of what we call “sin” flows from brokenness—not malice. What needs to be destroyed is sin itself, not the human being caught in its grip. What humanity needs is healing, not more pain and punishment.

A genuinely Christian heart should recoil at the idea of God eternally tormenting the majority of humanity. It should grieve, not defend it. Yet many in the West passionately protect this grotesque doctrine, propped up by a Romanized, retributive vision of justice that owes more to empire than to Christ. Test your own heart honestly: if the idea of endless suffering strikes you as “righteous,” then something has gone deeply wrong—not with God’s justice, but with how you have been taught to imagine it.

In the Hebrew Scriptures, "fire" is often used to describe God’s justice137—not as a tool of sadism, but as a force of refinement and purification. Fire refines gold, burns away impurities, and prepares something to be made new. In this context, divine fire isn’t about perpetual abuse—it’s about healing what’s broken, cleansing what’s defiled, and restoring what’s been lost:

I will put them into the fire; I will refine them like silver and test them like gold. They will call on my name, and I will answer them; I will say, "They are my people," and they will say, "The LORD is our God.” 

(Zechariah 13:9)

Infinite punishment for finite sins is neither just nor merciful. A crime committed in a human lifespan cannot deserve infinite, unending torture—that is not justice; it is sadism. The Augustinian-Calvinist hellfire overrides not only redemptive justice but also grace and mercy entirely—for they mean the offering of redemption, restoration, and forgiveness to the undeserving. Apparently, in the fundamentalist imagination, God’s mercy has an expiration date. It lasts only until the moment of death, after which grace is abruptly replaced by endless vengeance. Such a view not only shrinks mercy to a temporary feature of God rather than an everlasting expression of who He is, but also contradicts the Bible: "O give thanks unto the God of heaven: for his mercy endureth forever." (Psalm 136:26)

On the other hand, a temporary judgment serves a purpose. And if that purpose is not sadistic but loving—then, by simple logic, it must be restorative. Any punishment that comes to an end implies that something has been accomplished: purification, repentance, healing, or transformation. Therefore, even those who undergo judgment will ultimately be restored, because a finite correction can only make sense if it leads to a greater good. That's true, restorative justice.

This is not sentimentality—it's biblical logic grounded in the nature of God:

His anger lasts only a moment, but His favor lasts a lifetime.

(Psalm 30:5)

And in Lamentations:

For no one is cast off by the Lord forever. Though he brings grief, he will show compassion, so great is his unfailing love.

(Lamentations 3:31-32)

The doctrine of Eternal Conscious Torment has nothing to do with God's justice. God’s discipline is not unending, and His justice is always intertwined with His love. If hell has an end, its purpose is not to indulge a psychopath’s sadistic desire for revenge, but to bring about redemptive justice. Torturing someone forever—even when disguised in religious language and framed as "doctrine"—doesn’t make anything right; it’s simply the product of a sadist’s imagination.

Gladly, some ancient theologians, like St. Isaac the Syrian, grasped this truth: "Those who are tormented in hell are tormented by the invasion of love... It is absurd to assume that the sinners in hell are deprived of God's love."138

Early Church Fathers of the first and second centuries—among them the theologian, St. Clement of Alexandria—held that because the image of God is present in every human being, all people will ultimately be restored to God. This restoration, however, was not imagined as cheap or automatic; it involved a purifying process by which sin is stripped away from what God created good.

This hopeful view of universal restoration prevailed throughout the first three centuries, lasting until the time of Augustine. The belief was that God’s redemptive work in Christ would ultimately heal, restore, and reconcile all people, bringing the entire creation back into harmony with God like it was before the fall.

To deny such a hope would imply that God condemns His own image—that He consigns to endless ruin what bears His likeness. In other words, it would mean God condemns, forever, something of Himself that He placed within every person.

Conclusion

In the end, the question is not how far God is willing to punish, but how far His love is willing to go. The God revealed in Jesus does not save by fear, nor rule by terror, but heals by truth, patience, and mercy that refuses to let go. Hope is not naïve—it is faithful to the character of God. And if love is stronger than death, wiser than wrath, and more enduring than sin, then the story of humanity does not end in despair and torment, but in restoration, light, and a grace vast enough to bring every lost soul home.

c2YY

Chapter 41
Why "Free Will" Can’t Justify Eternal Conscious Torment

For centuries, the Augustinian–Calvinist interpretation of hell, known as Eternal Conscious Torment, has been defended on the grounds of free will. The common argument goes something like this: “God doesn’t send anyone to hell; they choose it by rejecting Him.” Yet ironically, the same infernalist voices also warn us to fear the God who will “cast you into hell” (Luke 12:5).

But is this really a choice? Or is it actually the worst form of coercion?

If free will is exercised under the threat of unending torment, then it is not free at all. A choice made under existential fear is a forced choice, no different from someone pressing a gun to your head and ordering you to your knees—or an abuser telling their captive, “If you don’t love me, I’ll burn you—but it’s your choice.”

Is God Like a Father Warning His Child of the Danger on the Highway?

Then I also often heard Eternal Conscious Torment defended with an analogy like this: we are toddlers playing on a busy highway, and our Father lovingly warns us not to run into traffic because we will be hit. God gives the warning, but ultimately leaves the choice to us—free will.

But let’s press into that fable a little further: What kind of parent lets toddlers play next to a highway in the first place? Any decent, loving parent would build a fence, lock the doors, hold their child’s hand, or remove the danger entirely—not step back, warn them once, and then say, “If you get hit, that’s on you!”

If God is truly loving and all-powerful, then placing “spiritually helpless children” near a deadly, infinite consequence—and then holding them responsible for not avoiding it—isn’t justice. It’s negligence at best, cruelty at worst.

Millions die without ever reading the Bible, understanding the Gospel, or even hearing the name Jesus in any meaningful way. Are they still toddlers playing on highways? And if so, who put them there?

The False Choice: Love or Infinite Suffering

What kind of loving parent would hurl their child into a fire pit or strap them to an eternal torture rack for failing a theology exam? If even flawed, broken humans recoil in horror at such cruelty, how dare we project it onto a perfect Father and call it “holiness”? A god who does that isn’t loving—he’s monstrous—and he bears no resemblance to the Father Jesus revealed.

Love me, or else...

Imagine a person being forced to sign a contract with a knife to their throat. Can we honestly say they made a “free” choice? No, they made a choice to avoid suffering. Love has nothing to do with it. That’s exactly what the monstrous doctrine of ECT does—it places a gun to the soul and calls it “free will.”

If rejecting God means unending torment, then there is no real alternative—only forced submission. It makes no actual sense to say that God lets people destroy themselves out of respect for their free will. If God truly loves us, He wouldn’t just watch us destroy ourselves forever in the name of “free will.” Just as a good parent wouldn’t let their hurting child walk into fire without stopping her, real love doesn’t abandon—it rescues.

Or worse, imagine a parent saying to their child, “Love me, or I’ll make sure you fall into a pit of fire where you will scream in agony forever.” Would we call that parent loving? Would we say the child has genuine free will? Of course not. Because love, by definition, must be freely and unconditionally given—not extorted through terror.

If your theology portrays God as less loving or caring than the parent you aspire to be, it’s time to rethink that theology.

Yet, fundamentalist theology tells us that God, the supposed source of perfect love, presents humanity with the very same ultimatum: "Worship me, or I’ll burn you forever!" The mere fact that rejecting God comes with such a threat makes the choice fundamentally illegitimate, morally absurd. It's the very definition of a fear-based religion.

No religious faith that relies on fear of unending punishment can be called just or loving. A choice made under the threat of permanent suffering is not a choice—it’s coercion.

Love is not something you can force. It must be freely given.139

(C. S. Lewis)

Fear-Based Love Is Not Love At All

The Bible repeatedly tells us that “perfect love casts out fear” (1 John 4:18). Yet, Eternal Conscious Torment depends and thrives on fear. The entire premise of hellfire theology is rooted in existential terror, not love.

What kind of love demands threats of perpetual torture? If God truly desires a loving relationship with us, why would He construct a system where fear is the primary motivator? An affection given under the threat of hellfire is not love at all—it’s survival at best.

Free will without true alternatives is meaningless.

Even in the Torah, Punishment Was Never Without End

Even within the supposedly “legalistic” Law of the Old Testament—where consequences are stated plainly and choice is taken seriously (e.g., Deuteronomy 11:26)—the harshest penalty for the most severe crimes was death, not perpetual torture. When execution was prescribed, it marked an end in this life, not the beginning of endless torment in the next. It was “game over” in this world—not infinite suffering in another.

If torment were truly the highest expression of justice, one would expect God to mention it in the Old Testament Law. But He does not. Instead, we find a legal system marked by restraint, proportionality, and mercy—one so cautious that executions were rare, surrounded by safeguards, and never meant to be cruel. Even when death was required, it was to be swift, not sadistically dragged out for years. Prolonged torture was the hallmark of pagan nations, not Israel. And nowhere in the Law of Moses is there a threat of eternal suffering after death. Not once.

What makes this even more striking is Scripture’s own testimony. Some of the Bible’s most revered figures committed crimes that, under the Law, deserved death—murder, idolatry, adultery, conspiracy, persecution. Yet they were not discarded. They were confronted, corrected, transformed, and ultimately redeemed. If God’s justice in this life could allow King David’s restoration after committing adultery with Bathsheba and orchestrating the death of her husband Uriah, why would He suddenly be more merciless and unforgiving in the next life?

Human free will—our stubbornness, ignorance, and self-destructive choices—is not a justification for Eternal Conscious Torment. It is the very reason transformation is necessary. A God who understands human weakness does not respond with infinite cruelty, but with patient, redemptive power aimed not at destroying the sinner, but at healing their brokenness.

How Free Is Our Mind, Anyway?

Now, let's be honest. To justify God tormenting us forever, our minds would have to be totally and genuinely free—unbiased, unclouded, unaffected by anything or anyone. But that is impossible for us. We are shaped by every experience, every influence, every wound, every fear, every voice that has ever spoken into our lives—even demonic ones. Human beings cannot achieve perfect objectivity or perfect clarity. Only God can.

We live in an era where ideas are traded like currency—endless controversial paragraphs scrolling through social media feeds and podcasts. Culture encourages us to “think out loud,” to share reactions freely and boldly, to broadcast interpretations and flood the world with personal and collective opinions. Yet how many of these thoughts and feelings truly belong to us? Psychologist Jordan Peterson often cites psychiatrist Carl Jung’s observation: “People don’t have ideas—ideas have people.” According to Peterson, 95% of what we think and say doesn’t originate from our true selves. Most of what runs through our minds comes from thought and emotional patterns implanted from the outside. It’s the voice of the world—not our own. When we think, feel, or say “we,” it’s often someone else’s voice speaking through us:

Most of what you think and most of what you say are the opinions of other people. There are things you've read and things people have told you. That's a benefit in some ways because you get all those thoughts that other people have spent a long time formulating, but it's a disadvantage in that it's not exactly you.140

(Jordan Peterson)

If human judgment is this fragile—shaped by wounds we didn’t choose, fears we never asked for, malevolent spiritual forces we cannot control, cultures we didn’t design, ideas that often “have us” more than we have them—then for God to punish anyone with infinite torment would be monstrously unjust. How could a perfectly good God condemn people forever for a will—thoughts and choices—that was never fully their own? For reactions formed by trauma? For beliefs shaped by upbringing, genetics, personality, pressures, or the thousand invisible influences that make us who we are? If our minds are not free in the fullest sense, then no verdict of infinite punishment could ever be fair. Everlasting hellfire would require perfect clarity, perfect freedom, perfect objectivity—and we do not possess these. Only God does.

In other words, we do have free will—but it is not “free” in the sense of pure objectivity or neutrality. Therefore, if God is truly just, He cannot hold finite, conditioned creatures accountable forever for decisions made with limited knowledge, limited understanding, limited freedom, and limited agency. The logic is inescapable: a God of perfect justice could not—would not—burn confused, wounded, influence-shaped humans forever.

Eternal Conscious Torment Diminishes the Gospel

If God is truly love, if He is truly merciful and full of grace, then His actions should reflect a love greater than human love—not less.

A truly loving God would:

Offer endless opportunities for reconciliation (Matthew 18:21–22), even in the age-to-come.

Bring healing, not inflict endless pain (Luke 5:31–32).

Aim at the restoration of people and the world—not their destruction (2 Corinthians 5:18-19).

Desire genuine love, not forced compliance (Romans 8:38–39).

Never create a religious system based on terror, coercion, and perpetual suffering (Lamentations 3:31-32).

Telling someone they will burn in hell unless they believe X is the very definition of coercion—and coercion stands in direct opposition to love, because love cannot be forced. For that reason, any “gospel” that adds a term or condition to God’s forgiveness is not the gospel at all. Anything that sounds like, “God has forgiven you in Christ, but only if…” or “You are forgiven if…” is a lie.

The salvific heart of the gospel is this: through Christ, God has “taken away the sin of the world” (John 1:29). No ifs. No buts. Just a period.

He is the atoning sacrifice for our sins, and not only for ours but also for the sins of the whole world.

(1 John 2:2)

God, who reconciled us to himself through Christ and gave us the ministry of reconciliation: that God was reconciling the world to himself in Christ, not counting people’s sins against them. And he has committed to us the message of reconciliation.

(2 Corinthians 5:18-19)

Conclusion

If Christianity is to reflect the God of love, it must abandon the abusive framework of Augustinian hellfire and return to the true God of Israel revealed through Jesus’ transforming grace. God forces no one into submission; He is wise and loving enough that, in the end, every person will come freely. For now, we see only fragments—but when the whole truth is unveiled, no knee will remain unbowed.

Jesus will ultimately gather every prodigal soul, no matter how far they’ve wandered or how deeply they’ve fallen into fear, pain, or resistance. Through Christ, God finds, binds wounds, and leads us home—even through seasons of regret, “gnashing of teeth,” and long nights of refusal. Some will resist the rescue, clinging to bitterness or confusion, but no heart can withstand the steady fire of perfect love forever.

When illusions fade and every wound is healed, all will awaken to the same truth: Jesus Christ is God’s relentless love reaching for us. No fall is too deep. No detour beyond His reach. In the end—only God knows how—all will find themselves, scarred yet cherished, gathered into His embrace.

c31B

Chapter 42
He Truly Sees Us

My Jewish mother refuses to accept Jesus—not out of rebellion or hardness of heart, but because the “Jesus” presented to her family was wrapped in antisemitism and soaked in hatred. To her, to her parents, and to her relatives, this so-called Messiah was inseparable from those who claimed His name while persecuting Jews: crusaders and inquisitors, pogroms and blood libels, and finally the horrors of the Nazis. What she was asked to believe in was not the Jewish Messiah of Israel, but a distorted figure weaponized against her people.

Before moving to Israel, she grew up in Eastern Europe and endured intense antisemitism from the surrounding Christians, who made sure she knew she was being mocked and bullied solely because she was Jewish.

And if that wasn’t enough, when she later met Messianic believers in Israel, they told her that the same god in whose name her family had been persecuted—the one she was supposed to trust—was now also cruelly burning them in hellfire, just like Hitler did to them, only this time for good.

Will God Ignore Christian Antisemitism in His Judgement of the Jewish People?

When so-called Christian public figures like Candace Owens spread antisemitism day after day—doing so beneath a visible cross and while calling themselves Christian—what do you think Jewish people conclude about Christianity? About Christ Himself? The message they receive is not love, mercy, or truth, but hostility baptized in religious symbols. Once again, the name of Jesus is dragged through the mud by those who claim to represent Him.

Now consider the harder question: is God really so narrow-minded, so limited, so lacking in understanding, empathy, and love, that He would turn away Jews like my mother simply because the only “Jesus” they ever knew was a twisted counterfeit—distorted, hateful, and used as a weapon against them? What kind of justice is that? What sort of ruthless judge does that portray God to be? Where, then, are the mercy, patience, and grace so often preached from pulpits and sung in worship songs? Are they real—or merely rhetorical?

The idea that a God of infinite wisdom, justice, love, power, and understanding would fail to save most of humanity—and instead condemn creatures of limited rationality and overwhelming emotion to Eternal Conscious Torment—is not merely disturbing; it is incoherent. To suggest that God allows people to damn themselves because of misunderstanding, deception, trauma, fear, pain, or inherited hatred—conditions bound up with the very fabric of human existence—is to portray Him as distant, indifferent, and blind to the human condition.

Such a god does not truly see us. He does not understand us. And he bears little resemblance to the God revealed in Jesus Christ.

Does God Ignore Christian Bigotry and Sectarianism?

What the Jewish people experienced is not an isolated case.

Consider the British Empire. Its leadership was almost entirely Christian, and Christianity—explicitly and implicitly—helped shape its moral imagination and imperial vision. India lived under British rule for nearly two centuries. And while colonial rule is not in itself evil, the reality on the ground was often oppressive, violent, and merciless toward native populations.

Now ask yourself what impression that legacy has left on the minds of over a billion Hindus. For generations, Christianity was not encountered as good news, but as the religion of the occupier—the faith associated with domination, humiliation, and injustice. When a so-called “Christian nation” ruled with cruelty, how would the average Hindu come to imagine the Christian God? Almost certainly not as a God of mercy, compassion, and love. Would they feel drawn to a Western religion under those conditions? Highly unlikely.

Now narrow the lens further. Imagine a fifteen-year-old Hindu girl in the 1800s who dies “without Christ”—having never encountered Jesus except as a symbol tied to the suffering and subjugation of her people. Would a just and loving God ignore her entire life context and cast her into Eternal Conscious Torment simply because she did not believe correctly?

If that were the case, then God would not be a righteous judge at all—a good judge always seeks understanding. He considers context. He weighs motive, background, trauma, and exposure. He understands how culture shapes belief, how history distorts truth, and how human hearts are formed long before conscious choices are made.

To ignore all of that would be to render judgment without mercy and justice without wisdom—something no good judge ever does. And the God revealed in Jesus is not a careless executioner of souls, but a discerning, compassionate, and infinitely just Father who sees the whole story before He renders a verdict.

And God is not just a good judge—He is the perfect and most merciful judge:

Therefore the Lord waits to be gracious to you; therefore he will rise up to show mercy to you. For the Lord is a God of justice; blessed are all those who wait for him.

(Isaiah 30:18)

Who Are Unbelievers Truly Rejecting?

We are all shaped by extremely limited knowledge, flawed understanding, emotional wounds, cultural conditioning, religious distortion, and environmental influence. A child born in North Korea or a teen raised in a spiritually abusive Christian household aren’t necessarily rejecting God—they’re rejecting what they’ve been told He is. They may be rejecting a god of terror, control, tribalism, hypocrisy, or injustice. But the true and living God—the Father revealed in Jesus—is often hidden from them. So, is it really rejection if they’ve never truly encountered Him, understood Him, grasped His message, or known Him? Remember, the very reason Jesus asked the Father to forgive His enemies was because "they know not what they do" (Luke 23:34).

Humanity is ignorant of its own sin because human consciousness is limited, conditioned, shaped, and often distorted—and it’s in that very gap where the grace of God is needed and where it appears, not to condemn, but to forgive.

We are all still in the fog, still sorting through shadows, lies, traumas, and half-truths. Many Christians project a smug sense of certainty, as if they alone—unlike the other 48,999 views—have finally “figured it all out.” But once pressed, even the most respected theologians, priests, rabbis, and mystics have admitted to me in countless theological discussions that their understanding is incomplete on so many topics. My experience includes many messianic pastors, several seminary professors at Dallas Theological Seminary, and even a Catholic cardinal.

Even the apostle Paul—who experienced visions and revelations—said, “Now we see through a glass, dimly… now I know in part” (1 Corinthians 13:12). If that was true for Paul, how much more so for the rest of us?

And if salvation is to come to the knowledge of Him, then that knowledge—by Paul’s own admission—is still partial, indirect, and incomplete: seen “through a glass,” and only “dimly.” Thus, it could be argued that rejection based on a partial or distorted understanding isn’t rebellion—it’s confusion. And God understands it.

I believe this logic applies both to those who pervert His name and image and those who "willfully" reject Him. But when they finally understand, when the veil is lifted and the truth of God’s goodness is fully revealed, awe and surrender will be the only natural response. Every knee will bow.

Conclusion

When the blinders are gone, when the distortions are burned away by perfect love, then—and only then—will the human soul truly be able to choose. When that soul sees God for who He really is, it will not walk away.

This is why all rejection of God must be temporary. This is why Hell—however one defines it—must also be temporary. Because the rejection is not of God Himself, but of a false image—a distortion. God’s plan is not to punish the confused, the brainwashed by the devil, the indoctrinated by sin, but to enlighten them. And once they are enlightened—truly, fully, gloriously—they will fall to their knees, not in shame or fear, but in praise.

Every knee will bow when every mind truly understands.

c32H

Chapter 43
God Understands Evil—and the Way It Shapes Us

No child is born a hater; no baby longs to murder. Even the most destructive personalities—narcissists included—are largely shaped by the wounds, neglect, and environments that formed them. It is mostly through trauma and indoctrination in hatred that innocent hearts are twisted into instruments of violence.

Boy Named Ahmed—An Illustration

From the moment Ahmed was born, his world was filled with the passionate voices of the muezzins echoing from loudspeakers. Before he could walk the streets of Gaza, his ears had already absorbed words heavy with meaning: Shahid, Jihad, evil Zionists, martyrdom, and being told Allah is the God of revenge.

At three years old, Ahmed was already chanting hateful phrases he didn’t understand but was praised for saying. His uncle placed a toy gun in his hand and told him he was Allah's lion against the Jews. “Allah will reward you. One day, you will bring honor to your family,” he said.

His picture books weren’t like those of Western children. They were filled with tales of young boys who died fighting the so-called “enemy”—Jews portrayed not as individuals, but as one cursed people, dehumanized and imagined with horns. In one story, a Jewish man betrayed the Prophet Muhammad and was justly punished. In another, a teenager’s soul was lifted to paradise because he threw a stone at a Jew and was killed. Ahmed learned that paradise was closest to the brave, and bravery meant resistance, and resistance meant bloodshed.

At five, Ahmed sat cross-legged in the mosque, where the imam taught that to fight the Zionists was not only political—it was divine will. The Quran was quoted to him in fragments, always ending in certainty: “You, little one, are chosen by Allah to resist. To fight is to believe.”

By age seven, Ahmed was playing “ambush” with his cousins in alleyways, pretending to sneak up on Jews made of cardboard. He practiced knife grips, praised by his older brother, who had photos of armed martyrs on his bedroom wall. At family dinners, the name of an uncle who had blown himself up at a Jerusalem bakery was mentioned with pride. “He is in paradise,” his aunt said, “and now his whole family is honored.”

Ahmed didn’t learn Hebrew. He didn’t meet Jewish children. He didn’t hear stories about peace or shared pain. He only knew what he had been indoctrinated with: fear, resistance, and the promise that Allah’s favor would rest on him if he fought unto death.

By thirteen, driven by sexual desire and swayed by warped beliefs, he was led to believe he’d be rewarded with 72 virgins if he died committing violence against the infidels.

By fifteen, Ahmed was ready. He no longer smiled when he played. His play had become preparation. When a recruiter from a militant group noticed him at the mosque, he praised his knowledge, his devotion, and his seriousness. “You are pure,” the man said. “The world hasn’t corrupted you. You are now ready. You will make Allah proud!” Ahmed believed him.

The boy who once held a toy gun now held a real one. He no longer saw the world in shades—only in black and white. Us and them. Allah and the cursed Jews. He was told that if he died fighting, he would awaken immediately after in the gardens of paradise, with angels and virgin girls attending to him, and countless heavenly rewards awaiting.

He didn’t know the faces of the people he was taught to hate. He never met them. He only knew the story he had been brainwashed with, over and over again, in the name of Allah. He didn't know better.

This story might sound like fiction to a Western “enlightened” ear, but it’s an everyday reality in parts of the Middle East and Africa, where Groups such as Hamas, Hezbollah, ISIS, Boko Haram, and Islamic Jihad have repeatedly weaponized children—brainwashing them into soldiers and then exploiting their deaths to manufacture sympathy and outrage among naïve Western audiences.

Child soldiering is evil in its most distilled form (Matthew 18:6). These children were never truly given a chance—never afforded the freedom, safety, education, or moral formation that Western Christians often take for granted. To equate the “choices” of Muslim children in Gaza with those of a pastor’s child raised in a comfortable Christian home in Texas is not just ignorant; it is morally obscene. Any theology that pretends these lives begin on equal footing is blind to reality—and any judgment that ignores such disparity is not justice, but cruelty masquerading as righteousness.

But I believe God sees everything. He understands how deeply evil shapes, wounds, and distorts His children, and He takes all of that into account in His just and merciful judgment.

We Don’t Realize What We Don’t Know

By definition, the human mind is incapable of grasping all truth, because we are not all-knowing. From birth, we are slowly formed—mostly unconsciously—by inherited narratives. We do choose freely, but our freedom is never absolute; it is always heavily conditioned.

And this is where I believe God’s understanding and mercy come into play. I’m not suggesting young terrorists are in for a garden of roses, but God is not binary. Divine justice isn’t a simple equation of bliss versus endless torment.

Being refined and purified by fire is hardly a metaphor for a pleasant experience, but losing reward or facing consequences for wrongdoing doesn’t have to mean endless torment in hell, as some Christian (and Islamic) traditions claim. Besides, if all will ultimately be restored, then even the most tragic and violent deaths are not the final word. Every person—whether lost traumatically or naturally—will be resurrected and restored again. In that light, even the worst death becomes a temporary condition, not an everlasting one.

If God’s grace isn’t bigger than our own, then it’s not really divine grace at all.

We Wrestle Not Against Flesh and Blood

And here is a crucial point that is often overlooked: many people are shaped not only by culture, but by a dimension of evil that runs far deeper—one reflected in struggles like Ahmed’s. This is something God understands far more profoundly than we do and fully takes into account in His judgment. Human beings are not formed in a vacuum. Spiritual forces are at work as well, and fragile, impressionable creatures that we are, we are profoundly susceptible to their influence and damage.

Scripture is unambiguous on this point: “We wrestle not against flesh and blood, but against principalities, against powers, against the rulers of the darkness of this world, against spiritual wickedness in high places” (Ephesians 6:12). Our struggle, at its core, is not merely human—it is spiritual. Behind every terrorist organization, every murderer, every system of brutality, there are dark powers at work, pulling strings like marionettes—sometimes metaphorically, and at times perhaps quite literally through demonic possession.

These forces do not merely influence individuals; they shape entire systems, cultures, and histories. And they do not spare the Church. Satan assaults Christian belief itself—its doctrines, its translations, its commentaries, its teaching. How else can we account for the staggering fragmentation of Christian theology, or for portrayals of God so grotesque and cruel that they bear no resemblance to the Father revealed in Jesus? Theological distortions—such as Eternal Conscious Torment—do not arise from divine truth, but from spiritual deception.

All of creation is caught up in a cosmic conflict. Most human beings are barely aware that they are participants in it. And if the struggle is spiritual in nature and universal in scope, then the solution—the salvation and redemption of humanity—must also be universal. A conflict that engulfs all creation demands a redemption that reaches just as far. Anything less would leave evil partially victorious and God’s saving purpose unfinished.

If the primary battle is truly between spiritual powers far stronger than we are, then human beings are not monsters by nature, but image-bearers under assault—wounded, manipulated, and distorted by forces beyond their control. In that case, God’s aim is not to annihilate sinners, but to destroy sin itself—and the powers of evil. Thus, His victory must look like healing a disease—not killing the patient, but eradicating the infection.

And if Christ truly accomplished that—atoning for the world so that, in the end, all are saved—then what we witness is a complete and decisive triumph over the powers of darkness. Nothing is left in their grasp. No victim is abandoned. No life is written off as collateral damage. God’s love and power prove so vast, so relentless, and so victorious that they redeem not merely a few survivors, but all of creation—leaving evil empty-handed and defeated at last.

Conclusion

Just as evil and death do not discriminate in their reach, neither does God’s grace and salvation. His redemptive love encompasses all, ensuring that every soul is eventually restored. This reflects the character of a God who does not abandon His creation to ruin, but rescues it fully—revealing both His supreme authority and power, and His limitless mercy.

When we finally see the story as God sees it—when we understand that evil is deeper than individuals and salvation wider than our categories—we discover that God’s answer to darkness is not abandonment or torment, but redemption. His justice does not end in exclusion, but in healing; not in destruction, but in restoration. Sin and evil are confronted, judged, and undone, yet the image-bearer is never discarded. This is the triumph of the gospel: not that a few escape while the rest are lost, but that light overcomes darkness, truth dispels deception, and love outlasts every distortion. A world apart from God fractures and decays. But in Christ, what was lost is being found, what was broken is being restored, and what was condemned is being redeemed. The world is not being thrown away—it is being saved!

c33R

Chapter 44
Love, Fear, and the Conditional “If”—Why the Gospel Cannot Be Rooted in Terror

In religious discussions, few words carry as much weight as “if.” At the center of much Christian theology stand conditional statements: “If you do or believe X, then Y will happen.” Depending on how that if is understood, it can either open the door to a living, transformative experience with God—or warp faith into a system of anxiety, insecurity, and fear.

The meaning of that “if” depends entirely on the framework through which we read it. And here, two very different theological paradigms emerge:

1. Existential Condition (Fear-Based Theology):

“If you don’t intellectually believe the right doctrine or behave a certain way, you’ll suffer Eternal Conscious Torment in hell.”

This view dominates much of Western theology. It places salvation on a razor’s edge, dependent on intellectual or moral precision. In other words, this "if" is a fear-driven faith.

I'm very familiar with the verses that speak about "fear of God" (e.g., Proverbs 9:10; 19:23; Matthew 10:28; 2 Corinthians 7:1). However, the commonly translated biblical term "fear," particularly the Hebrew yir and the Greek phobos, does not equate to the modern English understanding of terror or fright, but instead primarily signifies awe, reverence, and profound respect toward God. Distinct Hebrew words exist for the feeling of terror or dread, such as pachad and eimah. If you want to explore this further, especially how misunderstanding this word has shaped Western doctrines of God's character, I discuss it extensively in my book Lost in Translation: 15 Hebrew Words to Transform Your Christian Faith.

2. Relational/Consequential Condition (Love-Based Theology):

If my son misbehaved and refused to repent, I might deny him ice cream—a temporary consequence that affects our relationship. But I would never even consider taking his life simply because he refused to repent. In other words, the “if” shapes the quality of the relationship and the consequences within it—not the child’s existence or ultimate destiny.

This understanding—deeply rooted in Jewish thought—views God not as a tyrant seeking perpetual punishment, but as a loving Father who disciplines yet never abandons. This is the consistent pattern of God’s relationship with Israel throughout the Hebrew Scriptures.

When God Corrects, Not Forsakes: Israel as the Covenant Example

See, I am setting before you today a blessing and a curse—the blessing if you obey the commands of the Lord your God that I am giving you today; the curse if you disobey the commands of the Lord your God and turn from the way that I command you today by following other gods, which you have not known.

(Deuteronomy 11:26-28)

Yet even at Israel’s lowest moments—idolatry, rebellion, exile—God’s promise remained unwavering:

I will not reject them or abhor them so as to destroy them utterly and break my covenant with them; for I am the Lord their God.

(Leviticus 26:44–45)

This is what the Lord says, he who appoints the sun to shine by day, who decrees the moon and stars to shine by night, who stirs up the sea so that its waves roar—the Lord Almighty is his name: “Only if these decrees vanish from my sight,” declares the Lord, “will Israel ever cease being a nation before me.”

(Jeremiah 31:35–36)

Even "following other gods" did not cause God to abandon Israel.

Even today—despite her spiritual rebellion and the rejection of Christ—Israel stands as living proof that God does not break His promises.

If this is God’s love toward a rebellious, “stiff-necked” (Exodus 32:9; Deuteronomy 9:6, 13) and chronically unfaithful (Jeremiah 3:20; 32:33) nation, does it not apply to us as well?

These verses echo a central biblical truth: God corrects, but He does not cast off forever. His judgments are restorative, not annihilating. He doesn’t engage in cancel culture because His steadfast love remains constant, even when His people fall short.

The God who refused to forsake Israel in her rebellion will not forsake us either. His love for His creation endures even when ours does not, and His faithfulness remains even when ours falters.

The gospel is not a fragile contract that collapses at the first sign of human weakness. It is a relationship—covenantal, parental, rooted in fidelity rather than fear. God disciplines to heal, not to destroy; to guide, not to damn.

If our motivation to believe is rooted in existential fear rather than love, insecurity and anxiety inevitably take hold. We can never rest, because we are never truly certain that God will not punish us forever for believing the wrong thing or failing to behave in the right way.

When “If” Becomes a Threat Instead of an Invitation

If unending torture is held out as a conditional threat—based on performance or possessing the “right” faith—then the gospel is reduced to a transaction. Fear becomes the engine. Obedience is no longer born of love, but of terror. “Okay! I’ll believe! Just promise You won’t torture me forever!”

Unless one is Hannibal Lecter or the bogeyman, by any honest standard of logic, that is not love. It is coercion.

This is why the apostle John wrote:

There is no fear in love. But perfect love casts out fear, because fear has to do with punishment.

(1 John 4:18)

A relationship with God built on fear cannot be intimate. You cannot draw near to someone you’re afraid might destroy you for one reason or another. The soul becomes obsessed with getting the formula right—never resting, never sure, never free.

In fact, research shows that Scrupulosity and OCD are disproportionately high among fundamentalist Christians. A 2019 study, for example, found that the severity of OCD symptoms linked to scrupulosity differed between religious groups, with conservative Catholics and Protestants reporting the highest levels.141

If you're part of this group, please let me encourage you: No matter how far you fall, you cannot outpace the love of God. No matter how badly you stumble, you can never outdo God in love or forgiveness. You will never be better at sinning than He is at redeeming.

If God truly never changes, then neither do His mercy and grace. By definition, our capacity to sin can never exceed His power to redeem. To believe otherwise is to imagine that human failure can overpower divine compassion—to claim that sin is stronger than grace. That is an impossibility. No matter how deep the fall, His forgiveness always reaches deeper still.

The "if" of Divine Love

A faith rooted in love looks very different from one rooted in fear. Love leads to intimacy, joy, and a life of gratitude. Fear leads to anxiety, legalism, and performance-driven spirituality. When we fear God’s rejection, we become actors on a stage, hoping our performance will earn divine applause. But when we trust God’s acceptance, we become children in the arms of a Father—free to grow, learn, stumble, and rise again.

God's “if” isn’t about whether you’ll be endlessly forgiven or endlessly tormented, but about the consequences and rewards—blessings and curses—that flow from how you live your life.

Scripture is full of consequences—not Eternal Conscious Torment, but consequences nonetheless. Paul says:

Each one’s work will become manifest… and the fire will test what sort of work each one has done. 

(1 Corinthians 3:13)

We must all appear before the judgment seat of Christ… to receive what is due for what he has done in the body.

(2 Corinthians 5:10)

In this light, the “if” is no longer existential—it mainly shapes the status and rewards in the age to come. The judgment is not about sadistic wrath or punishment for its own sake, but refinement and purification. Just as a loving parent might discipline a child without ever withdrawing love or identity, so too God punishes for the sake of correction.

The outcome? Not annihilation. Not everlasting hellfire. But loss—or gain—of reward, honor, and role in God’s coming Kingdom. Just like a good parent disciplines but never disowns, God corrects without rejecting (Hebrews 12:6).

This distinction is crucial. If the “if” relates to our identity, inclusion, or immortality itself, then we live in constant dread. But if it relates to our calling, responsibility, and future reward, then it becomes a path to growth.

That’s the Gospel. Not “God threatened us into heaven,” but “God loved us into transformation.”

God still disciplines. There are still stakes. But God’s corrections are born of love, not hate. Like a physician cutting out disease—not to punish, but to heal. Like a fire that refines gold—not to destroy, but to purify.

Reclaiming the “If” as a Blessing

When we see the “if” in the right light, it turns into a medal, a trophy, a bouquet, a promise—not an existential threat. It calls us not to fear never-ending damnation, but to live with purpose. To win heavenly rewards. To reflect God. To steward our gifts. To store up treasures in heaven. To become Christlike. Every act of love, every sacrifice, every act of mercy—none of it is forgotten.

In this light, the “if” becomes a potential blessing, not a burden of existential threat. It challenges us, but never threatens what Christ did for us. It corrects us, but never condemns us. It sharpens our purpose without undermining our identity.

Any discipline that proceeds from a loving source must serve a loving purpose. By simple logic, that purpose can only be restorative. God’s judgment, then, is not vindictive fury but the fierce fire of His love—burning away what destroys, cleansing what corrupts, purging what enslaves.

Judgement that comes to an end implies that something has been achieved: purification, transformation, repentance. And if repentance is reached, then restoration necessarily follows.

Conclusion

Let us, then, walk not in existential fear, but in awe of the grace that welcomes us. Let us believe not for the sake of escaping, but to reflect the One who saves us in every way. For perfect love drives out fear—and draws us deeper into the joy of the Father’s heart.

Let us reject the existential dread masquerading as theology, and embrace the love that drives out fear. Let us obey not because we are terrified, but because we are loved. Let us remember: We are not being threatened into faith. We are being invited into glory.

The retributive vision of Eternal Conscious Torment portrays God as fragile, wounded, capricious, and vindictive—seeking to pacify Himself through the suffering of others. It imagines an offended ruler who lashes out, rather than a healer who restores. But if God truly is love, then His response must be compassion: to heal, not to harm; to restore, not to destroy. Eternal Conscious Torment is irreconcilable with a God who is love—good, perfect, self-sufficient, and complete in Himself.

c35X

Chapter 45
God Is Not a Nazi

If only born-again Christians go to heaven, then the piles of suitcases and bags of human hair displayed at the Holocaust Museum represent thousands upon thousands of men, women, and children suffering eternal agony at the hands of an angry God.142

(Rachel Held Evans)

I’m often amazed at how some Christians use Hitler as the ultimate example of evil—and rightly so, given that he burned countless people alive—yet simultaneously attribute to God the very same actions and character they condemn in Hitler.

The logic used to defend Eternal Conscious Torment for someone like Hitler does not stop with him. It inevitably extends to the six million Jewish men, women, and children he murdered—most of whom were not believers in Jesus—and condemns them to the very same fate. Under this framework, victims and perpetrators alike—the murdered and the Nazi murderers—end up suffering Eternal Conscious Torment.

That conclusion alone exposes the moral bankruptcy of the system.

Think about how absurd this is: under the ECT view, some of the kindest people who ever lived would endure endless torment just like the most monstrous figures in history, often the very people who killed them. And that, so we are told, is God's justice.

Preaching God's Name in Vain

Someone has said that no theology is worth believing that cannot be preached standing in front of the gates of Auschwitz. I, for one, could not stand at those gates and preach a version of God’s sovereignty that makes the extermination of six million Jews, including many children, a part of the will and plan of God such that God foreordained and rendered it certain.143

(Roger Olson, PhD in Theology)

If a “gospel” becomes morally grotesque and collapses into incoherence in the face of atrocities like the Holocaust, then it is not the “good news of great joy for all people” (Luke 2:10). It is a theological system in desperate need of reexamination.

A true gospel—if it is truly good—must be able to speak meaningfully into humanity’s darkest hours, offering a hope deeper than despair and a compassion strong enough to touch the wounds of history. There is hope in knowing we will see the six million—or anyone else, for that matter—again. There is no hope at all, only despair, in believing they are being tormented forever in hellfire.

If our theology becomes useless, or even cruel, when placed beside the worst horrors humanity has endured, then perhaps the problem is not with God—but with the way we have come to understand Him.

Hitler’s Final Moments

It’s one thing for people to have never heard of Jesus or to have only encountered a distorted image of Him, but what about those who are truly evil? What name comes to mind when you think of someone you could never imagine in heaven? For me, growing up as a descendant of Holocaust survivors, that name was Hitler—the monstrous German dictator responsible for millions of deaths, including members of my own Jewish family. Adolf Hitler, who ended his life by gunshot on April 30, 1945, in his Führerbunker in Berlin, embodied for many the very definition of irredeemable evil.

But there’s an overlooked part of Hitler's story that turns our assumptions on their head.

During Hitler’s meteoric rise to power, a German theologian named Dietrich Bonhoeffer became one of his fiercest critics—eventually participating in a plot to overthrow the Nazi regime. For this, Bonhoeffer was arrested, imprisoned, and finally executed in April 1945, just days before Germany’s defeat. His letters and papers from prison, published posthumously, remain one of the most profound testimonies to Christian conviction under tyranny.

According to a report online, a letter was found hidden deep within the ruins of Hitler’s bunker. This letter, allegedly written shortly before his suicide, revealed something shocking: Hitler had been haunted by Bonhoeffer’s teachings in the final days of the war. The words he had once dismissed as weakness now gnawed at him. His letter records overwhelming remorse for his crimes, particularly against the Jewish people. It describes a desperate cry for God’s mercy—a confession, a plea.

The Soviet propaganda machine, it is said, suppressed the letter. Allowing Hitler even the possibility of redemption would have undermined the black-and-white narrative. Hitler, in their view, could only be seen as monstrous and damned—not complex, not broken, not redeemable.

And yet, this possibility forces us to ask a deeper question:

What if he did repent? What if, in the last moments of his life, Adolf Hitler sincerely sought forgiveness from Christ?

Most Christians see no serious theological problem with granting salvation to someone evil—even Hitler—provided he repented before death, much like the thief crucified beside Jesus. In this framework, a sincere last-second prayer or “inviting Jesus into one’s heart” is enough to secure salvation, regardless of a lifetime of atrocities.

The irony is chilling. Under this logic, the six million Jews Hitler murdered would suffer Eternal Conscious Torment, while Hitler himself would walk free.

Think about that for a moment.

According to this belief, the murderer is embraced while the murdered are forever tortured.

The abuser finds peace while those he abused find everlasting agony.

The "born-again" oppressor is granted heaven, while his victims suffer permanent torment in hellfire.

But strangely, these same Christians erupt into spiritual outrage at even the possibility that repentance could be accepted and salvation granted if it occurred five seconds after death—when a soul finally sees undiluted reality face-to-face. In that case, they declare, it is "too late"—grace is cut off, God becomes a cosmic executioner, and the door to heaven is slammed forever.

How strange—how different the outcome, all because of ten seconds. Had Hitler whispered a prayer for mercy five seconds before death, many would eagerly welcome him into heaven. But had that same repentance occurred five seconds after his final breath—after facing pure truth—most would declare it impossible. As if the ticking of a clock, not the sincerity of the heart, determines the fate of an eternal soul. How fragile, how arbitrary, Western theology can be.

If this strikes you as not just logically contradictory but also morally disturbing, you’re definitely not alone.

The theology that flows from Augustine and Calvin, with its binary afterlife of instant heaven or hell, demands such contradictions. It forces you to celebrate Hitler’s "salvation" (if he repented) while condemning Anne Frank to endless burning—because inherited belief systems, not moral sanity, is running the show.

And yet, Scripture reveals a better, more coherent story—a story of justice that restores, not simply punishes; of mercy that outlasts death; of love that never fails.

The story of Hitler’s letter has been floating around the internet for years, though it’s never been proven real. Still, whether it’s genuine or not doesn’t really matter—it works as a fascinating mental exercise. It’s a way to explore how profoundly the Gospel pushes us to rethink our ideas about justice, mercy, and the value of every human life.

Which scenario really makes sense? That someone as monstrous as Hitler could mumble a last-minute confession and be instantly welcomed into heaven without any consequence or reckoning? Or that such a person would have to pass through a refining fire, fully face the evil they committed, and be transformed by it—regardless of their beliefs—before entering God’s presence?

One scenario is moral absurdity. The other is justice healed by mercy.

"Where Sin Increased, Grace Increased All the More" (Romans 5:20)

Salvation is not an on–off switch. It is not a mechanical transaction triggered by intellectual belief or the correct words spoken. And though it is not easy for me to say this as a Jew, I believe God’s mercy and compassion are far greater than my own. His love extends even to my greatest enemies.

In other words, I’d prefer to see Hitler—and anyone else deeply wicked—come to understand the truth, repent, bow in humility, and confess Jesus Christ as Lord, rather than be subjected to endless torture with no redemptive purpose. Eternal torment achieves nothing, but repentance changes everything.

Yes, even a soul as dark and grotesquely deformed as Hitler’s could, through a hell-like process of judgment and purification, be brought to genuine repentance and ultimately restored. Not to earn salvation, but to undergo a true transformation of heart and vision.

This does not trivialize evil in the slightest. It does the opposite. It declares that evil does not get the last word, that even the most corrupted will not eternally rival God’s goodness. It proclaims God’s final and complete victory over evil, not by endlessly sustaining it through punishment, but by overcoming it through judgment that heals, exposes, and ultimately redeems.

But not cheaply. Not instantly. And certainly not without facing the full, crushing horror of what was done. Restoration is never about excusing evil or sweeping it under the rug. That would be cheap grace—as obscene as the idea that Hitler could stroll into heaven simply for muttering the correct prayer seconds before death. That is not grace—it is moral absurdity.

True restoration is costly. It confronts evil head-on. It breaks the sinner under the unbearable weight of truth. It allows grace—slow, severe, and purifying grace—to do its work where nothing else can. For someone like Hitler, that would mean first passing through hell: not as sadistic vengeance, but as the full, unfiltered encounter with reality.

What that hell looks like, I do not pretend to define. But I imagine it as an encounter with truth so devastating it can no longer be avoided—seeing every act as it truly was, not from the safety of distance or ideology, but from within the suffering inflicted on others. Feeling the pain not abstractly, but personally. Bearing the moral weight in the depths of the soul until denial collapses, self-deception shatters, and repentance becomes unavoidable.

That is not cheap grace. That is justice fulfilled. And only a God whose love is stronger than evil could dare to bring even such a soul through fire and out the other side—not to excuse the crime, but to finally, completely, and forever purge sin.

Such repentance would not erase what had been done. It would transform the person who did it. They would not emerge honored or exalted. If anything, they would be “least in the kingdom.” Their honor would be stripped away, their rewards forfeited, their former power undone—exchanged for humility, service, and a long apprenticeship in love.

But restoration does not mean denial. Restoration means healing after justice has been satisfied—not human revenge, but God's perfect justice that disciplines to restore, not destroy.

In the end, if Hitler ends up in heaven, it will not be because his crimes were forgotten. Yesterday was Yom HaShoa—Israel's Holocaust Remembrance Day—and like every year, a nationwide loud siren screamed for two minutes while everyone stood still. We still remember. We still grieve. But no amount of vengeance can bring back our loved ones. And it's okay, because I know they, too, will see God, will meet Jesus, and will come to accept Him. If they too are to be saved, then all the harm done to them by evil Hitlers was only temporary.

Conclusion

In the end, if Hitler is in heaven, it will be because God's mercy is wider than we dared imagine—and because God's justice is not a mirror of human wrathful vengeance, but a fire that purifies even the worst among us. A fire that burns away the impure and forges what’s left into precious gold.

If that scandalizes you, remember: It is not Hitler who defines the limits of God's grace. It is Jesus—the One who said, "Father, forgive them, for they know not what they do." (Luke 23:34). God’s love for humanity is revealed most clearly at the cross. Even as humanity crucified Him, He pleaded for forgiveness—"because love covers over a multitude of sins" (1 Peter 4:8).

And if Jesus could pray that prayer for the very men nailing Him to the cross, then maybe the scandal of grace is exactly what we need to be confronted with—and transformed by.

c37T

Chapter 46
Life's Purpose Isn't Escapism
Conclusion

It feels almost unthinkable to imagine offering forgiveness to the very people who mocked, hammered nails into flesh, spat on, and ultimately crucified Christ.
