But what seems offensive and unthinkable to us was not unthinkable to Jesus.

From the cross—bleeding, suffocating, surrounded by hatred—in those final moments between life and death, He cried out:

“Father, forgive them!” (Luke 23:34)

Some might say there’s no forgiveness for those who turn away from Christ. But if Christ could ask the Father to forgive those who committed the very act of killing Him, then surely His love atones for every human heart, no matter how resistant or broken.

God’s love is not small.

It is not finite or bound by time.

It is not limited by resistance or human rebellion.

His love is boundless, relentless, and eternal—because He is.

The Gospel was never meant to terrify people into belief.

It was never designed to be a cosmic threat hanging over humanity’s head.

The Gospel is a proclamation of forgiveness and an invitation into something—into love, healing, transformation, truth, and life.

It is the announcement that God’s goodness is stronger than our darkest failures, that His mercy outlasts our stubbornness, and that His desire and ability to save are far greater than our ability to resist Him.

Hell's "Fire" Meant for Refinement, Cleansing, and Purification

In the ancient world, fire and sulfur were closely associated with the refining of metals, especially gold. Sulfur, often called brimstone, was used in the purification process. When added to molten metal in a crucible, it helped bind with impurities, forming sulfides that would react, smoke, and separate, allowing the purer metal to settle at the bottom. This method increased the purity and value of the metal.

Within this historical context, the biblical imagery of fire and sulfur points naturally to refinement and purification, not to endless sadistic torment. Just as a refiner’s fire burns away impurities to reveal pure gold, biblical language about fire often depicts a process that exposes, cleanses, and restores rather than one that exists merely to inflict pain without end.

Thus, perhaps the true purpose of what we call “hell”—understood metaphorically and biblically—is what several early Church Fathers actually taught: an encounter with God’s purifying fire. Not retribution for its own sake, but a refining, healing process. Origen of Alexandria, Gregory of Nyssa, Clement of Alexandria, Didymus the Blind, Theodore of Mopsuestia, Isaac of Nineveh, and others, all spoke of divine judgment not as endless revenge, but as corrective, medicinal, and ultimately restorative—fire that burns away corruption so that what God created good may finally be made whole.

As I said previously, I do not pretend to know exactly what this looks like. But I can imagine that it may involve facing, with full awareness, the sorrow and pain we have inflicted on others throughout our lives. Not as vengeance, but as transformation. Truth encountered without escape. Reality felt without denial. A reckoning that breaks self-deception and gives birth to genuine repentance.

For everyone will be salted with fire.

(Mark 9:49)

Such fire does not exist to destroy the person, but to destroy what dehumanizes them. And it is this process—costly, honest, and purifying—that leads to the day Scripture promises: when every knee will bow and every tongue will confess allegiance to Jesus. Not coerced by terror, but won by truth. Not crushed into submission, but healed into worship.

God disciplines us for our good, that we may share in His holiness. No discipline seems pleasant at the time, but painful. Later on, however, it produces a harvest of righteousness and peace for those who have been trained by it.

(Hebrews 12:10–11)

The teachings of the Lord are given so that we might repent now—freely, joyfully, and with open hearts—rather than learning the hard way through painful consequences. Repentance offered today is a gift; delaying it may only mean unnecessary suffering on the path to the same eventual restoration.

Repentance is not merely spoken words or crying eyes—it’s a realignment of our will, our values, and our moral behavior with the heart and character of God.

Think of it like this: a loving parent warns their child about the consequences of reckless choices. The wise child listens—and is spared the pain that follows rebellion. But sometimes, despite all the warnings, the child chooses their own path. When the consequences arrive—like a visit from the authorities or imprisonment—they become the teacher. Suffering opens the door to understanding. And from there, repentance flows.

Christ invites us to skip the needless pain. To follow Him now. To embrace His wisdom and walk the path of repentance—not as punishment, but as the way to true freedom, healing, and joy.

Preaching a Different Gospel

If today’s gospel focuses on escaping endless hellfire torment instead of the early message from the first two centuries about stepping into the life, light, and love of God through Christ, it raises the question: Who altered the biblical message, and for what reason?

The answer is straightforward: if Satan used Scripture in an attempt to tempt and deceive Jesus—if he dared to twist the Bible against Christ Himself—then we can be certain he employs the very same tactic to mislead the Church.

It only makes sense that one of the most effective ways for Satan to distort the Gospel is by using the Bible itself. He convinces well-meaning Christians they are being “biblical”—when in reality, they are cherry-picking verses out of context to support their own agendas and theological biases.

And tragically, this kind of selective misuse of Scripture leads people to believe they are preaching the Gospel, when in fact, they are driving others away from it.

If this is true—and given how Satan operates, it likely is—then some of the people most in need of salvation are already inside the church.

They need salvation from a distorted image of God—a frightening, wrathful, and condemning figure created by well-meaning Christians who truly believe they are honoring the Bible. They need saving from anxiety and depression, and a path into life.

Sometimes, the greatest deception isn’t outside the church. It’s inside—dressed in religious language, backed by isolated verses, and preached with the best of intentions, but far from the heart of Christ.

“But Eitan,” you might ask, “what about all those verses—being erased from the Book of Life, the Lake of Fire, outer darkness, weeping and gnashing of teeth, Lazarus and the Rich Man, the Sheep and the Goats, and so on?”

That’s a fair question. And it deserves a careful, honest answer—not soundbites, not fear-driven assumptions, and not readings torn from their historical and Jewish context.

If you genuinely want to understand what the so-called “hellfire verses” are actually saying—how they were heard by their original audiences, what metaphors they employ, and how later theology reshaped them—I invite you to continue this journey with me in my book HELL: A Jewish Perspective on a Christian Doctrine. There, we walk patiently through every difficult passage, refusing both denial and distortion, allowing Scripture to speak on its own terms.

I promise you this: it will challenge assumptions, dismantle fear-based readings, and open a far richer, more faithful vision of God’s justice and mercy. You won’t walk away unchanged.

c39B

Part VI: Your Life as an Audition

c39J

Introduction: So, Now What?

If the gospel is true—and if Jesus truly is the Lamb of God who takes away the sin of the world—then a staggering conclusion follows: Christ died for every human being, not merely for those who articulate the right theology, grasp the correct concepts, or arrive at proper doctrinal formulations. His love reached us long before we ever reached for Him. His mercy embraced humanity before humanity could even respond. That much is clear.

But this inevitably raises the question people fear to ask: if God has already acted decisively for us, then what is the meaning of our lives? What is the point of Christian existence? What is our role in this story? What is the purpose of judgment? Why this long, difficult journey on earth at all?

The answer is far deeper—and far more practical—than most expect.

Yes, Christ died for all. Yes, His love is universal. Yes, His mission is the reconciliation of all things. But that does not render this life meaningless. It does the opposite. It makes this life infinitely more meaningful. Because while redemption has already been accomplished, transformation has not yet been completed—and transformation is precisely where your life matters.

Scripture never teaches that God is testing our theological accuracy. Jesus never said, “Blessed are those who checked the correct doctrinal boxes.” What He did teach—again and again—is that every human being is living through a divine examination. Not an exam of intellectual mastery or doctrinal precision, but an examination of life itself (e.g., Matthew 7:24–27; 22:37–40; John 13:34–35; James 1:22–27; Romans 2:6–7; 2 Corinthians 5:10).

Each of us is given a span of years—long for some, painfully short for others—and within that time God watches what we do with what we have been given: our compassion, our generosity, our integrity, our willingness to forgive, our pursuit of justice, our love for neighbor and enemy alike. The test is not what we know. It is who we become.

This is why Jesus spoke so often about reward. Not as bribery. Not as fear management. But as the natural outflow of a life aligned with God. “The Son of Man will repay each person according to what they have done.” “Great is your reward in heaven.” “Store up treasures in heaven.” These are not coded threats about avoiding flames. They are promises of honor, joy, reward, and responsibility in God’s restored world.

If Christ died for all—and He did—then the central question of faith is no longer, “How do I avoid hell?” The question becomes, “How do I live in a way worthy of the life I’ve been given?” “How do I live so that when I stand before the King, my deeds, my choices, and my loves testify that I embraced His way?”

Some—perhaps all of us—will need to be salted with fire before entering the Kingdom, because nothing distorted can enter a realm built on love. Fire, here, is not rejection but refinement. Those who lived with mercy, generosity, and justice will shine like the brightness of the heavens—not because they earned salvation, but because they lived faithfully within the time entrusted to them.

Life, then, is not a waiting room before heaven or a trapdoor above hell. It is the arena where character is revealed. It is the proving ground where God sees not what we claimed to believe, but how we lived, how we loved, how we forgave, how we treated the least of these.

Judgment is not the threat of endless torture. It is the unveiling of truth. It is the moment when the life we lived becomes the life we receive. And because Christ has already reconciled the world, judgment is not about deciding who gets in. It is about determining what each person enters with—shame or glory, emptiness or fullness, ashes of wasted years or the treasure of a life well lived.

This is the invitation Jesus offers: not fear-driven compliance, but the dignity of a life examined by God and honored accordingly.

c3A5

Chapter 47
He Tests Your Heart, Not Your Knowledge

All will be resurrected—every soul, every life. And as Daniel 12:2 declares, Jesus confirms that a day of judgment awaits, when each person will be rewarded according to what they have done:

Do not marvel at this, for an hour is coming when all who are in the tombs will hear his voice and come out, those who have done good to the resurrection of life, and those who have done evil to the resurrection of judgment.

(John 5:28–29)

Notice that both groups—the righteous and the unrighteous—are resurrected. The difference lies not in whether one group is made alive again, but in the nature of that resurrection: life or judgment.

Resurrection of Life

The Bible isn’t a biology textbook, so here “life” isn’t about biology, especially since both have been resurrected.

Remember, life in the biblical sense never meant merely continuing to breathe. It meant blessing and joy, wholeness and peace, purpose, flourishing, and harmony with God. To live was to participate in the fullness of divine life—not merely to exist. Life was never defined by quantity or duration, but by quality: a life filled with meaning, joy, blessing, and flourishing in God’s presence.

Thus, “resurrection of life” signifies reward, honor, and greatness—what Jesus elsewhere calls being great in the kingdom (Matthew 5:19). The New Testament describes this kind of "life" in multiple ways: storing treasure (Matthew 6:20), winning the race (1 Corinthians 9:24), receiving rewards (1 Corinthians 3:14), inheriting the kingdom (Matthew 25:34), entering the kingdom (Matthew 7:21), receiving the crown of life (James 1:12), or walking the narrow path that leads to life (Matthew 7:14). These and others are biblical idioms and Jewish expressions that speak of spiritual blessings and achievements—depicting not those who merely intellectually accepted Christ but also lived out His way with faithfulness and love.144

Resurrection of Judgment

By contrast, the “resurrection of judgment” implies shame, regret, chastisement, and sanction. There’s a big difference between feeling the sting of shame and condemnation when your sins come to light and suffering Eternal Conscious Torment in hellfire.

Imagine a Christian family with two young siblings returning home from summer camp. One behaved well, and everyone has nothing but praise for them; the other behaved terribly. When they walk through the door, one is met with warmth and celebration, while the other faces correction and discipline. Yet no one imagines that the parents would throw the misbehaving child into a fiery pit in the backyard.

When the child of a loving parent is punished for wrongdoing and feels “damned,” it does not mean they are cast away or destroyed. It means they are being corrected within a relationship of love. If that’s true about a loving parent, then why would we believe that a God who IS love (1 John 4:8, 16) would behave in a way that only an abusive, psychopathic parent would?

The resurrection of judgment represents those who failed to live according to God’s ways—not a sentence to unending torture, but a form of loss. Jesus described it using culturally understood symbols like “weeping and gnashing of teeth” (Luke 13:28), a known Jewish/biblical idiom expressing deep remorse or jealousy (e.g., Psalm 35:16, 37:12, 112:10), much like the modern feeling of FOMO (Fear of Missing Out). Other terms like “outer darkness” (Matthew 22:13) or “will not inherit the kingdom” (1 Corinthians 6:9–10) don’t refer to annihilation or hellfire, but to missing out on the great honor, reward, and joy of being close to God in His kingdom.

In the ancient world, living inside a kingdom's walls was a privilege reserved for the wealthy, the inheritors, and those honored by the king. Those not granted entry would remain outside after dark, missing out on the safety, light, and feasting within. “Outer darkness” was a Jewish metaphor for missing out on blessings (e.g., Deuteronomy 28:29) or being left outside the celebration—not executed or tortured, but distanced.

Likewise, Paul writes about those who will be spared, “but only as through fire” (1 Corinthians 3:15)—they suffer loss, but are not condemned forever. They are still alive, yet without honor, reward, or inheritance.

The final judgment, then, is not simply a binary verdict of paradise or inferno, but a revelation of the quality of each life—its fruit, its faithfulness, its alignment with God’s love. Resurrection is God’s free gift to all, but reward is to be earned and based on how we lived.

Gaining Rewards in Heaven

Who, then, is the greatest in the kingdom of heaven?

(Matthew 18:1)

Knowledge of the Bible is crucial, yet countless Christians can quote verses, recite creeds, and defend doctrine with the speed of a trained lawyer—yet their lives remain anxious, angry, judgmental, fearful, or spiritually dry. But from time to time, you get to meet Christians with very little theological knowledge whose lives radiate peace, kindness, mercy, courage, and generosity. Here is a simple truth: God is far more interested in the kind of person you are becoming than in the amount of religious information you can memorize and recite. Biblical faith was never meant to be a trivia contest. Faith is a way of living, a way of becoming whole and one with God. That’s exactly what God is looking for in His followers. The point is transformation, not information.

In Second Temple Judaism, the role of a rabbi was not simply to accumulate knowledge or deliver theoretical teachings. His role was to embody divine truth—to live it out so fully that his own life became the lesson. That’s why ancient rabbis didn’t hide away in ivory towers and only appear once a week behind a pulpit for a 45-minute sermon. They lived with their disciples. They walked, ate, traveled, prayed, debated, and experienced the world alongside them. The goal was formation, not information—shaping not just minds, but entire lives. Of course, some rabbis did better (Acts 5:34–40), while others did worse (Matthew 23:13–37).

Yeshua—the Jewish rabbi from Nazareth—fulfilled this calling more perfectly than anyone in history. He didn’t simply preach truth; He incarnated it. His disciples didn’t attend His weekly lectures; they followed Him everywhere for years. They watched how He spoke to the broken, how He challenged the proud, how He healed the wounded, and how He lived every moment before God.

Jesus didn’t merely teach the way of God. He was the way of God—embodied, lived, breathed, and shared in real time with those who walked beside Him.

And it was the mission of His disciples—and ours today—to learn and reflect that kind of faith. This is what God is testing us for: a faith that isn’t just theoretical or abstract, but lived out in every part of life. When we follow Christ’s way—with generosity, peace, kindness, patience, humility, forgiveness, mercy, courage, and compassion—we enter into the kind of life God loves to bless.

A life shaped by such faith has no need to fear judgment or condemnation, because it already participates in the very life of the Kingdom. This is the faith Jesus modeled, the faith the apostles practiced, and the faith we are called to embody; not a checklist of doctrines, but a way of being in the world that reflects the heart of God.

Only then does Paul’s famous line about “no condemnation” begin to make sense.

No Condemnation to Them Which Are in Christ Jesus

There is therefore now no condemnation to them which are in Christ Jesus

(Romans 8:1a)

Contrary to how this verse is often quoted, Paul isn’t talking about someone who simply agrees intellectually with a doctrinal statement of belief. He speaks of those who live in alignment with Christ—whose lives reflect His Spirit, His values, and His way of love.

"In Christ" isn’t about reciting a prayer from a pamphlet, but about embracing an active way of living.

How do I know? Because of the second part of the verse that’s so often left out:

There is therefore now no condemnation to them which are in Christ Jesus, who walk not after the flesh, but after the Spirit.

(Romans 8:1)

In Scripture, “flesh” refers to selfishness. Those who live “according to the flesh” focus inward on themselves, while those who live “according to the Spirit” show love outward toward others (Galatians 5:14, 22–23). Living in a Christlike way is what truly brings blessing and keeps us from condemnation. Condemnation rests on selfishness, not on those who walk in love. Selfish people often steal, lie, exploit, and abuse, which is why selfishness itself is met with condemnation—sometimes in very real ways, like penalties, fines, or imprisonment.

However, Condemnation does not mean "In the future after you die, you'll burn in hellfire forever!" but rather to live under the weight of guilt, shame, judgment, sanction, alienation, fear, and spiritual drought—the condition of estrangement from God’s blessings and peace.

I’ve met both Christians and non-Christians who embody divine values, living lives that mirror the compassion and truth of Jesus. Yet it is expected of Christians—those who profess to follow Him—to hold themselves to a higher standard of love, the kind of love Jesus Himself demonstrated (James 3:1, Luke 12:48). Sadly, that is not always the case. And I’m not here to cast stones—lest I be found guilty as well.

True Faith Loves

There are few passages in the New Testament as blunt, as piercing, and as disruptive to comfortable religion as the second chapter of James. This is not theology in a seminar. This is theology in the street. In the hospital. In the prison. In the eyes of the one begging at your feet.

James begins with a question that should shake every modern believer:

What good is it, my brothers and sisters, if someone claims to have faith but has no deeds?

(James 2:14)

On judgment day, God will not ask for your statement of faith—what you believed—He will ask what your belief produced. He tests your heart, not your knowledge.

Faith, in this passage, is not a mental checklist. It is a living force that manifests itself in love. Anything less is counterfeit. James offers a concrete example:

Suppose a brother or a sister is without clothes and daily food. If one of you says to them, "Go in peace; keep warm and well fed," but does nothing about their physical needs, what good is it?

(James 2:15-16)

Here, faith and salvation come together in real life. Both are real and practical. Salvation means being rescued from a terrible situation, and faith is the action behind it, not just mentally agreeing in your head. James paints a striking picture: someone offering kind words, prayers, blessings, even spiritual-sounding comfort—yet doing nothing to actually help the person in need. That’s not just weak faith. It’s empty. Pointless. James calls it what it is:

Faith by itself, if it is not accompanied by action, is dead.

(James 2:17)

And then comes the challenge, sharp as a sword:

Show me your faith without deeds, and I will show you my faith by what I do.

(James 2:18)

Here lies the heart of the matter. Faith, if real, must be visible. It must incarnate itself. It must walk and weep and wash feet. You can’t preach Christ crucified while ignoring the suffering of the poor right in front of you.

Evangelism, in this sense, is not about handing out tracts or shouting through a megaphone. Evangelism is feeding the hungry, visiting the imprisoned, forgiving your enemy, and loving your neighbor.

James goes on to draw from the stories of Abraham and Rahab—two people whose righteousness was not a private intellectual thought but a risk-taking, sacrificial response to God's call. Abraham was willing to offer even his son. Rahab—a prostitute—protected spies at the risk of her own life. Their faith was not just declared—it was demonstrated.

Not What You Know, But What You Do

As the body without the spirit is dead, so faith without deeds is dead.

(James 2:26)

This isn’t about works-based salvation. It’s the contrast between lifeless doctrine—mere intellectual belief—and the kind of biblical faith that breathes, moves, and nourishes.

To say you follow Jesus, and yet turn away from the person in need, the hungry, the hurting, and the forgotten, is not just a contradiction. According to James—it is a corpse.

Genuine faith—the kind Jesus spoke of—naturally produces love. James makes this unmistakably clear: a “faith” that utters pious words to the poor while refusing to lift a finger to help is no faith at all—it’s just Gnosticism. The proof of living faith is not theological sharpness, not isolation from the world, not winning arguments on social media—but concrete acts of love!

The only thing that counts is faith expressing itself through love.

(Galatians 5:6)

Yes, acts of love are what God is after. God has never been impressed by ceremonial precision or doctrinal correctness. Through Isaiah, He bluntly declared that He hates empty rituals divorced from justice, mercy, and compassion (Isaiah 1:13–17; 58:5–7).

The New Testament makes it clear that faith is shown through love (Galatians 5:6). When love fades, any intellectual defense of any “salvation” doctrine has already missed the point.

The Illusion of Control

Jesus of Nazareth was not merely a moral teacher or mystic; He was a first-century Jewish rabbi who perfectly embodied the unity between heaven and earth—between God’s transcendent domain and our human condition. As such, He exposed the fundamental obstacle that keeps us from experiencing that divine unity: the ego. The self-centered drive to secure our future by accumulating wealth, status, and control is what distances us from inheriting the Kingdom.

That’s why Jesus said:

Do not store up for yourselves treasures on earth, where moth and rust consume and where thieves break in and steal. But store up for yourselves treasures in heaven, where neither moth nor rust consumes and where thieves do not break in and steal.

(Matthew 6:19–20)

Everyone accumulates something. We gather possessions, build savings, chase influence—not because we’re evil and greedy, but because these things offer a seductive illusion—a sense of security that we are safe, strong, and in control. Yet Jesus shatters this illusion. Earthly treasure, He warns, is fragile. It decays, disappears, and ultimately disappoints. As you came naked from your mother’s womb, so you will depart, taking nothing with you (Job 1:21).

Treasure in heaven is not stored by amassing wealth on earth, nor by memorizing Bible verses. It is stored by self-giving—by how much of yourself you pour out for the sake of others. He tests your heart, not your knowledge.

Conclusion

Jesus invites us into an economy radically unlike the one we know—an eternal economy where love is the currency, mercy is the measure, and faithfulness is the true wealth. “Store up treasures in heaven,” He says, not because heaven is far away, but because this way of living aligns us with what endures. Everything else fades. Possessions rust. Status dissolves. Control slips through our fingers. But love—lived, practiced, given away—remains.

The Kingdom of God is not secured by fear, nor entered by intellectual mastery. It is revealed in lives poured out for others. Treasure is gathered not by what we accumulate, but by what we surrender. Not by what we know, but by how deeply we love. This is why Jesus ties judgment to fruit, reward to faithfulness, and life to self-giving. Resurrection awaits all, but the quality of that resurrection—the joy, the honor, the nearness—will reflect the kind of life we chose to live.

What we have loved, whom we have served, how we have walked—these are the only things that follow us.

True faith, according to James, is expressed in acts of love. We are “saved” because God has saved humanity through Christ. But it is through faith—not intellectual assent, but the lived, embodied faith James describes—that we store up treasure and inherit the Kingdom of God.

This is the paradox of the Kingdom. To lose is to gain. To give is to receive. To surrender is to be secure. And when we live this way—walking in love, justice, humility, and compassion—we discover that the life to come has already begun, and the treasures of heaven are being stored even now.

c3D6

Chapter 48
The Path to Kingdom Greatness

Israel’s prophets longed and prophesied for the day when God would bring about the world’s final restoration. Isaiah's hope was not for escape, but for a healed world (Isaiah 11:6–9, 65:17–25, 66:22–23)—the reality known as "Tikkun Olam." A time when swords would be beaten into plowshares and “nation shall not take up sword against nation, nor will they train for war anymore” (Isaiah 2:4).

Here’s something easy to miss: if nations will no longer fight, it means they still exist in the age to come. God’s future isn’t about wiping out diversity but about reconciling all peoples—a world made new, not erased. The existence of nations suggests there will still be structure and roles.

Likewise, when Jesus and Paul spoke of the world to come, they too described a reality with roles, responsibilities, and authority (see, for example: Luke 19:17–19; 1 Corinthians 6:2–3; Revelation 21:24–26). The New Creation—the New Jerusalem, the age that follows—will be much like this one but finally healed and set right. Nations, cultures, function, purpose, and community will remain.

But the Kingdom of God is truly “upside-down”—those who are last today might be first tomorrow, because greatness is measured by faithfulness, humility, and love—attributes that can be demonstrated no matter what condition your life is in right now. That’s why this present life is not a meaningless prelude, but a holy preparation—a kind of audition for the age to come. Every moment, every choice, and every act of love or sacrifice shapes your path toward your heavenly calling. But take heart: to qualify for this “job,” you don’t need a suit or seminary degree. God isn’t looking for resumes or accolades—He’s searching for hearts that are humble, merciful, and faithful.

This life is not only an audition but also your training ground. Let every day prepare you—not with ambition or anxiety, but with hope, integrity, and love. For in God’s kingdom, the qualities that matter most are the ones the world often cares about least.

It’s Not About Your Religious Affiliation

Your faith and actions do not determine whether you will be resurrected—that is something God has already secured. But they do shape the quality of your life, both now and, perhaps even more importantly, in the age to come. This life, then, is not an audition for whether you get to live again, but for how deeply you will experience that life.

Make no mistake: you are not judged by the denomination you belong to. In fact, Jesus did not come to establish a new religion. He showed little interest in religious systems for their own sake; to Him, they were secondary—seasoning, not the meal itself. What He came to bring was something far greater: the Way, the Truth, and the Life—familiar Jewish and biblical expressions that described a way of living aligned with the heart of God (cf. Psalm 1:6; Jeremiah 6:16).

Religion so often fixates on appearances—rules, rituals, regulations, penalties, and outward conformity. Jesus, by contrast, came to redeem what lies beneath the surface. He came to heal hearts, renew minds, and restore the broken places within us that no external system of control or discipline could ever reach.

In other words: you do not glorify God by merely attending a weekly gathering, singing songs, and listening to a lecture. That, by itself, does not move His heart. Christ showed us what true worship looks like: forgiving those who wrong us, loving our enemies, defending the vulnerable, and caring for the poor, the orphan, and the widow. That is holiness. That is worship. That is the Kingdom of God made visible.

And here is the liberating truth: you do not need a religion, a denomination, or an institution to live this way. You can live this out even if you’re on a remote island or in prison with nothing but fellow inmates around you. You need only to walk the path Jesus revealed—loving kindness, justice, and faithfulness—because this is what truly honors God.

If you are part of a faith community—and you should be, as Hebrews 10:25 encourages—make sure it is a healthy one: a place where you can both give and receive, grow and heal, serve and be cared for, and where you would feel at peace inviting others to encounter the beauty of God’s Kingdom.

And if you are not yet part of such a community, choose wisely. Not every smiling, energetic, “spirit-filled” environment is healthy. Some of the most toxic, manipulative, abusive, and spiritually damaging communities hide behind friendly faces, polished worship, dramatic lighting, pretty websites, striking newsletters, dramatic video clips, and impressive branding. And I say this from personal experience.145

A true community of faith cultivates freedom, not control; healing, not fear; love, not judgment.

Don't Waste Your Life

Don’t spend your life trapped in hollow religion that leads only to anxiety, isolation, fear, depression, and emptiness.

Since there are over 49,000 Christian church groups in the world, then there are, in practice, 49,000 variations of the Gospel—each with its own emphasis, culture, theology, and traditions. Some are compassionate; others are controlling. Some reflect the humility of Christ; others, the pride of man. So how can you tell whether the version of the gospel being preached to you is anywhere close to the real thing?

Well, I hope my book offered you at least a few clues. But there is an even simpler test: test the fruit.

Does it produce love, joy, peace, patience, kindness, goodness, faithfulness, gentleness, and self-control (Galatians 5:22–23)?

Does it set the oppressed free, lift up the brokenhearted, heal the soul, and reconcile enemies?

Does it draw you closer to a loving Father?

Or does it breed fear, shame, and religious anxiety?

Does it bind consciences, crush the wounded, harden hearts, and divide people from one another?

Does it push you away from God—into dread, self-loathing, and a god you cannot fully trust or love?

The true gospel leads to life. Life means blessings and transformation—not just of intellect, but of heart, character, and relationships. If what you’re hearing doesn’t sound like good news to the weary, the wounded, and the wandering, then it’s not the Gospel He preached.

It’s not rare to meet someone who speaks eloquently about Jesus, defends every line of doctrine, and claims unwavering piety—yet whose life shows no sign of Christ’s Spirit. They may win debates, quote Scripture, and defend orthodoxy with zeal. But if their hearts are ruled by pride, their tone by condescension, their character by unkindness and lack of compassion, and their actions by fear, control, anxiety, or cruelty—then let us not be deceived: they may speak about Christ, they may even wear a cross on their neck, but they are not walking with Him.

For the true mark of the Holy Spirit is not merely intellectual accuracy—it is the transformation of the heart. Yes, a wrong understanding can certainly shape distorted ideas about God’s will and nature. But wherever the Spirit is genuinely at work, the character of Jesus begins to take root and grow within a person.

Even if you’re a new believer who hasn’t even read the Bible yet, you can still recognize whether the “gospel” you hear is truly from God and whether a faith community you’re considering genuinely reflects Christ. The Spirit reveals this through character:

Humility replaces arrogance.

Gentleness disarms harshness.

Peace quiets fear.

Love casts out judgment.

Compassion crosses boundaries.

Patience softens impatience.

Kindness overtakes cruelty.

These aren’t just spiritual accessories or decorative touches; they’re the proof of true biblical faith. Where these fruits show up, the Spirit is there. And when they’re missing—no matter what beliefs are spoken or how “theologically correct” someone appears—the Spirit isn’t at work.

Let’s be honest: a gospel that leads to hard hearts, judgmental spirits, and a condescending attitude isn’t the gospel of Jesus. A "truth" that makes someone more self-righteous, more divisive, more fearful and controlling—is not the good news that sets us free.

Conclusion

The greatest miracle of the Spirit is not speaking in tongues or memorizing complete chapters—it is loving your enemy. Forgiving your betrayer. Embracing the outcast. Because following in the footsteps of our Rabbi-Messiah means more than admiring Him from a distance or with your tongue; it means becoming like Him.

This is the Spirit's purpose: to transform people and form communities where heaven's love touches earth’s wounds, where people don’t just believe in God intellectually but embody Him. A people who make the invisible God visible.

Let’s not measure our faith by how loudly we declare it, but by how profoundly it transforms us. In the end, God won’t ask how many verses we could recite or tracts we passed out, but who we became and whose lives we touched. Those whose hearts and lives reflect the Spirit of the Son will be richly rewarded.

c3ER

Chapter 49
God's Kingdom is Upside-Down

The Jewish disciples of Jesus argued often—but never about who would “get saved” and who would burn forever. That question simply was not on their radar. Their debates revolved around something entirely different: Who is the greatest in the kingdom?

For them, reality was not a binary system of insiders and outsiders, heaven and hellfire. It was a spectrum—a question of rank, honor, and responsibility within God’s reign. These young Jewish men had no formal “Doctrine of Soteriology,” nor did they imagine salvation as rescue from an angry God or from a cosmic furnace designed to melt their skin for eternity. Such ideas appear nowhere in their Hebrew Torah and played no role in their spiritual imagination.

Instead, they were preoccupied with how to please God, how to live faithfully, and how to rise in status within His coming kingdom. Their concern was not whether they belonged, but how they would belong—what kind of people they would become, and what place they would hold when God set the world right:

At that time the disciples came to Jesus, saying, "Who is the greatest in the kingdom of heaven?" And calling to him a child, he put him in the midst of them and said, "Truly, I say to you, unless you turn and become like children, you will never enter the kingdom of heaven. Whoever humbles himself like this child is the greatest in the kingdom of heaven."

(Matthew 18:1-4)

Jesus often taught using antithesis—placing contrasting ideas side by side to sharpen their meaning. In this framework, “entering the kingdom” corresponds to being “great in the kingdom,” while not entering corresponds to being least in the kingdom. The contrast is not between heaven and hellfire, but between honor and loss, participation and exclusion, fullness and forfeiture.

In other words, the opposite of greatness in the kingdom is not Eternal Conscious Torment—it is missing out. It is being outside the celebration, away from the banquet table, beyond the walls when night falls. It is the grief of exclusion, not the agony of flames. Jesus’ imagery consistently points to loss of reward, status, and joy—not unending punishment. The faithful receive crowns, medals, and honors (1 Corinthians 9:24–25; 2 Timothy 4:7–8; James 1:12; 1 Peter 5:4; Revelation 2:10; Revelation 3:11); the unfaithful forfeit them.

To grasp this, Jesus insists we must become like children. Children do not live in terror that their parents will burn or kill them. But they do long to please them. They want to be praised, affirmed, and embraced. They strive—not out of fear—but out of trust, affection, and desire for approval.

By calling His disciples to become like children, Jesus exposes the true nature of kingdom motivation. Greatness is not achieved through power, status, theological sophistication, or self-sufficiency. It flows from humility, dependence, trust, and an open, teachable heart. Adults cling to control and hierarchy; children know they need guidance. Adults posture; children receive.

Jesus’ message is clear: the kingdom is not governed by terror but by love, not by threat but by invitation. True greatness is not earned through dominance or fear-driven obedience, but through humble faith—and the joyful desire to live in a way that delights the Father.

To Be Called Great in the Kingdom of Heaven

Therefore whoever relaxes one of the least of these commandments and teaches others to do the same will be called least in the kingdom of heaven, but whoever does them and teaches them will be called great in the kingdom of heaven.

(Matthew 5:19)

Want to be great in the kingdom? Then follow Jesus’ commandments. But what were they? Was it waking up at 5 a.m. every morning to pray and read a devotional? Never missing church on Sunday? Shutting yourself away from the “evil” world? No.

Instead of complicated theological language, Christ spoke in a simple, straightforward way—like explaining something to a child—laying out His commandments over three chapters:

— Always reconcile with people. (Matthew 5:24).

— Settle matters quickly with your adversary. (Matthew 5:25).

— Speak the truth. (Matthew 5:37).

— Don't take revenge. (Matthew 5:39).

— Be generous. (Matthew 5:42).

— Love your enemy. (Matthew 5:44).

— Stay humble. (Matthew 6:1).

— Give to the needy. (Matthew 6:4).

— Pray and seek justice. (Matthew 6:9-10).

— Abstain from evil. (Matthew 6:23).

— Do not worry. (Matthew 6:25).

— Do not be judgmental. (Matthew 7:1).

— Do not settle for shallow, empty religion. (Matthew 7:7-8).

And so on...

That's how one becomes great in His kingdom, and this is why Jesus summarized the Law and prophets as loving God with all your heart—and explained that you love Him by loving your neighbor. Then, Jesus redefined “neighbor” to include everyone, even your enemy (e.g., The Parable of the Good Samaritan—Luke 10:25-37).

Jesus wasn’t a woke activist—He was a strong Jewish rabbi. For Him, love was never a sentimental “yippie” feeling or a fleeting emotion. Love was concrete. It was expressed through real acts of sacrifice, care, kindness, truth, justice, and compassion toward all people—even those who did not deserve it.

Love is what impresses God and moves His heart; thus, the ability to love is what makes someone truly great in the kingdom (Matthew 22:37–39, John 13:34–35, 1 Corinthians 13:13, Galatians 5:14, and 1 John 4:7–8).

It is Grace that will resurrect you, but how you love determines the reward and joy you’ll carry into the next life.

Not Just Blessings and Rewards—But Roles and Responsibilities

Kingdom greatness is not merely about receiving rewards and blessings in the age to come. God is preparing—and selecting—people for leadership. Life, in a very real sense, is an audition. God is choosing those who will govern, serve, and steward His future kingdom—not on the basis of religious titles, wealth, influence, or earthly power, but on the basis of Christlike character.

Jesus promised His faithful disciples that they would “sit on thrones” and even “judge angels.” Authority, then, is part of the coming kingdom. But the decisive question is this: what kind of people will God trust with authority?

The qualities that matter to God are the very qualities Jesus embodied—humility, courage, compassion, integrity, generosity, steadfast love, justice, and mercy. These are the traits God looks for in those He entrusts with responsibility, both now and in the world to come.

Ask yourself honestly: if you were God, who would you trust to wield authority in your kingdom? Who would you appoint as leaders, judges, counselors, and caretakers of creation? Not the proud or the oppressive. Not the ruthless or the self-promoting. But the teachable, the gentle, the compassionate, the just, and the merciful—those who reflect the heart of Jesus.

The disciples once argued among themselves about who was the greatest. Jesus did not rank them or settle the debate with hierarchy. Instead, He wrapped a towel around His waist and washed their feet. In that single act, He redefined greatness forever. In God’s economy, greatness is not dominance but servanthood. It is not intellect, status, or force—but humility expressed through love.

Christ’s kingdom is not a continuation of the beastly empires of this world. It is their complete reversal. That is why Jesus declared, “The last will be first, and the first will be last.” The CEOs, presidents, generals, and celebrities of this age may find themselves far from the front of the line, while unseen caregivers, quiet peacemakers, and faithful servants are crowned with honor and entrusted with leadership.

The world promotes the loudest, the strongest, and the richest. God looks for those who love deeply, serve quietly, and lead with grace.

That is why the Kingdom of God has always been—and will always be—the upside-down kingdom.

Faith Is a Calling—Not to Privilege

The more light you receive, the more responsibility you bear. Scripture is explicit: we are judged according to the light given to us (Luke 12:47–48; James 4:17). A child raised in North Korea is not held to the same standard as a pastor raised in a Christian home in America, surrounded by Scripture, teaching, and freedom. A Christian—one who claims allegiance to the crucified Lord—cannot plead ignorance. The better you know Christ, the more you are called to reflect Him.

And what did He reveal? Radical compassion. Relentless forgiveness. Abundant mercy. Courageous truth-telling. A life poured out for the poor, the outcast, and the oppressed.

Greater knowledge brings greater accountability. If you know what Jesus taught—if you know you are called to love, including your enemies; to care for the poor, the wounded, and the forgotten—but choose not to live it, Scripture does not threaten you with Eternal Conscious Torment. It warns you of real loss: forfeited reward, diminished glory, squandered vocation in the age to come.

To walk with Jesus is not an elevation to privilege; it is an invitation to responsibility. In the Kingdom of God, greatness is not measured by doctrinal mastery but by love embodied. Not by how refined your theology sounds, but by how worn your hands are from serving others. Revelation always increases accountability. This is a consistent biblical pattern: “From everyone who has been given much, much will be required.” (Luke 12:48).

With revelation comes responsibility. If you claim His name while neglecting the poor, marginalizing the broken, or weaponizing religion to spread fear and division, you have missed the heart of the Gospel. Worse still, you may lose what you were meant to gain. Knowledge without love, as Paul warned, is nothing but noise (1 Corinthians 13:1). In God’s Kingdom, theology without mercy is spiritually bankrupt.

True Greatness Redefined

In Matthew 20:20–28, when the mother of James and John requests thrones of glory for her sons, Jesus gently corrects her. The path to glory, He says, is paved with sacrifice and service. “Whoever would be great among you must be your servant.”

In Luke 22:24–30, when His disciples argue over status, Jesus reverses the logic of the world: “Let the greatest among you become as the least, and the leader as one who serves.” Leadership in the Kingdom is not about control but self-giving love.

In Matthew 5:12, Jesus comforts those persecuted for righteousness, not with escape, but with reward: “Rejoice and be glad, for your reward is great in heaven.” Kingdom reward is not for the comfortable, but for the courageous. Don't allow fear to become your glass ceiling.

As Paul neared death, he wrote with confidence: “I have fought the good fight, I have finished the race... there is laid up for me the crown of righteousness” (2 Timothy 4:7–8). Not because he was perfect, but because he endured in love.

In Revelation 2:26, Jesus promises that those who remain faithful will share in His authority—“to the one who conquers, I will give authority over the nations.” And in Revelation 22:12, His final promise is also a warning: “Behold, I am coming soon, bringing my recompense with me, to repay each one for what he has done.”

The New Testament’s idea of reward and greatness isn’t about earning a place in heaven, but about reflecting its values here and now. Sacrificial love is the currency of the Kingdom. It’s how we store treasure in heaven—not by stockpiling religious rituals or moral superiority, but by embodying the same self-giving love that Christ demonstrated.

Conclusion

So, who will be great in the Kingdom?

Not the ones who mastered theology, won debates, or never missed church service—but those who fed the hungry, clothed the naked, visited the imprisoned, forgave the undeserving, and embraced the broken. Those who truly lived like the King.

In the end, the places of greatest honor in the Kingdom do not belong to those who merely believed the right things, but to those who became most like Him.

c3GW

Chapter 50
Then What's the Point in Evangelizing?

A common objection is raised: “If everyone will eventually be redeemed, then why evangelize at all?”

During an Iranian missile attack on Israel in June 2025, sirens tore through the air and panic erupted. My wife and I, along with the rest of the crowd, hurried frantically to find safety. With us, those caught outside rushed into the nearest bomb shelter across from us, desperate to survive. In the midst of the chaos, one family—ironically a Christian family—became visibly agitated. They insisted that only residents of nearby buildings should be allowed inside, not random people from the street. They demanded that the doors be shut and locked against “outsiders.”

Later, I sat with one of them. He was still shaken from the situation and also angry that strangers had sought refuge in what he perceived as their shelter. I told him I understood practical limits; sometimes shelters truly do become overcrowded and unsafe. But this one wasn’t full. There was still room. What I sensed wasn’t logistics—it was an emotional barricade.

So I asked him gently, “Don’t you care about those people? If the door had been closed and they were killed by the missile, how would you feel?”

He answered flatly, almost hollowly, “I don’t feel anything anymore.”

I paused, then asked, “Why? Something must have happened to you.”

His eyes immediately filled with tears. “I did something terrible when I was young and stupid,” he said. “Since then, I don’t feel anything. I stopped caring.”

Now return to the question: Why preach the gospel if every knee will eventually bow?

Like a good Jew, I’ll answer the question with another: Does the gospel still matter to someone like my neighbor across the street?

Does it matter to a soul that feels shattered, numb, almost dead inside? Can the Good News—that he is already forgiven in Christ, without conditions—begin to thaw a heart frozen by shame and self-loathing? Can the gospel breathe life into someone who feels like a walking corpse? Or is it merely a message about where you go after you die?

And what about the Jewish mothers whose children were kidnapped and murdered on October 7, 2023? Or the Palestinian mothers who buried their babies? Does the gospel matter to them? Can the proclamation that death will not have the final word—that all will be raised and live again—restore even a fragment of hope to hearts crushed by grief? Does the promise of reunion matter? Does it heal? Does it sustain?

This is why we evangelize: so "that they may have life, and that they may have it more abundantly." (John 10:10).

Not to scare people into heaven, but to awaken the dead now. Not to threaten with Eternal Conscious Torment, but to announce life. The gospel is not a message reserved for the afterlife; it is a medicine for the present. It tells the numb they are not beyond healing, the guilty they are not beyond forgiveness, and the grieving that love has not been erased by death.

If the gospel is true, then it is not merely about where we end up—it is about who we are becoming. And that makes all the difference in the world.

Is God truly so narrow and selective that the gospel belongs only to a small, enlightened elite—as so many assume—or is the Good News actually good news for all, especially for the grieving, the traumatized, the lost, the searching, and the broken?

If the gospel is nothing more than a ticket out of hell in the afterlife, then it is useless to those who are already living in hell now. And yet, those are precisely the people Christ came for. Listen to Isaiah’s vision of the Messiah:

The Spirit of the Sovereign LORD is upon me, for the LORD has anointed me to bring good news to the poor; He has sent me to bind up the brokenhearted, to proclaim freedom to the captives and release from darkness to the prisoners, to announce the year of the LORD’s favor and the day of His justice, to comfort all who mourn and to provide for those who grieve in Zion—bestowing on them a crown of beauty instead of ashes, the oil of joy instead of mourning, and a garment of praise instead of a spirit of despair; they will be called oaks of righteousness, a planting of the LORD, for the display of His splendor.

(Isaiah 61:1-3)

When the Very Question Exposes the Misunderstanding

But the very fact that we wrestle with this objection at all exposes how far the Western gospel has drifted from its first-century roots. The questions we instinctively ask betray a deep disconnect between the life-giving, present-tense gospel proclaimed by Jesus and His apostles and the diminished version many have inherited—a subtly gnostic gospel fixated on escapism and the afterlife rather than transformation here and now.

The point of sharing the gospel was never to “save” people from falling into a cosmic fire pit after death. The gospel exists to rescue people from the hell in which they are already living. It is about offering real hope to the hurting, direction to the lost, and healing to the broken. It awakens compassion where numbness has taken over and breathes life where the soul has gone cold.

The gospel is not merely a promise deferred to the future; it is an invitation into wholeness now—to stand upright, to breathe again, to live as we were created to live. That is why we proclaim it, even if God’s mercy ultimately reaches everyone: because people need to be saved from the hell that grips them today.

So Why Should You Evangelize?

You evangelize because you want people to truly live and live abundantly.

You evangelize because healed hearts heal others.

You evangelize because forgiven people forgive.

You evangelize because the Prince of Peace has come into the world—and through Him peace can be made even with enemies.

You evangelize because to know Jesus is to overflow with the life He gives—a life that cannot help but spill outward.

You evangelize because you love, and love cannot sit still.

Evangelism is not recruitment into a cosmic survival bunker. It’s an invitation into the healing flood of God's restoration.

We evangelize because we have tasted something beautiful—and beauty demands to be shared.

We evangelize because we have seen the face of love—and love demands to be reflected.

We evangelize because the Kingdom of God is not just something we wait for—it is something we build, one act of kindness, one word of truth, one spark of mercy at a time.

Evangelizing for a Better Society

The beauty of the gospel is that it was never meant to stop at the level of private belief or individual spirituality. From the beginning, its horizon has been public, communal, and even cosmic. Jesus spoke of a kingdom—not merely a personal rescue plan—and Acts tells us that this message was meant to spread outward, reshaping lives, communities, and nations (e.g., Matthew 28:19–20, Mark 16:15, Acts 1:8). Wherever the gospel has taken root in its fullness, it has carried with it a vision of human dignity, moral responsibility, justice tempered by mercy, and the worth of every person made in the image of God.

Now consider the world beyond the West. Have you ever been to Syria, Afghanistan, North Korea, Sudan, Iran, Gaza, or Yemen? These are places scarred by tyranny, endless cycles of violence, suppression of conscience, and the crushing of the weak. This is not a matter of cultural superiority, but of moral contrast. Societies shaped by Judeo-Christian values—however imperfectly—have tended to cultivate ideas that many now take for granted: the sanctity of human life, limits on state power, care for the poor, the dignity of women and children, and the idea that rulers themselves are accountable to a higher moral law.

The gospel challenges injustice not only in hearts but in systems. It confronts cruelty, tribalism, and oppression wherever they appear. It insists that power exists to serve, not to dominate; that truth matters; that love of neighbor is not optional; and that the strong are responsible for the weak. These ideas did not arise naturally in human history—they were seeded, slowly and often painfully, by a worldview rooted in the Hebrew Scriptures and embodied in the life of Jesus.

To say that the gospel is only about personal salvation is to shrink it far below its biblical scope. It is not merely about where an individual goes after death; it is about what happens to the world while we are alive. It is a message meant to humanize societies, restrain evil, inspire compassion, and cultivate justice. When received rightly, it does not erase cultures, but heals them—calling every nation to become more humane, more just, and more aligned with the goodness for which humanity was created.

The gospel, at its best, is not an escape from the world. It is God’s invitation to remake it.

But Wouldn't People "Backslide" Without the Threat of Endless Hellfire?

Some would argue that preaching hellfire is necessary to demonstrate God's justice. But in the apostolic era, God's justice was demonstrated in Christ’s victory over sin, death, and evil—not in the perpetual torment and suffering of souls. God's justice was about setting things right, healing creation, and "reconciling the world to Himself" (2 Corinthians 5:19).

Discipline, loss of rewards, and correction are still unpleasant, even if the punishment is only temporary. But the idea that Christians would "backslide" into terrible behavior without the looming threat of burning forever not only paints a grim picture of God but is also both logically and morally flawed.

If the only thing preventing someone from committing evil is the fear of Eternal Conscious Torment, then we’re not talking about righteousness—we’re talking about restraint. That’s not transformation; it’s just wickedness kept on a leash. Is that what Christianity is all about?

The God of the Hebrew Scriptures doesn’t manipulate with terror—He draws with chesed (lovingkindness). Grounding morality in existential fear undermines the very heart of the Gospel. The teachings and life of Jesus embody this: not a religion of fear, but a way of life rooted in mercy, truth, justice, and love.

Genuine faith is not driven by fear but by love, compassion, and a desire to do what is right. A relationship with God grounded in terror does not foster trust, growth, or genuine devotion. Fear may produce temporary compliance, but it cannot produce love—and love is exactly what God asks of us: “Love the Lord your God… and love your neighbor as yourself” (Deuteronomy 6:5; Leviticus 19:18; see also Matthew 22:37–39).

Religion Motivated by Fear vs. Faith Inspired by Love

Take my Jewish friends—religious and secular alike—as a living example. Many of them follow a rigorous moral vision shaped by Jewish ethics. They give generously, often to complete strangers. They sacrifice for the sake of others. They work toward justice and peace—not because they fear hellfire, but because it is the right thing to do. Their lives alone dismantle the claim that morality depends on the threat of Eternal Conscious Torment.

If your moral behavior requires the menace of unending punishment to keep you in line, then love is not what motivates you—fear is. At that point, morality is not transformed character but external control. Strip away the threat, and the virtue collapses. That is not faith; it is coercion. It is obedience on a leash.

Genuine faith has never been about avoiding punishment. It is about becoming like the One who forgives, heals, restores, and loves to the very end. It is about internal transformation, not external terror.

As we saw at the beginning of this book, the apostles did not evangelize by threatening people with flames. They proclaimed Jesus—His resurrection, His fulfillment of Israel’s hope, His call to turn from idols, His offer of forgiveness and blessing, and His promise of honor and life in God’s restored world for those who are faithful. They appealed to hope, not horror; to life, not fear; to transformation, not terror.

Fear may restrain behavior. Only love can redeem a soul.

Conclusion

Evangelism, then, is not about scaring people into heaven. It is about proclaiming the goodness of God, the beauty of Christ, the power of His resurrection, and the hope of transformation. It is about inviting all people to step into the abundant life God offers here and now—and in the world to come. It’s about proclaiming life over death, healing over brokenness, light over darkness. It’s about inviting people to live the life they were created for—now and forever.

Hellfire was never the driving force of Apostolic evangelism. Life was. And it still should be today.

c3JV

Chapter 51
Becoming an Ambassador of God's Kingdom

If you consider yourself a true disciple of Christ, it’s also your responsibility to help care for God's flock. Just like in Jesus’ time, some may be stubborn, irritating, or even outright foolish, but you’re called to shepherd them with love. To follow Christ is not merely to confess His name. It is to carry His heart—and take up His cross.

Taking up your cross is not accomplished by defending doctrine on social media. It is about love in action. It’s about aligning your life with the sacrifice, compassion, justice, and mercy of Jesus. And you cannot do that while walking past the poor, the hurting, the oppressed, and the forgotten.

In Matthew 25, Jesus gives us a startling glimpse into what final judgment looks like—and it's not based on how well we articulated a statement of faith, but on how we treated “the least of these”:

"For I was hungry and you gave me something to eat, I was thirsty and you gave me something to drink, I was a stranger and you invited me in, I needed clothes and you clothed me, I was sick and you looked after me, I was in prison and you came to visit me.” Then the righteous will answer him, "Lord, when did we see you hungry and feed you...?" And the King will reply, “Truly I tell you, whatever you did for one of the least of these brothers and sisters of mine, you did for me.”

(Matthew 25:35–40)

These words strip Christianity down to its core: Love. Kindness. Compassion. They leave no room for performative religion or self-righteous faith. Jesus doesn’t ask if we accepted Him into our hearts or recited the sinner’s prayer. He asks if we saw Him in the faces of the broken, the invisible, and the outcast, and responded with love.

To ignore “the least of these” is, according to Jesus, to ignore Him. To love them is to love Him.

Thus, taking up the cross means living in a way that mirrors His own sacrificial love. It means showing mercy to those the world forgets, welcoming the outcast, feeding the hungry, visiting the prisoner, and giving dignity to the downtrodden.

This is the gospel. Not just preached—but lived.

The Danger of Outsourcing Compassion

We live in a world of outsourcing. We outsource our meals to delivery apps. Our learning to TikTok videos. Our friendships to digital platforms. And—without realizing it—we’ve begun outsourcing our compassion.

"I give a tithe at church."

“I pay my taxes.”

“I give to charity.”

“There are social services for that.”

These phrases may sound responsible. But they often function as spiritual escape hatches—ways to quiet the conscience without engaging the heart. But Jesus never outsourced compassion. He didn’t send angels to bind the wounds of the bleeding and the sick.

In His parable about the Samaritan—someone viewed as theologically impure, ethnically inferior, and socially suspect—it was the Samaritan, not the passing religious elite, who became the true reflection of God’s heart.

Institutional help has its place. But it is often cold, bureaucratic, and transactional. It does not look someone in the eye. It does not sit with them in their pain. It does not call them by their first names.

And here is the tragedy: when we outsource compassion, we don’t just rob them of love—we rob ourselves of transformation.

The shepherd isn’t simply a role; it’s a refining fire. It is only when we touch the wounds of another that we begin to be healed of our own indifference.

Caring for His sheep is not a task we perform once we’ve “mastered theology.” It is theology in motion. It is faith with hands. It is Christ made visible not through sermons but through service.

Because the scariest danger isn’t that we’ll deny Christ with our words; It’s that we’ll deny Him with our inaction.

I’m not speaking of dropping coins into an addict’s plastic cup to quietly fund their chains and enable their addiction. I’m speaking of offering a meal, a seat at the table, and a listening heart for their pain and struggle. If Jesus is absent from your actions, no pamphlet will make Him visible.

Why It Matters for You

This call to care for His underprivileged sheep isn’t just for their sake. It’s for yours.

Modern faith often assumes a dangerous lie: that obedience is a burden and love is a sacrifice made at our own expense. But the truth is more mysterious and beautiful: when we give ourselves to others, we are also given back to—renewed, expanded, awakened.

Acts of compassion are scientifically proven to reduce anxiety and depression, thus boost the immune system, and increase feelings of joy and purpose.146,147 Generosity activates pleasure centers in the brain. Helping others literally makes us happier. Better. Christ-like.

But beyond the neuroscience lies something deeper, something ancient: when we serve others, we are drawn closer to the heart of God. Helping the poor cultivates empathy—it stretches your soul to include another’s pain. It nurtures gratitude—suddenly, the small becomes sacred.

It births humility—you realize how little separates you from the one in need. It grows faith—you begin to see God not in church buildings, but in broken people. In the cracks of society.

Jesus said, “It is more blessed to give than to receive.” (Acts 20:35). This wasn’t just poetic idealism. It was a blueprint for spiritual transformation.

You were not made to be a spectator of holiness. You were made to carry it. To touch others with it. And in doing so, you will find the Kingdom of God breaking forth not just in them, but in you.

Why It Matters for Them

We often think of poverty in material terms—lack of food, shelter, income. But poverty wears many faces. It is hunger, yes, but it is also loneliness. Abandonment. Shame. Guilt. Feeling unseen and unloved in a world that moves too fast to notice.

Institutions can feed a stomach. But only love can feed a soul.

A government agency may give food stamps. A church program may deliver groceries. But when you look someone in the eyes, smile at them, hug them, and choose to sit beside them rather than shy away from them—that is when dignity is restored. That is when someone begins to believe, maybe for the first time, that they are loved. They get to see Christ through you.

Not because they cleaned up. Not because they repented first. Not because they got their act together. But because there’s a God who loves them so deeply, He sent someone like you to extend that love to them.

When you show up in someone’s story—not as a superhero, but as a servant or friend—you become a living parable of grace. You become the whisper of God’s compassion made flesh.

Many who have been helped by systems remain emotionally and spiritually untouched. But those who have been loved by a person—with consistency, humility, and kindness—are forever changed.

Because when you love someone personally, they stop being a “need” and start being a name. And in that place where heaven and earth meet, they encounter the Shepherd—not in theory, but through your hands.

Why It Matters for Society

We tend to treat compassion as something private—a personal virtue, a matter of individual character rather than a force capable of reshaping the world. History tells a very different story.

Every time compassion has moved from the margins to the center, societies have changed. Mountains have shifted. Entire civilizations have been redefined by people who chose love over indifference, presence over policy, neighborliness over nationalism. Yes, group identity matters. Laws and policies matter too—but only because they are meant to protect and serve human beings. The moment systems eclipse the people they exist for, they lose their moral legitimacy.

When Jesus called His followers to feed the hungry, clothe the naked, visit the imprisoned, and care for the sick, He was not offering a checklist for private piety. He was planting the seeds of a new world—the arrival of a kingdom where justice streams like a river and mercy falls gently like spring rain.

And this kind of world does not begin with politicians. It does not start with institutions or systems. It begins with people—fishermen, tax collectors, laborers, mothers, neighbors—ordinary individuals who see a stranger and choose to treat them as family. Because love, when practiced publicly, becomes contagious.

Children raised in homes where generosity is normal grow into adults who lead with kindness.

Churches that go out to serve rather than condemn become refuges for the wounded—not museums for the flawless.

Communities that value compassion over competition cultivate trust, safety, and resilience.

We live in a moment when society feels stretched to the breaking point—fractured by politics, fear, tribalism, and apathy. But imagine what could change if even a small number of people began living as though every human life truly mattered.

What would happen if we treated every person—friend, stranger, even enemy—as someone bearing the image of God, worthy of dignity, patience, and care?

That shift, even in a handful of lives, could begin to heal what feels irreparably broken. It could soften hardened spaces in our culture and ignite a quiet revolution of love in a world starving for it.

What if we stopped asking, “What do they deserve?”

And started asking, “How can I love them the way Christ loved me?” “How can I love my enemies the way Christ loved His?”

What would follow would not be a revival of louder hymns or grander religious events, but of life itself—flowing back into forgotten neighborhoods, wounded families, and neglected souls. That's what true revival looks like.

Jesus did not gather kings or elites around Him. He chose poor Galileans, the overlooked, the wounded—people broken enough to know they needed transformation.

That was the spirit. That was the force. That was the gospel before it became a Western institution—when it was lived openly, breathed daily, and practiced publicly by ordinary Jewish people and gentiles who joined them, transformed by an extraordinary Love.

All the believers were together and had everything in common. They sold property and possessions to give to anyone who had need. Every day they continued to meet together in the temple courts. They broke bread in their homes and ate together with glad and sincere hearts, praising God and enjoying the favor of all the people. And the Lord added to their number daily those who were being saved.

(Acts 2:42-27)

The Gospel has never changed, but the way we understand and practice it has. We just need to reconstruct and reclaim the ancient Spirit behind it.

Don’t Just Preach It. Live It.

In the end, the question is painfully simple: Can you truly claim to follow Jesus if you walk past the people He would have stopped for? He never said, “They will know you are my disciples by your perfect doctrine.” He said, “They will know you are my disciples by your love.” (John 13:35).

And love, real love, is never abstract. It breaks bread. It binds wounds. It gets interrupted. It gets involved. It feeds both stomachs and souls.

Before you stand on a street corner soapbox, love. Before you quote, care. Before you proselytize, empathize.

The world is not waiting for another sermon on end-times tribulation. It is starving for the kind of people who are a sermon. Not with clever words, but with consistent love. Not with megaphones, but with open hands.

Because faith—real faith—is not merely something you say or believe. It is how you love.

Conclusion

You’ve just discovered one of the deepest secrets of life: God designed us so that true joy is often found on the other side of sacrificial love. Lasting joy and healing don’t come from personal comfort or momentary pleasure—they spring from the love and purpose of giving ourselves to others.

To love means choosing someone else before yourself, letting go of ego and ease, and pursuing something greater than your own preferences. And it’s precisely there—in loving others—that we not only find divine purpose and fulfilling joy but also a place where our own wounded hearts heal the most.

In the end, it is love that gives life its deepest meaning—not by pressuring people into intellectual agreement, but by reaching into suffering and pulling people out of the hell they are living in here and now. And from that kind of meaning, real joy begins to rise—quietly, steadily, and unmistakably.

If you want to explore this vision of love more deeply, I invite you to read my book The Theology of Love: Christianity's Most Underrated Doctrine. I truly believe it has the power not just to inform your mind, but to soften and transform your heart.

c3NA

Final Conclusion

The gospel no longer needs to sound like this: “God is furious with you—but if you manage to hold the correct intellectual beliefs, then and only then He will refrain from burning and tormenting you forever.” That is not good news; it is fear dressed up as theology.

The gospel can finally sound like what it always was meant to be:

God loves you—before you understand, before you believe correctly, before you get anything right. You are not an object of divine rage, but the object of relentless mercy. God has already acted for you in Christ, not to threaten you with fire, but to rescue you from death, heal what is broken, and restore you to life. You are invited—not coerced—into a way of living that leads to wholeness, joy, peace, and purpose. This is not about passing a theology exam or escaping punishment, but about becoming fully alive, learning to love, and participating in the renewal of the world.

That is the Gospel: not fear of God, but trust in Him; not terror of judgment, but hope of restoration; not survival after death, but life—real life—beginning now.

Final Conclusion in Theological Language

The earliest believers proclaimed a gospel centered not on the individual escaping to a distant heavenly realm, but on collective resurrection—bodily, earthly, tangible resurrection—because that is what they saw happened to Jesus. Christ rose here, on this earth, in a transformed yet physical body (Luke 24:39–43; John 20:27–28). His resurrection was not a symbolic event, nor a spiritual metaphor, but—as Paul insisted—“the firstfruits of those who have fallen asleep” (1 Corinthians 15:20). Messiah was the first among many; what happened to Him will happen to all of us.

For the first followers of Christ, this was the core logic of salvation and the gospel they preached:

Death entered through Adam and affected us all; resurrection overcame it through Christ for the sake of us all (1 Corinthians 15:21–22).

Sin brought mortality; God’s remedy is immortality (2 Timothy 1:10), not disembodied escape.

This is why the Bible consistently frames 'salvation' in terms of the resurrection of the dead (e.g., John 5:28–29; Acts 24:15), not as a postmortem relocation to some distant realm opposite hell. “Heaven”—whatever nuances the term carries—was never imagined as a galaxy far away where souls escape the earth.

The biblical hope moves in the opposite direction. The New Jerusalem comes down to earth (Revelation 21:2). God does not abandon creation; He returns to it. He makes His dwelling among humanity, here—not elsewhere (Revelation 21:3). Salvation is not escape from the world, but the resurrection and renewal of it.

In both Jewish and early Christian tradition, the Garden of Eden in Genesis 2–3 is understood as an earthly location—a real place where Adam and Eve lived and walked with God. The text itself places Eden “in the east” (Genesis 2:8), with rivers flowing out from it and through recognizable regions such as Cush and Assyria (Genesis 2:10–14). Nothing in the biblical narrative suggests a celestial, non-physical realm. Eden is grounded, earthy, geographic. In the Bible’s own worldview, Eden was on earth.

In Second Temple Judaism, “heaven” (shamayim in Hebrew—literally “the skies”) was understood primarily as God’s dwelling place, not as an afterlife reward destination. The dominant Jewish hope was resurrection.

Put simply: God and the angels dwelt in heaven (the skies) above, humans lived on the earth, and the dead went down—awaiting resurrection. Salvation was never about escaping the world but about the three dimensions merging—a return to Eden—which was not “up there.” It was here. And God walked with us here.

The Underworld

After humanity’s exile from Eden, death enters the story (Genesis 3:19). And once death became part of the human experience, ancient people naturally imagined the dead as going downward—to the underworld—because that is precisely where bodies were laid. The Bible calls this realm "Sheol" or "Hades" (Psalm 139:8; Acts 2:27)—often translated to "hell" in English—the shadowy place beneath the earth where all the dead—righteous and wicked alike—went (Genesis 37:35; Job 7:9; Ecclesiastes 9:10).

Jacob expected to go to Sheol when he died (Genesis 37:35).

Job described Sheol as darkness and stillness, not torment (Job 10:21–22).

The Psalmist said, “The dead… go down to silence” (Psalm 115:17).

No flames. No Eternal Conscious Torment. Just the grave beneath us.

Heaven

Above the earth lay the realm of the heavens—shamayim—believed to be inhabited by celestial beings.

the Malachim (angels),

the host of heaven (1 Kings 22:19),

the “sons of God” (Job 1:6),

and the divine council (Psalm 82:1).

And above them all, as Israel testified, stood the Lord of lords, the Maker of heaven and earth (Deuteronomy 10:17; Psalm 115:15)—Yahweh, the Holy One of Israel.

This three-tiered worldview—heavens above, earth here, Sheol below—shaped the imagination of the biblical authors, the ancient Israelites, and the earliest followers of Jesus. Their hope was never escape from this structure, but its healing. Salvation meant the reunion of what had been fractured—the restoration of heaven and earth back into harmony, as it was in Eden, back when God still walked the earth (Genesis 3:8).

This is precisely why Jesus ascends into heaven. His ascension is not an exit strategy from the world, but a declaration of its destiny. Heaven and earth are not meant to remain separated; they are moving toward reunion. And the way that reunion happens is not through domination, conquest, or force—but through self-giving love. In ascending, Jesus reveals that sacrificial love is the true bridge between heaven and earth, the path by which humanity is lifted and creation is restored.
