The “New Jerusalem” does not float believers away to heaven; instead, heaven descends to earth (Revelation 21:2).

The movement is always the same: the dead are raised back here and God comes down here, not us escaping elsewhere. He came to Eden. He came again as Jesus. He will bring down the New Jerusalem. It's all here.

This ancient pattern—Eden on earth, the grave below, the heavens above—set the stage for the earliest Christian proclamation: That Christ rose from the dead—here on earth—and therefore we will too (1 Corinthians 15:20–23). That was the gospel: world restoration. Tikkun Olam.

Paul’s entire argument in 1 Corinthians 15 hinges on this:

Since Christ was raised bodily, then all of us, too, will be raised bodily—not to float away, but to live renewed in God’s restored creation.

The Hebrew prophets envisioned the same hope:

“Your dead shall live; their bodies shall rise” (Isaiah 26:19, see also Daniel 12:2).

Early Christians carried on this same hope—not of escape, but of resurrection.

Thus, in the first and second centuries, the Gospel sounded like this:

"The wages of sin is death" (Romans 6:23), not afterlife torment.

Death is the enemy. (1 Cor. 15:26)

We have all been dying since Adam. (1 Cor. 15:22)

But we will all be made alive because of Christ. (1 Cor. 15:22)

Heaven comes here. (Revelation 21:2)

Christ resurrected here. (Matthew 28:6)

Thus, our resurrection happens here.

Hell—at least in the later medieval sense of endless fiery torment—was not part of this picture. The earliest Christian preaching centered on life from the dead, not an escape from Eternal Conscious Torment.

The Gospel is simple:

Because Christ rose, we will rise.

Because He lives, death will die.

And the world that began in an earthly garden will be restored in a renewed creation—“a new heaven and a new earth” (Isaiah 65:17; Revelation 21:1).

The earliest Church Fathers echoed this earthy, concrete hope:

Ignatius of Antioch (early 2nd century) wrote that we “shall rise again in the flesh, even as Christ has risen.”148

Justin Martyr insisted that the Christian hope is “the resurrection of the body,” rejecting the notion of purely spiritual salvation as a distortion influenced by Gnosticism.149

Irenaeus, representing the mainstream second-century church, taught that God intends to “renew creation itself,” and that resurrection means inheriting a restored earth, not fleeing it.150

In this worldview, the problem was death—“the last enemy.” The solution was life—immortal, resurrected, restored, eternal life that no illness or disease could destroy.

The gospel is so simple that even children can understand it:

Christ died and rose again, and so we too will die and rise again.

And when He returns, God will restore all things (Acts 3:21).

Heaven does not whisk us away; heaven comes down (Revelation 21:2).

God does not abandon creation; He renews it (Romans 8:19–21).

Each of us will be judged and rewarded based on our past on Earth (Matthew 16:27, Romans 2:6, 2 Corinthians 5:10, Revelation 20:12).

Hellfire—as later imagined in Western medieval theology—was simply not part of the original message.

Final Conclusion in My Own Words

You are not saved because of your theological precision. You are saved because Christ saved the world you are part of. Salvation rests on what He has done, not on how accurately your brain manage to understand it.

Jesus is not owned by the Baptists.

Jesus does not belong to the Pentecostals.

Jesus is not the property of Calvinists or the Arminians.

He isn’t Catholic nor Orthodox.

He does not belong to any church, any system, any theological institution.

You will not find Him behind a pulpit wearing a suit, nor teaching systematic theology at a seminary.

And yet—Jesus is near. Closer than breath.

He meets people. All kinds of people. In all kinds of places. At all hours of the day and night.

He meets the poor in spirit.

He meets those who mourn.

He meets those who hunger and thirst for righteousness.

He meets the merciful.

He meets the pure of heart.

He meets the peacemakers.

He meets the persecuted—those who suffer for the sake of justice and truth.

This is where He walks.

Not in the corridors of power, but on the dusty roads of longing hearts.

Not in religious ivory towers, but with broken people.

Not in echo chambers of doctrine, but in the silence of those who ache.

So if you wish to truly meet Jesus in the deepest way—you may have to leave your theological tribe.

You may have to unlearn your inherited system.

You may have to deconstruct man-made doctrine.

You may have to let go of the illusion that your church denomination has a monopoly on the Messiah.

Because He is not owned. He is not contained.

He is given—to the world.

And He walks freely among those who are open, humble, hurting, and hungry.

This is where the Son of Man still walks today.

And you don’t have to earn Jesus or His forgiveness through intellectual knowledge or religious deeds.

You’ve already been forgiven.

That is not a possibility.

Not a wish.

Not a future condition.

It is a fact sealed in history.

When Jesus declared, “It is finished” (John 19:30), something cosmic closed forever: the effect of sin, the record of debt, the lie that God’s love must be earned, the fear that we must twist His arm through rituals, guilt, or begging for forgiveness.

Christ did not go to the cross to change God’s mind about you.

He went to the cross to change your mind about God.

But forgiveness of sins is not the end of the story.

It is the beginning of a new one—yours!

Now comes response.

Now comes invitation.

Not to crawl under the weight of guilt, but to rise under the lift of grace.

Not to tremble in fear, but to walk fueled by love.

True repentance is not groveling, self-flagellation, or dutiful box-checking. It is turning—toward home, toward healing, toward a truer way of being. It is choosing the road Jesus walked: the narrow path of mercy, humility, courage, and self-giving love.

Jesus came not only to forgive, but to show us how to live forgiveness.

He didn’t just teach “love your enemies”—He gave His life for His enemies and prayed for their forgiveness even as they killed Him, so they too could be resurrected.

That is salvation. Not escapism, but becoming whole.

Not fleeing everlasting hellfire torment, but entering eternal, divine life.

Not earning afterlife heaven, but participating in His kingdom here and now—in the dust, the pain, the beauty of earth.

You just need to tap into eternal life—not endless duration, but cup overflowing.

It’s about a divine quality—a life that’s radiant, abundant, and deeply rooted in God’s very essence.

And it begins now.

“This is eternal life: that they know You…” (John 17:3).

Not a theological exam—a relationship so transformative that love begins to take on flesh in us.

The Gospel is not an evacuation plan.

It is creation remade.

It is God putting all things right.

Yes, sin still matters.

It fractures us.

It blocks blessing.

It destroys lives and relationships.

It poisons the soul.

But forgiveness is medicine.

Healing begins when we receive it—when we let grace seep into the bones and reshape how we think, act, and live.

Grace is not passive or an abstract idea.

Grace is power. The motivation to forgive others.

Salvation awakens the heart, renews the mind, and spills outward in action.

It feeds the hungry.

Clothes the naked.

Forgives the guilty.

Welcomes the outsider.

This is the Kingdom of God—not a future address, but a present revolution of love.

And yes, choices have consequences.

Those who cling to hatred, deception, pride, and cruelty will taste the bitter fruit of those choices.

Not because God delights in punishment, but because rejecting love is its own collapse. Because sin needs to be purged from the sinner.

Sin brings death—not by divine sadism but by spiritual gravity.

Yet even judgment is medicinal at its core: not to destroy, but to restore.

Every road of suffering is a road that leads back home.

The resurrection was not just one man rising. It was the first light of a world being remade. A promise that what happened to Him will happen to humanity—to those who you loved and are gone.

So we evangelize so “that they may have life, and have it abundantly.” (John 10:10)

Because we are not here to frighten people out of hell—we are here to awaken them to Love.

Because love must share itself.

Because we want to expand God's kingdom.

Because following Jesus means becoming a doorway through which others glimpse the Kingdom.

To follow Christ is to feed His sheep, to carry your cross, to choose love when it hurts, peace when it costs, and compassion when it’s inconvenient.

It means you don’t just believe the Gospel—you become it.

You become good news in human form—an ambassador.

In the end, salvation is not about passing a final exam or sneaking into heaven before the gates close.

It is about becoming fully human—fully alive—fully like Christ.

And that journey does not begin at death.

It begins now.

So come.

Step into the life of God.

Let grace be your breath.

Let love be your legacy.

Because the world does not need more doctrine, more labels, or more conflict.

It needs more Jesus.

And the only Jesus it may ever see—is the one it sees in you.

c3US

Afterword

I have a small favor to ask before you close this book (or turn off your e-reader). I've dedicated significant time and effort to creating this book (in a language not native to me). However, it has been my sad experience that Calvinists leave negative—sometimes outright malicious—reviews on my books without even bothering to read them first. Therefore, if you have found enjoyment and education in the book—and it's completely fine if we don't see eye-to-eye on every detail—please consider expressing your appreciation by sparing a few seconds to rate it on Amazon and/or Goodreads. If you also have two minutes to spare, leaving a short review would mean the world to me! As an indie-author I make it a point to read every review. If you want to go the extra mile, then feel free to post a photo of the book and recommend it on social media, or even get copies for friends!

Thank you so much for your attention and consideration, and please don't forget to check out my other books!

Dr. Eitan Bar

P.S. If you’d like to help me spread this grace-based restorative gospel of universal reconciliation—especially among the Jewish people in Hebrew/Israel (because no one else does!)—please visit www.eitan.bar/give or feel free to get in touch.
